What Is Kalam?

6 min
Mu'tazili Definitions of Knowledge and Their Invalidation
Nasafi reports and invalidates Mu'tazili definitions of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili definitions of knowledge presented by al-Ka'bi and Abu Hashim, and why Nasafi invalidates them.

Core Idea
Knowledge as mere belief is insufficient; Nasafi rejects Mu'tazili definitions.

This lesson examines the Mu'tazili approach to defining knowledge, as reported by Nasafi. Al-Ka'bi defines knowledge as 'belief of a thing as it is', and Abu Hashim adds 'tranquility of the soul'. Nasafi argues these definitions are inadequate. You'll see how Nasafi sets up his own preferred definition by first clearing the ground.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Mu'tazili definitions
The passage presents two definitions. Read the Arabic and translation, noting the specific wording: 'اعتقاد' for al-Ka'bi, and 'سكون النفس' added by Abu Hashim.
Notice that Nasafi mentions these definitions critically.
2
Identify Nasafi's judgment
The passage says 'ويبطل هذه التعريفات' — he invalidates them. Ask: Why might belief alone be insufficient for knowledge?
This connects to later kalam discussions about certainty.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this rejection sets the stage for alternative definitions from Ash'ari and Maturidi, which Nasafi will later evaluate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يبدأ في الحديث عن العلم, فيحدد معنى العلم, مناقشا بإطناب شديد أقوال المعتزلة من ذلك مثلا قول الكعبي بأن العلم 'اعتقاد' الشيء على ما هو به ونفس التعريف لأبي هاشم بإضافة سكون النفس إليه - ويبطل هذه التعريفات ثم يذكر تعريفات الأشاعرة وقد اختار منها قول الأشعري أن العلم هو الوصف الذي من قام به كان عالما, وقال في هذا التعريف: إنه شامل يطرد 'وينعكس إلا أن صاحب المواقف يقول في ص ١٠: 'إنه تعريف يؤدي إلى الدور ثم يعرض أبو المعين تعريفا لأبي منصور الماتريدي بأن العلم صفة يتجلى بها المذكور لمن قامت هي به ومعنى هذا التعريف كما يقول زين الدين قاسم في حاشيته على المسايرة ص ١١ أي صفة ينكشف بها ما يذكر, ويلتفت إليه انكشافا تاما لمن قامت به تلك الصفة والنسفي يختار هذا التعريف للماتريدي, ويقول بتعريف يتفق مع الأشعري, وهو : أن العلم ما يوجب كون من قام به كان عالما, أو هو الوصف الذي من قام به كان عالما. والعلم الذي يقصده هو علم المخلوقين خواصهم لا عوامهم, لأنهم هم المتفرغون للتأمل والبحث.
After that, he begins to discuss knowledge, and he determines the meaning of knowledge, discussing at great length the opinions of the Mu'tazila, for example the statement of al-Ka'bi that knowledge is 'belief' of a thing as it is, and the same definition by Abu Hashim with the addition of 'tranquility of the soul' to it. And he invalidates these definitions. Then he mentions the definitions of the Ash'aris, and from them he chose the statement of al-Ash'ari that knowledge is the description by virtue of which whoever possesses it is knowing. And he said about this definition: It is comprehensive, applicable and converse, except that the author of al-Mawaqif says on p. 10: 'It is a definition that leads to circularity.' Then Abu al-Mu'in presents a definition from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: that knowledge is an attribute by which the mentioned thing is manifested to the one in whom it subsists. And the meaning of this definition, as Zayn al-Din Qasim says in his gloss on al-Musayara, p. 11, is an attribute by which what is mentioned is disclosed and attended to with complete disclosure for the one who possesses that attribute. And al-Nasafi chooses this definition of al-Maturidi, and he states a definition that agrees with al-Ash'ari, which is: that knowledge is what necessitates that whoever possesses it is knowing, or it is the description by virtue of which whoever possesses it is knowing. And the knowledge he intends is the knowledge of the elite among creatures, not the common people, because they are the ones who devote themselves to reflection and research.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a comparative discussion of various definitions of knowledge ('ilm) in kalam. It begins by reporting the Mu'tazili definitions: al-Ka'bi defines knowledge as 'belief of a thing as it is', and Abu Hashim adds 'tranquility of the soul'. Nasafi invalidates these. Then it presents the Ash'ari definition by al-Ash'ari: knowledge is a description (wasf) that makes its possessor knowing. A criticism from al-Mawaqif is noted: this definition leads to circularity. Then Nasafi presents the Maturidi definition from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: knowledge is an attribute (sifa) by which the known thing is manifested to the knower. This definition is explained further by Zayn al-Din Qasim. Nasafi chooses the Maturidi definition as his preference but also states a definition that agrees with al-Ash'ari. Finally, he specifies that the knowledge under discussion is that of the elite scholars who are devoted to reflection and research, not the common people.

Key Terms
العلم
الاعتقاد
سكون النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think knowledge is simply correct belief.
Correction: Nasafi shows that belief alone is not sufficient, as Mu'tazili definitions are invalidated; true knowledge requires an attribute that makes the knower knowing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Abu Hashim add to al-Ka'bi's definition?
A) Certainty
B) Tranquility of the soul
C) Correspondence
D) Clarity

Key Takeaway
Nasafi invalidates the Mu'tazili definitions of knowledge as belief or belief with tranquility, showing they are insufficient for a robust account of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mistaken a deeply held belief for certain knowledge, only to later discover it was wrong? How does Nasafi's rejection of the Mu'tazili definitions expose the danger of relying on inner conviction alone as proof of truth? What specific belief do you hold today that you need to re-examine in light of what this lesson teaches about the insufficiency of mere belief?
?
Think of a time when you felt 'tranquility of the soul' about a religious matter—perhaps a practice or a doctrine. Was that feeling a reliable guide to the truth? How does Abu Hashim's addition of sukun al-nafs challenge you to distinguish between psychological comfort and genuine knowledge from revelation? Will you now test your tranquil beliefs against the Quran and Sunnah?
?
Both al-Ka'bi and Abu Hashim were intelligent, sincere scholars, yet their definitions of knowledge were invalidated by Nasafi. What does this tell you about the limits of human intellect in defining even basic concepts? Are there areas in your life where you rely on your own reasoning rather than submitting to divine guidance? How will you change this starting today?

Ash'ari and Maturidi Definitions: Nasafi's Preference
Nasafi presents and evaluates Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions, preferring the latter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions of knowledge, the criticism of circularity against the Ash'ari definition, and why Nasafi prefers al-Maturidi's definition.

Core Idea
Nasafi prefers al-Maturidi's definition: knowledge as an attribute that reveals the known.

After rejecting Mu'tazili definitions, Nasafi turns to the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. He presents al-Ash'ari's definition (knowledge is a description making one knowing), notes a criticism of circularity, then presents al-Maturidi's definition (knowledge is an attribute that manifests the known). Nasafi clearly chooses the Maturidi definition while also stating a definition that agrees with al-Ash'ari. This lesson clarifies his final position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari definition and its criticism
Al-Ash'ari defines knowledge as 'the description by which whoever possesses it is knowing'. Note that al-Mawaqif criticizes this as circular. Consider why.
Circular because 'knowing' is defined by knowledge, and knowledge by 'knowing'.
2
Read the Maturidi definition
Al-Maturidi defines knowledge as 'an attribute by which the mentioned thing is manifested to the one in whom it subsists'. Compare this to the Ash'ari definition.
This definition avoids circularity by focusing on manifestation.
3
Identify Nasafi's choice
The passage explicitly says: 'والنسفي يختار هذا التعريف للماتريدي' — Nasafi chooses al-Maturidi's definition. Also note he states a definition that agrees with al-Ash'ari, indicating compatibility.
Nasafi's final view synthesizes the two schools.
4
Understand the scope of knowledge
Nasafi specifies that this discussion applies to the knowledge of the elite, not common people, who are devoted to reflection and research.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يبدأ في الحديث عن العلم, فيحدد معنى العلم, مناقشا بإطناب شديد أقوال المعتزلة من ذلك مثلا قول الكعبي بأن العلم 'اعتقاد' الشيء على ما هو به ونفس التعريف لأبي هاشم بإضافة سكون النفس إليه - ويبطل هذه التعريفات ثم يذكر تعريفات الأشاعرة وقد اختار منها قول الأشعري أن العلم هو الوصف الذي من قام به كان عالما, وقال في هذا التعريف: إنه شامل يطرد 'وينعكس إلا أن صاحب المواقف يقول في ص ١٠: 'إنه تعريف يؤدي إلى الدور ثم يعرض أبو المعين تعريفا لأبي منصور الماتريدي بأن العلم صفة يتجلى بها المذكور لمن قامت هي به ومعنى هذا التعريف كما يقول زين الدين قاسم في حاشيته على المسايرة ص ١١ أي صفة ينكشف بها ما يذكر, ويلتفت إليه انكشافا تاما لمن قامت به تلك الصفة والنسفي يختار هذا التعريف للماتريدي, ويقول بتعريف يتفق مع الأشعري, وهو : أن العلم ما يوجب كون من قام به كان عالما, أو هو الوصف الذي من قام به كان عالما. والعلم الذي يقصده هو علم المخلوقين خواصهم لا عوامهم, لأنهم هم المتفرغون للتأمل والبحث.
After that, he begins to discuss knowledge, and he determines the meaning of knowledge, discussing at great length the opinions of the Mu'tazila, for example the statement of al-Ka'bi that knowledge is 'belief' of a thing as it is, and the same definition by Abu Hashim with the addition of 'tranquility of the soul' to it. And he invalidates these definitions. Then he mentions the definitions of the Ash'aris, and from them he chose the statement of al-Ash'ari that knowledge is the description by virtue of which whoever possesses it is knowing. And he said about this definition: It is comprehensive, applicable and converse, except that the author of al-Mawaqif says on p. 10: 'It is a definition that leads to circularity.' Then Abu al-Mu'in presents a definition from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: that knowledge is an attribute by which the mentioned thing is manifested to the one in whom it subsists. And the meaning of this definition, as Zayn al-Din Qasim says in his gloss on al-Musayara, p. 11, is an attribute by which what is mentioned is disclosed and attended to with complete disclosure for the one who possesses that attribute. And al-Nasafi chooses this definition of al-Maturidi, and he states a definition that agrees with al-Ash'ari, which is: that knowledge is what necessitates that whoever possesses it is knowing, or it is the description by virtue of which whoever possesses it is knowing. And the knowledge he intends is the knowledge of the elite among creatures, not the common people, because they are the ones who devote themselves to reflection and research.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a comparative discussion of various definitions of knowledge ('ilm) in kalam. It begins by reporting the Mu'tazili definitions: al-Ka'bi defines knowledge as 'belief of a thing as it is', and Abu Hashim adds 'tranquility of the soul'. Nasafi invalidates these. Then it presents the Ash'ari definition by al-Ash'ari: knowledge is a description (wasf) that makes its possessor knowing. A criticism from al-Mawaqif is noted: this definition leads to circularity. Then Nasafi presents the Maturidi definition from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: knowledge is an attribute (sifa) by which the known thing is manifested to the knower. This definition is explained further by Zayn al-Din Qasim. Nasafi chooses the Maturidi definition as his preference but also states a definition that agrees with al-Ash'ari. Finally, he specifies that the knowledge under discussion is that of the elite scholars who are devoted to reflection and research, not the common people.

Key Terms
الوصف
الصفة
التجلي
الانكشاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Nasafi adopts multiple contradictory definitions.
Correction: Nasafi clearly prefers al-Maturidi's definition but also states a definition agreeing with al-Ash'ari, showing compatibility rather than contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which definition of knowledge does al-Nasafi ultimately choose as his own?
A) Al-Ka'bi's definition (knowledge as belief)
B) Abu Hashim's definition (belief with tranquility)
C) Al-Ash'ari's definition (description making one knower)
D) Al-Maturidi's definition (attribute revealing the known)

Key Takeaway
Nasafi prefers al-Maturidi's definition of knowledge as an attribute that manifests the known, and he specifies that this applies to the knowledge of the elite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Ash'ari definition — 'knowledge is the description by which whoever possesses it is knowing' — do you recognize a danger in being content with just the label of 'knowing' without the reality of the known manifesting in your heart? What if Allah questions you not about your definition, but about how deeply the truth has taken hold of you? Commit today to one specific step you will take to move from a formal acknowledgment of truth to a living, visible manifestation of it in your character and actions.
?
Al-Nasafi specifies that the knowledge he intends is for the elite who devote themselves to reflection and research. Are you among the elite in your pursuit of knowledge, or have you settled for a superficial acquaintance with religious truths? If you were to die tonight, would you say you have truly reflected on this definition of knowledge, or have you merely read it as another piece of information? Write down one area of your life where you have stopped at 'knowing about' rather than 'letting the known manifest'.
?
The criticism that al-Ash'ari's definition is circular warns us that our understanding of knowledge can become self-referential, defined by itself rather than by its object. In your own life, is your 'knowledge' of Allah, the Hereafter, or the Qur'an truly something that opens you up to the reality of what is known, or does it trap you in a cycle of intellectual habit that changes nothing? Pray right now that Allah would grant you knowledge that manifests, not just labels.

Critiquing the Mu'tazilite Definitions of Knowledge
Nasafi presents and invalidates two Mu'tazilite definitions of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi invalidated the Mu'tazilite definitions of knowledge as belief and belief with tranquility of the soul.

Core Idea
Knowledge is more than mere belief; it requires an attribute of certainty.

Before presenting his preferred definition, al-Nasafi first engages with and refutes the definitions proposed by the Mu'tazila. This lesson examines their claims and his reasons for rejecting them, setting the stage for a sounder account of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the Mu'tazilite definitions and Nasafi's invalidation. Notice the specific terms used.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find al-Ka'bi's definition (knowledge as belief of a thing as it is) and Abu Hashim's addition of tranquility of the soul.
3
Understand the critique
The passage says Nasafi 'invalidates' these definitions. Consider why belief might not be sufficient for knowledge, especially in theological contexts.
Belief can be mistaken or not fully assured.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about what definition of knowledge would avoid these problems. This will connect to the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يبدأ في الحديث عن العلم، فيحدد معنى العلم، مناقشا بإطناب شديد أقوال المعتزلة من ذلك مثلا قول الكعبي بأن العلم "اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به" ونفس التعريف لأبي هاشم بإضافة سكون النفس إليه - ويبطل هذه التعريفات ثم يذكر تعريفات الأشاعرة وقد اختار منها قول الأشعري أن العلم هو الوصف الذي من قام به كان عالما، وقال في هذا التعريف: إنه شامل يطرد "وينعكس إلا أن صاحب المواقف يقول في ص ١٠: "إنه تعريف يؤدي إلى الدور ثم يعرض أبو المعين تعريفا لأبي منصور الماتريدي بأن العلم صفة يتجلى بها المذكور لمن قامت هي به ومعنى هذا التعريف كما يقول زين الدين قاسم في حاشيته على المسايرة ص ۱۱ أي صفة ينكشف بها ما يذكر، ويلتفت إليه انكشافا تاما لمن قامت به تلك الصفة والنسفي يختار هذا التعريف للماتريدي، ويقول بتعريف يتفق مع الأشعري، وهو : أن العلم ما يوجب كون من قام به كان عالما، أو هو الوصف الذي من قام به كان عالما. والعلم الذي يقصده هو علم المخلوقين خواصهم لا عوامهم، لأنهم هم المتفرغون للتأمل والبحث.
And after that he begins to speak about knowledge (al-ilm), defining the meaning of knowledge, discussing at great length the statements of the Mu'tazila, for example the statement of al-Ka'bi that knowledge is 'belief of a thing as it is,' and the same definition by Abu Hashim with the addition of 'tranquility of the soul' to it — and he invalidates these definitions. Then he mentions the definitions of the Ash'aris, and he selected from them the statement of al-Ash'ari that knowledge is the attribute by which whoever possesses it is knowing. He said concerning this definition: it is comprehensive, applying to all cases and excluding only that which is not knowledge, except that the author of al-Mawaqif says on page 10: 'It is a definition that leads to circularity.' Then Abu al-Mu'in presents a definition from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: that knowledge is an attribute by which the mentioned thing is manifested to the one who possesses it. The meaning of this definition, as Zain al-Din Qasim says in his gloss on al-Musayara, p. 11, is: it is an attribute by which what is mentioned is disclosed and turned to, with complete disclosure, to the one who possesses that attribute. And al-Nasafi chooses this definition of al-Maturidi, and he states a definition that agrees with al-Ash'ari, which is: knowledge is what necessitates that the one who possesses it is knowing, or it is the attribute by which whoever possesses it is knowing. And the knowledge he intends is the knowledge of created beings, their elite, not their common people, because they are the ones who devote themselves to reflection and research.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a report of Imam al-Nasafi's treatment of the definition of knowledge (ilm) as presented by the modern author. It begins by noting that after his methodology, Nasafi discusses the definition of knowledge. He first criticizes the Mu'tazilite definitions: al-Ka'bi defined ilm as 'belief of a thing as it is,' and Abu Hashim added 'tranquility of the soul' to that. Nasafi invalidates these definitions. He then turns to Ash'ari definitions, selecting al-Ash'ari's definition that knowledge is 'the attribute by which whoever possesses it is knowing.' However, Nasafi notes that this definition is comprehensive and consistent, but the author of al-Mawaqif (al-Jurjani) objects that it leads to circularity. Next, Nasafi presents al-Maturidi's definition: knowledge is an attribute by which the mentioned thing is manifested to the possessor. The modern author clarifies this via Zain al-Din Qasim: it is an attribute by which what is mentioned is fully disclosed to the one who has it. Nasafi ultimately chooses al-Maturidi's definition but also offers a definition that agrees with al-Ash'ari: knowledge is what necessitates that the possessor is knowing, or the attribute by which the possessor is knowing. Finally, the passage notes that the knowledge intended here is that of the elite among created beings, not the common people, because the elite are the ones who devote themselves to reflection and research.

Key Terms
العلم (knowledge)
اعتقاد (belief)
سكون النفس (tranquility of the soul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that knowledge is simply correct belief (i'tiqad sahih).
Correction: Nasafi rejects this because belief, even if correct, is not necessarily knowledge unless it is accompanied by a stable attribute. The Mu'tazilite definitions fail because they reduce knowledge to a mental state that can be transient or not grounded in certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following definitions of knowledge does al-Nasafi invalidate?
A) Al-Ash'ari's attribute definition
B) Al-Maturidi's manifestation definition
C) Al-Ka'bi's belief definition and Abu Hashim's addition
D) The definition from the author of al-Mawaqif

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi rejects the Mu'tazilite reduction of knowledge to belief, because belief alone does not constitute knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'I know' about matters of faith—but is your knowledge merely a comfortable belief you were raised with, or have you subjected it to the scrutiny that eliminates error? What would it mean for you if many of your 'certainties' turned out to be no more than al-Ka'bi's 'belief of a thing as it is'?
?
Think of one Islamic truth you hold most firmly. If someone asked you to prove it with the kind of rigorous definition Nasafi demands, could you? Or is your confidence based on tranquility of the soul rather than an unshakeable attribute?
?
Nasafi invalidates definitions that lack true certainty. Will you settle for a faith that is merely believed without being verified? Today, commit to identifying one belief you hold without solid evidence and take a concrete step to ground it in reliable knowledge.

intermediate
6 min
The Ash'ari Definition and the Charge of Circularity
Nasafi presents al-Ash'ari's definition and notes the circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's definition of knowledge as an attribute and the objection that it leads to circularity (dawr).

Core Idea
Defining knowledge as 'the attribute by which one is knowing' risks circularity.

After rejecting Mu'tazilite definitions, Nasafi turns to al-Ash'ari's definition. While he acknowledges its comprehensiveness, he also notes a serious logical objection raised by the author of al-Mawaqif: that it is circular. This lesson examines the definition and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic excerpt containing al-Ash'ari's definition and the circularity objection.
2
Identify the definition
Al-Ash'ari defines knowledge as 'the attribute by which whoever possesses it is knowing.'
3
Understand the objection
The author of al-Mawaqif says this definition leads to circularity (dawr). Circularity occurs when a term is defined using itself or a synonym.
If 'knowledge' is defined using 'knowing,' then the definition presupposes what it defines.
4
Reflect on why this matters
A definition should be clearer than the term defined. If it is circular, it fails to inform. This critique motivates seeking a better definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يبدأ في الحديث عن العلم، فيحدد معنى العلم، مناقشا بإطناب شديد أقوال المعتزلة من ذلك مثلا قول الكعبي بأن العلم "اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به" ونفس التعريف لأبي هاشم بإضافة سكون النفس إليه - ويبطل هذه التعريفات ثم يذكر تعريفات الأشاعرة وقد اختار منها قول الأشعري أن العلم هو الوصف الذي من قام به كان عالما، وقال في هذا التعريف: إنه شامل يطرد "وينعكس إلا أن صاحب المواقف يقول في ص ١٠: "إنه تعريف يؤدي إلى الدور ثم يعرض أبو المعين تعريفا لأبي منصور الماتريدي بأن العلم صفة يتجلى بها المذكور لمن قامت هي به ومعنى هذا التعريف كما يقول زين الدين قاسم في حاشيته على المسايرة ص ۱۱ أي صفة ينكشف بها ما يذكر، ويلتفت إليه انكشافا تاما لمن قامت به تلك الصفة والنسفي يختار هذا التعريف للماتريدي، ويقول بتعريف يتفق مع الأشعري، وهو : أن العلم ما يوجب كون من قام به كان عالما، أو هو الوصف الذي من قام به كان عالما. والعلم الذي يقصده هو علم المخلوقين خواصهم لا عوامهم، لأنهم هم المتفرغون للتأمل والبحث.
And after that he begins to speak about knowledge (al-ilm), defining the meaning of knowledge, discussing at great length the statements of the Mu'tazila, for example the statement of al-Ka'bi that knowledge is 'belief of a thing as it is,' and the same definition by Abu Hashim with the addition of 'tranquility of the soul' to it — and he invalidates these definitions. Then he mentions the definitions of the Ash'aris, and he selected from them the statement of al-Ash'ari that knowledge is the attribute by which whoever possesses it is knowing. He said concerning this definition: it is comprehensive, applying to all cases and excluding only that which is not knowledge, except that the author of al-Mawaqif says on page 10: 'It is a definition that leads to circularity.' Then Abu al-Mu'in presents a definition from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: that knowledge is an attribute by which the mentioned thing is manifested to the one who possesses it. The meaning of this definition, as Zain al-Din Qasim says in his gloss on al-Musayara, p. 11, is: it is an attribute by which what is mentioned is disclosed and turned to, with complete disclosure, to the one who possesses that attribute. And al-Nasafi chooses this definition of al-Maturidi, and he states a definition that agrees with al-Ash'ari, which is: knowledge is what necessitates that the one who possesses it is knowing, or it is the attribute by which whoever possesses it is knowing. And the knowledge he intends is the knowledge of created beings, their elite, not their common people, because they are the ones who devote themselves to reflection and research.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a report of Imam al-Nasafi's treatment of the definition of knowledge (ilm) as presented by the modern author. It begins by noting that after his methodology, Nasafi discusses the definition of knowledge. He first criticizes the Mu'tazilite definitions: al-Ka'bi defined ilm as 'belief of a thing as it is,' and Abu Hashim added 'tranquility of the soul' to that. Nasafi invalidates these definitions. He then turns to Ash'ari definitions, selecting al-Ash'ari's definition that knowledge is 'the attribute by which whoever possesses it is knowing.' However, Nasafi notes that this definition is comprehensive and consistent, but the author of al-Mawaqif (al-Jurjani) objects that it leads to circularity. Next, Nasafi presents al-Maturidi's definition: knowledge is an attribute by which the mentioned thing is manifested to the possessor. The modern author clarifies this via Zain al-Din Qasim: it is an attribute by which what is mentioned is fully disclosed to the one who has it. Nasafi ultimately chooses al-Maturidi's definition but also offers a definition that agrees with al-Ash'ari: knowledge is what necessitates that the possessor is knowing, or the attribute by which the possessor is knowing. Finally, the passage notes that the knowledge intended here is that of the elite among created beings, not the common people, because the elite are the ones who devote themselves to reflection and research.

Key Terms
صفة (attribute)
دور (circularity)
العلم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any definition using a cognate term is acceptable.
Correction: In logic, definitions should avoid circularity; using a term derived from the definiendum makes the definition uninformative.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what objection is raised against al-Ash'ari's definition?
A) It is too narrow
B) It is not comprehensive
C) It leads to circularity
D) It is not exclusive

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's definition, though comprehensive, is criticized for circularity because it defines knowledge using the term 'knowing.'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about yourself (e.g., 'I am knowledgeable') that relied on the very thing you were trying to prove? Pause and identify such a circular assumption in your own thinking today. Commit to breaking that pattern before you move on.
?
The definition of knowledge itself can be circular. How often do you seek knowledge without questioning whether your starting assumptions are sound? Write down one foundational belief you hold about knowledge that you have never examined. Will you examine it today?
?
Al-Ash'ari's definition was comprehensive yet flawed. Is there an area of your faith where you have accepted a comprehensive-sounding explanation without checking for internal consistency? Name it and state one step you will take to verify it properly.

Al-Maturidi's Definition and Nasafi's Synthesis
Nasafi presents al-Maturidi's definition, chooses it, and offers a compatible Ash'ari formulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's definition of knowledge as an attribute of disclosure and how al-Nasafi combines it with an Ash'ari-compatible formulation.

Core Idea
Knowledge is an attribute that reveals the known object to the knower.

Having reviewed and critiqued previous definitions, Nasafi presents the Maturidi definition, which emphasizes disclosure (inkishaf). He ultimately chooses this definition but also offers a version that aligns with al-Ash'ari's wording, thus synthesizing both traditions.

This passage is a report of Imam al-Nasafi's treatment of the definition of knowledge (ilm) as presented by the modern author. It begins by noting that after his methodology, Nasafi discusses the definition of knowledge. Definition of the World and Its Divisions
Al-Nasafi defines the world and its divisions into bodies, substances, and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of 'world' in kalam philosophy and how it is divided into bodies, substances, and accidents, as well as the alternative division by Abu Mansur.

Core Idea
The world is everything other than God – a sign pointing to its Creator.

In this lesson, al-Nasafi establishes the foundational definition of the world (al-alam) as used by the mutakallimun. He then presents the standard threefold division and notes an alternative classification by the founder of the Maturidi school, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. Pay close attention to how the terms are defined, as these concepts are the building blocks of all kalam arguments about creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall rhythm, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words sink in before analysis.
Notice that the passage begins with an explanatory 'He clarifies' (فيبين).
2
Identify the definition of 'world'
Find the explicit definition: 'the world according to the mutakallimun is everything other than God' and note the reason it is so named.
The word 'alam' (world) is related to 'alam' (sign) – it is a sign of the Creator.
3
Examine the two divisions
First, note the threefold division into bodies, substances, accidents. Then, note the alternative twofold division by Abu Mansur: accidents and entities (with bodies subsumed under substances).
The passage gives both views; understand why Abu Mansur's classification is different.
4
Learn the defining characteristic
Al-Nasafi clarifies that accidents cannot subsist by themselves, whereas entities are self-subsistent. This distinction is crucial for later arguments.
This definition will be used in proofs for the existence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيبين أن العالم عند المتكلمين هو ما سوى الله تعالى وقد سمي بذلك لكونه علما على ثبوت صانع له حي. وينقسم العالم إلى أجسام، وجواهر، وأعراض. إلا أن الشيخ أبا منصور يرى أن الأقسام أعراض، وأعيان؛ لأن الأجسام داخله في الجواهر. ويبين النسفي أن العرض ما يستحيل قيامه بنفسه أما الأعيان فتقوم بنفسها.
He clarifies that the world (al-alam) according to the mutakallimun is everything other than God, and it is so named because it is a sign of the existence of a living Maker for it. The world is divided into bodies (ajsam), substances (jawāhir), and accidents (a'rāḍ). However, Shaykh Abu Mansur holds that the divisions are accidents and entities (a'yān), because bodies are included in substances. And al-Nasafi clarifies that the accident is that which cannot subsist by itself, while entities subsist by themselves.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definition of the term 'world' (al-alam) in kalam: everything other than God. It is called 'alam because it is a sign (alam) pointing to the existence of a living Creator. The passage then presents the standard threefold division of the world into bodies (ajsam), substances (jawāhir), and accidents (a'rāḍ). It notes an alternative division by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who divides into accidents and entities (a'yān), with bodies subsumed under substances. Finally, it defines the accident as what cannot subsist by itself, whereas entities are self-subsistent.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage clearly states that the world is everything other than God, and it is a sign pointing to the Creator.

Checkpoint

B) Everything that exists including God

Key Takeaway
The world is everything other than God, serving as a sign of the Creator, and it is classified into bodies, substances, and accidents, with accidents being non-self-subsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything other than God is a sign (ʻalam) pointing to Him, what does your own existence—your body, your breath, your thoughts—point to? Are you living in a way that makes you a clear sign of God, or have you become a sign of something else?
?
Accidents cannot subsist by themselves; they depend on a substance. In your spiritual life, what 'accidents'—habits, possessions, relationships—are you depending on as if they could sustain you? What would it mean for you to rely only on the Self-Subsistent (al-Qayyum)?
?
You have learned that the world is everything other than God. Look at something in your immediate surroundings right now. Do you see it as a sign of the Creator, or do you see it as merely a thing? What would need to change in your heart for you to constantly see every created thing as a witness to God?

Definition of Accident and Critique of Al-Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite Views
Al-Nasafi defines 'arad, critiques al-Ash'ari's definition, and refutes a Mu'tazilite condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of accident ('arad) in Islamic theology, al-Nasafi's critique of al-Ash'ari's definition, and the refutation of the Mu'tazilite condition that accidents depend on a locus for existence.

Core Idea
Accidents are defined by their temporary nature, not by need for a locus.

In this passage, al-Nasafi presents the concept of accident ('arad) and critically examines two definitions: one by al-Ash'ari and another by those who say accidents cannot exist without a locus. His analysis reveals important theological assumptions about the attributes of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow from definition to critique to refutation.
The passage contains three distinct parts: definition/critique of al-Ash'ari, and refutation of another view.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find al-Ash'ari's definition of accident and the alternative definition that mentions 'cannot dispense with a locus'. Note how al-Nasafi evaluates each.
Al-Ash'ari's definition: 'what subsists in another and ceases without the ceasing of its locus.'
3
Understand al-Nasafi's critique of al-Ash'ari
Al-Nasafi says the definition is correct but al-Ash'ari contradicted his principle by making it composite of two descriptions. Consider why this is a contradiction.
Al-Ash'ari's original principle may have been about simplicity; making a definition compound violates it.
4
Grasp the refutation of the Mu'tazilite condition
Al-Nasafi argues that the statement 'accident cannot dispense with a locus' is only valid if by existence is meant origination (huduth). For divine attributes, absolute existence does not require a locus.
This refutation relies on the distinction between created existence and God's eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينتقل بعد ذلك إلى الحديث عن العرض، فيقدم تعريفه في اللغة، وعند المتكلمين عارضا تعريفات كثيرة ، ويذكر أن أبا الحسن الأشعري قال ـ إنه ما يعترض على غيره ويبطل من غير بطلان محله ويقول النسفي: إنه حد صحيح، إلا أن الأشعرى ناقض أصله حيث جعله مركبا من وصفين ويرد على من يقول: - إن العرض هو ما لا يستغني في وجوده عن محل - قائلا: - إن هذا لا يستقيم إلا إذا أريد بالوجود الحدوث، أما مطلق الوجود فهو منتقض بصفات الله تعالى، فلا يستقيم القول السابق إلا على أصول المعتزلة -
He then moves to discussing the accident (al-'arad). He presents its definition in language and among the mutakallimun, presenting many definitions. He mentions that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: 'It is what subsists in another and ceases without the ceasing of its locus.' Al-Nasafi says: 'This definition is correct, except that al-Ash'ari contradicted his principle by making it composite of two descriptions.' And he refutes those who say: 'The accident is that which cannot dispense with a locus in its existence,' saying: 'This is only sound if by existence is meant origination (huduth); as for absolute existence, it is invalidated by the attributes of God. Thus, the previous statement is only sound according to the principles of the Mu'tazila.'
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage focuses on the definition of the accident ('arad). It first mentions that the author presents various linguistic and technical definitions. Then it cites al-Ash'ari's definition: an accident is what subsists in another (i.e., in a substance) and ceases without the cessation of its locus. Al-Nasafi approves of this definition but notes that al-Ash'ari contradicted his own principle by making it composite of two descriptions (i.e., 'subsisting in another' and 'ceasing without the locus ceasing'). Next, the passage refutes a Mu'tazilite definition that 'the accident is that which cannot dispense with a locus in its existence.' Al-Nasafi argues that this definition only works if by 'existence' is meant 'origination' (huduth), because the absolute existence of God's attributes does not depend on a locus. Therefore, the definition is valid only according to Mu'tazilite principles that deny God's eternal attributes.

Key Terms
عرض
محل
حدوث
أصول المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that accidents always need a locus in their existence, as a necessary condition.
Correction: This passage clarifies that this is not true for God's attributes, which have absolute existence without a locus. The condition only applies to originated accidents (huduth).

Checkpoint

A) It is completely false
B) It is valid only if by existence is meant origination, and it fails for divine attributes
C) It is too vague
D) It contradicts al-Ash'ari's definition

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi distinguishes between originated existence (huduth) and absolute existence, using God's attributes to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that accidents always need a locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Nasafi points out that even a correct definition can be self-contradictory if it violates one's own principles. Can you identify an area in your own understanding of faith where you hold two positions that subtly contradict each other? What will you do to reconcile them?
?
God's attributes are eternal and need no locus—this refutes a definition that would treat them as accidents. Do you ever unconsciously think of God's attributes as if they were temporary or dependent on creation? How can you guard your mind against such comparisons?
?
The Mu'tazilite definition only works if 'existence' means 'origination.' When you think about God's existence, do you ever blur the line between His eternal existence and the originated existence of creatures? What specific step can you take today to ensure your thoughts about God remain distinct from His creation?

Defining Eternal and Originated
The author defines the eternal and the originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational definitions of 'eternal' (al-qadim) and 'originated' (al-hadith) in Islamic theology as presented in this text.

Core Idea
Eternal has no beginning; originated has a beginning.

Before any theological argument about the world’s origin, we must be clear on what 'eternal' and 'originated' mean. This passage gives crisp definitions that will be used throughout kalam reasoning. Let’s see how the author sets these terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise style of definitions.
The author attributes the definition of eternal to al-Ash'ari.
2
Identify the two definitions
Locate the definition of 'eternal' and 'originated'. What single feature distinguishes them?
3
Understand the significance
Consider why defining 'beginning' is crucial for proving the world's origination. These definitions are the building blocks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبدأ في بيان حد القديم فيقول : هو ما لا أول لوجوده، ويقرر أنه تعريف الأشعري، ويعرف المحدث بأنه ما لوجوده ابتداء.
And he begins to explain the definition of the eternal, saying: it is that whose existence has no beginning, and he confirms that it is the definition of al-Ash'ari, and he defines the originated as that whose existence has a beginning.

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms in kalam: the eternal (al-qadim) and the originated (al-hadith). The author states that the eternal is that whose existence has no beginning, and he attributes this definition to al-Ash'ari. The originated is defined as that whose existence has a beginning. This establishes a clear distinction between what is eternal and what is originated, which is foundational for the argument about the world's origination.

The Final Definition: Speech as Eternal Meaning, Expressions as Created
The author adopts the definition of speech as a meaning subsisting in the speaker, distinguishes eternal speech from created words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari-Maturidi definition of divine speech as an eternal meaning in God's essence, and how the created Qur'anic expressions relate to that eternal speech.

Core Idea
God's speech is one eternal meaning; the Qur'an's words are created indicators of it.

In this passage, the author finalizes his position on the nature of divine speech. He adopts the definition held by major Sunni theologians: speech is a meaning subsisting in the speaker's essence, expressed through created utterances. Drawing on Quranic verse and poetry, he argues that true speech is internal, not merely sounds. This allows him to affirm that God's speech is eternal and one, while the recited Qur'an—in Hebrew, Syriac, or Arabic—is created. This distinction resolves the classic debate over whether the Qur'an is created or uncreated.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's chosen definition and evidence
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the definition of speech as a meaning subsisting in the speaker, and the two pieces of evidence the author provides: the Quranic verse and the poetic line.
The key term 'ma'na' (meaning) is central here.
2
Understand how the evidence refutes the Mu'tazilite premise
The evidence shows that speech can exist internally without being uttered as letters and sounds. This directly contradicts the Mu'tazilite premise that speech is only letters and sounds.
Mu'tazilites argued that God's speech is created and composed of letters. This passage refutes that.
3
Apply the principle to God: eternal meaning and created expressions
Notice how the author distinguishes between God's eternal attribute of speech (one, not consisting of letters/sounds) and the created expressions (Hebrew, Syriac, Arabic) that indicate that eternal speech.
This distinction allows believing the Qur'an is uncreated in its essence while its recited form is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو في النهاية يختار تعريفا للكلام قال به ابن الراوندي، وأبو عيسى الوراق، وأبو الحسن الأشعري، والماتريدي، وهو أن الكلام هو المعنى القائم بذات المتكلم الذي يديره المتكلم في نفسه، ويعبر عنه بهذه الألفاظ المتركبة من الحروف. ويدلل على صحة هذا المعنى بقوله تعالى : يُخْفُونَ فِي أَنفُسِهِم مَّا لَا يُبْدُونَ لَكَ ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٥٤] يعني من الكلام. ويقول الأخطل : إن الكلام لفي القوائد وإنما جعل اللسان على الفؤاد دليلا وهذا يبطل مقدمة المعتزلة التي تقول : إن الكلام في الشاهد يكون من جنس الحروف والأصوات. وعلى هذا فإن كلام الله تعالى صفة قائمة بذاته، ليست من جنس الحروف، والأصوات، وهي صفة واحدة وهي أمر بما أمر به ونهي عما نهى عنه، وهي صفة أزلية. ثم هذه العبارات العبرية أو السريانية عبارات عنه، وهو يتأدى بها، وهذه العبارات حروف وأصوات وهي محدثة، مخلوقة في محلها، دلالات وهي على الكلام الذي هو صفة أزلية الله تعالى.
And finally he chooses a definition of speech (kalam) held by Ibn al-Rawandi, Abu Isa al-Warraq, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and al-Maturidi, which is that speech is the meaning (ma'na) subsisting in the essence of the speaker, which the speaker conducts within himself, and he expresses it by these utterances composed of letters. And he provides evidence for the correctness of this meaning by His statement—exalted is He: 'They conceal within themselves what they do not reveal to you' (Al Imran 154), meaning of speech. And al-Akhtal says: 'Verily speech lies in the heart, and the tongue is made a guide to the heart.' This invalidates the Mu'tazilite premise that in the observed world, speech belongs to the genus of letters and sounds. On this basis, God's speech—exalted is He—is an attribute subsisting in His essence, not of the genus of letters and sounds, and it is a single attribute, and it is a command for what He has commanded and a prohibition for what He has prohibited, and it is an eternal (azali) attribute. Then these expressions, whether Hebrew or Syriac, are expressions concerning it, and it is conveyed through them, and these expressions are letters and sounds and they are originated (muhdatha), created (makhluqa) in their locus, as indications (dilalat) upon the speech which is the eternal attribute of God—exalted is He.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the final definition of speech that the author adopts, aligning himself with Ibn al-Rawandi, Abu Isa al-Warraq, al-Ash'ari, and al-Maturidi. The definition: speech is a meaning (ma'na) that subsists in the essence of the speaker. The speaker 'runs' or 'conducts' it within himself, and then expresses it using composed utterances. This is proven by the Qur'anic verse 'They conceal within themselves what they do not reveal to you' (3:154), which refers to internal speech, and by the poetic line of al-Akhtal that 'speech lies in the heart, and the tongue is a guide.' These proofs refute the Mu'tazilite premise that speech in the witnessed world is only letters and sounds. Then the author applies this to God: His speech is one eternal attribute, not composed of letters or sounds. It is simultaneously command, prohibition, and all that He commands and prohibits. The actual scriptural expressions (in Hebrew, Syriac, Arabic) are created indicators that point to that eternal speech. They are created in their respective loci, but the speech they indicate is eternal. This harmonizes the belief that the Qur'an is the uncreated speech of God while its recitation and written forms are created.

Key Terms
معنى قائم بذات المتكلم
ألفاظ مركبة
حروف
أصوات
صفة أزلية
دلالات
عبارات عبرية أو سريانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equating the eternal speech of God directly with the Arabic Qur'an as we recite it, without distinction.
Correction: The author distinguishes between the eternal meaning (ma'na) and the created expressions (letters/sounds) that indicate it. The Arabic Qur'an is a created expression of the eternal speech.

Checkpoint
What is the evidence the author uses to show that speech can be a meaning in the heart rather than just letters and sounds?
A) The verse 'We have made it an Arabic Qur'an'
B) The verse 'They conceal within themselves what they do not reveal to you' and the poetry of al-Akhtal
C) The verse 'God created the heavens and the earth'
D) The verse 'Say: He is Allah, the One'

Key Takeaway
The eternal speech of God is a single meaning in His essence; all scriptural expressions are created indicators of that meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you ever feel the weight that the words you utter are only a created expression, while the real speech of Allah is an eternal meaning in His essence? How does this distinction change your relationship with the Qur'an?
?
The author quotes al-Akhtal: 'Speech lies in the heart, and the tongue is a guide.' When you speak, do you ensure that your internal meaning corresponds to the truth? What would happen if your tongue expressed something your heart did not believe?
?
If Allah's speech is one attribute that is both command and prohibition, then His command to you today is the same as His command to the first believers. Are you responding to that eternal command as if it were spoken directly to you right now?

The Definition of Divine Speech as Inner Meaning
Nasafi defines divine speech as meaning subsisting in the essence, with evidence and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi-Ash'ari definition of divine speech as an inner meaning subsisting in God's essence, distinct from its verbal expression.

Core Idea
True speech is the meaning in the soul, not the sounds on the tongue.

This lesson introduces the foundational definition of divine speech (kalam) according to the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools. Nasafi presents a definition that distinguishes between inner, eternal meaning and outward expression, supported by Quran and poetry. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Islamic stance on the Qur'an as uncreated.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its proponents
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the list of scholars who held this definition: Ibn al-Rawandi, Abu Isa al-Warraq, Ash'ari, and Maturidi.
This shows the definition is not novel but agreed upon by early theologians.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Read the Quran verse (3:154) and see how Nasafi uses it to argue that concealed inner speech is real speech.
The verse says 'they conceal in themselves what they do not reveal to you' – the concealed thing is speech.
3
Analyze the poetic evidence
Read al-Akhtal's line: speech is in the ribcage (heart), and the tongue is only an indicator. This reinforces that true speech is inner.
Poetry is used as a linguistic argument, not as scripture.
4
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
Note how the definition and evidence directly invalidate the Mu'tazila premise that speech is letters and sounds.
This is the key doctrinal point: God's speech is an eternal attribute, not created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو في النهاية يختار تعريفا للكلام قال به ابن الراوندي، وأبو عيسى الوراق، وأبو الحسن الأشعري، والماتريدي، وهو أن الكلام هو المعنى القائم بذات المتكلم الذي يديره المتكلم في نفسه، ويعبر عنه بهذه الألفاظ المتركبة من الحروف. ويدلل على صحة هذا المعنى بقوله تعالى : يُخْفُونَ فِي أَنفُسِهِم مَّا لَا يُبْدُونَ لَكَ [آل عمران: ١٥٤] يعني من الكلام. ويقول الأخطل : إن الكلام لفي القوائد وإنما جعل اللسان على الفؤاد دليلا وهذا يبطل مقدمة المعتزلة التي تقول : إن الكلام في الشاهد يكون من جنس الحروف والأصوات.
And in the end he chooses a definition of speech (kalam) that was held by Ibn al-Rawandi, Abu Isa al-Warraq, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and al-Maturidi; it is that speech is the meaning subsisting in the essence of the speaker which the speaker conducts in himself, and he expresses it by these composed words of letters. And he provides evidence for the correctness of this meaning by His statement Most High: 'They conceal in themselves what they do not reveal to you' (Quran 3:154) meaning of speech. And al-Akhtal says: 'Indeed speech is in the ribcage [heart], and the tongue is made only an indicator of the heart.' And this invalidates the Mu'tazila premise which says that speech in the witnessed world is of the genus of letters and sounds.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of kalam (speech) according to the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools, attributed to Ibn al-Rawandi, Abu Isa al-Warraq, Ash'ari, and Maturidi. Kalam is defined as 'the meaning subsisting in the essence of the speaker that the speaker conducts in himself and expresses by these composed letters and words.' This definition distinguishes between inner speech (the meaning in the soul) and outer expression. Nasafi then provides evidence from the Quran: the verse 'They conceal in themselves what they do not reveal to you' (Quran 3:154) – the concealed thing is speech (kalam) inside themselves, not letters and sounds. He also cites a line from the poet al-Akhtal: 'Indeed speech is in the ribcage [heart], and the tongue is made only an indicator of the heart.' This shows that the true speech is the inner meaning, not the audible sounds. This refutes the Mu'tazila premise that in the witnessed world, speech is of the genus of letters and sounds. Thus, divine speech is an inner attribute, not a created sound.

Key Terms
كلام
معنى قائم بالذات
ألفاظ
حروف

المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That speech (kalam) is essentially the same as audible letters and sounds.
Correction: Nasafi argues that true speech is the meaning subsisting in the essence of the speaker; letters and sounds are merely expressions of that inner speech.

Checkpoint

A) The letters and sounds that are heard.
B) The meaning subsisting in the essence of the speaker.
C) The written text in the Preserved Tablet.
D) The combination of letters and meanings.

Key Takeaway
True speech is the meaning that resides in the speaker's soul, not the vocal sounds or letters we hear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would change if you treated your prayer as the expression of an inner reality rather than a recitation of sounds? Starting today, will you pause before each prayer to set the meaning in your soul?
?
Think of a time your words did not reflect what was truly in your heart. Did you consider that the real speech was the hidden meaning, not the uttered words? How can you align your inner state with your outer expressions in your daily interactions, especially when speaking about your faith?
?
The Mu'tazila reduced speech to mere sounds. In what areas of your life have you reduced something deep and spiritual to mere external actions? How will you reclaim the inner meaning behind your worship, your words, and your relationships, starting with one concrete change today?

Permissibility of Calling Qur'anic Expressions a 'Hikāyah' of Allah's Speech
Nasafi asks whether expressions can be called a ḥikāyah of Allah's speech and reports the disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some scholars allowed or disallowed using the term ḥikāyah for the Qur'anic expressions, and how this reflects different views on the relationship between the eternal speech of Allah and its created expressions.

Core Idea
The term ḥikāyah was debated because it might reduce divine speech to a mere copy.

Nasafi raises a precise question about theological terminology: can we call the recited Qur'an a 'story' (ḥikāyah) of Allah's eternal speech? The answer divides early and later Maturidis, and also separates them from Ash'aris. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Nasafi's question and answer in Arabic and translation. Note the key terms: ḥikāyah, ʿibārah, and the groups mentioned.
The passage is concise; pay attention to who allowed and who disallowed.
2
Identify the two opinions
Distinguish the early Maturidi position (allowed, with argument that ḥikāyah is like ʿibārah) from the opposing position (Ash'ari, Qalānisī, later Maturidis – disallowed).
Later Maturidis changed from the earlier view.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider why the term ḥikāyah might be problematic. Does it imply that the Qur'an is merely a representation rather than directly God's attribute?
This connects to the deeper issue of the uncreatedness of Allah's speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسأل النسفي هل يجوز القول بأن العبارات حكاية عن كلام الله؟ ويجيب بأن الأوائل من أصحابه قالوا بجواز ذلك، محتجين بأن لفظة الحكاية مثل لفظة العبارة، أما الأشعري، والقلانسي فلا يجوزان ذلك، وينضم إليهما المتأخرون من الماتريدية.
And al-Nasafī asks: Is it permissible to say that the expressions are a story (ḥikāyah) of the speech of Allah? And he answers: that the early ones from his companions allowed that, arguing that the word 'story' is like the word 'expression' (al-ʿibārah). As for al-Ashʿarī and al-Qalānisī, they do not allow that, and the later Maturidis join them.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a short question-answer unit regarding the permissibility of using the term ḥikāyah (story, narrative) to refer to the Qur'anic expressions in relation to Allah's eternal speech. Nasafi poses the question and then gives the answer. The early Maturidi scholars (al-awā'il min aṣḥābihī) allowed it, arguing that the word ḥikāyah is equivalent in meaning to ʿibārah (expression), which was already accepted. However, al-Ash'arī and al-Qalānisī did not permit this usage, and the later Maturidis (al-muta'akhkhirūn min al-Māturīdiyyah) joined them in rejecting it. This reflects a development in the Maturidi school; the earlier generation was more flexible in terminology, while later scholars, in agreement with the Ash'arīs, found the term ḥikāyah problematic because it might imply a mere copy or representation, potentially diminishing the directness of the divine attribute.

Key Terms

العبارة
الأشعري
القلانسي
الماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the term ḥikāyah is always permissible because it appears in ordinary language.
Correction: Nasafi shows that respected scholars like al-Ashʿarī and later Maturidis rejected it, likely because it could imply a created copy rather than the eternal attribute itself.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The debate over ḥikāyah reveals the careful attention scholars give to how human language relates to God's attribute of speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a time you described something about Allah's attributes in your own words. Did you ever use a phrase that, upon reflection, might have diminished His majesty? What would it take for you to treat every word about Allah with the same caution as the scholars did with ḥikāyah?
?
You now know that later Maturidis changed their position on this term. Is there a belief or practice in your own faith that you hold dear, but which you have never questioned? Would you be willing to re-examine it if evidence from scholars suggested a more precise understanding?

The Four Topics and Proof of Takwin's Eternity
The author lists four topics and gives a proof for takwin's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four key issues in the debate with the Mu'tazila concerning takwin (the attribute of bringing into existence) and the rational proof for its eternity.

Core Idea
Takwin is an eternal attribute of God, distinct from the created world.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla outlines the major points of contention with the Mu'tazila on the nature of takwin. He first enumerates four issues and then provides a concise argument establishing that takwin is eternal. As you read, notice how the proof is built on the premise that the world is originated and requires an attribute of origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two parts: a list of four topics and a proof.
2
Identify the four topics
The passage begins by stating that the discussion covers four topics. Find each one and note how they are ordered.
The first topic is already completed, while the other three are to be demonstrated.
3
Examine the proof for takwin's eternity
The proof starts with the statement that the world is originated (hadith). Trace the logical steps that lead to the conclusion that takwin is eternal.
The proof relies on the impossibility of self-origination and the need for an attribute of origination.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why each topic matters for the broader theological debate. How does establishing takwin as eternal safeguard divine transcendence?
The fourth topic addresses a common objection that eternity of takwin would imply eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول: إن حديثنا معهم يتناول مواضع أربعة أولا: مع المعتزلة في جواز وجود صفة أزلية بذات الباري - وقد انتهى من ذلك في مبحث الصفات وثانيا : بيان أن التكوين غير المكون ، وثالثا: بيان أن التكوين أزلي. ورابعا بيان أن القول بقدم التكوين لا يؤدي إلى القول بقدم المكونات. ثم يبرهن على ثبوت التكوين في الأزل بأن العالم محدث، ويثبت أن الله تعالى هو الذي أحدثه من العدم، وإحداثه يكون بصفة الإحداث، وهي التكوين، لأنه يستحيل أن يحدث العالم نفسه بنفسه، كما يستحيل أن يكون إيجاده بجزء منه، كما هو مبين في كتب علم الكلام، وخاصة المواقف.
He says: Our discussion with them covers four topics: First, with the Mu'tazila regarding the permissibility of an eternal attribute subsisting in the Essence of the Creator - and he has completed that in the chapter on attributes. Second, the demonstration that takwin (bringing into existence) is other than the mukawwan (thing brought into existence). Third, the demonstration that takwin is eternal. Fourth, the demonstration that the statement of the eternity of takwin does not lead to the statement of the eternity of the things brought into existence. Then he proves the establishment of takwin in pre-eternity by [saying] that the world is originated, and he establishes that God most high is the one who originated it from nothing, and its origination is by the attribute of origination, which is takwin, because it is impossible for the world to originate itself, just as it is impossible for its bringing into existence to be by a part of it, as is explained in the books of kalam, especially al-Mawaqif.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a twofold statement: first, a list of four topics to be discussed with the Mu'tazila; second, a brief proof for the eternity of takwin. The four topics are: (1) the possibility of an eternal attribute in God's essence (already covered earlier), (2) that takwin is distinct from the created thing, (3) that takwin is eternal, and (4) that the eternity of takwin does not entail the eternity of created things. The proof then follows: the world is originated (hadith), and it must have an originator. God originated it from nothing, and this origination is impossible without an attribute of origination, which is takwin. The world cannot originate itself, nor can its creation be by a part of itself. This argument relies on the principle that an originated thing requires an efficient cause, and that cause must possess an attribute that brings about the effect. The reference to 'al-Mawaqif' shows reliance on earlier kalam works.

Key Terms
التكوين (takwin)
المكون (mukawwan)
أزلي (eternal)
حادث (originated)
صفة الإحداث (attribute of origination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Takwin is identical to the act of creating or the created thing itself.
Correction: Takwin is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, distinct from both the act (which is temporal) and the created thing (mukawwan).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the third topic discussed with the Mu'tazila?
A) The possibility of an eternal attribute
B) That takwin is other than the mukawwan
C) That takwin is eternal
D) That the eternity of takwin does not lead to the eternity of the mukawwanat

Key Takeaway
Takwin is an eternal attribute of God, distinct from created things, and its eternity does not imply the eternity of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's attribute of bringing things into existence (takwin) is eternal, meaning that He has always been the Creator even before creation existed? How does this shape your understanding of God's perfection and your own dependence on Him? What would it mean for your worship if you instead thought God became Creator only at the moment of creation?
?
The passage argues that the world cannot originate itself, and that it needs God's attribute of origination. In your own life, do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that every moment of your existence is brought about by God's eternal attribute? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of your need for God's creative power every moment?
?
If takwin is eternal but its effects (the world) are not eternal, then God's act of creation is not a change in Him. Does your heart find rest in the fact that God's attributes are perfect and unchanging, or do you struggle with the idea that God's relation to the world is not a change in Him? What does this mean for your trust in God's immutability?

Definition of Divine Will (Irada) and its Relation to Mashiyya
Nasafi defines irada linguistically and technically, and distinguishes it from mashiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi defines divine will (irada) both linguistically and technically, and the difference between the majority view that irada and mashiyya are synonymous and the Karramiyya view that distinguishes them.

Core Idea
Divine will and mashiyya are one attribute for most theologians, but not for the Karramiyya.

Nasafi begins by clarifying a point from the previous discussion about takwin and mukawwan, then turns to define irada. He gives its linguistic root and technical meaning as a divine attribute that opposes aversion and compulsion. He then addresses a key theological disagreement: while most mutakallimin equate irada with mashiyya, the Karramiyya separate them, considering mashiyya eternal and irada temporally originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow from the concluding remark about takwin to the definition of irada and the discussion of the Karramiyya.
The first sentence is a conclusion from a prior argument, not the main focus.
2
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions
Find Nasafi's linguistic derivation of irada from al-rawd, and his technical definition as a meaning that opposes aversion and compulsion.
The linguistic meaning involves seeking/inclination; the technical meaning is a specific attribute.
3
Contrast the majority and Karramiyya views
Note that most mutakallimin consider irada and mashiyya to indicate one meaning, while the Karramiyya distinguish them, making mashiyya eternal and irada temporal.
This distinction has implications for understanding divine attributes.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for theology. How does it affect the understanding of God's will?
The Karramiyya view implies multiple temporal wills, which the majority avoids.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقتض هذا أن يكون المعلوم والمقدور قديمين فكذا التكوين والمكون لا يقتضى الأول قدم الثاني. الإرادة صفة أزلية الله تعالى: يعرف النسفي الإرادة بأنها مشتقة في اللغة من الرود، ومن معاني الرود الطلب والميل. أما حدها فهي : معنى ينافر الكراهية والاضطرار، ويوجب خروج المفعولات على وجه دون وجه وعلى هذا فالإرادة والمشيئة ينبئان عن معنى واحد عند المتكلمين إلا الكرامية الذين قالوا: إن الإرادة غير المشيئة، فالمشيئة أزلية والإرادة حادثة في ذات الله تعالى، وله إرادات كثيرة على عدد المرادات.
This necessitates that the known and the predetermined be eternal, and likewise takwin (bringing into existence) and mukawwan (the brought into existence) do not necessitate the first requiring the second to be eternal. Al-Irada is an eternal attribute of Allah the Exalted: Al-Nasafi defines al-irada as derived in language from al-rawd, and among the meanings of al-rawd is seeking and inclination. As for its technical definition, it is: a meaning that opposes aversion and compulsion, and necessitates the occurrence of effects in one manner rather than another. On this basis, al-irada and al-mashiyya indicate one meaning according to the mutakallimin, except the Karramiyya who said: al-irada is other than al-mashiyya, so al-mashiyya is eternal and al-irada is temporally originated in the essence of Allah the Exalted, and He has many iradat according to the number of objects of will.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage begins with a concluding remark from a prior argument about divine attributes: if knowledge and power are eternal, then takwin (the act of bringing into existence) does not imply that the mukawwan (the created thing) is also eternal; they are distinct. Then Nasafi turns to define al-irada (divine will). He gives its linguistic derivation from al-rawd, meaning seeking or inclination, and its technical definition as a meaning (ma'na) that repels aversion and compulsion, causing actions to occur in a specific manner. He then clarifies that for most mutakallimin, irada and mashiyya are synonymous, but the Karramiyya differentiate: they hold mashiyya is eternal in God's essence, while irada is temporally originated (haditha) and multiple according to the number of objects of will.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المشيئة
الكرامية
الرود
الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that irada and mashiyya are always distinct attributes in Islamic theology.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that for most mutakallimin (including the Maturidis and Ash'aris), irada and mashiyya indicate one meaning; the distinction is only held by the Karramiyya.

Checkpoint
According to most mutakallimin, what is the relationship between al-irada and al-mashiyya?
A) They are distinct attributes
B) They indicate one meaning
C) Al-irada is eternal and al-mashiyya is temporal
D) They are both temporal

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defines irada as an eternal attribute of Allah, synonymous with mashiyya for most theologians, but the Karramiyya disagree by making irada temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your own will clashes with what you know of Allah's will, do you find yourself resisting the very attribute of irada that Nasafi describes as 'opposing aversion and compulsion'? How will you train your heart to embrace His will over your own today?
?
The Karramiyya thought irada must be multiple and temporal because it relates to many objects. In your life, do you act as if God's will is fragmented or reactive, rather than a single eternal will that encompasses everything? What would change if you truly believed His irada is one and eternal?
?
Nasafi defines irada as a meaning that 'necessitates the occurrence of effects in one manner rather than another.' Are you living with the certainty that every event in your life occurs by His specific will, not by chance? How would your attitude towards difficulty change if you remembered this?

Defining Prophethood as Ambassadorship
Al-Nasafi defines risala as ambassadorship and its purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's definition of prophethood (risala) as an ambassadorship between God and people of intellect, intended to remedy human shortcomings in matters beyond reason.

Core Idea
Prophethood is God's embassy to complete what reason cannot reach.

Al-Nasafi begins his proof of prophethood by first defining what it is. He clarifies that risala is not a mystical state or an emanation, but a purposeful ambassadorship from God to rational beings. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage where al-Nasafi defines risala as 'sifara al-'abd bayna Allah ta'ala wa bayna dhawi al-albab'. Reflect on each term.
Notice the terms: سفارة (ambassadorship), العبد (servant), الله (God), ذوي الألباب (people of intellect).
2
Understand the purpose
Al-Nasafi states that the purpose is to remove defects in what intellects fell short of regarding the benefits of their abode (the afterlife). Ask yourself: what are these defects?
This implies reason alone cannot fully grasp all matters of salvation.
3
Connect to broader kalam context
Consider why defining prophethood first is important. In kalam, definitions set the stage for proofs. This definition positions prophethood as a possible, purposeful act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر النسفي حل شبه الخصوم، ثم يبدأ في إقامة الدلالة على صحة الرسالة فيعرفنا أولا بأنها سفارة العبد بين الله تعالى وبين ذوي الألباب، ليزيح بها عللهم فيما قصرت عنه عقولهم من مصالح دارهم. ويبين ثانيا أنها ممكنة، فإن ورود التكليف بالإيجاب والحظر ممن له الملك ليس مما تأباه العقول، والمالك يتصرف في ملكه أين شاء، ومتى شاء، وكيف شاء، وبأي وجه يريد سواء بإعطاء العقل قدرة معرفة كل شيء، أو بإرسال الرسل.
And al-Nasafi mentions the resolution of the doubts of the opponents, then he begins to establish the proof of the validity of the message (al-risala), first defining it as the ambassadorship of the servant between God Almighty and those of intellect, to remove thereby their defects in what their intellects fell short of regarding the benefits of their abode. Secondly, he shows that it is possible, for the occurrence of obligation (taklif) by command and prohibition from the One Who possesses dominion is not something that intellects reject, and the Owner acts in His dominion however He wills, whenever He wills, and in whatever manner He wills, whether by giving the intellect the ability to know everything, or by sending messengers.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage defines prophethood (risala) and establishes its possibility. Al-Nasafi first states that he will resolve the doubts of opponents and prove the validity of risala. He defines it as an ambassadorship (sifara) from God to people of intellect, serving to remedy human shortcomings in matters beyond reason's reach. Then he argues for its possibility: imposing commands and prohibitions by the true Owner (God) is not rationally objectionable. God has absolute dominion and may choose to communicate directly through intellect or through messengers. This establishes that prophethood is a possible act within God's power, not impossible.

Key Terms
الرسالة
سفارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the primary purpose of prophethood (risala)?
A) To perform miracles
B) To remedy intellectual shortcomings about the afterlife
C) To establish political authority
D) To create a legal system

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is an ambassadorship from God to rational beings, filling the gaps where reason alone cannot reach.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely on your own intellect to guide you in matters of the afterlife, or have you accepted the ambassadorship of the prophets? What defect in your reasoning about the akhirah is this lesson exposing right now?
?
If prophethood is an ambassadorship sent to fill what your intellect fell short of, what does that say about your need to seek knowledge from revelation? Are you actively seeking to remove your defects through the message, or are you content with what you think you know?
?
Al-Nasafi defines prophethood as a removal of defects. What specific defect in your understanding of your purpose in this life has this lesson addressed? Will you commit to studying the prophetic message more deeply to remove that defect?

The Possibility of Prophethood
Al-Nasafi proves the possibility of prophethood via God's ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that prophethood is rationally possible, based on God's absolute dominion and the nature of obligation.

Core Idea
Because God is the absolute Owner, He can send messengers — it's not impossible.

After defining prophethood, al-Nasafi addresses a key objection: could God really send messengers? He argues that prophethood is not only possible but logically sound, using the principle that the owner of all dominion has full authority to communicate with His creation however He wishes.

Guided Path
1
Read the possibility argument
Read the second part of the passage where al-Nasafi says 'wa yubayyin thaniyan annaha mumkina...' and trace the reasoning.
He connects obligation (taklif) to the authority of the Owner (al-malik).
2
Understand the key premise
The key premise: the Owner (God) can command and prohibit in His dominion. Since He owns everything, no one can object to His methods of communication.
This is based on the rational principle of ownership.
3
Contrast two methods of guidance
Al-Nasafi mentions two ways God can guide: giving intellect the ability to know everything, or sending messengers. Prophethood is one possible method.
This shows prophethood is not necessary, but possible — God could have chosen another way.
