Definition and Types of Miracles
Nasafi defines miracle, its basis, types, and evidentiary value.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological definition of a miracle (mu'jiza), its two types, and how it proves the truthfulness of a prophet.

Core Idea
A miracle is a supernormal event that proves prophethood by the challenger's inability to match it.

This passage establishes the fundamental concept of miracles in Islamic theology. Al-Nasafi explains that a miracle is not merely a wonder; it is a deliberate sign from Allah that confirms a prophetic claim, and it requires the opponent's inability to produce anything similar. Understanding this definition and its divisions clarifies why miracles are decisive proofs of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structured flow: source, definition, division, and indication.
2
Identify the definition elements
Look for the key components: 'appearance of something contrary to custom', 'in the abode of obligation', 'to show truthfulness of claimant of prophethood', and 'with the inability of others to oppose it'.
Each element is essential; skipping one distorts the concept.
3
Understand the two types
The passage divides miracles into: (1) an act that is not customary (e.g., splitting the sea), and (2) incapacitation regarding a customary act (e.g., stopping the sun). Reflect on examples.
Both types involve a challenge (tahaddi) and the opponent's inability.
4
Grasp the proof mechanism
The passage says God confirms the prophet by enabling him to do what is contrary to natural laws. This establishes the miracle as a divine testimony to the prophet's truthfulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ذلك يتعرض للحديث عن المعجزة، فيبين لنا مآخذها، وحدها، وأقسامها، ووجه كيفية دلالتها على صدق الآتي بها. أما مآخذها فهو العجز وحدها عند المتكلمين : أنها ظهور أمر خلاف العادة في دار التكليف لإظهار صدق مدعي النبوة مع نكول وعجز الغير عن معارضته بمثله. وتنقسم إلى قسمين: الأول: فعل غير معتاد والثاني: تعجيز عن الفعل المعتاد. أما وجه دلالتها على صدق الآتي بها فهو أنه تعالى يصدقه بما يقدره على فعله مخالفا للمادة.
Then after that he discusses the miracle, explaining its sources (ma'akhidh), its definition (hadd), its divisions (aqsam), and the way of its indication of the truthfulness of the one who brings it. As for its source, it is incapacity (al-'ajz). Its definition according to the theologians (al-mutakallimin): that it is the appearance of a thing contrary to custom (khilaf al-'adah) in the abode of obligation (dar al-taklif) to manifest the truthfulness of the claimant of prophethood, with the refusal and incapacity of others to oppose it with its like. And it is divided into two types: the first: an act that is not customary (ghayr mu'tad); the second: incapacitation (ta'jiz) regarding a customary act. As for the aspect of its indication of the truthfulness of the one who brings it, it is that He, exalted, confirms him by what He empowers him to do, contrary to matter.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the miracle (mu'jiza) and its features. The author states that al-Nasafi explains the sources (ma'akhidh) of the miracle, its definition (hadd), its divisions (aqsam), and how it proves the truthfulness of the prophet. The source of the miracle is 'incapacity' (al-'ajz), meaning that the miracle demonstrates the inability of others to produce the like. The theologians define the miracle as a supernormal event occurring in this world of obligation to prove the truth of a prophetic claim, accompanied by the opponent's failure to match it. Then it is divided into two types: (1) an act that is outside normal custom (e.g., splitting the sea), and (2) incapacitating someone from performing a customary act (e.g., stopping the sun). The aspect of proof is that God confirms the prophet by enabling him to do something that goes against the normal course of matter.

Key Terms
معجزة
مآخذ
حد
أقسام
العجز
خلاف العادة
دار التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a miracle is simply any extraordinary event, regardless of context or challenge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a true miracle must be connected to a prophetic claim and must involve the opponent's inability (nukul wa 'ajz) to produce its like. It is not just any wonder; it is a proof of prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two types of miracles?
A) Acts of nature and acts of God.
B) Acts that are not customary and incapacitation regarding a customary act.
C) Magical acts and natural acts.
D) Acts performed by prophets and acts performed by saints.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is defined as a supernormal event, accompanied by a challenge and the opponent's inability to match it, which serves as divine proof of a prophet's truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that a miracle is not just a wonder but a divine proof tied to a challenge. When you reflect on your own faith, do you base it on such clear evidence, or do you rely on feelings and habit? What would it mean for you to truly accept that God's signs are unmistakable and demand a response?
?
The passage says a miracle proves prophethood because opponents are unable to match it. Have you ever considered the implications of this for the Quran as a miracle? If you are convinced by this argument, why are you not living every moment in awe of the One who sent it? What holds you back from surrendering more completely?
?
The two types of miracles—unusual acts and incapacitation—show that God can command even the laws of nature. Does this thought make you feel secure in His power, or does it unsettle you? Examine your heart: are you truly comfortable with a God who can override everything you rely on? How should this change your daily trust in Him?

The Unique Composition and Challenge of the Qur'an
Al-Nasafi presents the first aspect of i'jaz: the unique nazm and the challenge to produce similar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first aspect of the Qur'an's miraculousness: its unique composition that defies all known Arabic genres and the ongoing challenge to humanity that proves its divine origin.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's inimitable composition proves its divine origin.

Al-Nasafi begins his discussion of the Qur'an's miracle by focusing on its distinctive literary form. He explains that the Qur'an does not resemble any known Arabic genre—prose, poetry, sermons, or letters. It stands alone, and its challenge to produce even a single surah remains unanswered. This lesson examines that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how al-Nasafi introduces the first aspect of i'jaz and the specific challenge.
The passage is concise but rich in meaning.
2
Identify the unique composition
Al-Nasafi lists several Arabic forms (prose, poetry, rajaz, sermons, letters) and states the Qur'an differs from all of them. This is the basis of its miraculous nature.
3
Understand the challenge (tahaddi)
The Qur'an challenges all of humanity to produce its like, or ten surahs, or even one surah. This challenge has never been met.
The challenge is progressive: from the whole book to a single surah.
4
Consider the Arab psychology
Al-Nasafi notes that the Arabs were eloquent and proud; they would not hide their ability. If they could have opposed the Qur'an, they would have. Their failure is proof of their incapacity.
This argument refutes the idea that they simply chose not to respond.
5
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
The Qur'an's unique composition and the unfulfilled challenge demonstrate that it is beyond human capacity, hence a divine miracle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتحدث بعد ذلك عن الإعجاز القرآني، وكونه كتابا باقيا للأبد، فيه صلاح الدنيا والدين، ويرجع إعجازه إلى أمور منها. 1 - نظمه العجيب، فقد باين المنثور، والمنظور، والرجز، والخطبة والرسالة، وهو بنظمه يتحدى جميع البشر أن يأتوا بمثله، أو بعشر سور من مثله، أو بسورة من مثله، وما زال يتحدى ولا أحد يستطيع أي شيء، وفي هذا يتحدث عن طبيعة العرب وكيف أن طبيعتهم تمنعهم من إخفاء ما يعرفون، فهم لو قدروا على معارضة القرآن لعارضوه.
And afterwards he speaks about the Qur'anic i'jaz, and that it is an eternal book, containing the righteousness of this world and religion, and he attributes its i'jaz to matters among them: 1 - Its wonderful nazm (composition), for it differs from prose, poetry, rajaz, sermons, and epistles, and by its composition it challenges all of humanity to produce something like it, or ten surahs like it, or one surah like it, and it continues to challenge and no one is able to do anything. And in this he speaks about the nature of the Arabs and how their nature prevents them from hiding what they know; if they had been capable of opposing the Qur'an, they would have opposed it.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage explains the first aspect of the Qur'an's miraculousness: its unique composition (nazm). Al-Nasafi states that the Qur'an is not like any known form of Arabic speech—prose, poetry, rajaz (metrical short verse), sermons, or letters. It is distinct. Then he describes the tahaddi (challenge) that the Qur'an poses to all humanity: to produce its like, or ten surahs, or even one surah. This challenge has remained unfulfilled. He then highlights a psychological point: the Arabs were proud of their eloquence and would not suppress criticism; if they could have imitated it, they would have. Their failure is proof that they were incapable, not unwilling.

Key Terms

نظم
تحدي
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Qur'an's miracle is only its content, not its form.
Correction: This passage emphasizes that the unique composition (nazm) itself is a primary aspect of the miracle, not just the content.

Checkpoint

A) They were too busy with wars to respond.
B) Their nature prevents them from hiding their inability.
C) They were ignorant of Arabic.
D) They admired the Qur'an but chose not to imitate it.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's unique composition, distinct from all known Arabic forms, and its unfulfilled challenge to produce even one surah, constitute a primary proof of its divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Nasafi says the Qur'an's nazm is unlike any other speech. When you recite the Qur'an, do you feel its distinctiveness, or have your ears become accustomed to its words? What specific change will you make this week to read it with fresh awareness of its uniqueness?
?
The Arabs would have opposed the Qur'an if they could, but they could not. You have never attempted to produce a verse like it—does that mean you accept it as divine, or have you simply never put your eloquence to the test? If you truly believe it is inimitable, why do your words often contradict its teachings?
?
The challenge continues until today. What does your inability to produce a single verse like it reveal about your own pride and submission? Will you stop using your tongue for arguments and start using it to declare the greatness of the One whose speech you cannot match?

Definition of Istita'a and Its Two Divisions
Nasafi defines istita'a and divides it into two types with Qur'anic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of istita'a (capacity) as presented by Nasafi, including its two distinct divisions and the Qur'anic evidence for each.

Core Idea
Istita'a has two layers: external means and internal created power.

Before we can discuss human capacity in relation to divine action, we need a clear definition of what 'capacity' (istita'a) actually means. Nasafi, following earlier mutakallimūn, provides a precise definition and then splits the concept into two essential categories—one relating to external instruments, the other to an internal, created power. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding how voluntary actions are possible within a theistic framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of istita'a
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Nasafi defines istita'a by naming three synonyms: qudra, quwwa, and tāqa. All three are close in meaning.
2
Identify the two divisions
Look for the two types Nasafi enumerates: (1) soundness of causes and health of instruments, and (2) a meaning that is an accident (‛araḍ) created by Allah. Note how each is defined differently.
The first division is about readiness and means; the second is about the actual power to act.
3
Examine the Qur'anic proofs
For each division, Nasafi cites a verse. Understand why each verse supports that particular type. The first verse (58:4) refers to inability to perform an act due to lack of means; the second verse (11:20) refers to inability despite having the means—thus blameworthy.
This distinction is crucial for later theological debates about human responsibility.
4
Reflect on the key insight
Ask yourself: Why does Nasafi split istita'a into two? How does this affect our understanding of human action and divine creation? The commentary will help clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الاستطاعة: يعرف الاستطاعة بما عرفها به الكثير من المتكلمين مثل الأشعري ووالباقلاني بأنها : القدرة والقوة والطاقة، وكلها معان متقاربة، ويقسمها إلى قسمين: ١ - سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات وهذه تتقدم الأفعال وهي من نعم الله سبحانه وتعالى. وهي تحد بالتهيؤ لتنفيذ الفعل عند إرادة المختار . ۲- فهو معنى يتعذر حده وهو عرض يخلقه الله تعالى للحيوان ليفعل به أفعاله الاختيارية، وهو علة للفعل. ثم يدلل النسفي على وجود الاستطاعة وانقسامها إلى هذين القسمين، فعن القسم الأول يستشهد بقوله تعالى : فَمَن لَّمْ يَسْتَطِعْ فَإِطْعَامُ سِيِّينَ مِسكينًا ﴾ [المجادلة: ٤] والمراد هنا سلامة الأسباب والآلات. ويستشهد للقسم الثاني بقوله تعالى: مَا كَانُوا يَسْتَطِيعُونَ السَّمْعَ ﴾ [هود: ٢٠] والمراد منه نفي حقيقة القدرة لا نفي الأسباب والآلات، لأنها كانت ثابتة. وهذا موجب للذم ، لأنه تضييع للقدرة المعطاة من الله تعالى.
The discussion on istita‛a (capacity): istita‛a is defined as what many of the mutakallimūn (theologians) such as al-Ash‛arī and al-Bāqillānī defined it, namely: power (qudra), strength (quwwa), and energy (ṭāqa), all of which are close in meaning. He divides it into two parts: 1) Soundness of causes (salāmat al-asbāb) and health of instruments (ṣiḥḥat al-ālāt) – these precede actions and are among the blessings of Allah, Exalted is He. They are defined as the readiness (tahayyu’) to execute the action when the free agent wills. 2) A meaning (ma‛nā) that is difficult to define, which is an accident (‛araḍ) that Allah creates in the living being so that he may perform his voluntary acts through it, and it is the cause (‛illa) of the act. Then al-Nasafī provides evidence for the existence of istita‛a and its division into these two parts. For the first part he cites the saying of the Exalted: “But if one does not find [the ability], then a fast of three days” [al-Mujādila 4] – and what is meant here is soundness of causes and instruments. For the second part he cites the saying of the Exalted: “They were not able to hear” [Hūd 20] – and what is meant by it is the negation of the reality of power (ḥaqīqat al-qudra), not the negation of causes and instruments, because they were [physically] present. And this warrants blame, because it is squandering the capacity given by Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the concept of istita‛a (capacity). The author first states that istita‛a is defined by major mutakallimūn as qudra, quwwa, and ṭāqa, all synonymous. He then divides it into two types. The first type is 'soundness of causes and health of instruments' – these precede the action and are gifts from Allah. They are described as readiness to perform an act when the free agent decides. The second type is a subtle meaning that is defined as an accident (‛araḍ) created by Allah in the living being, through which voluntary actions are performed, and it is the cause (‛illa) of the act. After defining them, al-Nasafī provides Qur'anic evidence for each type: for the first, Q. 58:4 (concerning the expiation of ẓihār, where the inability refers to the means); for the second, Q. 11:20 (where the inability refers to actual power, not means, because the means were present – hence they are blamed for not using the power given). Thus, the passage establishes a dual conception of istita‛a: external means and internal created power.

Key Terms
استطاعة (istita‛a)
قدرة (qudra)
قوة (quwwa)
طاقة (ṭāqa)
سلامة الأسباب (salāmat al-asbāb)
عرض (‛araḍ)
علة (‛illa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Istita'a is only the external means (salāmat al-asbāb) and not a separate internal power.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly distinguishes a second type of istita'a: an accident (‛araḍ) created by Allah in the creature, which is the cause of voluntary acts. Both types together constitute the full concept.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafī, what are the two types of istita‛a?
A) Power and weakness
B) Soundness of causes/instruments and a created accident
C) Knowledge and action
D) Free will and predestination

Key Takeaway
Istita'a comprises both external readiness (means) and an internal created power (accident), and each is supported by distinct Qur'anic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your current ability to obey Allah, do you take it for granted, or do you recognize that both your external means (health, wealth, time) and your internal capacity (your desire and ability to act) are gifts directly from Him? What is one external blessing you often overlook?
?
The Quran says of the disbelievers that 'they were not able to hear' even though their ears were intact. Is there an area of your life where you have the external means (knowledge, opportunity) but lack the internal drive or sincerity? What would it mean for you to stop squandering the capacity Allah has given you?
?
Nasafi says the second type of istita'a is an accident created by Allah in you. How would your daily actions change if you truly believed that your every ability to do good is an instantaneous creation of Allah? Pause right now and thank Allah for the ability to read and understand this lesson.

The First Meaning of Istita'a: Safety of Causes
Nasafi defines the first meaning of istita'a and its properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct meanings of istita'a and the properties of the first meaning: safety of causes and soundness of instruments.

Core Idea
The first meaning of istita'a is universally accepted as prior to the act and valid for both opposites.

This lesson begins with two fundamental questions about istita'a: does it come before or with the act, and can it apply to both opposites? Nasafi first answers regarding the first meaning of istita'a, which is based on physical soundness. This initial answer enjoys broad agreement among theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noticing the two questions and the answer.
2
Identify the first meaning
Note that the first meaning is defined as 'safety of causes and soundness of instruments'.
The term 'istita'a' is being defined in a specific sense.
3
Understand its properties
The passage says this type is prior to the act and valid for both the act and its opposite.
4
Recognize the consensus
Nasafi says this is agreed upon among those who affirm istita'a.
This sets the first meaning apart from the second.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسأل هل الاستطاعة متقدمة على الفعل أم مقارنة له؟ وهل هي صالحة للضدين أم لا؟ ويجيب على ذلك : بأن الاستطاعة بمعنى سلامة الأسباب، وصحة الآلات متقدمة على الفعل وهي صالحة للفعل وضده بلا خلاف بين من يثبتون الاستطاعة. أما الاستطاعة الثانية بمعنى القدرة فالمعتزلة يقولون بتقدمها على الفعل ويستحیل اقترانها به أما النسفي ،وأصحابه والنجارية، والأشاعرة فيقولون: إنها تكون مقارنة للفعل ومحال تقدمها عليه.
He asks: Is istita'a (capacity) prior to the act or simultaneous with it? And is it valid for both opposites or not? And he answers that: istita'a in the sense of safety of causes and soundness of instruments is prior to the act and is valid for the act and its opposite, without dispute among those who affirm istita'a. As for the second istita'a in the sense of power (qudra), the Mu'tazila say it is prior to the act and impossible for it to coincide with it, while al-Nasafi, his companions, al-Najjariyya, and the Ash'aris say: it is simultaneous with the act and impossible for it to be prior to it.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. It begins by posing two questions: whether istita'a (capacity) precedes or accompanies the act, and whether it is valid for both opposites. The author then answers by distinguishing two meanings of istita'a. The first meaning is safety of causes and soundness of instruments; this type is prior to the act and is valid for both the act and its opposite, universally agreed upon. The second meaning is power (qudra); regarding this, the Mu'tazila hold that it precedes the act and cannot coincide with it, while al-Nasafi, his companions, al-Najjariyya, and the Ash'aris hold that it coincides with the act and cannot precede it. The author thus presents the basic terminological clarification and the doctrinal split on the issue of precedence.

Key Terms
الاستطاعة
سلامة الأسباب
صحة الآلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that istita'a always refers to the same thing and has a single ruling.
Correction: In fact, istita'a has two distinct meanings, each with its own ruling regarding precedence. The first meaning is prior and agreed upon.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the first meaning of istita'a?
A) Power to do an act
B) Safety of causes and soundness of instruments
C) Freedom to choose
D) Divine assistance

Key Takeaway
The first meaning of istita'a is safety of causes and soundness of instruments, which is agreed to precede the act and be valid for both opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every capacity you have right now—your health, your limbs, your opportunities—is a trust from Allah. Do you truly use these gifts for what He loves, or do you squander them on what He has forbidden?
?
The first meaning of istita'a is valid for both obedience and sin. Without increased divine grace, you are equally capable of error. When you sin, do you realize that you had the capacity to obey but chose otherwise? How will you seek more grace from Allah to use your capacity for good?
?
Since the first meaning of istita'a is prior to the act, your ability to act is given before you choose. This means every action you intend is only possible because Allah has already prepared the means. Have you ever thanked Allah for your ability to move, speak, or think? Will you begin each day with gratitude for the capacity He grants?

The Debate on Precedence of Istita'a as Power (Qudra)
Nasafi presents the doctrinal split regarding the second meaning of istita'a (qudra).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting positions of the Mu'tazila and the majority (Nasafi, Ash'aris, etc.) regarding whether the second meaning of istita'a (qudra) precedes or coincides with the act.

Core Idea
For istita'a as qudra, Mu'tazila say it precedes; Nasafi and others say it coincides.

Now Nasafi turns to the second meaning of istita'a: power (qudra). This meaning generates a major theological dispute. The Mu'tazila hold that this power must exist before the act, while the majority—including Nasafi, his school, al-Najjariyya, and the Ash'aris—insist that it is simultaneous with the act. This lesson presents both sides clearly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second meaning
Note that the second meaning is 'power' (qudra), not safety of causes.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila position
They say it is prior to the act and impossible for it to coincide.
3
Understand the majority position
Nasafi, his companions, al-Najjariyya, and the Ash'aris say it coincides with the act and cannot be prior.
4
Contrast the views
Compare the two positions and note the key difference: timing of power relative to the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسأل هل الاستطاعة متقدمة على الفعل أم مقارنة له؟ وهل هي صالحة للضدين أم لا؟ ويجيب على ذلك : بأن الاستطاعة بمعنى سلامة الأسباب، وصحة الآلات متقدمة على الفعل وهي صالحة للفعل وضده بلا خلاف بين من يثبتون الاستطاعة. أما الاستطاعة الثانية بمعنى القدرة فالمعتزلة يقولون بتقدمها على الفعل ويستحیل اقترانها به أما النسفي ،وأصحابه والنجارية، والأشاعرة فيقولون: إنها تكون مقارنة للفعل ومحال تقدمها عليه.
He asks: Is istita'a (capacity) prior to the act or simultaneous with it? And is it valid for both opposites or not? And he answers that: istita'a in the sense of safety of causes and soundness of instruments is prior to the act and is valid for the act and its opposite, without dispute among those who affirm istita'a. As for the second istita'a in the sense of power (qudra), the Mu'tazila say it is prior to the act and impossible for it to coincide with it, while al-Nasafi, his companions, al-Najjariyya, and the Ash'aris say: it is simultaneous with the act and impossible for it to be prior to it.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. It begins by posing two questions: whether istita'a (capacity) precedes or accompanies the act, and whether it is valid for both opposites. The author then answers by distinguishing two meanings of istita'a. The first meaning is safety of causes and soundness of instruments; this type is prior to the act and is valid for both the act and its opposite, universally agreed upon. The second meaning is power (qudra); regarding this, the Mu'tazila hold that it precedes the act and cannot coincide with it, while al-Nasafi, his companions, al-Najjariyya, and the Ash'aris hold that it coincides with the act and cannot precede it. The author thus presents the basic terminological clarification and the doctrinal split on the issue of precedence.

Key Terms

القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all theologians agree on the timing of power relative to the act.
Correction: In fact, there is a major disagreement: the Mu'tazila say power precedes, while the majority say it coincides.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of the Ash'aris regarding the second meaning of istita'a (qudra)?
A) It precedes the act and cannot coincide with it.
B) It coincides with the act and cannot precede it.
C) It is both prior and simultaneous.
D) It depends on the type of act.

Key Takeaway
For istita'a as power (qudra), the Mu'tazila argue for precedence, while the majority (including Ash'aris) argue for simultaneity with the act.

?

Nasafi's Definition of Rizq
Nasafi defines rizq and cites scholarly agreement on its wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's definition of rizq (provision) as nourishment decreed by Allah, regardless of consumption.

Core Idea
Provision is what Allah decrees as nourishment, not just what is eaten.

Al-Nasafi presents a precise theological definition of rizq, distinguishing it from mere consumption. He aligns with earlier scholars who held that disagreements about rizq are merely verbal. This lesson unpacks his definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the wording. Then read the English translation.
Notice the key terms: الرزق and الغذاء.
2
Identify the definition
Locate where Nasafi states his definition of rizq. He says it is 'al-ghidha' (nourishment) that Allah has decreed for an individual.
3
Understand the nuance
Reflect on the phrase 'لا يأكله أحد أبدا' (no one ever eats it). This shows that rizq is not dependent on actual consumption.
This corrects the common misunderstanding that provision is only what is physically eaten.
4
Consider the scholarly context
Note that Nasafi references two earlier scholars who said the disagreement is in expression. This indicates that the definition is widely accepted despite apparent differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعرف الرزق ويقول في النهاية: إن الشيخين الرستغفني، وأبا إسحاق الأسفراييني قالا: إن الخلاف في العبارة. وهو يرى أن هذا هو الصواب. ويعرف النسفي الرزق بأنه الغذاء، فما قدره الله تعالى ليكون غذاء لفرد لا يأكله أحد أبدا.
And he defines provision (al-rizq) and says in the end: The two shaykhs, al-Rustughfani and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, said that the disagreement is in expression. And he sees that this is correct. And al-Nasafi defines provision as nourishment, so whatever Allah Almighty has decreed to be nourishment for an individual, no one ever eats it.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents al-Nasafi's definition of rizq (provision). It begins by noting that the earlier scholars al-Rustughfani and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held that the disagreement about rizq is merely verbal. Al-Nasafi affirms this view. He then defines rizq as 'al-ghidha' (nourishment) – that is, the sustenance that Allah has determined to be nourishment for a particular creature, even if that creature never actually consumes it. This definition is significant because it separates the concept of provision from actual consumption: something is provision if Allah decrees it as such, regardless of whether it reaches the intended being.

Key Terms
الرزق
الغذاء
الشيخين الرستغفني
أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that rizq only includes what a person actually eats or benefits from.
Correction: Nasafi defines rizq as the nourishment that Allah has decreed for a being, even if it is never consumed. Thus, rizq is based on divine decree, not on actual consumption.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defines rizq as the nourishment Allah decrees for a creature, irrespective of actual consumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the statement: 'Whatever Allah has decreed to be nourishment for an individual, no one ever eats it.' How does this affect your fear of losing sustenance or your envy of others' wealth?

The Mu'tazilite Objections to Divine Will
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite scriptural and rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main Mu'tazilite objections – scriptural and rational – against the attribution of evil will to Allah.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that willing evil makes one evil, even for God.

The Mu'tazila raise both a scriptural proof and a rational analogy to argue that Allah cannot will disbelief or sin. Their scriptural proof cites a verse that seems to show Allah only wills worship, while their rational proof uses analogy to human agents. This lesson presents these objections before we see Nasafi's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the structure: introduction, then the two proofs.
The passage begins with 'ويبدأ في الرد...'
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the scriptural proof (Quran 51:56) and the rational proof (analogy to the witnessed).
3
Understand the rational analogy
How does the analogy from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib) work? What is the implication for Allah?
The Mu'tazila argue that if a human willing foolishness is foolish, then Allah willing unbelief would also be attributed with unbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبدأ في الرد على شبه المعتزلة النقلية والعقلية فمن النقلية قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ﴾ [الذاريات: ٥٦] ومن العقلية قولهم: مريد السفه سفيه اعتبارا بالشاهد، ومريد الكفر كافر في الشاهد، فكذا في الغائب. وبعد رده يذكر لتأكيد كلامه قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا يَحْسَبَنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا أَنَّمَا نُمْلِي هُمْ خَيْرٌ لأَنفُسِهِمْ إِنَّمَا نُمْلِي لَهُمْ لِيَزْدَادُوا إِنْما } [آل عمران: ۱۷۸] فقد دلت الآية أنه أراد منهم أزدياد الإثم، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَقَدْ ذَرَأْنَا لِجَهَنَّمَ كَثِيرًا مِّنَ الْجِنِّ وَالْإِنسِ ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۷۹] أي أراد منهم ما به يصيرون لجهنم. ويقول : إن المعتزلة تزعم أن اللام في الآيتين لام العاقبة، لا لام التعليل ويرد قولهم هذا قائلا: إنما يتصور أن يريد أحد من أخر ما لا يكون منه أن لو كان الفاعل جاهلا بالعواقب، فأما من لم يجهل العواقب كيف يريد عاقبة لفعل يعلم حقيقته أنها لا تكون على ما يريد، وهل يفعل هذا إلا سفيه - تنزه الله عن ذلك و استعمال لام العاقبة فيمن يجوز عليه الجهل.
He begins to refute the transmitted and rational doubts of the Mu'tazila. Among the transmitted proofs is His statement: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me' (Quran 51:56). And among the rational proofs is their saying: The one who wills foolishness is foolish by analogy to the witnessed, and the one who wills unbelief is an unbeliever in the witnessed, and likewise in the unseen. After his refutation, he mentions to confirm his statement His statement: 'And let not those who disbelieve think that We prolong their respite for their own good; We only prolong it for them so that they may increase in sin' (Quran 3:178). For the verse indicates that He willed for them increase of sin. And His statement: 'And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' (Quran 7:179) meaning He willed for them that by which they become for Hell. And he says: The Mu'tazila claim that the lam in the two verses is the lam of consequence, not the lam of causation. And he refutes their statement saying: It is only conceivable that one would intend from another what is not from him if the agent were ignorant of the consequences. As for one who is not ignorant of the consequences, how can he intend a consequence for an act while he knows the reality that it will not be as he intends? And does one do this except a fool – exalted is God above that! And the use of the lam of consequence is for one upon whom ignorance is permissible.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite objections and Nasafi's refutation. First, he lists their scriptural proof: the verse 'I did not create... except to worship Me,' implying that God willed worship from all, but if some do not worship, then God's will is not done. Their rational proof: in the witnessed world, one who wills foolishness is called foolish; similarly, one who wills unbelief is called an unbeliever; by analogy, if God wills unbelief from someone, He would be attributed with that. Nasafi then brings additional verses to support his own position: Quran 3:178 shows God willed increase of sin for disbelievers, and Quran 7:179 shows God willed for many to be for Hell. This establishes that God can will unbelief. The Mu'tazila respond that the 'lam' in these verses is for consequence (لام العاقبة) not purpose (لام التعليل). Nasafi refutes this by arguing that the lam of consequence is only used when the agent is ignorant of outcomes, but God is all-knowing. If God willed a consequence knowing it would not happen, that would be foolishness – which is impossible for God. Therefore, the lam must indicate purpose, not mere consequence.

Key Terms
شبه المعتزلة
اعتبار بالشاهد
إرادة السفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Mu'tazilite arguments are purely scriptural.
Correction: They also rely on rational analogy from human behavior.

Checkpoint
Which verse do the Mu'tazila use as their scriptural proof?
A) Quran 3:178
B) Quran 51:56
C) Quran 7:179
D) Quran 2:30

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that willing evil is incompatible with divinity, using both a verse and a rational analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God wills all that occurs, including evil, how does that affect the way you respond to personal suffering or injustice? Will you now consciously surrender your 'why' to His wisdom, or will you cling to your own sense of fairness?
?
The Mu'tazilites were so concerned with God's justice that they denied He could will evil. But this lesson shows they based their argument on human analogy. Are you willing to let God be God, even when His actions seem to conflict with your moral intuition? What would it cost you to trust His will over your own reasoning?
?
When you sin, do you blame your circumstances, your nafs, or even secretly protest that God allowed it? This passage forces you to examine: Do you really believe that God's will is always good, even when it permits your own failure? Commit to one concrete change in how you view your next mistake.

Nasafi's Refutation: The Lam of Consequence vs. Causation
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite interpretation of the lam as consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that the 'lam' in certain verses indicates consequence rather than purpose.

Core Idea
The lam of consequence requires ignorance, impossible for Allah.

After presenting additional Quranic evidence that Allah indeed wills increase of sin and creates for Hell, Nasafi tackles the Mu'tazilite counterargument that the 'lam' in these verses is for consequence, not purpose. He argues that using the lam of consequence implies ignorance of outcomes, which cannot be attributed to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage containing additional verses
Read the Arabic passage focusing on Quran 3:178 and 7:179.
These verses show Allah willing negative outcomes.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim about the lam
They claim the lam is for consequence (lam al-‘aqiba), not causation (lam al-ta‘lil).
3
Grasp Nasafi's rational refutation
Why does Nasafi say that using the lam of consequence implies ignorance? How does he conclude it is foolishness?
Allah knows all outcomes, so He cannot will a consequence knowing it won't happen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبدأ في الرد على شبه المعتزلة النقلية والعقلية فمن النقلية قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ﴾ [الذاريات: ٥٦] ومن العقلية قولهم: مريد السفه سفيه اعتبارا بالشاهد، ومريد الكفر كافر في الشاهد، فكذا في الغائب. وبعد رده يذكر لتأكيد كلامه قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا يَحْسَبَنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا أَنَّمَا نُمْلِي هُمْ خَيْرٌ لأَنفُسِهِمْ إِنَّمَا نُمْلِي لَهُمْ لِيَزْدَادُوا إِنْما } [آل عمران: ۱۷۸] فقد دلت الآية أنه أراد منهم أزدياد الإثم، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَقَدْ ذَرَأْنَا لِجَهَنَّمَ كَثِيرًا مِّنَ الْجِنِّ وَالْإِنسِ ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۷۹] أي أراد منهم ما به يصيرون لجهنم. ويقول : إن المعتزلة تزعم أن اللام في الآيتين لام العاقبة، لا لام التعليل ويرد قولهم هذا قائلا: إنما يتصور أن يريد أحد من أخر ما لا يكون منه أن لو كان الفاعل جاهلا بالعواقب، فأما من لم يجهل العواقب كيف يريد عاقبة لفعل يعلم حقيقته أنها لا تكون على ما يريد، وهل يفعل هذا إلا سفيه - تنزه الله عن ذلك و استعمال لام العاقبة فيمن يجوز عليه الجهل.
He begins to refute the transmitted and rational doubts of the Mu'tazila. Among the transmitted proofs is His statement: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me' (Quran 51:56). And among the rational proofs is their saying: The one who wills foolishness is foolish by analogy to the witnessed, and the one who wills unbelief is an unbeliever in the witnessed, and likewise in the unseen. After his refutation, he mentions to confirm his statement His statement: 'And let not those who disbelieve think that We prolong their respite for their own good; We only prolong it for them so that they may increase in sin' (Quran 3:178). For the verse indicates that He willed for them increase of sin. And His statement: 'And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' (Quran 7:179) meaning He willed for them that by which they become for Hell. And he says: The Mu'tazila claim that the lam in the two verses is the lam of consequence, not the lam of causation. And he refutes their statement saying: It is only conceivable that one would intend from another what is not from him if the agent were ignorant of the consequences. As for one who is not ignorant of the consequences, how can he intend a consequence for an act while he knows the reality that it will not be as he intends? And does one do this except a fool – exalted is God above that! And the use of the lam of consequence is for one upon whom ignorance is permissible.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite objections and Nasafi's refutation. First, he lists their scriptural proof: the verse 'I did not create... except to worship Me,' implying that God willed worship from all, but if some do not worship, then God's will is not done. Their rational proof: in the witnessed world, one who wills foolishness is called foolish; similarly, one who wills unbelief is called an unbeliever; by analogy, if God wills unbelief from someone, He would be attributed with that. Nasafi then brings additional verses to support his own position: Quran 3:178 shows God willed increase of sin for disbelievers, and Quran 7:179 shows God willed for many to be for Hell. This establishes that God can will unbelief. The Mu'tazila respond that the 'lam' in these verses is for consequence (لام العاقبة) not purpose (لام التعليل). Nasafi refutes this by arguing that the lam of consequence is only used when the agent is ignorant of outcomes, but God is all-knowing. If God willed a consequence knowing it would not happen, that would be foolishness – which is impossible for God. Therefore, the lam must indicate purpose, not mere consequence.

Key Terms
لام العاقبة
لام التعليل
تنزه الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the lam of consequence can be used for Allah.
Correction: Nasafi shows it requires ignorance, which is impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why can the lam of consequence not be applied to Allah?
A) Because Allah does not create consequences
B) Because it implies ignorance of outcomes
C) Because the verses contradict it
D) Because the Mu'tazila misinterpret the grammar

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim by showing that the lam of consequence implies ignorance, which cannot be attributed to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own life, do you tend to explain difficult events as mere 'consequences' rather than purposeful from Allah? How does Nasafi's refutation challenge you to see even hardship as intentionally willed by Him?
?
If you truly believed that every delay in your repentance is not random but a deliberate prolonging for you to increase in sin or to turn back, how would that change your urgency in seeking forgiveness tonight?
?
What does your discomfort with the idea that Allah intentionally created some for Hell reveal about your own assumptions regarding divine justice? Are you willing to submit to His wisdom, even when it challenges your sense of fairness?

The Various Definitions of Iman in Islamic Theology
Nasafi lists definitions of iman from five theological schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major theological definitions of iman (faith) and why al-Nasafi chooses the definition of iman as affirmation (tasdiq) in the heart.

Core Idea
Faith is defined differently by different schools—al-Nasafi traces the debate to its core.

What is faith? This question sparked intense debate among early Muslim theologians. In this passage, al-Nasafi surveys the major definitions proposed by different schools, ranging from a comprehensive view including action to a minimal definition of mere knowledge. By examining these views, we see how the precise boundaries of iman have been contested—and where al-Nasafi himself stands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's own presentation come through.
2
Identify each definition and its proponents
The passage lists five definitions. For each, note which group holds it and what components of faith are included or excluded.
Proponents include Ahl al-Hadith, mutakallimun, Karamiyya, Jahmiyya, and the tasdiq view.
3
Compare the components of each definition
Observe how the definitions differ in including knowledge, confession, action, and affirmation. Create a mental table.
Action is only in the first definition; confession alone in the third; knowledge alone in the fourth.
4
Reflect on al-Nasafi's choice
Al-Nasafi explicitly states he chooses the last opinion, which defines iman as affirmation in the heart. Ask yourself why this might be his preferred view.
This definition is attributed to major figures like Abu Hanifa and al-Ash'ari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما هو الإيمان؟
الكلام في الإيمان.
يبدأ النسفي إجابة عن هذا السؤال بعرض مجموعة من تعريفات للإيمان قالت بها الفرق الكلامية ناسبا كل تعريف إلى صاحبه.
من ذلك القول بأن الإيمان معرفة بالقلب وإقرار باللسان وعمل بالأركان وينسب هذا القول إلى أصحاب الحديث.
ومنهم من قال إن الإيمان يكون بالقلب واللسان وينسب هذا القول إلى كثير من طوائف المتكلمين، وعلى رأسهم الحنفية، والنجارية.
أما الكرامية وعبد الله بن سعيد وغيرهما فإنهم يقولون: إن الإيمان يكون باللسان فقط.
وهناك طائفة الجهمية وغيرهم يقولون: إن الإيمان هو المعرفة.
ومنهم من قال: إن الإيمان هو التصديق بالقلب، وهو مروي عن أبي حنيفة وأبي منصور الماتريدي، والحسين بن الفضل البجلي، وأبي الحسن الأشعري. وقد اختار النسفي الرأي الأخير.
What is faith?
The discourse on faith.
Al-Nasafi begins answering this question by presenting a set of definitions of faith that the theological schools have stated, attributing each definition to its proponent.
Among them is the statement that faith is knowledge in the heart, confession with the tongue, and action with the limbs, and this statement is attributed to the people of hadith.
And among them are those who said that faith is in the heart and the tongue, and this statement is attributed to many groups of the theologians, foremost among them the Hanafis and the Najjariyya.
As for the Karamiyya and 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id and others, they say: faith is only with the tongue.
And there is the group of the Jahmiyya and others who say: faith is knowledge.
And among them are those who said: faith is affirmation in the heart, and it is reported from Abu Hanifa, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, al-Husayn ibn al-Fadl al-Bajali, and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. And al-Nasafi chose the last opinion.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This is a classification passage. Al-Nasafi introduces the subject of faith (iman) and presents the various definitions given by different theological schools. He attributes each definition to its proponents: (1) The People of Hadith (Ahl al-Hadith) hold that faith consists of knowledge (ma'rifa) in the heart, confession (iqrar) with the tongue, and action (amal) with the limbs. (2) Many theologians (mutakallimun), especially the Hanafis and Najjariyya, say faith is in the heart and the tongue (i.e., knowledge and confession, but not necessarily action). (3) The Karamiyya and 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id say faith is only verbal confession (bi al-lisan faqat). (4) The Jahmiyya and others say faith is simply knowledge (ma'rifa). (5) Finally, there is the view that faith is affirmation (tasdiq) in the heart, related from Abu Hanifa, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, al-Husayn ibn al-Fadl al-Bajali, and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Al-Nasafi explicitly states that he chooses the last opinion (the tasdiq view).

Key Terms
إيمان
معرفة
إقرار
عمل
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iman includes action by definition.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that many scholars separate action from the essence of iman; only Ahl al-Hadith include action in the definition.

Checkpoint
Which group defines iman as only confession by the tongue?
A) Ahl al-Hadith
B) The majority of mutakallimun
C) Karamiyya
D) Jahmiyya

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi presents five definitions of iman and chooses the view that faith is affirmation (tasdiq) in the heart, a position held by many leading theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading al-Nasafi's list of definitions, which one do you find yourself instinctively leaning toward? Does your daily life reflect that definition, or do you act as if a different definition is true?
?
Al-Nasafi chose tasdiq (heart affirmation) as the essence of faith—not requiring action or confession. If you truly believe that, how does it change the way you view your own sins or the sins of other Muslims? Are you able to still call them believers without hesitation?
?
Consider the Jahmiyya definition: faith is mere knowledge. You know Allah exists, but is there a gap between that knowledge and a heart that is fully submitted? What would it take for you to move from ma'rifa to tasdiq in a way that transforms your life?

The Linguistic Meaning of Iman as Tasdiq
Nasafi gives linguistic definition and first Qur'anic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that iman (faith) linguistically means tasdiq (affirmation) and that the Qur'an distinguishes it from action in Surah Hud 23.

Core Idea
Iman is affirmation in the heart, not action.

Nasafi begins by establishing the linguistic foundation: iman means tasdiq. He then cites the Qur'anic verse in Hud 23 that separates faith and righteous deeds with 'and,' showing they are distinct. This lesson clarifies the core definition that will be supported by further evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the linguistic definition and the first Qur'anic proof.
Pay attention to the conjunction 'wa' in the verse.
2
Identify the key terms
Note the words 'tasdiq' (affirmation) and 'takdhib' (denial) as opposites.
3
Analyze the verse
Understand how the verse from Hud 23 shows that iman and 'amal (action) are separate because they are joined by 'and'.
This is a common argument against including action in the definition.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this distinction matter? It means a person can have iman without having performed all righteous deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخذ يقيم الأدلة على صحته. فالإيمان عند أهل اللغة التصديق، وضده التكذيب.
والله سبحانه يقول: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ ﴾ [هود: ٢٣] فالإيمان غير العمل. ثم إن الله تعالى خاطب من ارتكب المعاصي الموجبة للحدود باسم المؤمنين في مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتْلَى ﴾ [البقرة: ١٧٨] ويؤكد معنى التصديق أن الأعداء يلجأون إلى التصديق دون غيره عند معاينة العذاب نحو قول فرعون حينما أدركه الغرق: ﴿ آمَنتُ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا الَّذِي آمَنَتْ بِهِ بَنُو إِسْرَائِيلَ ﴾ [يونس: ٩٠] أي صدقت، والله سبحانه وتعالى خاطب باسم الإيمان، ثم أوجب بعد ذلك الأعمال في نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٨٣].
ثم إن الإيمان إذا كان عبارة عن التصديق والإقرار والعمل فمعنى ذلك أنه إذا زال ركن من الأركان الثلاثة زال الإيمان مع أن المسلمين أثبتوا حكمه بمجرد الاعتقاد قبل وجود غيره من الأعمال.
ثم إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حينما سأله جبريل عليه السلام عن الإيمان أجاب: "أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته.. إلخ" أي أن تصدق، ولو كان الإيمان غير التصديق لكان مجيء جبريل ليلبس على الناس لا ليعلمهم وكان النبي مقصرا في الجواب.
وقول النبي حينما سئل عن أفضل الأعمال: "إيمان لا شك فيه، وجهاد لا غلول فيه، وحج مبرور" ففي كلامه عليه السلام مغايرة بين الإيمان والجهاد والحج. ويناقش النسفي كلام من قال بغير ذلك، ليبطله، ويدعم رأي أهل السنة والجماعة.
And he began to adduce the proofs for its correctness. So faith according to the people of language is affirmation, and its opposite is denial.
And God, exalted, says: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds' [Hud: 23] — so faith is other than action. Then God, exalted, addressed those who committed sins necessitating legal punishments by the name of believers in such as His statement, exalted: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is legal retribution in cases of murder' [al-Baqara: 178]. And the meaning of affirmation is confirmed by the fact that the enemies resort to affirmation without anything else at the sight of punishment, like the statement of Pharaoh when drowning overtook him: 'I believe that there is no god except the One whom the Children of Israel believe in' [Yunus: 90] — meaning 'I affirmed.' And God, exalted, addressed [them] with the name of faith, then obligated actions after that in such as His statement, exalted: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is fasting' [al-Baqara: 183].
Then if faith were an expression of affirmation, confession, and action, that would mean that if one of the three pillars is removed, faith is removed, whereas the Muslims have confirmed its ruling by mere belief before the existence of other actions.
Then the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, when Gabriel, peace be upon him, asked him about faith, answered: 'That you believe in God, His angels, etc.' — that is, that you affirm. And if faith were other than affirmation, the coming of Gabriel would be to confuse people, not to teach them, and the Prophet would have been deficient in the answer.
And the saying of the Prophet when asked about the best deeds: 'Faith without doubt, jihad without cheating, and an accepted hajj' — in his speech, peace be upon him, there is a distinction between faith, jihad, and hajj. And al-Nasafi discusses the speech of those who say otherwise, to invalidate it, and to support the view of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This is a proof passage. Al-Nasafi begins with the linguistic definition: iman (faith) means tasdiq (affirmation) in the language, and its opposite is takdhib (denial). He then provides several Qur'anic proofs: (1) The verse 'Those who have believed and done righteous deeds' (Hud 23) separates faith from action by the conjunction 'and,' showing that they are distinct. (2) God addresses believers who commit sins subject to legal punishment as 'believers' (al-Baqara 178), indicating that faith remains despite sin. (3) Pharaoh, at the moment of drowning, said 'Amantu' meaning 'Saddaqtu' (I affirmed), confirming that iman refers to affirmation. (4) God addresses people as believers before obligating fasting (al-Baqara 183), showing that faith precedes and is distinct from action. Next, he gives a logical argument: if iman included action, then losing any component would remove faith; but Muslims consider a person a believer merely by conviction before performing deeds, proving that action is not part of the definition. Then he uses hadith: (a) The Prophet's response to Jibril about iman: 'that you believe in God, His angels...' which means 'that you affirm,' so if iman were not affirmation, Jibril's question would have caused confusion. (b) The hadith about best deeds lists iman, jihad, and hajj as distinct, showing that iman is not identical to actions. Finally, al-Nasafi mentions that he refutes opposing views to support the position of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a (that iman is tasdiq in the heart).

Key Terms
تصديق
تكذيب
عمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iman includes righteous deeds in its definition.
Correction: The Qur'anic verse Hud 23 uses 'and' to connect iman and righteous deeds, showing they are separate.

Checkpoint
Which Qur'anic verse does al-Nasafi use to show that iman is separate from action?
A) Hud 23
B) al-Baqara 178
C) Yunus 90
D) al-Baqara 183

Key Takeaway
Iman linguistically means affirmation, and the Qur'an itself distinguishes it from action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you have iman, but do you rely on your outward deeds to feel secure about your faith? Examine your heart: is your inner affirmation (tasdiq) as strong as your actions appear?
?
If iman is separate from action, then your deeds do not automatically prove your faith. Could you be performing many good deeds while your heart's affirmation of Allah's oneness is weak? What will you do today to strengthen that inner conviction?
?
The verse in Hud mentions both 'believed' and 'done righteous deeds' as distinct. Are you treating your deeds as a substitute for genuine heart affirmation? Spend a few minutes in sincere self-accounting: is your heart truly affirming what your tongue professes?

Qur'anic and Logical Proofs for Iman as Tasdiq
Nasafi presents three Qur'anic proofs and one logical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three additional Qur'anic proofs (addressing sinners, Pharaoh's testimony, faith before action) and a logical argument that iman cannot include action.

Core Idea
Faith remains even in sinners and precedes action.

Nasafi continues with more Qur'anic evidence and a logical argument. He shows that God calls sinners 'believers' in the law of retaliation, that at the moment of drowning Pharaoh affirmed (saddaqtu), and that God addresses believers before obligating fasting. Then he argues: if iman included action, then losing action would remove faith, but Muslims consider faith valid before any deed.

Guided Path
1
Read the three Qur'anic proofs
Read the verses from Baqara 178, Yunus 90, and Baqara 183, and understand how each supports that iman is tasdiq.
2
Understand the logical argument
Nasafi argues that if iman were composed of three parts (tasdiq, iqrar, 'amal), losing any would remove iman, but Muslims affirm iman by belief alone before actions.
3
Consider the implications
This shows that action is a consequence of iman, not part of its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخذ يقيم الأدلة على صحته. فالإيمان عند أهل اللغة التصديق، وضده التكذيب.
والله سبحانه يقول: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ ﴾ [هود: ٢٣] فالإيمان غير العمل. ثم إن الله تعالى خاطب من ارتكب المعاصي الموجبة للحدود باسم المؤمنين في مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتْلَى ﴾ [البقرة: ١٧٨] ويؤكد معنى التصديق أن الأعداء يلجأون إلى التصديق دون غيره عند معاينة العذاب نحو قول فرعون حينما أدركه الغرق: ﴿ آمَنتُ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا الَّذِي آمَنَتْ بِهِ بَنُو إِسْرَائِيلَ ﴾ [يونس: ٩٠] أي صدقت، والله سبحانه وتعالى خاطب باسم الإيمان، ثم أوجب بعد ذلك الأعمال في نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٨٣].
ثم إن الإيمان إذا كان عبارة عن التصديق والإقرار والعمل فمعنى ذلك أنه إذا زال ركن من الأركان الثلاثة زال الإيمان مع أن المسلمين أثبتوا حكمه بمجرد الاعتقاد قبل وجود غيره من الأعمال.
ثم إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حينما سأله جبريل عليه السلام عن الإيمان أجاب: "أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته.. إلخ" أي أن تصدق، ولو كان الإيمان غير التصديق لكان مجيء جبريل ليلبس على الناس لا ليعلمهم وكان النبي مقصرا في الجواب.
وقول النبي حينما سئل عن أفضل الأعمال: "إيمان لا شك فيه، وجهاد لا غلول فيه، وحج مبرور" ففي كلامه عليه السلام مغايرة بين الإيمان والجهاد والحج. ويناقش النسفي كلام من قال بغير ذلك، ليبطله، ويدعم رأي أهل السنة والجماعة.
And he began to adduce the proofs for its correctness. So faith according to the people of language is affirmation, and its opposite is denial.
And God, exalted, says: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds' [Hud: 23] — so faith is other than action. Then God, exalted, addressed those who committed sins necessitating legal punishments by the name of believers in such as His statement, exalted: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is legal retribution in cases of murder' [al-Baqara: 178]. And the meaning of affirmation is confirmed by the fact that the enemies resort to affirmation without anything else at the sight of punishment, like the statement of Pharaoh when drowning overtook him: 'I believe that there is no god except the One whom the Children of Israel believe in' [Yunus: 90] — meaning 'I affirmed.' And God, exalted, addressed [them] with the name of faith, then obligated actions after that in such as His statement, exalted: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is fasting' [al-Baqara: 183].
Then if faith were an expression of affirmation, confession, and action, that would mean that if one of the three pillars is removed, faith is removed, whereas the Muslims have confirmed its ruling by mere belief before the existence of other actions.
Then the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, when Gabriel, peace be upon him, asked him about faith, answered: 'That you believe in God, His angels, etc.' — that is, that you affirm. And if faith were other than affirmation, the coming of Gabriel would be to confuse people, not to teach them, and the Prophet would have been deficient in the answer.
And the saying of the Prophet when asked about the best deeds: 'Faith without doubt, jihad without cheating, and an accepted hajj' — in his speech, peace be upon him, there is a distinction between faith, jihad, and hajj. And al-Nasafi discusses the speech of those who say otherwise, to invalidate it, and to support the view of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This is a proof passage. Al-Nasafi begins with the linguistic definition: iman (faith) means tasdiq (affirmation) in the language, and its opposite is takdhib (denial). He then provides several Qur'anic proofs: (1) The verse 'Those who have believed and done righteous deeds' (Hud 23) separates faith from action by the conjunction 'and,' showing that they are distinct. (2) God addresses believers who commit sins subject to legal punishment as 'believers' (al-Baqara 178), indicating that faith remains despite sin. (3) Pharaoh, at the moment of drowning, said 'Amantu' meaning 'Saddaqtu' (I affirmed), confirming that iman refers to affirmation. (4) God addresses people as believers before obligating fasting (al-Baqara 183), showing that faith precedes and is distinct from action. Next, he gives a logical argument: if iman included action, then losing any component would remove faith; but Muslims consider a person a believer merely by conviction before performing deeds, proving that action is not part of the definition. Then he uses hadith: (a) The Prophet's response to Jibril about iman: 'that you believe in God, His angels...' which means 'that you affirm,' so if iman were not affirmation, Jibril's question would have caused confusion. (b) The hadith about best deeds lists iman, jihad, and hajj as distinct, showing that iman is not identical to actions. Finally, al-Nasafi mentions that he refutes opposing views to support the position of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a (that iman is tasdiq in the heart).

Key Terms
تصديق
عمل
اعتقاد
إقرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Iman is lost when a believer commits major sins.
Correction: God addresses sinners as believers in Baqara 178, so sin does not negate iman.

Checkpoint
Which verse shows that God addresses sinners as believers?
A) al-Baqara 178
B) al-Baqara 183
C) Yunus 90
D) Hud 23

Key Takeaway
Iman is affirmation in the heart, and even sinners and those before action are called believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe in your heart that you are a believer even when you sin, or do you let your failures shake your certainty? How does the Qur'anic address to sinners as 'believers' challenge your self-doubt?
?
When you hear 'O you who have believed' recited, do you assume it applies only to the perfect? How can you live today with the confidence that your faith is already accepted before you act?
?
Pharaoh's affirmation at drowning was rejected—but what in your life might be a last-minute, desperate 'faith' without heartfelt tasdiq? What is your relationship with God's mercy now, before any crisis?

Hadith Proofs for Iman as Tasdiq
Nasafi presents two hadith proofs and refutes opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two hadith proofs (Jibril hadith and best deeds hadith) that demonstrate iman is tasdiq and distinct from action.

Core Idea
The Prophet defined iman as affirmation and listed it separately from deeds.

Nasafi now turns to the Prophetic tradition. When Gabriel asked about iman, the Prophet answered 'that you believe in God...' meaning 'that you affirm.' If iman were not tasdiq, Gabriel's question would cause confusion. Also, when asked about best deeds, the Prophet listed iman separately from jihad and hajj, showing they are distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the Jibril hadith
Notice the Prophet's answer: 'an tu'mina billah...' which Nasafi interprets as 'an tusaddiq' (that you affirm).
2
Understand the logical implication
If iman were not affirmation, Gabriel would have confused people and the Prophet would be deficient.
3
Read the best deeds hadith
The Prophet lists iman, jihad, and hajj separately, showing iman is not an action.
4
See the refutation
Nasafi refutes opposing views, supporting Ahl al-Sunna's position that iman is tasdiq.

?

?

Are you relying on your actions to feel secure about your faith, rather than on the inner conviction that must be there first? ?

Defining Knowledge: Al-Ka'bi's Definition and the Mu'tazila Objection
Nasafi presents al-Ka'bi's definition, an objection, and a classification of acquired knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ka'bi's definition of knowledge as 'belief of a thing as it really is' was rejected by other Mu'tazila, and how acquired knowledge is classified into necessary and inferential types.

Core Idea
True belief is not always knowledge without proper epistemic grounds.

This passage opens a crucial debate in Islamic epistemology: what qualifies as knowledge? Al-Ka'bi offers a minimal definition, but his peers object that it is too broad, including mere true belief. Nasafi then introduces a classification that distinguishes genuine knowledge by its origin—either self-evident or inferential. As you read, notice how the objection targets the epistemic quality of belief, not its truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Ka'bi's definition
Read the first sentence. Al-Ka'bi defines knowledge as 'belief of a thing as it really is'. Note the Arabic phrase.
This is a correspondence theory of truth applied to belief.
2
Understand the objection
The other Mu'tazila argue that a commoner's true belief in basic doctrines (e.g., world's temporality) fits the definition but is not knowledge. Why? Because it lacks necessary or inferential grounding.
The commoner's belief is true but not epistemically justified in the required way.
3
Classify acquired knowledge
Nasafi states that acquired knowledge (al-ilm al-muhdath) must be either necessary (daruri) or inferential (istidlali). Necessary includes sense perception and self-evident truths (badahih). Inferential includes conclusions from reasoning.
Examples: senses (five), intuition (body cannot be in two places), reasoning (world's temporality).
4
Note the incomplete ending
The Arabic cuts off at 'wa la'. What do you think Nasafi was about to conclude? Likely that the commoner's belief does not fall into either category.
The missing part probably completes the argument that the commoner's belief is not knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول الكلام في تحديد العلم اختلف أهل الكلام في تحديد العلم، فزعم أبو القاسم البلخي المعروف بالكعبي أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به. ولم يرض به غيره من المعتزلة وزعموا أنه باطل باعتقاد العامي الذي اعتقد حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع، ووحدانيته، وصحة الرسالة، فإن هذا الاعتقاد اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به، لأن ما اعتقده على ما اعتقد ومع ذلك ليس هذا الاعتقاد بعلم. لأن العلم المحدث، لابد من أن يكون ضروريا، كالعلم الثابت بالحواس الخمس، التي هي حاسة السمع والبصر والشم والذوق، واللمس، والثابت بالبديهة كالعلم باستحالة وجود جسم واحد في واحد في حالة واحدة في مكانين، وكون الشيء أعظم من جزئه أو استدلاليا كالعلم بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع. ولا
Chapter One: Discourse on Defining Knowledge. The theologians differed on the definition of knowledge. Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, known as al-Ka'bi, claimed that it is a belief of a thing as it really is. Others among the Mu'tazila were not satisfied with this and claimed that it is invalidated by the belief of the common man who believes in the temporality of the world, the existence of the Creator, His oneness, and the veracity of the message. For this belief is a belief of a thing as it really is, because what he believes is as he believes, yet this belief is not knowledge. For acquired knowledge must necessarily be either necessary, such as knowledge established by the five senses—hearing, sight, smell, taste, and touch—or established by intuition, such as the knowledge of the impossibility of a single body existing in two places at one time, and that a part is less than the whole; or inferential, such as knowledge of the temporality of the world and the existence of the Creator. And not...
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory argument in the science of kalam. It opens with the chapter heading on defining knowledge. Nasafi reports that al-Ka'bi (a Mu'tazili) defined knowledge as 'belief of a thing as it really is' (i'tiqad al-shay' 'ala ma huwa bihi). The other Mu'tazila rejected this definition, arguing that it would wrongly classify a common person's (al-'ammi) belief in basic Islamic doctrines as knowledge, because that belief matches reality but is not genuine knowledge. The objection is that the common person's belief is merely blind acceptance, not a reasoned conviction. Nasafi then begins to argue that acquired knowledge (al-ilm al-muhdath) must be either necessary (daruri) or inferential (istidlali). Necessary knowledge includes sense perception and self-evident truths (badahih). He gives examples: the impossibility of one body being in two places at once, and that a part is less than the whole. Inferential knowledge includes knowledge of the world's temporality and the existence of the Creator. The passage ends abruptly with 'and not' (wa la), likely leading to a conclusion that the commoner's belief lacks the required epistemic quality. The classification is meant to show that true knowledge has specific origins, not merely correspondence with reality.

Key Terms

اعتقد
اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به
العلم المحدث
ضروري
بديهة
استدلالي
حواس خمس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection to al-Ka'bi's definition is that the commoner's belief is false.
Correction: No, the objection is that the commoner's belief is true (matches reality) but fails to meet the epistemic criteria for knowledge—it is neither necessary nor inferential.

Checkpoint
Why do other Mu'tazila reject al-Ka'bi's definition of knowledge?
A) Because it includes beliefs that are not true.
B) Because it would classify a commoner's true belief as knowledge, though it lacks necessary or inferential grounds.
C) Because it requires knowledge to be intuitive.
D) Because al-Ka'bi is not a reliable authority.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's definition was rejected because true belief without rational foundation is not considered knowledge in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a core Islamic belief you hold. Did you arrive at it through rational investigation (inference) or through acceptance from others (taqlid)? If the latter, does that mean your belief, even if true, might not qualify as 'knowledge' in the sense discussed here? What will you do about that gap?
?
The common man in this lesson believed the truth, but his belief was not considered knowledge because it lacked foundation. Is there any area of your faith where you are satisfied with merely 'believing the truth' without striving for certainty? How long will you let that stand?
?
If you were to die today, would you be able to say that your most important beliefs are grounded in necessary or inferential knowledge, or would they be mere assumptions? What one step can you take this week to turn one assumption into genuine certainty?

beginner

The dispute over al-Ka'bi's definition of knowledge
Al-Ka'bi's definition of knowledge and the Mu'tazilite objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ka'bi defined knowledge and why other Mu'tazila rejected his definition using the example of the common person's belief.

Core Idea
True belief is not enough; knowledge requires a valid epistemic source.

Theologians debated the precise definition of knowledge (al-ilm). Al-Ka'bi proposed a simple definition: affirmation of a thing as it is. But his fellow Mu'tazila objected, pointing out that a common person's correct beliefs about God and the world would then count as knowledge, even though they are based on imitation. This lesson examines the definition and the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: definition, objection, example.
Pay attention to the key terms 'العلم' and 'الاعتقاد'.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's definition
Find the sentence where al-Ka'bi defines knowledge as 'اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به'. Understand what this entails.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
Identify why they reject the definition by using the example of the common person ('العامي'). What is the common person's belief? Why is it not considered knowledge?
The common person holds correct beliefs about theological truths but these are not based on personal reasoning or direct experience.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What does this objection tell us about the criteria for knowledge? Why is true belief alone insufficient?
This connects to the learning objective about the necessity of a valid source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول الكلام في تحديد العلم اختلف أهل الكلام في تحديد العلم، فزعم أبو القاسم البلخي المعروف بالكعبي أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به. ولم يرض به غيره من المعتزلة وزعموا أنه باطل باعتقاد العامي الذي اعتقد حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع، ووحدانيته، وصحة الرسالة، فإن هذا الاعتقاد اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به، لأن ما اعتقده على ما اعتقد ومع ذلك ليس هذا الاعتقاد بعلم. لأن العلم المحدث، لابد من أن يكون ضروريا، كالعلم الثابت بالحواس الخمس، التي هي حاسة السمع والبصر والشم والذوق، واللمس، والثابت بالبديهة كالعلم باستحالة وجود جسم واحد في حالة واحدة في مكانين، وكون الشيء أعظم من جزئه أو استدلاليا كالعلم بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع. ولا هذا العامي، والعلم بهذه الأمور ليس بضروري
Chapter One: Speech on defining knowledge. The theologians differed on defining knowledge. Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, known as al-Ka'bi, claimed that it is the affirmation (i'tiqad) of a thing as it is. Others among the Mu'tazila were not satisfied with this and claimed that it is invalid according to the affirmation of the common person who has affirmed the temporality of the world, the existence of the Maker, His oneness, and the truthfulness of the message. For this affirmation is the affirmation of a thing as it is, because what he has affirmed is as he has affirmed, yet this affirmation is not knowledge. Because originated knowledge must necessarily be either necessary (daruri) — like knowledge established by the five senses: hearing, sight, smell, taste, and touch, and what is established by self-evidence (badiha) like the knowledge of the impossibility of a single body existing in two places at one time, and that a part is smaller than the whole — or inferential (istidlali) like knowledge of the temporality of the world and the existence of the Maker. But this common person does not have that, and knowledge of these matters is not necessary (daruri) for him.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a debate over the definition of 'ilm (knowledge). The author presents a dispute among theologians. Al-Ka'bi defines knowledge as a true belief (i'tiqad al-shay' 'ala ma huwa bihi). Other Mu'tazila reject this, arguing that a common person's belief in basic theological truths (e.g., the world's temporality, God's existence and oneness, prophethood) is correct and matches reality, yet it is not considered knowledge because it is acquired through imitation (taqlid) rather than through a valid proof. They then classify 'originated knowledge' (al-'ilm al-muhdath) into two main categories: necessary (daruri) and inferential (istidlali). Necessary knowledge includes what is obtained through the five senses and self-evident truths (badihiyyat) such as the impossibility of a body being in two places at once. Inferential knowledge is that which is reached through reasoning, like knowledge of the world's temporality or the existence of the Creator. The common person's belief does not fall into either category for him, because it is not based on direct sense perception or self-evidence, nor is it the result of his own reasoning. Therefore, according to the objectors, al-Ka'bi's definition is too broad, as it would incorrectly label the common person's belief as knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم
الاعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think knowledge is simply true belief irrespective of how it is acquired.
Correction: This passage shows that the Mu'tazila rejected al-Ka'bi's definition because true belief alone does not constitute knowledge; it must be necessary or inferential.

Checkpoint
Why did the Mu'tazila reject al-Ka'bi's definition of knowledge?
A) Because it excludes sense perception.
B) Because it would include the common person's correct but ungrounded belief.
C) Because it defines knowledge as inferential only.
D) Because it contradicts divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's definition of knowledge as true belief was rejected because it fails to exclude beliefs that are correct but not properly justified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold your beliefs about God and the afterlife because you have personally examined the evidence, or merely because you were raised to believe them? What would it take for you to move from inherited belief to certain knowledge?
?
The common person in the passage held correct beliefs, yet they were not considered knowledge. If you were tested on your faith, could you provide a rational justification for why you believe in the temporality of the world and the oneness of God?
?
This lesson challenges you to seek knowledge, not just true belief. What specific step will you take this week to strengthen your faith through study and reflection, rather than relying on what you already accept as true?
