The classification of originated knowledge: necessary and inferential
Classification of originated knowledge into necessary and inferential

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main categories of originated knowledge in Islamic theology: necessary (daruri) and inferential (istidlali), including their subcategories.

Core Idea
All human knowledge is either self-evident or derived through reasoning.

After rejecting al-Ka'bi's definition, the Mu'tazila clarify what true originated knowledge must be: it must be either necessary (daruri) or inferential (istidlali). Necessary knowledge includes sense perception and self-evident truths, while inferential knowledge is reached through reasoning. This lesson explores these categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation focusing on the classification. Notice the structure: condition, categories, examples.
Pay attention to the key terms 'الضروري', 'البديهة', 'الاستدلالي'.
2
Identify the two main categories
Find the two categories: necessary (daruri) and inferential (istidlali). Understand what each means.
Necessary knowledge is unavoidable; inferential knowledge is reached through reasoning.
3
Explore subcategories of necessary knowledge
Note that necessary knowledge includes sense perception (five senses) and self-evident truths (badiha). Identify examples of each.
Self-evident truths: impossible for a body to be in two places at once, whole greater than part.
4
Understand inferential knowledge
Inferential knowledge is based on proofs. Examples include knowledge of the world's temporality and God's existence.
5
Apply the classification
Consider the common person's belief from Lesson 1. Why does it not fit any category? This reinforces why it is not considered knowledge.
The common person's belief is neither sensory/self-evident nor based on personal inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول الكلام في تحديد العلم اختلف أهل الكلام في تحديد العلم، فزعم أبو القاسم البلخي المعروف بالكعبي أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به. ولم يرض به غيره من المعتزلة وزعموا أنه باطل باعتقاد العامي الذي اعتقد حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع، ووحدانيته، وصحة الرسالة، فإن هذا الاعتقاد اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به، لأن ما اعتقده على ما اعتقد ومع ذلك ليس هذا الاعتقاد بعلم. لأن العلم المحدث، لابد من أن يكون ضروريا، كالعلم الثابت بالحواس الخمس، التي هي حاسة السمع والبصر والشم والذوق، واللمس، والثابت بالبديهة كالعلم باستحالة وجود جسم واحد في حالة واحدة في مكانين، وكون الشيء أعظم من جزئه أو استدلاليا كالعلم بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع. ولا هذا العامي، والعلم بهذه الأمور ليس بضروري
Chapter One: Speech on defining knowledge. The theologians differed on defining knowledge. Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, known as al-Ka'bi, claimed that it is the affirmation (i'tiqad) of a thing as it is. Others among the Mu'tazila were not satisfied with this and claimed that it is invalid according to the affirmation of the common person who has affirmed the temporality of the world, the existence of the Maker, His oneness, and the truthfulness of the message. For this affirmation is the affirmation of a thing as it is, because what he has affirmed is as he has affirmed, yet this affirmation is not knowledge. Because originated knowledge must necessarily be either necessary (daruri) — like knowledge established by the five senses: hearing, sight, smell, taste, and touch, and what is established by self-evidence (badiha) like the knowledge of the impossibility of a single body existing in two places at one time, and that a part is smaller than the whole — or inferential (istidlali) like knowledge of the temporality of the world and the existence of the Maker. But this common person does not have that, and knowledge of these matters is not necessary (daruri) for him.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a debate over the definition of 'ilm (knowledge). The author presents a dispute among theologians. Al-Ka'bi defines knowledge as a true belief (i'tiqad al-shay' 'ala ma huwa bihi). Other Mu'tazila reject this, arguing that a common person's belief in basic theological truths (e.g., the world's temporality, God's existence and oneness, prophethood) is correct and matches reality, yet it is not considered knowledge because it is acquired through imitation (taqlid) rather than through a valid proof. They then classify 'originated knowledge' (al-'ilm al-muhdath) into two main categories: necessary (daruri) and inferential (istidlali). Necessary knowledge includes what is obtained through the five senses and self-evident truths (badihiyyat) such as the impossibility of a body being in two places at once. Inferential knowledge is that which is reached through reasoning, like knowledge of the world's temporality or the existence of the Creator. The common person's belief does not fall into either category for him, because it is not based on direct sense perception or self-evidence, nor is it the result of his own reasoning. Therefore, according to the objectors, al-Ka'bi's definition is too broad, as it would incorrectly label the common person's belief as knowledge.

Key Terms
الضروري
البديهة
الاستدلالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think inferential knowledge is inferior or not real knowledge.
Correction: The passage treats inferential knowledge as a valid category of originated knowledge.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of necessary knowledge according to the passage?
A) Knowledge that God exists.
B) Knowledge that the world is temporal.
C) Knowledge that a body cannot be in two places at once.
D) Knowledge of prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Originated knowledge is either necessary (self-evident or sensory) or inferential (reasoned), and the common person's imitation-based belief does not qualify as either.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say you believe in God, is your belief based on your own reasoning (istidlali) or on imitation? If it is imitation, then according to this passage, does your belief count as knowledge? What does that call you to do?
?
Think of a belief you consider 'necessary' because it seems obvious. Is it actually self-evident (badihi) or is it something you've accepted without reflection? How can you test which category it falls into?
?
If inferential knowledge is derived from reasoning, and necessary knowledge is immediate, which type of knowledge do you rely on more in your daily life? Does that affect the strength of your convictions?

Refutations of Abu Hashim and Abu Ali on Knowledge
Refutes Abu Hashim's and Abu Ali's modified definitions of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why adding 'tranquility of the soul' to the definition of knowledge fails to exclude common belief, and how Abu Ali's alternative condition of necessity or proof might avoid that problem.

Core Idea
A condition to exclude common belief must itself be absent in the common person's belief.

This passage examines two Mu'tazilite attempts to refine the definition of knowledge in response to a devastating objection. Abu Hashim adds a psychological condition, but the common person meets it. Abu Ali shifts the requirement to the source of belief. Let's see if either succeeds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection they face
The objection is that the earlier definition of knowledge ('affirmation of a thing as it is') would include the common person's belief, which they consider not genuine knowledge. These modifications aim to exclude that.
2
Examine Abu Hashim's addition and its refutation
Read Abu Hashim's definition with the condition 'tranquility of the soul.' Then see how the author refutes it by showing that the common person has tranquility, even to the point of dying for his beliefs.
Notice the author's strong example of the common person's willingness to shed blood.
3
Consider Abu Ali's alternative
Abu Ali defines knowledge as affirmation based on necessity or proof. This condition seems to exclude the common person's belief because it is not based on his own reasoning. Reflect on whether this definition escapes the objection.
The passage ends without explicit refutation; think about why the author might accept or critique it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرام أبو هاشم التخلص عن هذا الإلزام فزاد عليه شريطة فقال: العلم اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به، مع سكون النفس إليه، ولا تخلص له بهذا عن الإلزام، لأن العامي ساكن النفس على هذا الاعتقاد، مطمئن القلب عليه لا اضطراب له فيه حتى إن إنسانا لو رام إزالته عن هذا الاعتقاد، لقصد العامي إراقة دمه وإتلاف مهجته. وزعم أبوه على الجبائي أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به عن ضرورة أو دليل.
Abu Hashim sought to escape this objection, so he added a condition and said: 'Knowledge is the affirmation of a thing as it is, accompanied by tranquility of the soul (sukun al-nafs) toward it.' But he does not escape the objection by this, because the common person's soul is tranquil toward this affirmation, his heart is at ease with it, and he has no disturbance in it — so much so that if someone tried to remove him from this belief, the common person would intend to shed his blood and destroy his life. And his father Abu Ali al-Jubba'i claimed that it is 'the affirmation of a thing as it is, based on necessity (darura) or proof (dalil).'
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage contains two attempts by Mu'tazilite theologians to refine the definition of knowledge in response to the earlier objection. Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i adds the condition of 'tranquility of the soul' (sukun al-nafs), meaning psychological certainty or contentment. However, the author refutes this by pointing out that the common person (al-'ammi) is fully convinced and even willing to die for his beliefs; thus, his belief satisfies the added condition. Therefore, Abu Hashim's definition still includes the common person's belief as knowledge, which the objectors deny. Next, Abu Ali al-Jubba'i proposes that knowledge is affirmation based on a source: either necessity (darura) — such as sensory or self-evident knowledge — or a proof (dalil). This would exclude the common person's belief because it is not based on such a source. The author does not explicitly refute this here, but the context implies that the common person's belief may not be based on his own reasoning or direct necessity; however, the passage ends without further comment, leaving the reader to understand that Abu Ali's definition might escape the objection. The author's point is to show the successive modifications and their adequacy.

Key Terms
سكون النفس
الضرورة
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adding the condition of certainty (tranquility of the soul) to the definition of knowledge excludes common beliefs that are not based on proof.
Correction: The common person often has strong certainty and tranquility of soul about their beliefs, so this condition does not exclude them.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does Abu Hashim's addition of 'tranquility of the soul' fail to exclude the common person's belief from being knowledge?
A) Because the common person lacks tranquility.
B) Because the common person is actually tranquil and would even die for his beliefs.
C) Because tranquility is not a valid condition for knowledge.
D) Because the common person's belief is based on proof.

Key Takeaway
Psychological certainty is insufficient to distinguish genuine knowledge from common belief; focusing on the source (necessity or proof) is a more effective criterion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the common person is so certain of his belief that he would fight and die for it. Yet, according to the theologians, this certainty is not knowledge. Now ask yourself: What is the basis of your own certainty? If your belief were challenged, would your defense be rooted in evidence or merely in emotional conviction? Are you willing to examine the foundations of what you hold most dear?
?
The passage shows that adding a condition like 'tranquility of the soul' does not automatically make a belief knowledge. Think of a belief you hold that brings you peace. Could it be that your peace comes from familiarity rather than truth? Will you commit to seeking proof (dalil) for your most comforting beliefs, even if it disturbs your tranquility?

Abu Hashim's Definition of Knowledge and Its Refutation
Abu Hashim's definition and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i's definition of knowledge and why adding calmness of soul does not resolve the problem of distinguishing necessary from acquired knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot be defined simply as belief with calmness of soul – even the common person's firm belief meets that description.

Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, a major Mu'tazili theologian, attempted to refine the definition of knowledge by adding the condition of calmness of soul. This passage presents his definition and then refutes it by showing that the common person's firm beliefs already satisfy that condition, making the definition inadequate for distinguishing necessary knowledge from acquired knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a clear sense of the definition and the refutation. Note the key terms: 'ilm, i'tiqād, sukūn al-nafs, and 'āmmī.
The passage is short but dense; take your time with each phrase.
2
Identify the components of the definition
Abu Hashim defines knowledge as belief (i'tiqād) that matches reality, plus calmness of soul (sukūn al-nafs). Understand why calmness is added as a condition.
The condition is meant to exclude doubt or hesitation.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation argues that the common person ('āmmī) also possesses calmness of soul regarding his beliefs, even to the point of violent defense. This shows that calmness does not distinguish knowledge from mere belief.
Consider: What is the broader problem about necessary vs. acquired knowledge that this definition was trying to solve?

Source Text

تحديد أبي هاشم الجبائي والرد عليه]

Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of knowledge ('ilm) proposed by Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, the Mu'tazili scholar, and then refutes it. The definition states that knowledge is a belief (i'tiqād) that corresponds to reality, accompanied by calmness of soul (sukūn al-nafs). The refutation argues that this definition does not rescue Abu Hashim from the original objection (that the common person's knowledge is not necessary but acquired through reflection). The common person ('āmmī) already possesses calmness of soul regarding his beliefs, and is so firm in them that he would violently defend them. Thus the added condition of calmness does not differentiate necessary knowledge from acquired knowledge, and the definition fails to solve the problem.

Abu Ali al-Jubba'i's Definition and Its Refutation
Abu Ali's definition as division, not true definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Ali al-Jubba'i's definition of knowledge is considered a division (taqsīm) rather than a proper definition (ḥadd) in classical Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A proper definition must include all instances; dividing knowledge by source fails.

Abu Ali al-Jubba'i defined knowledge as belief of a thing as it is, derived from either necessity or proof. But is this a true definition? Classical logicians required that definitions be both inclusive and exclusive. This lesson examines why his proposal falls short of these standards.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Try to identify the definition and the main critique.
Pay attention to the terms 'iṭṭirād' and 'inʿikās'.
2
Understand the conditions of a valid definition
The passage explains that a proper definition must be coextensive (iṭṭirād) and convertible (inʿikās), ensuring it includes all instances of the defined (jāmiʿ) and excludes everything else (māniʿ).
3
Apply the conditions to Abu Ali's definition
See how his definition fails: if 'from necessity' is part of the definition, then inferential knowledge would be excluded—yet it is still knowledge. Thus the definition is not inclusive.
This is the core refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحديد أبي علي الجبائي والرد عليه:
وزعم أبوه على الجبائي أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به عن ضرورة أو دليل.
وهذا أيضا فاسد في التحديد إذ هو تقسيم العلم المحدث دون تحديده، لأن من شرط صحة التحديد أن يوجد جميع صفات الحد في كل فرد من أفراد المحدود إذ من شرطه الاطراد والانعكاس؛ ليحصل بهما الجمع والمنع إذ الحد ما يجمع جميع محدوده ويمنع غيره عن مشاركته، ولن يحصل هذا إلا باشتمال الحد أفراد المحدود، وفيما قسم لا يوجد هذا المعنى فإن علما ما لا يكون على جميع عن الضرورة والاستدلال لا يكون ضروريا، فلو كان اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به عن ضرورة علما لأنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به عن ضرورة لخرج الاستدلالي عن كونه علما، لخروجه عن الحد، ولو بقي علما مع خروجه عن الحد لبطل الحد، لخروجه عن أن يكون جامعا. وكذا هذا الاعتبار في العلم
The definition of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and the refutation of it: He claimed that [knowledge] is belief of a thing as it is, from necessity or from proof. This too is corrupt as a definition, for it is a division of originated knowledge without defining it, because among the conditions of a valid definition is that all the attributes of the definition exist in every individual of the defined, since its conditions are coextensiveness and convertibility; so that by them inclusion and exclusion are achieved. For the definition is what includes all of its defined and prevents others from sharing in it. This cannot be achieved except by the definition encompassing the individuals of the defined. In what is a division, this meaning does not exist. For some knowledge is not entirely from necessity and inference is not necessary. So if belief of a thing as it is by necessity were knowledge, then inferential knowledge would be excluded from being knowledge, because it would be outside the definition. And if it remained knowledge while being outside the definition, the definition would be invalid, because it would fail to be inclusive. And likewise this consideration in knowledge.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents and then refutes the definition of knowledge given by Abu Ali al-Jubba'i. He defined knowledge as belief of a thing as it is, derived either from necessity (ḍarūra) or from proof (dalīl). The refutation argues that this is not a proper definition (ḥadd) but merely a division (taqsīm) of knowledge. A proper definition must meet two criteria: iṭṭirād (coextensiveness: the definition applies to every instance of the defined) and inʿikās (convertibility: the definition applies only to instances of the defined). These ensure the definition is inclusive (jāmiʿ) and exclusive (māniʿ). The author then applies this to Abu Ali's definition: if we take the phrase 'from necessity' as part of the definition, then knowledge that is from inference would not be covered by the definition, yet it is still knowledge. Therefore the definition fails to include all instances of knowledge, violating the requirement of being jāmiʿ. Hence the definition is invalid. The passage ends by noting that the same consideration applies to knowledge in general, though the argument is cut off at the page break.

اطراد
انعكاس
جمع
منع

علم استدلالي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Defining knowledge by its sources (necessity/proof) is a correct definition.
Correction: A proper definition must encompass all instances; a division is not a definition.

Checkpoint

A) It is too broad
B) It is a division rather than a definition

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must include all instances of the defined; otherwise it is merely a division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on the sources of your knowledge—revelation, reason, experience—but can you define what knowledge actually is? This lesson shows that dividing knowledge by its sources fails to define it. When you say you 'know' your Lord, are you merely listing proofs, or do you possess a unified certainty that encompasses both necessary and reasoned belief? What would it mean for you to truly define your faith in a way that includes all its aspects without fragmentation?
?

Al-Jubba'i's definition of knowledge was taken as a definition but was only a division. In your own understanding of tawḥīd, are you guilty of defining it by its types (like 'it is knowing that Allah is One in His essence, attributes, and actions') rather than grasping the single reality that binds them? The passage insists that a definition must be both inclusive and exclusive. Is there an area of your life where you have allowed a too-narrow or too-broad definition to guide your actions? For instance, have you defined 'knowledge of Allah' as only what comes from scholarly study, neglecting the intuitive knowledge of the heart? Why Defining Knowledge as 'Thing' Fails
Critiques three definitions tied to 'thing' and the dispute over non-existent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why definitions of knowledge that rely on the term 'thing' (shay') are invalid, and how the dispute over non-existent beings and the case of impossibilities expose their flaw.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge through 'thing' fails because the status of non-existents is disputed and impossibilities are known but not things.

This lesson examines a key objection to definitions of knowledge that include the word 'thing'. The author shows that such definitions are only as strong as the answer to a prior question: Is a non-existent a thing? Different sects disagree, and even if resolved, knowledge of impossible things (like God having a partner) would be left out. This passage reveals why careful definition in kalam is essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the sense.
Notice the two key issues: the non-existent and impossibilities.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two views on whether non-existent is a thing: Mu'tazila say yes, Hisham says it is not known. Find these in the text.
3
Examine the objection
The author objects with knowledge of impossibilities. Why does this show the definition fails?
Impossibilities are known but not considered things by consensus.
4
Connect to the three definitions
Understand that the author's argument aims to invalidate any definition that uses 'thing' because it cannot cover all cases of knowledge.
This is the overall point of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي يبطل الحدود الثلاثة أنها علقت بالشيء ولن يستقيم هذا الأمر إلا بأحد أمرين:

هل المعدوم شيء أم ليس بشيء؟:

إما أن يجعل المعدوم شيئا لكونه معلوما كما هو مذهب المعتزلة، وإما أن يكون المعدوم غير معلوم كما هو مذهب هشام بن عمرو وقيام الدلالة على بطلان المذهبين جميعا، يبطل هذا التحديد ونقيم الدلالة بعد هذا على بطلانهما - إن شاء الله تعالى ـ ونوقضوا بالعلم باستحالة المحالات فإن ما يستحيل وجوده من الشريك والصاحبة والأولاد، الله تعالى تعلم استحالته، وذلك ليس بشيء بالإجماع، لأن اسم الشيء عندهم يقع على معدوم هو جائز الوجود، لا على ما يستحيل وجوده
Then what invalidates the three definitions is that they are tied to 'thing' (al-shayʾ). And this matter cannot be sound except by one of two possibilities: Is the non-existent a thing or not a thing? Either they make the non-existent a thing because it is known, as is the doctrine of the Muʿtazila, or the non-existent is not known, as is the doctrine of Hishām ibn ʿAmr. And the existence of evidence for the invalidity of both doctrines invalidates this definition. And we will present the evidence for their invalidity after this, God willing. And they objected by (knowledge of the impossibility of impossibilities): for that whose existence is impossible, like a partner, wife, or children (for God), God knows its impossibility, and that is not a thing by consensus. Because the name 'thing' in their view applies to a non-existent that is possible of existence, not to that whose existence is impossible.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage addresses the invalidity of three definitions of knowledge that include the term 'thing' (shayʾ). The author states that such definitions depend on an unresolved issue: whether a non-existent (maʿdūm) is considered a thing. Two positions exist: the Muʿtazila say the non-existent is a thing because it is known; Hishām ibn ʿAmr says the non-existent is not known. The author asserts that both positions are invalid (their proof will come later). He then raises an objection: knowledge of impossibilities (like God having a partner) is known to be impossible, yet these impossibilities are not things by consensus, because 'thing' only applies to a non-existent that can possibly exist. Thus, any definition relying on 'thing' fails.

Key Terms
الشيء
المعدوم
المعتزلة
هشام بن عمرو
المحالات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'thing' applies to anything that can be thought of.
Correction: The passage clarifies that in kalam, 'thing' only applies to possible existents, not to impossibilities.

Checkpoint
What is the author's objection to defining knowledge in terms of 'thing'?
A) It leads to skepticism.
B) It depends on a disputed issue about whether non-existents are things, and fails for impossible things.
C) It was rejected by the Salaf.
D) It allows too many things to be known.

Key Takeaway
Definitions of knowledge that rely on the term 'thing' are invalid because they depend on the disputed status of non-existents and fail to account for knowledge of impossibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you define knowledge in a way that quietly excludes what you cannot name or describe? How does the lesson's critique challenge you to expand your understanding of what Allah knows, even impossibilities?
?
Have you ever dismissed a theological position because it seemed absurd, without exploring the reasoning behind it? How does the author's method of presenting both Mu'tazila and Hisham ibn 'Amr's views before refuting them call you to be more rigorous in your own thinking?
?
When you say 'Allah knows everything,' do you truly include impossibilities? How does realizing that Allah's knowledge covers even what cannot exist reshape your awe of Him?

The First Objection to Defining Knowledge as Belief
First argument against knowledge as belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first kalamic argument against equating knowledge with belief based on linguistic derivation.

Core Idea
Knowledge and belief are not the same; their derived forms differ.

This lesson examines the first of several arguments against defining knowledge as mere belief. The author points out a linguistic inconsistency: if knowledge were belief, the knower would have to be called a believer, but the two terms are not interchangeable. This linguistic observation forms the basis of a deeper theological distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting that it is incomplete due to page break. Focus on the argument structure.
The text cuts off at 'لما'.
2
Understand the core argument
Identify the conditional statement: if knowledge were belief, then the knower would be a believer. The author argues this is invalid because the names are derived differently.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this linguistic point matters in kalam. It shows that knowledge and belief are distinct categories, even though they are often conflated.
This argument is the first of several aspects the author will mention.

And similarly in all these definitions, they make knowledge a belief (iʿtiqād), and it is invalid from several aspects. One of them: if knowledge were belief, then the knower would be a believer, for it is a name derived from this meaning, just as when sitting (al-quʿūd) for...

Commentary
This passage begins a new critique: all the aforementioned definitions erroneously define knowledge as belief (iʿtiqād). The author states this is invalid from multiple aspects, and he gives the first: if knowledge were belief, then the knower (ʿālim) would be a believer (muʿtaqid), because the name is derived from the meaning. He draws an analogy with 'sitting' (quʿūd) and something that is cut off. This is an argument against the reduction of knowledge to belief.

Knowledge and belief are distinct concepts; equating them leads to linguistic and theological inconsistencies.

Knowledge is Not Belief: The Divine Argument
Sanusi refutes the definition of knowledge as belief using the impossibility of divine belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining knowledge as belief leads to the absurd conclusion that God would be a believer, proving that knowledge and belief are distinct.

Core Idea
If knowledge were belief, God would be a believer—an impossibility.

The author begins by refuting the Mu'tazilite equation of knowledge with belief. He argues that if knowledge were belief, then every knower would be a believer. Applying this to God leads to an impossible conclusion: God cannot be described as having belief, so He cannot be described as knowing—a clear reductio ad absurdum. This lesson unpacks the first two arguments from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first argument
Start by reading the Arabic passage that presents the initial objection and the first aspect of the refutation. Notice how the author uses an analogy with 'sitting' to illustrate the derivation of the noun from the meaning.
The author says: if knowledge were belief, then the knower would be a believer, just as one who sits (qa'id) is sitting (jalis).
2
Analyze the application to God
The author then states that since it is impossible for God to be described as having belief (i'tiqad), it would also be impossible for Him to be described as knowing—which is absurd because God is knower. This is the first reductio.
3
Consider the second argument
The second argument: God is proven to be knower by revelation and reason. If knowledge were belief, then God would be a believer, which is invalid. Therefore knowledge is not belief.
This reinforces the conclusion from a positive angle.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes: 'It is established that He is knowing and not a believer, so it indicates that knowledge is not belief.' This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونوقضوا بالعلم باستحالة المحالات فإن ما يستحيل وجوده من الشريك والصاحبة والأولاد، (الله تعالى) تعلم استحالته، وذلك ليس بشيء بالإجماع، لأن اسم الشيء عندهم يقع على معدوم هو جائز الوجود، لا على ما يستحيل وجوده، وكذا في هذه الحدود كلها جعل العلم اعتقادا وهو فاسد من وجوه، أحدها: أن العلم لو كان اعتقادا لكان العالم معتقدا، إذ هو اسم مقدر من المعنى هذا كما أن القعود لما كان جلوسا كان القاعد جالسا، فبعد هذا نقرر الكلام من وجهين: أحدهما: أن الله تعالى لما استحال أن يوصف بكونه معتقدا، استحال أن يوصف بكونه عالما، وهذا محال. والآخر: أنه تعالى لما كان عالما بدليل الشرع والعقل كان معتقدا، وهو أيضا فاسد وثبت أنه عالم وليس بمعتقد، فدل أن العلم ليس هو الاعتقاد. والآخر: أنهم إنما يجعلون العلم اعتقادا ليتمكنوا من دفع علم الله تعالى، لاستحالة اتصافه تعالى بالاعتقاد، وحيث ثبت بالدلائل الموجبة أن الله تعالى له علم، على ما نبين في مسألة الصفات، ويستحيل أن يكون ذلك العلم اعتقادا، كان ذلك دليلا على بطلان هذا التحديد. وبهذين الجوابين يبطل قول النظام: إن العلم حركة القلب لوجدان ما يجد وقول من يقول في تحديد العلم: إنه رؤية القلب المنظور إليه. والآخر أن الاعتقاد هو ربط القلب على شيء، فإن العقد والاعتقاد لفظان ينبئان عن معنى واحد يقال عقد واعتقد، والعقد هو تركيب بعض أجزاء جسم
And they objected by knowledge of the impossibility of impossibilities: for that whose existence is impossible—like a partner, consort, or children (God Almighty)—He knows its impossibility, and that is not a thing by consensus, because the name 'thing' with them applies to a non-existent that is possible of existence, not to what is impossible of existence. Likewise, in all these definitions, they made knowledge belief (iʿtiqād), and that is invalid in several respects. One of them: if knowledge were belief, then the knower would be a believer, since it is a noun derived from the meaning, just as when sitting (quʿūd) is sitting (julūs), the sitter (qāʿid) is sitting (jālis). After this, we state the matter from two aspects. One: since it is impossible for God Almighty to be described as having belief, it is impossible for Him to be described as knowing, and that is absurd. The other: since He Almighty is knowing by proof of revelation and reason, He would be a believer, and that is also invalid. And it is established that He is knowing and not a believer, so it indicates that knowledge is not belief. The other [aspect]: they only make knowledge belief to be able to deny God's knowledge, because it is impossible for Him to be described with belief. And since it is established by compelling evidences that God Almighty has knowledge—as we will explain in the matter of attributes—and it is impossible for that knowledge to be belief, that is evidence of the invalidity of this definition. And with these two answers, the statement of al-Nazzām is invalidated: that knowledge is a movement of the heart for finding what it finds, and the statement of whoever says in defining knowledge: it is the heart's vision of the perceived object. And the other [point] is that belief is tying the heart to something, for contract (ʿaqd) and belief (iʿtiqād) are two words that indicate one meaning; it is said 'he contracted' (ʿaqada) and 'he believed' (iʿtaqada), and contract is the combination of some parts of a body.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the definition of knowledge as belief (اعتقاد) and of al-Nazzām's definition of knowledge as a movement of the heart. The author begins by noting an objection: knowledge of impossible things (like partners of God) is knowledge of something that is not a 'thing' by consensus, because 'thing' applies only to possible non-existents. This undermines definitions that tie knowledge to 'thing'. Then he argues that defining knowledge as belief is invalid from several angles. First, if knowledge were belief, then any knower would be a believer (معتقد); but this would imply that God, who is a knower, would be a believer, which is impossible because belief entails a mental state unsuitable for God. Second, since God is proven knower by revelation and reason, if knowledge were belief, God would be a believer, which is false. Thus knowledge is not belief. Third, the motive for defining knowledge as belief is to deny God's knowledge, but God's knowledge is established, and it cannot be belief. The author then states that with these two answers (the two arguments about God), the definition of al-Nazzām (knowledge as heart's motion) and another definition (knowledge as the heart's vision of the perceived) are also invalidated. Finally, he adds that belief is essentially 'tying the heart' to something, which is a combination (of meanings), reinforcing the incongruity with divine knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم
الاعتقاد
العالم
المعتقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge and belief are synonyms in Islamic theology.
Correction: The author shows they are distinct because God has knowledge but not belief; belief implies a mental state unsuitable for God.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument the author gives to show that knowledge is not belief?
A) If knowledge were belief, then God would be a believer, which is impossible.
B) Knowledge is a movement of the heart, but belief is tying the heart.
C) The Mu'tazila and Hisham ibn Amr agree on this point.
D) Impossible things are not known.

Key Takeaway
Defining knowledge as belief leads to the impossible conclusion that God would be a believer; hence knowledge and belief are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever confused your personal beliefs (convictions) with certain knowledge, where in your life have you been presumptuous, assuming your opinion is absolute truth? How does this lesson's distinction between knowledge and belief force you to reevaluate that area?
?
The author argues that God cannot be described as having belief because it is a mental state. Does your daily worship treat God's knowledge as absolute certainty that demands action, or do you act as if Allah's knowledge were just a belief you hold without consequence? What is one decision today that would change if you truly internalized that Allah's knowledge is not mere belief?
?
You claim to know Allah's attributes like His knowledge, yet you often doubt His promises or hesitate to obey. Is your 'knowledge' of Allah actually just belief? What would it take for you to transform your theological convictions into the kind of certain knowledge that leaves no room for hesitation in your obedience?

Refuting al-Nazzam's Definition of Knowledge
The author invalidates al-Nazzam's and others' definitions of knowledge using the same divine arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nazzam's definition of knowledge as a movement of the heart and similar definitions are invalid, and how the author clarifies the nature of belief.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not a motion of the heart; it is a divine attribute free from physicality.

Having established that knowledge is not belief, the author turns to other flawed definitions. Al-Nazzam defined knowledge as a movement of the heart for finding what it finds, while others defined it as the heart's vision of the perceived. The author dismisses these with the same reasoning: they would imply God has a heart or mental processes, which is impossible. This lesson examines those refutations and concludes with the true nature of belief as tying.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Nazzam
Start with the sentence: 'With these two answers, the statement of al-Nazzam is invalidated: that knowledge is a movement of the heart for finding what it finds.' Notice how the author links this to the previous arguments about God.
The 'two answers' refer to the divine arguments from the previous lesson.
2
Understand the refutation of heart's motion
The author implies that if knowledge were a heart movement, then God would have a heart that moves, which is absurd. The previous arguments (God cannot be a believer) apply equally here.
3
Consider the heart's vision definition
The author also invalidates the definition of knowledge as 'the heart's vision of the perceived.' This too would attribute a physical organ and act to God, which is impossible.
4
Learn the definition of belief
The passage ends with: 'The other [point] is that belief is tying the heart to something...' This clarifies that belief is a human mental act, not applicable to God.
This reinforces why knowledge cannot be belief.

Refutation of Nazzam's Definition and Invalidity of Metaphorical Definitions
The author refutes Nazzam's definition and argues against metaphorical definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects al-Nazzam's definition of knowledge as a movement of the heart and why metaphorical language is invalid in definitions.

Core Idea
Metaphors obscure meaning; definitions must be literal and clear.

This lesson examines two important refutations in the study of definition. First, the author disproves al-Nazzam's claim that knowledge is a movement of the heart. Then, he argues that using metaphorical language in definitions undermines their purpose of clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the two main refutations.
2
Identify the two refutations
The author first refutes definitions of knowledge as a movement or seeing of the heart, then argues against using metaphor in definitions.
The example of i'tiqad is used to illustrate metaphorical language.
3
Understand why metaphorical definitions fail
Contemplate why metaphorical terms cause ambiguity and require evidence for interpretation, which contradicts the purpose of definition.
Definitions are meant to clarify, not obfuscate.
4
Connect to the purpose of definition
Reflect on how this principle applies to other theological terms and why clarity is paramount in kalam definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال قول النظام وغيره أيضا. وبهذين الجوابين يبطل قول النظام: إن العلم حركة القلب لوجدان ما يجد وقول من يقول في تحديد العلم: إنه رؤية القلب المنظور إليه. والآخر أن الاعتقاد هو ربط القلب على شيء، فإن العقد والاعتقاد لفظان ينبئان عن معنى واحد يقال عقد واعتقد، والعقد هو تركيب بعض أجزاء جسم على بعض، وضم جسم إلى جسم، وذلك مما لا يتحقق في القلب، لاستحالة ربط بعض أجزاء القلب على البعض، فهو إذا لفظ يستعمل مجازا في هذا، واستعمال الألفاظ المجازية في التحديد مضاد لما وضع له التحديد، فإنه وضع للإبانة والإعلام، ولا تخلو الألفاظ المجازية عن ضرب ليس لانصراف الأوهام عند البتة سماع الألفاظ إلى محالها التي وضعت هي بضرب دليل، واستعمال ما فيه الالتباس عند إرادة الإعلام والعدول عما لا التباس فيه من حقائق الألفاظ، مضاد للغرض الذي وضع له التحديد، والله الموفق.
Invalidating the statement of al-Nazzam and others as well. And by these two answers the statement of al-Nazzam is invalidated: that knowledge is a movement of the heart due to finding what it finds, and the statement of the one who says in defining knowledge: that it is the heart's seeing of the perceived object. And the other that belief (i'tiqad) is tying the heart to something, for 'aqd and i'tiqad are two words that indicate one meaning; one says 'aqada and i'taqada, and 'aqd is the composition of some parts of a body upon others, and joining a body to a body, and that is not actualized in the heart, due to the impossibility of tying some parts of the heart to others; so it is a word used metaphorically in this, and using metaphorical words in a definition contradicts what the definition is established for; for it is established for clarification and notification; and metaphorical words are not free from a kind [of ambiguity] that prevents the minds from immediately turning upon hearing the words to their original meanings without some evidence; and using that which contains ambiguity when intending notification, and turning away from that which has no ambiguity among the literal meanings, contradicts the purpose for which the definition is established. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Nazzam's definition of knowledge as a movement of the heart and another definition as the heart's seeing. The author then expands on the concept of belief (i'tiqad) as tying the heart, arguing that this is a metaphorical usage because literal tying cannot occur in the heart. Using metaphorical language in definitions is invalid because definitions are meant to clarify, and metaphors introduce ambiguity that requires additional evidence to interpret. Thus, using ambiguous terms when clarity is intended contradicts the purpose of definition.

Key Terms
علم
حركة القلب
رؤية القلب
اعتقاد
عقد
مجاز
تحديد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Metaphorical terms are acceptable in definitions as long as they are commonly understood.
Correction: Metaphors introduce ambiguity and require evidence, contradicting the clarity that definitions demand.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject using metaphorical terms in definitions?
A) Because metaphors are only used in poetry.
B) Because metaphors are ambiguous and require evidence for interpretation, contradicting the purpose of definition.
C) Because the heart cannot be tied.
D) Because al-Nazzam was an innovator.

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical language has no place in definitions because definitions must be unambiguous and self-evident.

?

?
If you were asked to define your faith without any metaphor, could you? What part of your creed do you avoid defining precisely because it would demand too much of you?

Refutation of al-Baqillani's Definition of Knowledge
The author refutes al-Baqillani's definition that knowledge is cognition of the known as it is.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Baqillani's definition of knowledge as cognition (ma'rifa) is rejected in Maturidi theology.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not synonymous with cognition because God is 'alim, not 'arif.

This lesson addresses an important debate in kalam: can knowledge be defined simply as cognition of the known? Al-Baqillani, a leading Ash'ari scholar, proposed such a definition. However, the Maturidi school rejects it based on a subtle linguistic and theological point. Read the passage to see how a single word difference can undermine a definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author states the definition and then immediately refutes it.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the Arabic words for knowledge ('ilm) and cognition (ma'rifa). Understand their usage in the Quran and Islamic theology.
This passage argues they are not interchangeable for God.
3
Focus on the logical argument
Trace the author's logic: if knowledge were ma'rifa, then a knower would be called 'arif. But Allah is called 'alim, not 'arif. So the definition fails.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why the divine names are important. In Islam, names for God must be derived from revelation. Since 'arif is not used for Allah, the definition is invalid.
This shows the carefulness of Maturidi theologians in preserving the language of revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأي الباقلاني والرد عليه. ذكر القاضي أبو بكر محمد بن الطيب الباقلاني من جملة الأشعرية أن العلم هو معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به وهو أيضا فاسد، لأن العلم لو كان معرفة، لكان العالم عارفا، والله تعالى يوصف بأنه عالم، ولا يوصف بأنه عارف.
The opinion of al-Baqillani and the refutation of it. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Baqillani, from the Ash'ari group, stated that knowledge is the cognition (ma'rifa) of the known as it is. And that is also invalid, because if knowledge were cognition, then the knower would be an 'arif (cognizer), and Allah is described as 'alim (knowing) and is not described as 'arif.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Baqillani's definition of knowledge as ma'rifa. The author argues that if knowledge were identical to ma'rifa, then anyone who knows would be an 'arif. But in the case of Allah, He is described as 'alim, not as 'arif in Islamic usage. Therefore, the definition is invalid because it would require a term for God that is not used.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'ilm and ma'rifa are synonyms even for God.
Correction: The passage shows that if they were synonyms, Allah would be called 'arif, but He is only called 'alim in revelation.

Checkpoint

C) Ma'rifa requires sense perception.
D) Al-Baqillani was not a reliable scholar.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani's definition is invalid because it would require applying the term 'arif to Allah, which is not used in revelation.

?
If the definition of knowledge as cognition is invalid for Allah, what does that mean for how you approach learning about His attributes? Are you willing to let revelation define your understanding, even if it contradicts human logic?

Refutation of Ibn Furak's and the Ash'ari Definitions of Knowledge
Refutes Ibn Furak's and the Ash'ari definitions of 'ilm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two prominent definitions of knowledge (ʿilm) are rejected in the Maturidi tradition: Ibn Furak's definition as perfection of action and the Ash'ari definition as perception.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge requires a universal and unambiguous term; both definitions fail these criteria.

In this passage, the author critiques two definitions of knowledge proposed by other theological schools. The first, by Ibn Furak, ties knowledge to the ability to perfect actions, but the author shows it excludes knowledge of God and impossibilities. The second, from some Ash'aris, defines knowledge as perception, but the term 'perception' is ambiguous. This lesson unpacks both critiques and their implications for a sound definition of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the passage and note the two definitions being refuted: Ibn Furak's and the Ash'ari's.
The passage clearly separates them with 'wa baʿḍ al-ashāʿirah qāla'.
2
Understand the first critique
Ibn Furak's definition fails because it is not universal (لا يطرد). Our knowledge of God, His attributes, and impossibilities does not produce action perfection.
The author uses examples of divine knowledge that do not result in action.
3
Understand the second critique
The Ash'ari definition uses the term 'dark' (درك) which is ambiguous, having multiple meanings. A definition must be precise.
List the four meanings given: encompassing, catching up, understanding, ripening.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why a definition of knowledge must be both universal and unambiguous in theological discourse.
This prepares for understanding the Maturidi definition that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر أبو بكر بن فورك أن العلم صفة يتأتى بها من القادر أحكام الفعل وإتقانه. وهذا التحديد لا يطرد، فإنا علمنا بالقديم تعالى وبصفاته وبالمحالات ولا يتأتى بهذا العلم إحكام الفعل وإتقانه، فلا تأثير لهذا النوع من العلم في إحكام الفعل وإتقانه. وبعض الأشعرية قال: إنه درك المعلوم على ما هو به، وهو فاسد أيضا لأن لفظة الدرك مشتركة، يقال: أدركه إذا أحاط به، وأدركه إذا لحق به، وأدركه إذا فهمه، وأدركت الثمار إذا نضجت. وكذا الله تعالى يُعلم ولا
And Abu Bakr ibn Furak mentioned that knowledge (ʿilm) is an attribute by which the capable person brings about the perfection (aḥkām) of action and its mastery (itqān). This definition is not universal (yaṭṭarid), for we know the Eternal (al-qadīm), exalted, and His attributes, and impossibilities, and through this knowledge the perfection of action and its mastery are not brought about. So this type of knowledge has no effect in the perfection of action and its mastery. And some Ash'aris said: It (knowledge) is the perception (dark) of the known as it is, and this is also invalid because the term 'dark' is ambiguous (mushtaraka); it is said: 'adrakahu' meaning he encompassed it, and 'adrakahu' meaning he caught up with it, and 'adrakahu' meaning he understood it, and 'adrakat al-thimār' meaning they ripened. And likewise Allah, exalted, is known (yuʿlamu) and not...
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage critiques two definitions of knowledge. First, Ibn Furak's definition: knowledge is an attribute by which the capable person (al-qādir) produces the perfection and mastery of actions. The author argues that this definition is not universal (lā yaṭṭarid), because our knowledge of God, His attributes, and impossibilities does not result in any action perfection or mastery. Hence, that type of knowledge has no effect on action proficiency, so the definition fails. Second, the Ash'ari definition: knowledge is the perception (dark) of the known as it is. The author rejects this because the word 'dark' is ambiguous (mushtaraka); it has multiple meanings (encompassing, catching up, understanding, ripening). Therefore it cannot serve as a precise definition. The passage ends with the beginning of a statement that God is known but not 'perceived' (yuʿlamu wa lā...), likely completing the refutation by distinguishing knowledge from perception.

صفة
إحكام
إتقان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is solely for action perfection, or knowledge is equivalent to perception.
Correction: Knowledge includes knowledge of God and impossibilities, which have no effect on action; and 'perception' is ambiguous and therefore unsuitable as a definition.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject Ibn Furak's definition of knowledge?
A) Because knowledge cannot be an attribute.
B) Because the definition does not apply to knowledge of God and impossibilities.
C) Because it only applies to human actions, not divine.
D) Because it contradicts the Ash'ari definition.

Key Takeaway
A sound definition of knowledge must be both universal and unambiguous; Ibn Furak's fails universality and the Ash'ari's fails precision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defined something in your faith (like knowledge or belief) in a way that unknowingly excluded some legitimate aspects? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the definitions you hold?
?
When you think of 'knowledge of Allah', do you tend to associate it with practical outcomes or actions? How might this lesson reshape your understanding of knowing Allah purely for His own sake, even if no action follows?
?
The Ash'ari definition used an ambiguous word 'perception'. Are there words or phrases you use when discussing Allah that might be unclear or carry unintended meanings? How can you commit to greater precision and clarity in your speech about Him starting today?

The Purpose of Definition and the Reality of Knowledge
The author argues that definition clarifies the essence of knowledge, not its general concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that definition is meant to clarify the distinctive reality of a thing, not to establish initial knowledge, and how the reality of knowledge is deduced by reflecting on its effects.

Core Idea
Definition refines vague knowledge into precise understanding of a thing's essence.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi addresses a critical question: What does a definition actually do? Is it meant to give us knowledge of something we were completely ignorant of, or does it serve a different purpose? He argues that everyone already knows knowledge in a general sense, but the definition uncovers its true nature. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and shows how he arrives at the definition of knowledge as the attribute that makes its bearer a knower.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
Begin by reading the passage and noticing that the author is asking what definition (تحديد) is supposed to achieve. He introduces an example to clarify.
He contrasts the common person's vague knowledge with the theologian's precise knowledge.
2
Follow the illustration
The author uses the example of knowing a human being. The common person knows Zayd is human but not the distinguishing essence, while the theologian knows both. This shows that definition targets the distinctive reality.
The author insists that the common person's knowledge is still real—just not detailed.
3
Apply the reasoning to knowledge itself
The author then applies the same logic to the concept of knowledge: we all know knowledge vaguely, but we need to uncover its reality. He reflects on the effects of knowledge to deduce that its reality is to make its bearer a knower, not to cause other attributes like motion or color.
This step concludes with the definition: knowledge is the attribute that necessitates its bearer being knowing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنطق، ويمثل بمن علم [؟] من حذاق المتكلمين أو الفلاسفة الآدمي بحقيقته التي بها يمتاز عن غيره كان علمه به علما حقيقيا، ولست أعني بهذا أن علم العامي بكون زيدا آدميا ليس بعلم حقيقة، بل هو علم حقيقة ينافي الجهل والظن والشك أضداد العلم إنما أعني به أنه مع هذا العلم، ليس بواقف على حقيقته التي بها يمتاز بمن ليس بآدمي، والمتكلمون يعلمون أنه آدمي، ويعلمون مع هذا تلك الحقيقة التي بها يمتاز عن غير الآدمي، وإذا ثبتت هذه المقدمة نقول: إن التحديد ما وضع لإثبات العلم المبهم المطلق، بل هو حاصل لمن لا علم له بالحد، بل وضع لإثبات العلم بتلك الحقيقة التي بها يمتاز عن غيره، ولهذا يحصل به جميع أجزاء المحدود ومنع غيره عن مشاركته فيه، وإذا كان كذلك كان العلم معلوما عند كل أحد على طريق الإبهام والإطلاق، وكذا العلم كان معلوما عندهم على طريق الإبهام والإطلاق فإن كل عامي يعرف أن زيدا عالم بصناعة كذلك، وأن له بها علما، وكذا كل أحد يعلم من نفسه أنه عالم بمعلومات كثيرة، وأن له بها علما، وإن كان لا يعلم حقيقة العلم التي بها يمتاز عن غيره، فوقعت الحاجة إلى بيان تلك وكذا في حق العالم فاختلفنا في بيان تلك الحقيقة، فقلنا نحن : حقيقة العلم يوجب كون من قام به عالما، أو هو الوصف الذي من قام به كان عالما، وهذا لأنا عرفنا العلم والعالم على الإطلاق، غير أنا جهلنا الحقيقة، التي بها يمتاز كل واحد منهما عن أغيارهما فتأملنا فعلمنا أن العالم ما كان عالما لكونه أسود وقيام السواد به لأنا نشاهد السواد في أجسام ليست بعالمة، وكذا في البياض والحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق، والطول والقصر، وكذا في الطعوم والروائح كلها، فظهر أنه ما كان عالما إلا لقيام العلم به، وكان هذا حقيقة العالم وكذا العلم تأملنا فيه فعلمنا أنه لا يوجب كون من قام به متحركا ولا ساكنا، ولا مجتمعا ولا مفترقا، ولا أسود ولا أبيض، فعلمنا أن حقيقته أنه يوجب كون من قام به عالما، إذ لا أثر له إلا هذا، يظهر بالتأمل في أحوال ما هو معلوم في نفسه الحقيقة التي بها يمتاز عن غيره، فإذا عرفنا حقيقة كل واحد منهما بالتأمل في أحواله وأوصافه، لا بصاحبه بعدما ثبت علمنا بكل واحد منهما على الإطلاق بخلاف ما أورد في المثال فإن زيدا وأباه كل واحدا منهما مجهول الذات، فلا يحصل معرفة كل واحد منهما بصاحبه، والله الموفق.
And logic, and it is exemplified by one who knows [؟] from among the skilled theologians or philosophers the human being by his reality by which he is distinguished from others – his knowledge of him is real knowledge. And I do not mean by this that the common person's knowledge that Zayd is a human is not real knowledge; rather it is real knowledge that contradicts ignorance, conjecture, and doubt – the opposites of knowledge. I only mean that along with this knowledge, he is not standing upon his reality by which he is distinguished from one who is not human. And the theologians know that he is human, and along with this they know that reality by which he is distinguished from the non-human. And when this premise is established, we say: The definition is not placed to establish absolute vague knowledge; rather it is present for one who has no knowledge of the definition, but it is placed to establish knowledge of that reality by which he is distinguished from others. For this reason, by it all parts of the defined thing are obtained and others are prevented from sharing in it. And when it is so, knowledge is known to everyone in a vague and absolute way. Likewise, knowledge was known to them in a vague and absolute way, for every common person knows that Zayd is knowledgeable in such a craft, and that he has knowledge of it. Similarly, everyone knows from himself that he is knowledgeable of many things, and that he has knowledge of them, although he does not know the reality of knowledge by which it is distinguished from others. So there arose a need to explain that, and likewise regarding the knower. So we differed in explaining that reality. We said: The reality of knowledge necessitates that the one in whom it subsists be knowing, or it is the attribute the one in whom it subsists is knowing. This is because we knew knowledge and the knower absolutely, but we were ignorant of the reality by which each of them is distinguished from their others. So we reflected and knew that the knower is not knowing because he is black and blackness subsists in him, for we observe blackness in bodies that are not knowing; likewise with whiteness, motion, rest, conjunction, separation, length, shortness, and likewise with tastes and all smells. So it became apparent that he is not knowing except due to knowledge subsisting in him, and this was the reality of the knower. And likewise knowledge: we reflected on it and knew that it does not necessitate that the one in whom it subsists be moving or still, conjoined or separated, black or white. So we knew that its reality is that it necessitates that the one in whom it subsists be knowing, for it has no effect except this. This appears by reflecting on the states of what is known – in itself the reality by which it is distinguished from others. So when we know the reality of each of them by reflecting on their states and attributes, not by the other, after it is established that we know each of them absolutely, unlike what was mentioned in the example, for Zayd and his father each of them is unknown in essence, so knowledge of each of them through the other is not obtained. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the nature of definition and the reality of knowledge. The author begins by illustrating that true knowledge of something requires knowing its distinctive reality, not just a vague recognition. He gives the example of a theologian or philosopher who knows a human being by his essence, whereas a common person knows Zayd is human but not the essence that distinguishes him. However, this common knowledge is still real knowledge, just not detailed. The definition is intended to bring knowledge of that distinguishing reality. The author then applies this to knowledge itself: everyone knows knowledge in a general sense, but the precise reality needs to be uncovered. He recounts how scholars differed, and the author's group (the Maturidis?) defined knowledge as that which makes the one in whom it subsists a knower. They arrived at this by observing that a knower is not knowing due to accidental attributes like color, motion, etc., but solely due to knowledge. Similarly, knowledge itself does not produce those accidents but only the attribute of being knowing. So by reflecting on the effects, they deduced the reality. The conclusion is that the reality of a thing is known by examining its own states, not by comparison with another, as in the case of Zayd and his father where each is unknown in essence. The passage ends with a prayer.

Key Terms
علم
تحديد
حقيقة
عالم
إبهام
إطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Definition is for creating knowledge of something completely unknown.
Correction: Definition actually clarifies the distinctive reality of a thing already known vaguely.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the purpose of a definition (تحديد)?
A) To create knowledge of something that was completely unknown.
B) To establish knowledge of the distinctive reality of a thing already vaguely known.
C) To prove the existence of a thing.
D) To list all attributes of a thing.

Key Takeaway
Definition does not give you first knowledge; it gives you precise understanding of what distinguishes a thing from others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that Allah exists – that is vague, common knowledge. But have you ever earnestly reflected on His names and attributes as the theologians do? If not, are you content with a superficial faith? What concrete step will you take today to seek the reality of your Lord?
?
In your daily life, you have vague knowledge of many things – your own soul, your purpose, the meaning of tawheed. This lesson insists that definition refines vague knowledge into precise understanding. What is one Islamic concept you only understand vaguely right now, and how will you move to a deeper, reflected knowledge of it?
?
The author shows that reflection (تأمل) on the attributes of a thing leads to its reality. When was the last time you truly sat and reflected on an attribute like the power or mercy of Allah? If your knowledge is still vague, what is holding you back from seeking the 'reality' that the theologians pursued?

A Concise Definition of Knowledge from Some Companions
A concise definition of knowledge from some companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a concise definition of knowledge used by some companions, which focuses on its effect rather than its essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge is the attribute that removes ignorance, doubt, conjecture, and forgetfulness.

In this brief passage, the author presents a definition of knowledge attributed to some companions of the school. Instead of describing what knowledge is in itself, they define it by what it does: it negates four specific states from a living being. This pragmatic definition is praised for being light and decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm, then the translation. Notice the concise structure.
The passage is a single sentence with two parts: definition and praise.
2
Identify the four negated things
The definition says knowledge negates ignorance, doubt, conjecture, and forgetfulness. List them and think about why these four are chosen.
These are all states that hinder certainty.
3
Understand the definition's advantage
The author says this definition is lighter in burden and more decisive in silencing opponents. Consider why a definition that focuses on effects might be easier to use in debate.
It avoids complex metaphysical discussions.
4
Reflect on the practical use
Ask yourself: how would this definition help a theologian or student of kalam? It provides a clear criterion for when knowledge is present.
It also guards against common misunderstandings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أصحابنا رحمهم الله من قال : إن العلم صفة ينتفي بها عن الحي الجهل والشك والظن والسهو، وهذا التحديد أخف مؤنة، وأقطع الشغب الخصوم.
And among our companions – may God have mercy on them – is one who said: Knowledge is an attribute by which ignorance, doubt, conjecture, and forgetfulness are negated from the living being. And this definition is lighter in burden and more decisive in silencing the disputes of opponents.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents a concise definition of knowledge attributed to some companions of the author's school (likely the Maturidi or Hanafi tradition). They define knowledge as an attribute (ṣifa) that, when present in a living being (al-ḥayy), negates four things: ignorance (jahl), doubt (shakk), conjecture (ẓann), and forgetfulness (sahw). The author notes that this definition has two advantages: it is lighter in burden (akhaff ma'ūna) meaning easier to grasp and apply, and it is more decisive in cutting off the disputes (ashga‘ al-khuṣūm) of opponents, presumably because it is straightforward and not open to lengthy debate. The definition focuses on the effect of knowledge rather than its essence.

Key Terms
علم
صفة
الجهل
الشك
الظن
السهو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the definition says knowledge itself is the negation of ignorance, doubt, etc.
Correction: The definition says knowledge is the attribute by which these things are negated—knowledge is the cause, not the negation itself.

Checkpoint
According to the definition from some companions, what does knowledge negate?
A) Ignorance and doubt only.
B) Ignorance, doubt, conjecture, and forgetfulness.
C) Ignorance, doubt, conjecture, and error.
D) Ignorance and forgetfulness only.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is the attribute that, when present, negates ignorance, doubt, conjecture, and forgetfulness from a living being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author calls this definition 'lighter in burden.' What burden of spiritual uncertainty are you carrying right now that true, actionable knowledge could lift? What is one step you can take in the next 24 hours to acquire that knowledge?

Two Definitions of Knowledge in the Maturidi Tradition
Presents two definitions of knowledge from Hanafi-Maturidi scholars with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key definitions of knowledge (ʿilm) presented by Hanafi-Maturidi scholars—one based on negation of ignorance and doubt, and one based on disclosure—and why they are considered sound in the tradition.

Core Idea
Knowledge is defined either by what it excludes or by what it reveals.

The Maturidi school offers distinct yet complementary definitions of knowledge. One definition focuses on what knowledge negates—ignorance, doubt, suspicion, forgetfulness—while another highlights knowledge’s positive function of disclosure. This lesson presents both definitions as recorded by Imam al-Nasafi and his commentary on their validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get a sense of the two definitions and the author's remarks.
Pay attention to the structure: first a definition by some companions, then by al-Maturidi.
2
Identify the two definitions
Locate the precise wording of each definition. The first defines knowledge by what it negates; the second defines it by what it discloses.
3
Understand the author's commentary
Read the author's evaluation: he says the first definition is 'lighter in burden' and 'more decisive', and the second is 'sound, universal, reversible'.
Consider what 'universal and reversible' (yaṭrad wa yan'akis) means for a definition.
4
Reflect on the criteria of a sound definition
Ask yourself: why are these definitions considered valid even though they don't state the essence directly? The commentary gives clues.
The author says the second definition is immune to invalidating objections.
5
Summarize in your own words
Write a short summary comparing the two definitions and explaining why both are accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف لبعض الأصحاب] ومن أصحابنا رحمهم الله من قال : إن العلم صفة ينتفي بها عن الحي الجهل والشك والظن والسهو، وهذا التحديد أخف مؤنة، وأقطع الشغب الخصوم. تعريف للماتريدي] والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي - بيض الله وجهه - يشير في أثناء كلامه، إلى أن العلم صفة يتجلى بها لمن قامت هي به المذكور ولم يأتِ بهذه العبارة على هذا النظم والترتيب، وهو حد صحيح يطرد وينعكس ولا يرد عليه شيء من الاعتراضات المفسدة، يعرف ذلك بالتأمل، والله الموفق.
Definition by some companions: Among our companions (may God have mercy on them) is one who said: Knowledge is an attribute by which ignorance, doubt, suspicion, and forgetfulness are negated from the living being. And this definition is lighter in burden and more decisive in cutting off the obstinacy of opponents. Definition by al-Maturidi: And the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (may God brighten his face) indicates in the course of his speech that knowledge is an attribute by which the mentioned thing is disclosed to the one in whom it subsists. And he did not express it in this wording and arrangement. And it is a sound definition, universal and reversible, and no invalidating objection occurs against it. That is known by reflection. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents two definitions of knowledge (ilm) from the Maturidi tradition. The first definition, attributed to some of the author's companions (asḥāb), defines knowledge as an attribute (ṣifa) that negates four things from the living being: ignorance (jahl), doubt (shakk), suspicion (ẓann), and forgetfulness (sahw). The author notes that this definition is 'lighter in burden' (akhaff mu'na) and more effective in cutting off the obstinacy of opponents. The second definition is attributed to Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (d. 333 AH), the eponym of the Maturidi school. Al-Maturidi indicates in his speech that knowledge is an attribute by which the 'mentioned thing' (al-madhkūr) is disclosed (yatajallā) to the one in whom that attribute subsists. The author notes that al-Maturidi did not phrase it exactly in this wording and order, but this is the meaning. The author then comments that this definition is sound (ṣaḥīḥ), universal (yaṭrad) i.e., applies to all cases, and reversible (yan'akis) i.e., only such cases apply, and it is immune to invalidating objections. Understanding this requires reflection (ta'ammul).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A definition of knowledge must state its essence directly, such as 'knowledge is certainty'.
Correction: In the Maturidi tradition, definitions can be based on negation of opposites or on functional disclosure, both of which are considered sound and valid.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Knowledge in the Maturidi tradition is defined either as an attribute that negates ignorance or as an attribute that discloses the known, and both definitions are considered sound.

The Definition of Knowledge and Its Soundness
The author defines 'ilm and asserts its definition is sound and objection-free.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'ilm presented by the author and why it is considered sound, coextensive, and reciprocally exclusive.

Core Idea
A sound definition of knowledge must include all instances and exclude all non-instances.

In this passage, the author presents a concise definition of knowledge ('ilm) and immediately validates it. He emphasizes that this definition is not arbitrary but meets the criteria of a proper logical definition: it is coextensive and reciprocally exclusive. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the key terms: العلم, الحد الصحيح, يطرد وينعكس.
2
Understand the definition's components
Break down the definition: knowledge is a quality (ṣifa) that discloses the known (al-madhkūr) to the one in whom it subsists (the knower). Ask yourself: What does 'quality' mean here? What does 'disclose' imply?
This definition emphasizes the relational aspect of knowledge.
3
Grasp the criteria of soundness
The author calls the definition 'sound' because it is coextensive (yaṭṭarid) and reciprocally exclusive (yanʿakis). Coextensive means it covers all instances of knowledge; reciprocally exclusive means it excludes everything that is not knowledge. Consider why this is important in defining terms.
A definition that fails either condition is considered defective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أثناء كلامه، إلى أن العلم صفة يتجلى بها لمن قامت هي به المذكور، ولم يأتِ بهذه العبارة على هذا النظم والترتيب، وهو حد صحيح يطرد وينعكس ولا يرد عليه شيء من الاعتراضات المفسدة، يعرف ذلك بالتأمل، والله الموفق.
During his speech, [he said] that knowledge is a quality by which the mentioned [object] is disclosed to the one in whom it subsists, and he did not produce this expression in this arrangement and order, and it is a sound definition that is coextensive and reciprocally exclusive, and no corrupting objection is brought against it; that is known by reflection, and God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a definition and validation. The author presents a definition of knowledge ('ilm) as a quality (ṣifa) by which the known (al-madhkūr) becomes manifest to the one who possesses it. Then he comments that this definition is given in this precise wording and arrangement, and it is a sound definition (ḥadd ṣaḥīḥ) that is coextensive (yaṭṭarid) and reciprocally exclusive (yanʿakis), meaning it includes all instances of knowledge and excludes all non-knowledge. Moreover, no corrupting objection can be brought against it. He concludes that this can be recognized by reflection, and God is the Grantor of success.

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge as a quality that discloses the known to the knower is sound because it includes all knowledge and excludes what is not knowledge.
