The True Meaning of Iman: Security Through Evidence
Nasafi defines iman as evidence-based tasdiq through a linguistic analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that iman, in its linguistic and theological sense, is not mere mental assent but a secure conviction based on rational evidence.

Core Idea
Iman means entering into security, and only evidence can provide that security.

Imam Nasafi begins by challenging a common assumption: that faith is simply affirmation. He argues that the Arabic root of iman implies entering into safety, which can only be achieved through rational evidence. This lesson unpacks his linguistic and logical case for why mere tasdiq without proof falls short of true faith.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise about knowledge
The passage opens with a conditional: if a belief is neither necessary nor inferential, it is not knowledge. This sets the criterion for valid belief.
This premise is implied, not explicitly stated in full.
2
Follow the linguistic argument
Nasafi explains that the root of iman ( '-m-n') means bringing oneself into security, which requires reflection on evidence.
Contrast with tasdiq, which is mere affirmation.
3
Recognize the conclusion
The passage concludes that plain affirmation without evidence is not iman. This directly refutes the idea that faith is blind belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضرورة فلم يكن ضروريا، ولا عن دليل فلم يكن استدلاليا، فلم يكن علما، ومن المحال أن يكون من لا علم له بحدوث العالم، وثبوت الصانع ووحدانيته وثبوت الرسالة مؤمنا، وهذا لأن التصديق وإن وجد إلا أن مطلق التصديق ليس بإيمان بل الإيمان هو التصديق المقيد بكونه مبنيًا على الدليل، وهذا لأن الإيمان في حقيقة اللغة ليس هو التصديق بل هو إدخال النفس في الأمان يقال: أمنته فأمن كما يقال: أجلسته فجلس، فكل من أخبر بخبر فتأمل في دليله فعرف بالدليل صدقه فصدقه يقال: آمنه أي صدقه بعدما أدخل نفسه بالتأمل في دلائل صدقه في الأمان، من غير أن يكون مكذوبا أو مخدوعا أو ملبسا عليه في هذا الخبر، فإذا كان الأمر على هذا تبين أن التصديق العاري عن الدليل ليس بإيمان، وإذا ثبت هذا فبعد ذلك يقول أبو الحسن الأشعري والمعتزلة: إن الدليل لابد من أن يكون عقليا، إذ لا وجه إلى جعل قول الرسول دليل حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع لأن قول الرسول لا يكون حجة ما لم يثبت رسالته، ولا وجه إلى القول برسالته إلا بعد معرفة مرسله ولن يتهيأ معرفة مرسله إلا بعد ثبوت المعرفة بحدوث العالم، وإذا كان كذلك لن يتصور حصول المعرفة بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع بقول الرسول لأن المعرفة بصحة قوله مترتبة على معرفة حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع يحققه أن العاقل ما لم يعرف أن صانع العالم [؟] حكيم لا يظهر المعجزة على يد المتنبي، كما يذهب إليه القائلون بأن السفه ما خلا عن العاقبة الحميدة. أو يعرف أن صانع العالم تام القدرة يستحيل عليه العجز، وظهور المعجزة على يد المتنبي، يعجزه عن إظهار ما يحق من الأديان، والتمييز بين الحق والباطل منها وهذا محال. كما يذهب إليه القائلون أن السفه ما نهي عنه وإذا كان الأمر هكذا لن يتصور حصول المعرفة بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع وكمال حكمته وقدرته بقول الرسول لكون المعرفة بكون قوله حجة مترتبة على هذه المعارف - وإذا خرج هذا من أن يصلح أن يكون دليلا يقترن بالتصديق - والمعارف ليست بضرورة – فلابد من القول باقتران دليل عقلي به.
If it were necessary (darura) then it would not be necessary, and if not from evidence (dalil) then it would not be inferential (istidlali), so it would not be knowledge (ilm). And it is impossible that someone who has no knowledge of the temporality of the world (huduth al-alam), the existence of the Maker (thubut al-sani), His oneness (wahdaniyyatihi), and the confirmation of prophethood (thubut al-risala) could be a believer (mu'min). This is because affirmation (tasdiq), even if it exists, mere absolute tasdiq is not faith (iman); rather, iman is tasdiq restricted by being based on evidence. This is because iman in the true linguistic sense is not tasdiq; rather, it is bringing oneself into security (idkhal al-nafs fi al-aman). It is said: 'amantu-hu fa amina' just as it is said 'ajlastu-hu fa jalasa.' So whoever reports a report and then reflects on its evidence and knows by the evidence its truthfulness and then affirms it, it is said: 'amana-hu,' i.e., he affirmed him after he brought himself through reflection on the evidences of his truthfulness into security, without being lied to or deceived or confused about that report. When the matter is this, it becomes clear that plain affirmation without evidence is not faith. Once this is established, then after this Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila say: indeed the evidence must necessarily be rational ('aqli), because there is no way to make the speech of the Prophet evidence for the temporality of the world and the existence of the Maker, since the speech of the Prophet is not a proof (hujja) until his prophethood (risala) is established. And there is no way to affirm his prophethood except after knowing his Sender (mursil), and it will not be possible to know his Sender except after the confirmation of knowledge of the temporality of the world. And if that is the case, obtaining knowledge of the temporality of the world and the existence of the Maker through the speech of the Prophet cannot be conceived, because knowledge of the validity of his speech depends on knowledge of the temporality of the world and the existence of the Maker. This is verified by the fact that the rational person, as long as he does not know that the Maker of the world is [؟] wise (hakim), does not consider the miracle (mu'jiza) to appear at the hand of the false prophet (mutanabbi), as goes the view of those who say that futility (safah) is whatever lacks a praiseworthy outcome; or he knows that the Maker of the world is perfect in power (tamm al-qudra) and incapability ('ajz) is impossible for Him, and the appearance of a miracle at the hand of a false prophet would incapacitate Him from manifesting what is correct among religions and distinguishing between truth and falsehood among them, and this is impossible—as goes the view of those who say that futility is what is forbidden. And when the matter is thus, obtaining knowledge of the temporality of the world, the existence of the Maker, and the perfection of His wisdom and power through the speech of the Prophet cannot be conceived, because the knowledge that his speech is a proof is dependent on these items of knowledge. And when this has been excluded from being suitable as evidence to accompany affirmation—and these items of knowledge are not necessary (darura)—then it is necessary to say that a rational evidence accompanies it.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational argument in Maturidi epistemology of faith. It begins with an implied premise: if a belief is not necessary (daruri) nor inferential (istidlali), it cannot be knowledge ('ilm). The author applies this to the claim that someone who lacks knowledge of the temporality of the world, the existence and oneness of the Creator, and the truth of prophethood cannot be a true believer. He then distinguishes between mere affirmation (tasdiq) and faith (iman) by arguing linguistically: iman, from the root '-m-n,' means 'to bring oneself into security.' One who reflects on the evidence and thereby affirms the truthfulness of a report is said to 'amanahu'—he entered into security. This security is absent if the affirmation is not based on evidence, because one could be deceived. Therefore, plain tasdiq without evidence is not iman. After establishing this, the author reports the consensus of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila that the required evidence must be rational, not based solely on the Prophet's words. The reason is circular: to accept the Prophet's statement as evidence for God's existence and the world's temporality, one must first know that the Prophet is truthful; but knowing that requires knowing that God exists and is wise and powerful, otherwise miracles could be performed by false prophets. Thus rational knowledge is prior. The author then supports this with an argument about the definition of 'futility' (safah) in relation to God: a false prophet's miracle cannot succeed because it would imply God is either foolish or powerless. Hence, rational evidence is necessary.

Key Terms
تصديق
إيمان
أمان
علم ضروري
علم استدلالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think iman is simply mental assent or blind belief without any rational basis.
Correction: This passage shows that iman requires evidence; mere tasdiq without evidence is not faith.

Checkpoint
According to the linguistic analysis in this passage, what is the root meaning of iman?
A) Intellectual assent
B) Emotional conviction
C) Entering into security
D) Verbal confession

Key Takeaway
Iman is not mere affirmation but a conviction grounded in rational evidence that brings the believer into a state of security.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm 'I believe in Allah,' have you genuinely brought yourself into security through personal reflection on the evidence, or are you resting on inherited certainty? What might it mean for your iman to be secure from deception?
?
The passage says one cannot be a believer without knowledge of God's existence, oneness, and prophethood. Is your belief in these matters built on evidence you have examined yourself? If not, what one step will you take this week to move from mere tasdiq to iman?
?
Do you feel a deep sense of security (aman) in your faith, or are there lingering doubts that unsettle you? If your iman lacks that security, what evidence have you neglected that could ground you firmly?

Why Faith Requires Rational Proof, Not Just Prophetic Reports
Nasafi argues for the necessity of rational evidence over prophetic reports for foundational beliefs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof for the fundamental tenets of faith must be rational rather than based solely on prophetic statements.

Core Idea
Prophetic testimony cannot be the primary evidence for God because it depends on prior rational knowledge.

Having established that iman requires evidence, Imam Nasafi now turns to the nature of that evidence. He reports the consensus of Ash'aris and Mu'tazila that it must be rational, and then provides a sophisticated argument showing that using the Prophet's words as the initial proof leads to circular reasoning. This lesson walks through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim that evidence must be rational
Nasafi states that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila agree that the required evidence is rational.
This is a consensus among major theological schools.
2
Follow the circularity argument
The Prophet's statement cannot prove God's existence because accepting the Prophet requires prior knowledge of God.
This is a logical critique of using scripture as foundational proof.
3
Understand the miracle argument
Nasafi explains that to distinguish a true prophet from a false one, one must already know God's wisdom and power, which are rational truths.
This reinforces the need for rational grounding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضرورة فلم يكن ضروريا، ولا عن دليل فلم يكن استدلاليا، فلم يكن علما، ومن المحال أن يكون من لا علم له بحدوث العالم، وثبوت الصانع ووحدانيته وثبوت الرسالة مؤمنا، وهذا لأن التصديق وإن وجد إلا أن مطلق التصديق ليس بإيمان بل الإيمان هو التصديق المقيد بكونه مبنيًا على الدليل، وهذا لأن الإيمان في حقيقة اللغة ليس هو التصديق بل هو إدخال النفس في الأمان يقال: أمنته فأمن كما يقال: أجلسته فجلس، فكل من أخبر بخبر فتأمل في دليله فعرف بالدليل صدقه فصدقه يقال: آمنه أي صدقه بعدما أدخل نفسه بالتأمل في دلائل صدقه في الأمان، من غير أن يكون مكذوبا أو مخدوعا أو ملبسا عليه في هذا الخبر، فإذا كان الأمر على هذا تبين أن التصديق العاري عن الدليل ليس بإيمان، وإذا ثبت هذا فبعد ذلك يقول أبو الحسن الأشعري والمعتزلة: إن الدليل لابد من أن يكون عقليا، إذ لا وجه إلى جعل قول الرسول دليل حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع لأن قول الرسول لا يكون حجة ما لم يثبت رسالته، ولا وجه إلى القول برسالته إلا بعد معرفة مرسله ولن يتهيأ معرفة مرسله إلا بعد ثبوت المعرفة بحدوث العالم، وإذا كان كذلك لن يتصور حصول المعرفة بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع بقول الرسول لأن المعرفة بصحة قوله مترتبة على معرفة حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع يحققه أن العاقل ما لم يعرف أن صانع العالم [؟] حكيم لا يظهر المعجزة على يد المتنبي، كما يذهب إليه القائلون بأن السفه ما خلا عن العاقبة الحميدة. أو يعرف أن صانع العالم تام القدرة يستحيل عليه العجز، وظهور المعجزة على يد المتنبي، يعجزه عن إظهار ما يحق من الأديان، والتمييز بين الحق والباطل منها وهذا محال. كما يذهب إليه القائلون أن السفه ما نهي عنه وإذا كان الأمر هكذا لن يتصور حصول المعرفة بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع وكمال حكمته وقدرته بقول الرسول لكون المعرفة بكون قوله حجة مترتبة على هذه المعارف - وإذا خرج هذا من أن يصلح أن يكون دليلا يقترن بالتصديق - والمعارف ليست بضرورة – فلابد من القول باقتران دليل عقلي به.
If it were necessary (darura) then it would not be necessary, and if not from evidence (dalil) then it would not be inferential (istidlali), so it would not be knowledge (ilm). And it is impossible that someone who has no knowledge of the temporality of the world (huduth al-alam), the existence of the Maker (thubut al-sani), His oneness (wahdaniyyatihi), and the confirmation of prophethood (thubut al-risala) could be a believer (mu'min). This is because affirmation (tasdiq), even if it exists, mere absolute tasdiq is not faith (iman); rather, iman is tasdiq restricted by being based on evidence. This is because iman in the true linguistic sense is not tasdiq; rather, it is bringing oneself into security (idkhal al-nafs fi al-aman). It is said: 'amantu-hu fa amina' just as it is said 'ajlastu-hu fa jalasa.' So whoever reports a report and then reflects on its evidence and knows by the evidence its truthfulness and then affirms it, it is said: 'amana-hu,' i.e., he affirmed him after he brought himself through reflection on the evidences of his truthfulness into security, without being lied to or deceived or confused about that report. When the matter is this, it becomes clear that plain affirmation without evidence is not faith. Once this is established, then after this Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila say: indeed the evidence must necessarily be rational ('aqli), because there is no way to make the speech of the Prophet evidence for the temporality of the world and the existence of the Maker, since the speech of the Prophet is not a proof (hujja) until his prophethood (risala) is established. And there is no way to affirm his prophethood except after knowing his Sender (mursil), and it will not be possible to know his Sender except after the confirmation of knowledge of the temporality of the world. And if that is the case, obtaining knowledge of the temporality of the world and the existence of the Maker through the speech of the Prophet cannot be conceived, because knowledge of the validity of his speech depends on knowledge of the temporality of the world and the existence of the Maker. This is verified by the fact that the rational person, as long as he does not know that the Maker of the world is [؟] wise (hakim), does not consider the miracle (mu'jiza) to appear at the hand of the false prophet (mutanabbi), as goes the view of those who say that futility (safah) is whatever lacks a praiseworthy outcome; or he knows that the Maker of the world is perfect in power (tamm al-qudra) and incapability ('ajz) is impossible for Him, and the appearance of a miracle at the hand of a false prophet would incapacitate Him from manifesting what is correct among religions and distinguishing between truth and falsehood among them, and this is impossible—as goes the view of those who say that futility is what is forbidden. And when the matter is thus, obtaining knowledge of the temporality of the world, the existence of the Maker, and the perfection of His wisdom and power through the speech of the Prophet cannot be conceived, because the knowledge that his speech is a proof is dependent on these items of knowledge. And when this has been excluded from being suitable as evidence to accompany affirmation—and these items of knowledge are not necessary (darura)—then it is necessary to say that a rational evidence accompanies it.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational argument in Maturidi epistemology of faith. It begins with an implied premise: if a belief is not necessary (daruri) nor inferential (istidlali), it cannot be knowledge ('ilm). The author applies this to the claim that someone who lacks knowledge of the temporality of the world, the existence and oneness of the Creator, and the truth of prophethood cannot be a true believer. He then distinguishes between mere affirmation (tasdiq) and faith (iman) by arguing linguistically: iman, from the root '-m-n,' means 'to bring oneself into security.' One who reflects on the evidence and thereby affirms the truthfulness of a report is said to 'amanahu'—he entered into security. This security is absent if the affirmation is not based on evidence, because one could be deceived. Therefore, plain tasdiq without evidence is not iman. After establishing this, the author reports the consensus of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila that the required evidence must be rational, not based solely on the Prophet's words. The reason is circular: to accept the Prophet's statement as evidence for God's existence and the world's temporality, one must first know that the Prophet is truthful; but knowing that requires knowing that God exists and is wise and powerful, otherwise miracles could be performed by false prophets. Thus rational knowledge is prior. The author then supports this with an argument about the definition of 'futility' (safah) in relation to God: a false prophet's miracle cannot succeed because it would imply God is either foolish or powerless. Hence, rational evidence is necessary.

Key Terms
دليل عقلي
رسالة
معجزة
متنبي
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that quoting the Quran or hadith is sufficient to prove God's existence.
Correction: This passage shows that such an approach is circular; rational proof is needed first to establish the truth of the Prophet's message.

Checkpoint
Why can't the Prophet's statement serve as evidence for the existence of God, according to Nasafi?
A) Because the Prophet's statements are not reliable
B) Because accepting the Prophet requires prior knowledge of God
C) Because the Prophet never spoke about God's existence
D) Because rational evidence is weaker than prophetic reports

Key Takeaway
Rational evidence is indispensable for faith because prophetic testimony depends on prior rational knowledge of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to believe in God, is your faith based on rational certainty from reflecting on creation, or on what you were taught by others? What would happen if that teaching were taken away?
?
In moments of doubt about your faith, do you turn to rational proofs or to repetition of what you've heard? Which approach does this lesson demand of you?
?
If you were challenged to prove God's existence to someone without using any scripture or prophetic reports, could you do it today? If not, what concrete step will you take this week to build that rational foundation?

Faith Despite Doubt: Refuting the Mu'tazila
Refutation of Mu'tazila on doubt and unbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to the author, a believer who is actively seeking to resolve a doubt remains a believer, and how the Mu'tazila objection is self-refuting.

Core Idea
Seeking to repel doubt preserves faith, even while doubt remains.

This passage addresses a critical point of contention with the Mu'tazila: what happens when a believer faces a doubt they cannot immediately resolve? The author asserts that the very act of seeking to repel the doubt keeps one within the fold of faith. He then turns the Mu'tazila's own logic against them, showing that their objection would condemn even their own leaders to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first states his position, then presents the Mu'tazila objection, and then refutes it with a powerful counterargument.
Pay attention to the logical structure: principle → objection → refutation.
2
Understand the author's principle
The author states that a person who is in the state of seeking to repel a doubt is still a believer, even if knowledge is absent, because of the presence of a counter situation. Ask yourself: what is that 'counter situation'? It is the person's active quest for the truth.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila object that if a doubt arises and one cannot immediately repel it, they become a disbeliever, even if they are seeking. Consider how this differs from the author's view. What is the key disagreement?
The Mu'tazila focus on the outcome (doubt leads to disbelief), while the author focuses on the intention (seeking leads to faith).
4
See the reductio ad absurdum
The author refutes by pointing out that if the Mu'tazila objection were true, then their own leaders and imams would be disbelievers because they too faced doubts they couldn't repel. This shows the objection is self-defeating.
This is a powerful argument ad hominem but logically valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن حالة طلب ما يتمكن به من دفع الشبهة كان مؤمنا وإن انعدم العلم لثبوت المعارضة للحال لما ذكرنا وكذا هذا، وكل ما يعتمدون عليه هناك فهو معتمدنا هاهنا، فإن كابروا وقالوا: لما اعترضت الشبهة ولم يتمكن للحال من دفعها يكفر وإن كان في طلب دفعها، فقيل لهم: قد أقررتم على أنفسكم أولا، ثم على كل رئيس من رؤسائكم ثانيا، ثم على كل إمام كان في الأمة ثالثا، بالكفر وبطلان الأعمال مرارا إذ لا يخلو أحد من ذلك في عمره في الأحايين ثم نقول لهم: ما من رئيس من رؤسائكم إلا وقد اعترضت له شبهة لم يتمكن من دفعها، وارتكب لأجلها محالا أكفره به جميع أهل نحلته فضلا عمن خالفهم فيكونون كلهم كفارا
He verifies that the state of one who seeks what he can repel doubt by is a believer, even if knowledge is absent due to the presence of a counter situation for the reason we mentioned. And likewise this. And everything they rely on there is our reliance here. Then if they argue and say: When the doubt arises and he is not able at the moment to repel it, he becomes a disbeliever even if he is in the process of seeking to repel it, it is said to them: You have admitted against yourselves first, then against every leader among your leaders second, then against every imam in the Ummah third, disbelief and invalidity of deeds many times, since no one is free from that in his lifetime at times. Then we say to them: There is no leader among your leaders except that a doubt confronted him which he was unable to repel, and due to it he committed an impossibility which made him a disbeliever according to all his sect, let alone those who oppose them, so they would all be disbelievers.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents an argument regarding the state of a believer who encounters a doubt. The author first states that one who is actively seeking to repel a doubt remains a believer even if knowledge is absent due to a conflicting situation. He then notes that this principle is the same as what the Mu'tazila themselves rely on in other cases. The Mu'tazila object that if a doubt arises and one cannot immediately repel it, he becomes a disbeliever even if he is seeking to repel it. The author refutes this by showing that this leads to the absurd consequence that the Mu'tazila themselves, their leaders, and all imams would be disbelievers because they too have encountered doubts they couldn't repel and committed absurdities. Thus, the objection is self-refuting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Encountering a doubt that one cannot immediately resolve makes a person a disbeliever.
Correction: According to the author, one who is actively seeking to resolve the doubt remains a believer, because the intention to seek truth preserves faith.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the status of a person who is seeking to repel a doubt but has not yet achieved knowledge?
A) He is a disbeliever
B) He is a believer
C) His status depends on the doubt
D) He is an unbeliever until he repels it

Key Takeaway
A believer who is actively seeking to resolve a doubt remains a believer, and the Mu'tazila objection is self-defeating because it would condemn their own leaders.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a doubt that has troubled you in your faith. Are you actively seeking to resolve it, or have you allowed it to linger? What is the state of your iman in that moment? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to address that doubt.
?
If the Mu'tazila's standard—that unresolved doubt equals disbelief—were applied to you, how many times would you have been a disbeliever? What does that reveal about your need for Allah's mercy and the importance of sincere seeking?
?
This lesson teaches that the act of seeking to repel doubt is itself evidence of faith. When was the last time you sincerely sought to understand a matter that confused you about your religion? What prevented you? Commit to seeking one answer this week.

Faith, Knowledge, and the Status of the Muqallid
The author discusses Jahm's definition of iman as knowledge and 'Abd al-Qahir's view that the muqallid is a believer but not a scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether iman is simply knowledge (ma'rifa) and the status of the muqallid as a believer without being a scholar.

Core Idea
Faith requires conviction, not necessarily intellectual proof.

This passage examines two key issues: the definition of faith as knowledge (Jahm ibn Safwan's view) and the status of the muqallid. The author argues that rejecting Jahm's equation leads to an important conclusion about the common believer's faith. We'll see how 'Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi reconciles these points.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The debate over the definition of iman (faith) was central to early kalam. Some equated it with knowledge (ma'rifa). Read the passage with this background.
Jahm ibn Safwan is known for defining iman as knowledge.
2
Identify Jahm's view and why it is rejected
The author rejects making iman merely knowledge. Consider why this equation might be problematic.
The rejection implies faith can exist without demonstrative knowledge.
3
Analyze 'Abd al-Qahir's two statements
The author reconciles two statements of 'Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi to show his view that the muqallid is a believer but not a scholar.
The common person's belief in the creation of the world is correct belief but not knowledge.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The sum of the statements leads to the conclusion that the muqallid is considered a believer even without having knowledge.
This supports the idea that faith does not require detailed intellectual proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أبينا جعل الإيمان معرفة كما جعله جهم بن صفوان والتلفيق بين كلامي عبد القاهر البغدادي من جملة متكلمي أهل الحديث، يدل على أنه كان يرى هذا الرأي أن المقلد مؤمن وليس بعالم، فإنه مال إلى جعله مؤمنا ثم أبطل على تحديده للعلام أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به باعتقاد العامي حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به وليس بعلم، فكان مجموع هذين الكلامين دليلا على أنه كان يجعل المقلد مؤمنا وإن لم يكن علم بما اعتقده وبمن آمن به والله الموفق.
And if we reject making iman merely knowledge as Jahm ibn Safwan made it, and [also reject] the reconciliation between the statements of 'Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi, one of the theologians of the people of hadith, [then] it indicates that he held the view that the muqallid (the uncritical follower) is a believer and not a scholar. For he inclined to making him a believer, then he invalidated his own definition for the scholar: that it is belief of a thing as it is, since the belief of the common man in the temporal creation of the world and the existence of the Creator is belief of a thing as it is, and it is not knowledge. So the sum of these two statements proves that he considered the muqallid a believer even if he does not have knowledge of what he believes and in whom he believes. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of iman. The author notes that if we reject making iman merely knowledge (as Jahm ibn Safwan defined it), and also reject the reconciliation between two statements of 'Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi, we find that 'Abd al-Qahir held that the muqallid (one who follows without proof) is a believer but not a scholar. He argued that the common person's belief in the creation of the world and the existence of the Creator is a correct belief but not knowledge. Therefore, the muqallid is considered a believer even without having demonstrative knowledge. This reconciles with the earlier point that faith does not require detailed knowledge.

Key Terms

معرفة
مقلد
عالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith in God requires intellectual proof and cannot be based on taqlid.
Correction: The passage shows that the muqallid's belief, though not based on demonstrative knowledge, is valid faith and he is considered a believer.

Checkpoint
According to 'Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi's view as presented, what is the status of the muqallid?
A) He is a disbeliever
B) He is a believer and a scholar
C) He is a believer but not a scholar
D) He is neither a believer nor a scholar

Key Takeaway
A muqallid can be a true believer even without scholarly knowledge, showing that faith does not require intellectual proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If your belief is based on taqlid, does this lesson assure you that your faith is still valid, and how does that affect your responsibility to seek knowledge?
?

?
You may consider yourself a believer, but are you content to remain a muqallid? If today you were asked by a skeptic why you believe, would you be able to give a reasoned answer, or would you merely say 'I was raised this way'? What is one thing you will learn to defend your faith with knowledge?

The Necessary Preliminaries to Proving World's Creation
Nasafi lists three preliminaries for proving world's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three preliminary steps required before proving the creation of the world.

Core Idea
Knowledge of terms and divisions precedes proof of creation.

Before diving into the proof that the world is created, Nasafi insists on three preparatory steps: understanding what 'world' means, knowing its divisions, and defining 'created' and 'eternal'. This lesson introduces that framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: after epistemology, the author turns to the world's creation.
This passage is transitional—it sets the stage.
2
Identify the three preliminaries
Find the three things Nasafi says are necessary before explaining the world's creation. They are listed after 'مقدمات لابد منها'.
3
Reflect on the order
Why does Nasafi list these in this order? Consider that each builds on the previous.
This order reflects a logical progression from definition to classification to temporal concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة الأدلة لأبي المعين النسفي ج ١
الفصل الثاني
الكلام في حدوث العالم

ثم بعد الوقوف على ثبوت الحقائق وتمييز ما هو من أسباب المعارف مما ليس من أسباب المعارف، يجب أن نصرف العناية إلى الإبانة عن حدوث العالم بأقسامه من أعراضه وأجسامه.

مقدمات لابد منها

ولا يمكن الوقوف على ذلك إلا بعد معرفة ما ينطلق عليه اسم العالم، ومعرفة أقسامه وماهية كل قسم منه، ومعرفة معنى الحدوث والقدم، فنقدم بيان ذلك كله ليسهل الإبانة عما هو الغرض من الباب، وهو معرفة حدوث العالم فنقول وبالله التوفيق.

ما هو العالم؟

أما المراد من لفظة العالم عند المتكلمين، فهو جميع ما سوی الله تعالى من الموجودات من الأعيان والأعراض، سمي عالما لكونه علما على ثبوت صانع له حي سميع بصير عليم قدير خارج عن حكمه متعال عما فيه من سمات الحدوث وأمارات النقصان غير مشابه لشيء من أقسامه، ولا مماثل لجزء من أجزائه
ليس كمثله شي.
وهو السميع البصير.

أقسام العالم عند المتكلمين

وأما أقسامه فعامة المتكلمين يزعمون أنها أقسام ثلاثة جواهر، وأجسام وأعراض.
Tabsirat al-Adilla by Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, vol. 1
Chapter Two
Discourse on the Creation of the World

Then, after establishing the reality of things and distinguishing what is from the means of knowledge from what is not from the means of knowledge, we must direct our attention to explaining the creation (huduth) of the world with its divisions: its accidents (a'rad) and its bodies (ajsam).

Necessary Preliminaries

And it is not possible to achieve that except after knowing what the name 'world' ('alam) applies to, and knowing its divisions and the quiddity (mahiyya) of each division of it, and knowing the meaning of creation (huduth) and eternity (qidam). So we present all of that to make easy the explanation of what is the purpose of the chapter, which is knowing the creation of the world. So we say, and with Allah is success.

What is the world?

As for what is intended by the word 'world' (al-'alam) according to the mutakallimun, it is all existents other than Allah Almighty, from substances (a'yan) and accidents (a'rad). It is called 'world' (al-'alam) because it is a sign ('alam) of the existence of a Maker for it, Living, Hearing, Seeing, Knowing, Powerful, outside of His decree, exalted above what is in it of marks of creation and signs of deficiency, not similar to any of its divisions, nor resembling any part of its parts. There is nothing like Him. And He is the Hearing, the Seeing.

Divisions of the World according to the Mutakallimun

And as for its divisions, the generality of the mutakallimun claim that they are three divisions: jawahir (substances), ajsam (bodies), and a'rad (accidents).
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to proving the creation (huduth) of the world. It begins by noting that after establishing epistemology (distinguishing valid sources of knowledge from invalid), the author will focus on demonstrating that the world is created. He then states three necessary preliminaries: (1) knowing what the term 'world' means, (2) knowing its divisions and the essence of each, and (3) knowing the meanings of 'created' (huduth) and 'eternal' (qidam). He then defines 'world' as everything other than Allah, including substances (a'yan) and accidents (a'rad). He explains that it is called 'alam (world) because it is a sign ('alam) pointing to its Maker, who is described as Living, Hearing, Seeing, Knowing, Powerful, etc. The definition emphasizes that the world is completely unlike its Creator. Finally, he states that the majority of mutakallimun divide the world into three categories: jawahir (substances), ajsam (bodies), and a'rad (accidents). This classification is essential for the subsequent proof of creation, as each category will be analyzed separately.

Key Terms
عالم
حدوث
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the three things Nasafi says are needed before explaining the world's creation?
A) The meaning of 'world', its divisions, and the meaning of 'created' and 'eternal'
B) The existence of God, the reality of prophets, and the afterlife
C) The definition of substance, accident, and body
D) The proofs for the world's creation, its permanence, and its purpose

Key Takeaway
Three pieces of knowledge are necessary prerequisites before proving the world's creation: the definition of 'world', its divisions, and the concepts of creation and eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before you can prove that the world is created, you must define 'world'. Have you ever honestly defined what 'the world' means to you personally—your ambitions, relationships, possessions? Will you take time today to write down one thing you consider 'worldly' and reflect on whether you treat it as temporary or eternal?
?
The lesson insists you must know the meaning of 'created' and 'eternal' before proceeding. Do you truly believe that everything you see—your body, your health, your success—is created from nothing? If you really believed that, would you cling to any of it as if it were permanent? What would change if you lived today with that certainty?
?
Nasafi requires knowing the 'quiddity' of each division of the world. What is the quiddity of your time? Do you spend it as if it were eternal or as a created gift? This week, will you examine one hour of your daily routine and ask yourself: 'Is this hour spent in light of the fact that it is created and will end?'

Definition of the World and Its Three Divisions
Nasafi defines the world and enumerates its three divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's definition of the world as all existents besides Allah, and its three categories: substances, bodies, and accidents.

Core Idea
The world is everything other than Allah—a sign pointing to its Maker.

Nasafi now answers: what exactly is the 'world'? He gives a precise definition used by the mutakallimun, explains why it is called 'alam (sign), and then introduces the three categories into which all worldly existents fall. This classification is the foundation for the proof of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and translation
Read the Arabic definition of al-'alam. Notice it includes both substances (a'yan) and accidents (a'rad).
The term 'world' here is broader than the physical universe.
2
Understand the 'sign' etymology
Reflect on why it is called 'alam — because it points to its Creator, who is described with attributes like Living, Knowing, etc.
This is a key theological point: the world is evidence for God.
3
Identify the three divisions
Find the three categories Nasafi lists: jawahir (substances), ajsam (bodies), and a'rad (accidents).
These are the building blocks of all created things according to the mutakallimun.

Tabsirat al-Adilla by Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, vol. 1
Chapter Two
Discourse on the Creation of the World

Then, after establishing the reality of things and distinguishing what is from the means of knowledge from what is not from the means of knowledge, we must direct our attention to explaining the creation (huduth) of the world with its divisions: its accidents (a'rad) and its bodies (ajsam).

Necessary Preliminaries

And it is not possible to achieve that except after knowing what the name 'world' ('alam) applies to, and knowing its divisions and the quiddity (mahiyya) of each division of it, and knowing the meaning of creation (huduth) and eternity (qidam). So we present all of that to make easy the explanation of what is the purpose of the chapter, which is knowing the creation of the world. So we say, and with Allah is success.

What is the world?

As for what is intended by the word 'world' (al-'alam) according to the mutakallimun, it is all existents other than Allah Almighty, from substances (a'yan) and accidents (a'rad). It is called 'world' (al-'alam) because it is a sign ('alam) of the existence of a Maker for it, Living, Hearing, Seeing, Knowing, Powerful, outside of His decree, exalted above what is in it of marks of creation and signs of deficiency, not similar to any of its divisions, nor resembling any part of its parts. There is nothing like Him. And He is the Hearing, the Seeing.

Divisions of the World according to the Mutakallimun

And as for its divisions, the generality of the mutakallimun claim that they are three divisions: jawahir (substances), ajsam (bodies), and a'rad (accidents).
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to proving the creation (huduth) of the world. It begins by noting that after establishing epistemology (distinguishing valid sources of knowledge from invalid), the author will focus on demonstrating that the world is created. He then states three necessary preliminaries: (1) knowing what the term 'world' means, (2) knowing its divisions and the essence of each, and (3) knowing the meanings of 'created' (huduth) and 'eternal' (qidam). He then defines 'world' as everything other than Allah, including substances (a'yan) and accidents (a'rad). He explains that it is called 'alam (world) because it is a sign ('alam) pointing to its Maker, who is described as Living, Hearing, Seeing, Knowing, Powerful, etc. The definition emphasizes that the world is completely unlike its Creator. Finally, he states that the majority of mutakallimun divide the world into three categories: jawahir (substances), ajsam (bodies), and a'rad (accidents). This classification is essential for the subsequent proof of creation, as each category will be analyzed separately.

Key Terms
عالم
جوهر
جسم
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'world' (alam) refers only to the physical universe (earth and heavens).
Correction: In kalam terminology, 'world' includes all existents other than Allah, even abstract substances and accidents.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is the world called 'alam?
A) Because it is vast and complex
B) Because it is a sign pointing to its Creator
C) Because it contains many marvels
D) Because it is the opposite of the Hereafter

Key Takeaway
The world is all existents besides Allah, and it is divided into three categories: substances, bodies, and accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the physical world around you—your body, the objects you use, even your own thoughts—do you see them as signs pointing to Allah, or have you become so accustomed to them that you no longer see the sign? What would it take to restore that vision?
?
The world is called 'alam because it is a sign of its Maker. What is one specific 'sign' in your life today that you have been ignoring? Will you pause right now to acknowledge the Maker behind it?
?
If the world is everything other than Allah, then where does your attachment lie—with the world (which is a sign) or with the One to whom it points? What would change if you truly lived as if nothing besides Allah deserves your ultimate devotion?

Introduction and Definition of the World
Nasafi outlines preliminaries and defines the world according to the mutakallimun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of the world (al-ʿālam) in kalam and its role as a sign pointing to the Creator.

Core Idea
The world is everything other than Allah, and it is a sign (ʿalam) pointing to its Maker.

Before proving that the world is temporally originated, Nasafi lays the ground by defining what 'the world' actually means in kalam discourse. The mutakallimūn do not use the term in its everyday sense; they have a precise technical definition that excludes Allah and includes all other existents. This definition is crucial because it sets the scope of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the translation. Notice that Nasafi begins by stating his overall purpose before giving the definition.
2
Identify the purpose and preliminaries
Nasafi states that the purpose is to demonstrate the temporality of the world. He then lists three necessary preliminaries: knowing what the name 'world' applies to, its divisions, and the meanings of temporality and pre-eternity.
These preliminaries show the systematic approach of kalam.
3
Analyze the definition of the world
The definition states: 'the world is all existents other than Allah Most High, from substances and accidents.' Note that it includes both substances and accidents, and explicitly excludes Allah.
Substances (aʿyān) are self-subsisting entities; accidents (aʿrāḍ) are qualities that inhere in substances.
4
Reflect on the etymology
The world is called ʿālam because it is a sign (ʿalam) pointing to its Maker, who is described with attributes of perfection and is absolutely unlike creation.
This etymological point reinforces the teleological argument for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد الوقوف على ثبوت الحقائق وتمييز ما هو من أسباب المعارف مما ليس من أسباب المعارف، يجب أن نصرف العناية إلى الإبانة عن حدوث العالم بأقسامه من أعراضه وأجسامه. مقدمات لابد منها ولا يمكن الوقوف على ذلك إلا بعد معرفة ما ينطلق عليه اسم العالم، ومعرفة أقسامه وماهية كل قسم منه، ومعرفة معنى الحدوث والقدم، فنقدم بيان ذلك كله ليسهل الإبانة عما هو الغرض من الباب، وهو معرفة حدوث العالم فنقول وبالله التوفيق. ما هو العالم؟ أما المراد من لفظة العالم عند المتكلمين، فهو جميع ما سوی الله تعالى من الموجودات من الأعيان والأعراض، سمي عالما لكونه علما على ثبوت صانع له حي سميع بصير عليم قدير خارج عن حكمه متعال عما فيه من سمات الحدوث وأمارات النقصان غير مشابه لشيء من أقسامه، ولا مماثل لجزء من أجزائه ليس كمثله شي وهو السميع البصير.
Then after establishing the reality of things and distinguishing what is from the causes of knowledge from what is not from the causes of knowledge, we must turn our attention to the demonstration of the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world (al-ʿālam) with its divisions—its accidents (aʿrāḍ) and its bodies (ajsām). Indispensable preliminaries: it is not possible to achieve that except after knowing what the name 'world' applies to, and knowing its divisions and the quiddity (māhiyya) of each division thereof, and knowing the meaning of temporality (ḥudūth) and pre-eternity (qidam). So we present the explanation of all that to facilitate the demonstration of what is the purpose of the chapter, which is knowing the temporality of the world; we say, and with Allah is success. What is the world? As for what is meant by the word 'world' (al-ʿālam) according to the mutakallimūn, it is all existents other than Allah Most High, from substances (aʿyān) and accidents (aʿrāḍ). It is called 'world' (ʿālam) because it is a sign (ʿalam) pointing to the affirmation of a Maker for it, Living, Hearing, Seeing, Knowing, Powerful, outside its governance, exalted above whatever it contains of marks of temporality and signs of deficiency, not resembling any of its divisions, nor similar to any part of its parts—'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11)—and He is the Hearing, the Seeing.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is an introduction and definition. It explains that after establishing epistemology (that realities are known and which sources of knowledge are valid), the next task is to prove that the world is temporally originated (ḥudūth). To do this, we must first define the world, know its divisions and the meaning of temporality and pre-eternity. The definition given by the mutakallimūn is that the world is everything other than Allah, comprising substances (aʿyān, entities that subsist by themselves) and accidents (aʿrāḍ, qualities that inhere). The world is called ʿālam because it is a sign (ʿalam) pointing to its Maker, who is described with attributes of perfection and is utterly unlike His creation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world includes Allah or refers only to the physical universe without accidents.
Correction: The mutakallimūn define the world as all existents other than Allah, including both substances and accidents.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of the world according to the mutakallimūn in this passage?
A) All existents including Allah
B) All existents other than Allah from substances and accidents
C) Only substances (aʿyān)
D) Only accidents (aʿrāḍ)

Key Takeaway
The world (al-ʿālam) in kalam is a technical term meaning all existents other than Allah, comprising substances and accidents, and it is called a 'world' because it is a sign pointing to its Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you—your possessions, relationships, ambitions—do you see them as ultimate ends or as signs (āyāt) pointing to their Creator? What would change if you truly saw them as signs?
?
The passage says the world is everything 'other than Allah'. Is there something in your life that you treat as if it is not 'other than Allah'—something you have made equal to Him in your heart? Name it now and commit to returning it to its proper place as a sign.
?
If the world is a sign pointing to a Maker who is Living, Hearing, Seeing, Knowing, Powerful, how does the state of your heart today reflect that reality? Are you listening to what the world says about its Maker, or have you been deaf to it? Decide on one specific action today to intentionally contemplate a part of creation as a sign of Allah's attributes.

Definition of Jawhar and al-Ash'ari's Criticism
The author defines jawhar and analyzes al-Ash'ari's critique of the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of jawhar (substance) in Islamic theology, including its linguistic and technical meanings, and how al-Ash'ari's own definition contradicts his principles according to the author.

Core Idea
Jawhar is defined as self-subsistent and receptive of opposites — but this definition creates a problem for al-Ash'ari.

This passage from Tabṣirat al-Adilla lays out the foundational division of entities into substances and bodies, then defines 'jawhar' carefully. The author then examines al-Ash'ari's objection to this definition, revealing tensions in Ash'ari's own methodology. By working through this discussion, you will see how kalam definitions are constructed and critiqued.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic source passage carefully, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the structure: division, definition, objection, response.
2
Identify the primary division
Notice the author divides entities into uncomposed (jawhar) and composed (jism). Understand that every body is a substance.
This sets the stage for the definition.
3
Understand the definition of jawhar
The technical definition is 'self-subsistent, receptive of opposites.' The author explains 'receptive of opposites' as the capacity to receive opposites alternately, using blackness as an example.
4
Examine al-Ash'ari's two objections
The author presents two ways al-Ash'ari's use of this definition contradicts his own principles: 1) only God is self-subsistent, 2) definitions must have a single attribute.
The author then shows how Ash'ari could have avoided the contradiction.
5
Consider the alternative definitions
At the end, the author lists several other definitions of jawhar but notes they are not consistent with the principles of innovators.
This shows the diversity of opinions on the topic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ثبوت هذه القسمة الأولية نقول : تنقسم الأعيان إلى غير المركبة وهي المسماة في عرف المتكلمين جواهر، والمركبة وهي المسماة في عرفهم أجساما، وبهذا يعرف أن كل جسم جوهر، فإذا عرف هذا التقسيم الجامع لجميع أجزاء المقسوم نشتغل ببيان تحديد كل قسم من هذه الأقسام. [تعريف الجوهر]: أما الجوهر فهو في اللغة عبارة عن الأصل، يقال: لفلان جوهر شريف (أي أصل شريف) وهذا ثوب جوهري : أي جيد الأصل. وأما حده فهو: أنه القائم بالذات القابل للمتضادات هذا هو حده المتداول فيما بين المتكلمين والمنطقيين، ومعنى قولهم القابل للمتضادات أنه يصح قبوله المتضادات على البدل، فإنه إذا كان السواد قائما به وكان قابلا له للحال صح قبوله لضده على البدل من السواد، المتضادات. وكذا هذا في جميع . وأبو الحسن الأشعري ذكر هذا التحديد بهذه العبارة وهذا منه نقض لأصله من وجهين: أحدهما أنه يقول : لا قائم بالذات إلا الله تعالى وقد وصف الجوهر بأنه القائم بالذات. والآخر أن من أصله أن الحد ينبغي أن يكون مستقلا بصفة واحدة، وهو يأبى تركيب الحد من الوصفين فصاعدا، ويزعم أن الحد هو الإبانة عن حقيقة المحدود، والشيء الواحد لن يكون له إلا حقيقة واحدة وغيره من المتكلمين والفلاسفة لا يبالون من تركيب الحد من الوصفين فصاعدا فيقولون: الغرض من التمييز بين المحدود بجميع أجزائه وبين غيره مما ليس هو ولا جزء منه، فلا هذا يبالي بعد حصول الغرض من كونه مركبا من وصفين وزيادة، ثم هو مع الأصل ذكر الحد المركب ، ولو اكتفى بقوله : القابل للمتضادات لكان كافيا ولم ينتقض شيء من أصوله، وظهرت صحته بالامتحان بالطرد والعكس، ولعله قال هذا على طريق المساهلة. وقد قيل في حده إنه الجزء الذي لا يقبل التجزئة فعلا ولا وهما . وقيل : هو ما يقبل من كل جنس من الأعراض عرضا واحدا. وقيل: هو ما يشغل الحيز . وقيل : هو حادث يستغني عن محل وأكثر هذه الحدود لا تستمر على أصول أهل البدع، لو اشتغلنا بالإبانة عن ذلك، لطالب الكتاب وخرج عن الغرض المقصود به.
Then, after the establishment of this primary division, we say: The entities (al-a'yān) are divided into the uncomposed, which in the terminology of the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) are called substances (jawāhir), and the composed, which in their terminology are called bodies (ajsām). And by this it is known that every body is a substance (jawhar). So when this division, which encompasses all parts of the divided thing, is known, we occupy ourselves with explaining the definition (taḥdīd) of each of these divisions. [Definition of al-jawhar]: As for al-jawhar, in language it denotes the origin (al-aṣl); it is said: 'So-and-so has a noble jawhar (i.e., a noble origin),' and 'This is a jawharī garment (i.e., good in origin).' As for its technical definition (ḥadd), it is that it is self-subsistent (al-qā'im bi al-dhāt), receptive of opposites (al-qābil li al-mutaḍāddāt). This is its definition current among the theologians and logicians (al-manṭiqiyyūn). And the meaning of their saying 'receptive of opposites' is that it is valid for it to receive opposites alternately; for if blackness subsists in it and it is receptive of it at the moment, it is valid for it to receive its opposite alternately from the blackness of opposites. And likewise this is in everything. And Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ash'arī mentioned this definition with this expression, and this from him is a refutation (naqḍ) of his own principle in two respects: one is that he says: 'There is no self-subsistent except God,' yet he described the substance as self-subsistent. The other is that it is from his principle that the definition (ḥadd) should be independent with a single attribute, and he rejects the composition (tarkīb) of the definition from two or more descriptions, and he claims that the definition is the clarification of the reality (ḥaqīqa) of the defined thing, and a single thing cannot have except a single reality. But others among the theologians and philosophers do not mind the composition of the definition from two or more descriptions, and they say: 'The purpose is to distinguish the defined thing with all its parts from what is not it nor a part of it.' So this one does not mind, after the purpose is achieved, that it is composed of two or more descriptions. Then he, along with his principle, mentioned the composed definition. And if he had sufficed with his saying: 'receptive of opposites,' it would have been sufficient, and nothing of his principles would have been contradicted, and its correctness would have appeared by testing through coextensiveness (ṭard) and co-exclusiveness ('aks). Perhaps he said this by way of leniency (musāhala). And it has been said in its definition: it is the part that does not accept division actually nor in estimation (wahm). And it is said: it is that which accepts from every genus of accidents a single accident. And it is said: it is that which occupies space (ḥayyiz). And it is said: it is an originated thing that is independent of a subject (maḥall). And most of these definitions are not consistent with the principles of the people of innovation (ahl al-bida'); if we occupied ourselves with clarifying that, it would extend the book and go beyond the intended purpose.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a discussion of objections. It begins by presenting the primary division of entities into uncomposed (substances) and composed (bodies), establishing that every body is a substance. Then it defines 'jawhar' linguistically and technically. The technical definition is 'self-subsistent, receptive of opposites.' The author explains 'receptive of opposites' as the capacity to receive opposites alternately, using blackness as an example. Then he discusses al-Ash'ari's criticism: Ash'ari himself uses this definition, but it contradicts two of his own principles. First, Ash'ari holds that only God is self-subsistent, yet he applies 'self-subsistent' to the substance. Second, Ash'ari insists that a definition should consist of a single attribute, yet this definition has two. The author notes that other theologians and philosophers do not mind composite definitions as long as the purpose of distinguishing the defined is achieved. He suggests that Ash'ari could have sufficed with 'receptive of opposites' alone, which would avoid the contradiction. Finally, he lists several alternative definitions of jawhar (e.g., indivisible part, etc.) but does not discuss them in detail, stating that most are incompatible with the principles of innovators and that a full discussion would be too lengthy.

Key Terms
الجوهر (al-jawhar)
الجسم (al-jism)
الحد (al-ḥadd)
القائم بالذات (al-qā'im bi al-dhāt)
القابل للمتضادات (al-qābil li al-mutaḍāddāt)
الطرد والعكس (al-ṭard wa al-'aks)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the definition of jawhar as 'self-subsistent' implies it is uncreated.
Correction: The author clarifies it is a technical term meaning independent of a subject, not that it is uncreated.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ash'ari's use of the definition 'self-subsistent, receptive of opposites' contradict his own principle according to the author?
A) Because he believes only God is self-subsistent
B) Because he believes definitions must be composed of two attributes
C) Because he believes everything is composed
D) Because he rejects the term 'jawhar'

Key Takeaway
The definition of jawhar as 'self-subsistent, receptive of opposites' is standard, but al-Ash'ari's use of it reveals tensions in his own methodological commitments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own understanding of faith, do you hold principles that you break when convenient, like al-Ash'ari did with his definition? Where might you be inconsistent in how you define your relationship with God? Will you examine your definitions and ask God for integrity?
?
The author suggests that al-Ash'ari could have used a simpler definition. Are there aspects of your worship or beliefs that you have overcomplicated, missing the simple core? What is one thing you can simplify today to draw closer to the essence of your faith?
?
Al-Ash'ari's definition of substance included both 'self-subsistent' and 'receptive of opposites', but it caused him trouble. What 'definitions' in your life—of success, goodness, or piety—might be composed of conflicting elements that lead to inner contradiction? Will you ask God to purify your intentions?

Definition of Jawhar: Self-Subsistent and Receptive of Opposites
Nasafi defines jawhar and explains the meaning of 'receptive of opposites'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of jawhar (substance) as 'self-subsistent and receptive of opposites' and what 'receptive of opposites' means in Islamic kalam.

Core Idea
A substance is defined by its capacity to receive opposite attributes at different times.

Nasafi begins by giving the standard definition of jawhar (substance) used by mutakallimun and logicians. He then clarifies the key phrase 'receptive of opposites' by explaining that it refers to the ability to receive opposite qualities alternately, not simultaneously. This definition is foundational for understanding the classification of entities in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms: jawhar, al-qa'im bi al-dhat, al-qabil lil-mutadaaddat.
The definition is standard among both mutakallimun and logicians.
2
Understand 'receptive of opposites'
The passage explains that it means the substance can receive opposites alternately (على البدل). The example of blackness and whiteness makes this concrete: a substance can have blackness now and whiteness later.
It does NOT mean containing opposites at the same time.
3
Reflect on why this definition matters
This capacity to receive opposites distinguishes substances from other categories like attributes or accidents. It is a key concept in kalam for discussing the nature of created things.
Later lessons will build on this foundation.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp the meaning of 'receptive of opposites'. Then review the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالذات القابل للمتضادات هذا هو حده المتداول فيما بين المتكلمين والمنطقيين، ومعنى قولهم القابل للمتضادات أنه يصح قبوله المتضادات على البدل، فإنه إذا كان السواد قائما به وكان قابلا له للحال صح قبوله لضده على البدل من السواد، المتضادات.
The self-subsistent (al-qa'im bi al-dhat) receptive of opposites (al-qabil lil-mutadaaddat) — this is its commonly used definition among the mutakallimun and logicians. The meaning of their statement 'receptive of opposites' is that it is valid for it to receive opposites alternately. For if blackness is subsisting in it and it is receptive to it at the moment, then it is valid for it to receive its opposite [whiteness] alternately in place of blackness — [the reception of] opposites.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a definition. Nasafi begins with the standard definition of jawhar (substance) as 'the self-subsistent entity that is receptive of opposites.' He then explains the meaning of 'receptive of opposites' by giving an example: if blackness inheres in a jawhar, the jawhar can later receive whiteness (the opposite) instead of blackness. This capacity to receive opposite attributes at different times is what defines jawhar according to the mutakallimun and logicians.

Key Terms
الجوهر (jawhar)
القائم بالذات (al-qa'im bi al-dhat)
القابل للمتضادات (al-qabil lil-mutadaaddat)
البدل (al-badal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'receptive of opposites' means a substance holds opposites at the same time.
Correction: It means the substance can receive opposite attributes at different times, not simultaneously.

Checkpoint
What does 'receptive of opposites' (القابل للمتضادات) mean in the definition of jawhar?
A) The substance contains opposites at the same time.
B) The substance can receive opposite attributes at different times alternately.
C) The substance is opposite to all other things.
D) The substance is composed of opposites.

Key Takeaway
The definition of jawhar (substance) hinges on its capacity to receive opposite attributes alternately.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You constantly change: from happiness to sadness, faith to doubt, good deeds to bad. This lesson says your capacity to receive opposites is part of your very essence as a created substance. Where in your life have you been resisting the change that could bring you closer to Allah? What would it cost you to surrender to that change today?
?
Your soul can receive either divine guidance or its opposite. Which opposite has been inscribing itself in your heart lately? If it is the opposite of what you truly want, what concrete step can you take this week to let faith replace doubt?
?
This definition of jawhar reminds you that you are not self-subsistent; you depend on Allah for every attribute. Yet you still choose which opposites to host. Is there an attribute of disobedience you have been allowing to take residence, knowing it should be replaced? Will you make a firm intention to evict it and invite its opposite?

Alternative Definitions of Jawhar
Nasafi lists four alternative definitions of jawhar and comments on their adequacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four alternative definitions of jawhar (substance) given in classical kalam and why Nasafi considers them problematic from a Sunni perspective.

Core Idea
The definition of jawhar is contested, but most alternatives clash with Sunni principles.

Nasafi presents several definitions of jawhar that were proposed by different schools. While these definitions attempt to capture the essence of substance, many of them conflict with the foundational principles of Ahl al-Sunna. In this lesson, we will examine each definition and understand Nasafi's caution about engaging with them.

Guided Path

Definition of Body and Maturidi's Strategic Approach
Definition of body and Maturidi's lenient handling of it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of body (jism) in kalam and how Imam al-Maturidi strategically uses it to argue for God's transcendence.

Core Idea
Maturidi uses the definition of body not to define it, but to disprove its applicability to God.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi addresses the technical definition of 'body' (jism) as used by the theologians, including the requirement of eight atomic parts and three dimensions. However, his purpose is not to endorse this definition but to show that whatever body means, it cannot apply to Allah. He adopts a lenient approach, allowing his opponents to commit to a definition that inevitably leads to composition, which is impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over‑analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of Maturidi's argument come through before interpreting.
2
Identify the two definitions of body
The passage presents an initial definition based on depth/thickness and then a more technical definition from early Hanafis and Mu'tazila based on length, width, and depth. Locate both.
3
Notice Maturidi's attitude
The commentary says he mentions the definition 'not as one clinging to it, but as one being lenient.' Observe how the passage describes his approach.
This is key to understanding his strategy.
4
Understand Maturidi's argument
He states that if body in the witnessed world means having directions, ends, or three dimensions, then it cannot be said of God. Consider why each possibility leads to impossibility.
All three imply contingency and composition.
5
Reflect on why he avoids defining realities
Maturidi's habit is to define only what is necessary for religion. Here he uses the opponent's definition to defeat them without committing to a specific definition himself.
This reflects his methodological leniency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأسفل أو من الجانب الأعلى فحصل له عمق وسمك الآن يسمى جسما، ويقولون: لا يحصل الجسم بأقل من ثمانية أجزاء كل جزء نقطة، وساعدهم أوائل أصحابنا والمعتزلة بأسرهم على هذا، وقالوا: الجسم ما له طول و عرض وعمق، والشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي ذكر هذا الحد لا كالمتشبث به، بل كالمتساهل الملقي زمام كلامه إلى خصومه ليقوده إلى مرامه ثقة منه بضعفه وعجزه عن مقاومته في محل النزاع والجدال، وقال : إن كان الجسم في الشاهد اسم ذي الجهات، أو اسم محتمل النهايات أو اسم ذي الأبعاد الثلاثة. فغير جائز القول به في الله تعالى، وربما يشير في أثناء كلامه إلى معنى التأليف وذلك لأن من دأبه أنه لا يشتغل بتعريف حقيقة شيء ما إذا لم يكن به إلى ذلك حاجة في أمر دينه، وهذا من هذا القبيل، فإن الجسم لابد أنه اسم لما له التركيب، فبعد ذلك لا فرق في استحالته على الله تعالى بين أن يكون اسما لمطلق التركيب أم لتركيب مخصوص يحصل به الأبعاد الثلاثة
the lower or from the upper side, so depth and thickness resulted for it, now it is called a body. And they say: the body does not occur with less than eight parts, each part a point. And the early ones of our companions and the Mu'tazila as a whole assisted them on this, and they said: body is what has length, width, and depth. And Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned this definition not as one clinging to it, but as one being lenient, throwing the reins of his speech to his opponents to lead him to his aim, confident in their weakness and inability to resist him in the place of dispute and argument. And he said: If body in the witnessed world is a name for that which has directions, or a name for that which admits of ends, or a name for that which has three dimensions, then it is not permissible to say it of God. And perhaps he alludes in the course of his speech to the meaning of composition (ta'lif), and that is because it is his habit that he does not occupy himself with defining the reality of anything as long as he has no need for that in the matter of his religion, and this is of that kind. For the body necessarily is a name for that which has composition (tarkib). So after that, there is no difference in its impossibility for God between its being a name for absolute composition or for a specific composition by which the three dimensions are obtained.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'body' (jism) as used by the mutakallimun, then presents Imam al-Maturidi's approach. It begins by stating that something that has depth and thickness is called a body. It reports the position that a body requires at least eight parts (atoms), each a point, and that early Hanafis and Mu'tazila agreed that body is what has length, width, and depth. Then it describes al-Maturidi's method: he mentions this definition not as someone attached to it, but leniently, handing the debate to opponents to lead them to his conclusion, confident in their weakness. He argues that if body in the witnessed world is defined as having directions, ends, or three dimensions, it cannot be attributed to God. He hints that the real issue is composition (ta'lif). His habit is not to define realities unless necessary for religion. The key point is that body necessarily implies composition (tarkib), and once that is established, it makes no difference whether it is absolute composition or a specific composition producing three dimensions—both are impossible for God.

Misconception: Maturidi rejects the definition of body outright.
Correction: In fact, he presents the definition leniently, using it as a tool to argue that body cannot apply to God, not because he disputes the definition itself.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's crucial insight is that any definition of body implies composition, which is impossible for Allah, making the exact definition irrelevant.

Definition of Accident: Language and Technical Usage
Nasafi defines 'ard linguistically and technically, then critiques several definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of 'accident' (al-`arad) in Islamic theology, and the key points of debate among mutakallimun regarding its precise definition.

Core Idea
Accident is that which inheres in a substance and is impermanent, but its exact definition was debated among theologians.

The term 'accident' is fundamental in kalam. In this passage, Nasafi first explains its linguistic origin, then gives the technical sense used by mutakallimun, and finally surveys several competing definitions with critical comments. This will give you a clear picture of how theologians understood accidents and why they differed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage uses a technical vocabulary; note key terms like عرض, جوهر, محل.
2
Understand the linguistic meaning
Identify the linguistic definition: 'that which has no permanence or stability.' Notice the Quranic example of clouds being called 'arid.
3
Grasp the technical definition as attributes
The technical definition: accident is a name for attributes subsisting in originated things, additional to their essences. This covers colors, modes, tastes, smells, etc.
This is the common ground before definitions diverge.
4
Explore the listed definitions
There are five definitions cited. Go through each one: (1) that whose subsistence is impossible; (2) that which exists by the atom; (3) that which does not remain two moments; (4) that whose existence is only between two non-existences; (5) al-Ash'ari's definition.
The author critiques al-Ash'ari's definition as contradicting his own principle.
5
Analyze the final critique
The last definition (dependent on a locus) is evaluated: if 'existence' means 'origination' (huduth) it is fine; if absolute existence, it fails because God's attributes exist without a locus.
This reveals the Mu'tazili position that denies eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العرض فهو في موضع اللغة اسم لما لا دوام له ولا ثبات يقال: عرض لفلان عرض وفلان في عرض شغل أو مرض، ولهذا سمي السحاب عارضا على ما قال تعالى خبرا عن قوم عاد: هَذَا عَارِضٌ مُمْطِرُنَا وهو في عرف المتكلمين اسم للصفات الثابتة للمحدثات زائدة على ذواتها كالألوان والأكوان والطعوم والروائح وأشباه ذلك، فاختلفت عبارات المتكلمين في تحديده، فقال بعضهم: العرض ما يستحيل بقاؤه. وقيل: ما يوجد بالجوهر. وقيل: ما لا يبقى زمانين. وقيل: هو ما يستحيل وجوده إلا بين عدمين. وذكر الأشعري في بعض كتبه: أنه ما يعترض على غيره ويبطل من غير بطلان محله. وهو حد صحيح غير أنه ناقض أصله حيث جعله مركبا من وضعين، وقيل: ما لا يستغني في وجوده عن محل، غير أنه إن أراد بالوجود الحدوث فهو صحيح، وإن أراد به مطلق الوجود فهو منتقض بصفات الله تعالى فلا يستقيم إلا على أصول المعتزلة، لإنكارهم صفات الله تعالى، والله الموفق.
As for accident (al-`arad), it is in the place of language a name for that which has no permanence and no stability. It is said: so-and-so has an accident, and so-and-so is in an accident of occupation or illness. For this reason the cloud is called 'arid (passing), as Allah Most High said, informing about the people of 'Ad: 'This is a cloud giving us rain.' And in the terminology of the mutakallimun, it is a name for the attributes subsisting in originated things, additional to their essences, such as colors, modes (akwan), tastes, smells, and the like. Then the expressions of the mutakallimun differed concerning its definition. Some said: The accident is that whose subsistence is impossible. And it was said: That which exists by (or in) the atom. And it was said: That which does not remain for two moments. And it was said: It is that whose existence is impossible except between two non-existences. And al-Ash'ari mentioned in some of his books: It is that which occurs upon another and passes away without the passing away of its locus. And it is a sound definition, except that he contradicted his own principle (asl) when he made it composite from two positions. And it was said: That which is not independent in its existence from a locus, except that if by existence he means origination (huduth), then it is correct; but if he means absolute existence, then it is refuted by the attributes of Allah Most High, so it is not valid except on the principles of the Mu'tazila, due to their denial of the attributes of Allah Most High. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'accident' (al-`arad) from both a linguistic and a technical perspective. Linguistically, it refers to anything impermanent and unstable; it is used for events that befall a person, and clouds are called 'awarid (passing) because they are transient, as evidenced by the Quranic verse. In the technical terminology of the mutakallimun, accident is a name for attributes that inhere in originated beings and are superadded to their essences, such as colors, modes (akwan), tastes, smells, and similar qualities. The author then lists several definitions given by different mutakallimun: (1) that which is impossible to subsist; (2) that which exists by/in the atom; (3) that which does not remain for two 'times' (zamanayn); (4) that whose existence is impossible except between two non-existences; (5) al-Ash'ari's definition: that which occurs upon something else and passes away without the passing away of its locus (mahall). The author comments that al-Ash'ari's definition is sound in itself but inconsistent with his own principle, because it makes the accident 'composite' from two aspects (the occurrence and the passing). Finally, there is the definition: that which cannot exist independently of a locus. The author critically notes that if by 'existence' one means 'origination' (huduth), it is fine; but if one means absolute existence, then it is refuted by the attributes of Allah (which are eternal and not accidents), so it only holds on Mu'tazili principles that deny divine attributes. The passage ends with praise to Allah.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents are defined simply as 'whatever depends on a locus for existence' without qualification.
Correction: If 'existence' is taken absolutely, this definition incorrectly applies to God's attributes, which exist without a locus. The correct definition must specify 'origination' (huduth) to exclude divine attributes.

Checkpoint

A) Because it applies to God's attributes as well
B) Because it is impossible to prove

D) Because it only works for the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
The definition of accident is contested; the safest definition ties it to origination (huduth) to avoid applying to God's eternal attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you commit to studying definitions carefully, even when they feel difficult?

This lesson shows that even great scholars like al-Ash'ari can have definitions that are 'sound but contradictory to their principle'. None of us is immune from inconsistency.
