Critique of the Definition of Accident
Nasafi critiques a definition of accident, distinguishing existence as temporal origination vs. absolute existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a definition of accident (ʿaraḍ) can be critiqued by distinguishing between temporal existence and absolute existence, and why it is only valid within Mu'tazilite theology.

Core Idea
The same word 'existence' can mean either temporal origination or absolute being—and that makes all the difference.

Theological definitions are not just technical; they carry hidden assumptions. In this passage, Imam Nasafi examines a definition of 'accident' (that which depends on a substrate). He points out an internal contradiction and then unpacks the ambiguous term 'existence.' By doing so, he reveals that the definition only works on Mu'tazilite premises. This lesson will walk you through his precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author first states the definition, then offers a critique and an alternative.
The word 'محله' at the start is a reference to the substrate (place) of the accident.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage presents two formulations: the original definition (composed of two positions) and the alternative ('that which does not dispense with a place in its existence'). Note the role of 'existence' (wujūd).
3
Understand the key distinction
The author splits the meaning of 'existence' into two possibilities: temporal origination (ḥudūth) and absolute existence. See how each leads to a different judgment.
This distinction is the core of the argument.
4
Trace the consequence for God's attributes
If existence means absolute being, then God's attributes (like knowledge or power) would also need a substrate, which is impossible. Therefore the definition fails unless one denies the reality of attributes (as the Mu'tazila do).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the definition is only valid on Mu'tazilite principles because they reject God's eternal attributes. Consider how this critique defends Sunni orthodoxy.

This passage is a critical analysis of a definition of 'accident' (ʿaraḍ). Therefore, the definition only works on Mu'tazilite principles, since they deny the reality of God's attributes. The author ends with a prayer for divine assistance.

The Accident in Kalam: Definition and Essential Attribute
Definition of 'arad and its essential characteristics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of an accident ('arad) in Islamic theology and why its defining characteristic is the impossibility of persistence, not merely its dependence on a substance.

Core Idea
An accident is defined by the impossibility of its persistence.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's work introduces one of the foundational concepts in kalam: the accident ('arad). The author goes beyond a simple definition to clarify the essential characteristic that distinguishes an accident from a substance. Understanding this precise definition is crucial for building sound theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to get a clear sense of the author's argument.
Notice how the author moves from observation to technical definition.
2
Identify the two key characteristics
The passage mentions that an accident is that whose persistence is impossible, and that it depends on a self-subsisting entity. Identify both and see how they relate.
The author emphasizes that the impossibility of persistence is the primary criterion.
3
Reflect on the clarification
The author clarifies that an accident is not called an accident simply because it is not self-subsisting, but because its persistence is impossible. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
This guards against a common misunderstanding.

ثم رأينا أشياء لا بقاء لها فسميناها أعراضا ، فإذا العرض ما يستحيل بقاؤه في عرف المتكلمين ، وهذا الاسم له مأخوذ من الدلالة اللغوية، ثم وجدنا كل عرض له وصف لازم لا يزايله ويستحيل وجوده غير موصوف به، وهو كونه مما يستحيل قيامه بذاته، ويفتقر إلى قائم بذات يقوم به، ثم هو ما كان عرضا لكونه غير قائم بذاته إذ ليس فيه ما ينبئ عن معنى كونه عرضا في اللغة بل كان عرضا لاستحالة بقائه.
Then we saw things that have no persistence, so we called them accidents. So the accident ('arad) is that whose persistence is impossible in the terminology of the theologians (mutakallimūn). This name is taken from linguistic indication. Then we found that every accident has a necessary attribute that does not separate from it and it is impossible for it to exist without being described by it: that it is something whose subsistence by itself is impossible, and it depends on a self-subsisting entity to subsist in. Then it is an accident because it is not self-subsisting, since there is nothing in it that indicates the meaning of its being an accident in language, rather it is an accident because of the impossibility of its persistence.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'arad (accident) in Islamic theology. The author proceeds empirically: we observe things that do not persist, so we name them accidents. The definition in the theologians' convention is 'that whose persistence is impossible'. The term is derived from the linguistic meaning of transience. Then the author identifies a necessary attribute of every accident: its inability to subsist by itself and its dependency on a self-subsisting substance (a jawhar). Importantly, the author clarifies that the true reason for calling something an accident is not merely its lack of self-subsistence, but the impossibility of its persistence. This distinction will be crucial in the later refutation.

Misconception: An accident is defined only by its dependence on a substance (jawhar).
Correction: The defining characteristic is the impossibility of persistence; dependence on a substance is a necessary attribute but not the essence of the definition.

The defining characteristic of an accident in kalam is the impossibility of its persistence, not merely its dependence on a substance.

The Definition of Jawhar and the Logic of Causal Connection
Logical analysis of jawhar's definition and causal relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of jawhar as 'self-subsisting' (qaim bidhat) is justified through the logical principles of itrad, in'ikas, and the inseparability of cause and effect.

Core Idea
A correct definition must be inseparable from its subject: that is the mark of a true cause.

This passage tackles the Christian doubt that describing Allah as a jawhar implies He is self-subsisting like created substances. Imam Nasafi responds by analyzing the definition logically: a valid definition (hadd) must be universal and convertible (itrad and in'ikas) in the witnessed realm. Then he shows that the relationship between being a jawhar and being self-subsisting is one of inseparable connection (infikak), making self-subsistence the cause of being jawhar. Through examples like movement and body, he establishes a universal principle that applies to both witnessed and unseen realms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Christian doubt
The Christians argued that since jawhar is defined as self-subsisting, attributing it to Allah would imply He is self-subsisting like created substances. The author begins by addressing this doubt.
This sets the context for the logical analysis.
2
Learn the criteria for a correct definition
The author states that a correct definition must have itrad (universality) and in'ikas (converse) in the shahid: every jawhar is self-subsisting and vice versa. This ensures the definition is neither too broad nor too narrow.
Itrad means the definition applies to all instances of the defined; in'ikas means the defined applies to all instances of the definition.
3
Distinguish between separable and inseparable attributes
The author explains that attributes which can separate from the subject (like being an agent from being a body) are not causes or definitions, while those that cannot separate (like movement from a moving body) are causes and definitions. This is the key logical principle.
The example of a body being an agent (separable) vs. movement (inseparable) clarifies the principle.
4
Apply the principle to the jawhar case
Since being jawhar and being self-subsisting are inseparable in the shahid, they are linked as cause and effect. This establishes that self-subsistence is the definition and cause of jawhar, making the relationship necessary in both shahid and gha'ib.
The same causal relation must hold in the unseen realm (gha'ib) due to the principle of analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبه النصارى في كونه تعالى جوهرا قائم بالذات، وهذا حد صحيح لاطراده وانعكاسه، لأن كل جوهر في الشاهد قائم بالذات وكل قائم بالذات في الشاهد جوهر، وإذا كان كذلك ثبت تعلق كونه جوهرا بكونه قائما بالذات تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما فثبت أن الجوهر جوهر، لكونه قائما بالذات فكان كونه قائما بالذات علة اتصافه بكونه جوهرا، إذ بهذا يمتاز وصف العلة عن وصف الوجود أعني أن ما يقبل من الأوصاف الانفكاك بينه وبين الموصوف لا يكون حدا ولا علة لكونه متصفا به وما لا يقبل الانفكاك يكون حدا وعلة لكونه متصفا به، ألا ترى أن كونه جسما لما انفك عن كونه فاعلا لاستحالة كون الأجسام الجمادية فاعلة، لم يكن كونه فاعلا علة لكونه جسما، فلم يكن من ضرورة ثبوت كون الذات فاعلا أن يكون جسما ، وكون الجسم متحركا، لما كان لا ينفك عن قيام الحركة به فإنه يستحيل متحرك لم تقم به الحركة، وقيام حركة بما ليس بمتحرك بها، كان قيام الحركة به علة لكونه متحركا، وكذا انفكاك الحد عن المحدود والمحدود عن الحد محال ، وإذا ثبتت هذه القاعدة، فبعد ذلك كان من ضرورة ثبوت أحدها ثبوت الآخر في الشاهد والغائب جميعا، كالحركة والمتحرك، فإن قيام حركة في الشاهد بما ليس بمتحرك لما كان محالا، وكذا ثبوت متحرك لم يقم به الحركة كان من ضرورة ثبوت متحرك في الشاهد والغائب جميعا قيام الحركة به ومن ضرورة ثبوت قيام حركة بذات أن يكون متحركا، فكذا فيما نحن فيه لما ثبت تعلق كونه جوهرا بكونه قائما بالذات وكونه قائما بالذات بكونه جوهرا تعلقا بلا انفكاك عرف أن تعلق ما بينهما تعلق العلة بالمعلول وكونه قائما بالذات حد الجوهر، فيستحيل ثبوت أحدهما بدون الآخر في الشاهد
The Christians' doubt regarding His being, exalted, a jawhar is self-subsisting (qaim bidhat), and this is a correct definition (hadd) for its universality (itrad) and its converse (in'ikas), because every jawhar in the witnessed realm (shahid) is self-subsisting, and everything self-subsisting in the witnessed realm is a jawhar. And if it is so, it is established that the connection (ta'alluq) of His being a jawhar with His being self-subsisting is a connection with no separation (infikak) between them. So it is established that the jawhar is jawhar because of its being self-subsisting. So His being self-subsisting became the cause ('illa) of His being described as a jawhar, since thereby the description of the cause is distinguished from the description of existence. That is, any attribute that admits separation between it and the described is neither a definition nor a cause for being described by it, while that which does not admit separation is a definition and a cause for being described by it. Do you not see that His being a body (jism) is separate from His being an agent (fa'il) because it is impossible for inanimate bodies to be agents, so His being an agent is not a cause for His being a body. So it is not necessary that if the essence is established as an agent, it be a body. And the body being moving (mutaharrik), since it does not separate from the occurrence of movement in it—for it is impossible for something moving without movement subsisting in it, and the occurrence of movement in something not moving by it—the occurrence of movement in it is a cause for its being moving. And similarly, the separation of the definition from the defined and the defined from the definition is impossible. And when this principle is established, then after that it is necessary that the establishment of one entails the establishment of the other in both the witnessed and the unseen (gha'ib), like movement and the moving. For the occurrence of movement in the witnessed realm in what is not moving is impossible, and similarly the establishment of a moving thing without movement subsisting in it is impossible. So it is necessary that the establishment of a moving thing in both the witnessed and the unseen implies the occurrence of movement in it, and it is necessary that the establishment of the occurrence of movement in an essence implies that it is moving. Likewise in our subject, when it is established that the connection between being a jawhar and being self-subsisting is a connection without separation, it is known that the connection between them is the connection of cause and effect, and that being self-subsisting is the definition of jawhar. So it is impossible to establish one without the other in the witnessed realm.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis of the definition of jawhar as 'qaim bidhat' (self-subsisting). The author addresses the Christians' doubt that Allah being a jawhar means He is self-subsisting. He argues that this definition is correct in terms of 'itrad (universality) and 'in'ikas (converse), meaning every jawhar in the shahid is self-subsisting and vice versa. Therefore, the connection (ta'alluq) between being jawhar and being self-subsisting is inseparable (la infikak). This establishes that being self-subsisting is the cause ('illa) for being described as jawhar. The author then distinguishes between attributes that can separate from the described and those that cannot. Attributes that can separate (like being an agent from being a body) are not definitions or causes; attributes that cannot separate (like movement from a moving body) are definitions and causes. He gives examples: a body can be an agent or not, so being an agent is not a cause for being a body. But a moving body cannot be without movement, so movement is the cause for being moving. He then applies this principle to the shahid and gha'ib: the relationship between being a jawhar and being self-subsisting is like that between movement and the moving—it is necessary and causal. Thus, in both the witnessed and unseen realms, one cannot exist without the other. The author concludes that the connection is causal ('illa-ma'lul) and that being self-subsisting is the definition (hadd) of jawhar, making it impossible for one to be established without the other in the shahid.

حد (hadd)

انفكاك (infikak)
اطراد (itrad)
انعكاس (in'ikas)
تعلق (ta'alluq)
متحرك (mutaharrik)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the definition of jawhar as qaim bidhat applies universally to both Creator and creatures, implying that Allah would be a substance like created things.
Correction: The author shows that the definition in terms of itrad and in'ikas only applies within the witnessed realm (shahid) and that the necessary causal connection is used to argue that the definition cannot be transferred to Allah without implying contingency. In reality, Allah is not a jawhar as it implies self-subsistence, but as the passage shows, the argument refutes the Christian doubt by logical consistency.

Checkpoint

A) A cause attribute is always a definition (hadd).
B) A cause attribute cannot be separated from the described object.
C) A cause attribute applies only in the witnessed realm.
D) A cause attribute is always an accident ('arad).

Key Takeaway
The relationship between a definition and its subject must be one of necessary causal connection, not mere coincidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage argues that a true definition must be inseparable from its subject. In your own faith, do you hold any beliefs that you treat as separable—something you affirm with your tongue but that does not cling to your heart? What would it mean for your worship if you truly lived as though jawhar and self-subsistence are inseparable in Allah?
?

What would it take for your character to become a necessary cause of your deeds, as movement is to a moving body?
?

The Definition of Jawhar and Its Inseparable Cause
Nasafi defines jawhar by self-subsistence and proves the inseparable causal relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that for Nasafi, being a substance (jawhar) is defined by being self-subsistent (qa'im bi-dhatih), and that these two are inseparably connected as cause and definition.

Core Idea
A substance is defined by its self-subsistence, and the two are inseparable.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi establishes the foundational definition of jawhar (substance/atom) in the Maturidi tradition. He argues that being a jawhar is not an arbitrary label but is causally tied to being self-subsistent (qa'im bi-dhatih). Understanding this inseparable relation is key to later arguments about the nature of God and the universe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Focus on Nasafi's opening statement: that jawhar is defined by being qa'im bi-dhatih with no separation between them. This is the core thesis.
Notice the dense style; take your time.
2
Examine the distinction between separable and inseparable attributes
Nasafi provides examples: being an agent can separate from a body, but motion cannot separate from a moving thing. Identify these examples and understand why only inseparable attributes can serve as definitions or causes.
This distinction is crucial for the argument.
3
Apply the principle to the witnessed and unseen
See how Nasafi extends the rule to both shahid (witnessed) and gha'ib (unseen). If being a jawhar and being self-subsistent are inseparable in the witnessed, they must be so in the unseen as well. This leads to the conclusion that if the unseen is self-subsistent, it is necessarily a jawhar.
This application is key for kalam proofs about God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جوهرا بكونه قائما بالذات تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما فثبت أن الجوهر جوهر، لكونه قائما بالذات فكان كونه قائما بالذات علة اتصافه بكونه جوهرا، إذ بهذا يمتاز وصف العلة عن وصف الوجود أعني أن ما يقبل من الأوصاف الانفكاك بينه وبين الموصوف لا يكون حدا ولا علة لكونه متصفا به وما لا يقبل الانفكاك يكون حدا وعلة لكونه متصفا به، ألا ترى أن كونه جسما لما انفك عن كونه فاعلا لاستحالة كون الأجسام الجمادية فاعلة، لم يكن كونه فاعلا علة لكونه جسما، فلم يكن من ضرورة ثبوت كون الذات فاعلا أن يكون جسما، وكون الجسم متحركا، لما كان لا ينفك عن قيام الحركة به فإنه يستحيل متحرك لم تقم به الحركة، وقيام حركة بما ليس بمتحرك بها، كان قيام الحركة به علة لكونه متحركا، وكذا انفكاك الحد عن المحدود والمحدود عن الحد محال، وإذا ثبتت هذه القاعدة، فبعد ذلك كان من ضرورة ثبوت أحدها ثبوت الآخر في الشاهد والغائب جميعا، كالحركة والمتحرك، فإن قيام حركة في الشاهد بما ليس بمتحرك (لما كان محالا)، وكذا ثبوت متحرك لم يقم به الحركة كان من ضرورة ثبوت متحرك في الشاهد والغائب جميعا قيام الحركة به ومن ضرورة ثبوت قيام حركة بذات أن يكون متحركا، فكذا فيما نحن فيه لما ثبت تعلق كونه جوهرا بكونه قائما بالذات وكونه قائما بالذات بكونه جوهرا تعلقا بلا انفكاك عرف أن تعلق ما بينهما تعلق العلة بالمعلول وكونه قائما بالذات حد الجوهر، فيستحيل ثبوت أحدهما بدون الآخر في الشاهد والغائب جميعا، وإذا ثبت أن الغائب قائم بالذات ثبت ضرورة أنه جوهر، هذا كما أنكم تثبتون للباري جل وعلا علما ضرورة اتصافه بكونه عالما، وكذا في جميع الصفات بهذا الطريق فكذا هذا.
A substance (jawhar) by its being self-subsistent (qa'im bi-dhatih) has a connection with no separation between them, so it is established that the substance is a substance because it is self-subsistent. So its being self-subsistent is the cause (illa) of its being described as a substance, since by this the description of the cause is distinguished from the description of existence, meaning that what admits separation between itself and the described is not a definition (had) nor a cause for its being described by it, and what does not admit separation is a definition and a cause for its being described by it. Do you not see that its being a body, when it was separate from its being an agent due to the impossibility of inanimate bodies being agents, its being an agent was not a cause for its being a body, so it was not necessary from the establishment of the essence's being an agent that it be a body. And the body's being moving, since it does not separate from the subsistence of motion in it (for it is impossible for a moving thing in which motion does not subsist, and for motion to subsist in what is not moving by it), the subsistence of motion in it was a cause for its being moving. Likewise, the separation of the definition from the defined and the defined from the definition is impossible. When this principle is established, then after that it is necessary from the establishment of one of them the establishment of the other in both the witnessed (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib), like motion and the moving thing. For the subsistence of motion in the witnessed in what is not moving was impossible, and likewise the establishment of a moving thing in which motion does not subsist was necessary from the establishment of a moving thing in both the witnessed and the unseen the subsistence of motion in it, and from the necessity of the subsistence of motion in an essence that it be moving. So likewise in our subject, when it was established that the connection of its being a substance with its being self-subsistent, and its being self-subsistent with its being a substance, is a connection without separation, it is known that the connection between them is the connection of cause to effect, and its being self-subsistent is the definition of substance. So it is impossible to establish one of them without the other in both the witnessed and the unseen. And when it is established that the unseen is self-subsistent, it is necessarily established that it is a substance. This is just as you establish for the Exalted and Majestic Creator knowledge necessarily from His being described as knowing, and likewise in all the attributes by this path, so also this.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing the definition of jawhar (substance/atom) as that which is qa'im bi-dhatih (self-subsistent), and demonstrating the inseparable causal relationship between being a jawhar and being self-subsistent. The author begins by stating that a jawhar, by virtue of being self-subsistent, has a connection with no separation between the two. Therefore, being self-subsistent is the cause (illa) of being described as a jawhar. He then distinguishes between two types of attributes: those that can be separated from the described (like being an agent from a body) and those that cannot (like motion from a moving body). The former cannot serve as a definition or cause, while the latter can. He provides examples: a body can be a body without being an agent, so being an agent is not a cause for being a body. But a moving thing cannot be moving without motion subsisting in it, so motion is a cause for being moving. He then generalizes: the separation of the definition from the defined is impossible. Once this principle is accepted, it follows that in both the witnessed world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib), if one of the two (jawhar and being self-subsistent) is established, the other is necessarily established. He draws an analogy with motion and the moving thing. Applying this to the case at hand, since jawhar and being self-subsistent are inseparably connected, they are related as cause and effect, and being self-subsistent is the definition of jawhar. Therefore, in both the witnessed and the unseen, one cannot exist without the other. Consequently, if the unseen (God) is established as self-subsistent, He is necessarily a jawhar. The author concludes by comparing this to the way one establishes knowledge for God from His being knowing, and other attributes.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
قائم بالذات (qa'im bi-dhatih)
علة (illa)
حد (had)
شاهد (shahid)
غائب (gha'ib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That jawhar is defined by indivisibility (like atoms) rather than by being self-subsistent.
Correction: This passage shows that the defining characteristic of jawhar is being qa'im bi-dhatih (self-subsistent) and that this attribute is inseparably tied to being a jawhar. Divisibility is not mentioned; the focus is on causal relation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between being a jawhar and being qa'im bi-dhatih?
A) They are separable attributes.
B) They have a causal relationship with no separation.
C) Being qa'im bi-dhatih is an accidental attribute of jawhar.
D) They are independent of each other.

Key Takeaway
Being a jawhar and being self-subsistent are causally inseparable, so if the unseen is self-subsistent, it is necessarily a jawhar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that God must be a jawhar because He is self-subsistent. Does this intellectual conclusion align with your heart's feeling of awe before a God who is beyond all categories? How do you respond to the tension between logical necessity and divine transcendence?
?
You rely on analogies from the seen world to understand the unseen. Is there a specific way you have wrongly projected human attributes onto Allah? Repent and commit to learning His names as He revealed them.
?
If being self-subsistent is the very definition of a substance, then your own existence is derivative—you are not self-subsistent. What does this mean for your daily sense of independence and control? How should it change your reliance on Allah?

5 min
Why 'Receptive of Accidents' Is Not a Definition of Substance
Author applies causal attachment rule to reject a definition of substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the definition of substance as 'receptive of accidents' based on the possibility of attributing substance to another meaning.

Core Idea
A substance can be defined by more than one attribute; thus, a co-existing property is not a definition.

The author has established a principle: if one thing's attribution to another must be to a single meaning, then the attachment is causal. Here he applies this to the definition of substance. The common definition 'receptive of accidents' might seem correct because substance and accident always coexist, but the author argues that being a substance can be attributed to something else entirely, like being the origin of a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice how the author first states the general rule and then applies it to the definition of substance.
The passage starts with 'فأما ما لم يشتمل...'
2
Identify the rejected definition
The author rejects the definition 'قابل للأعراض' (receptive of accidents) for substance. Why? Because it is possible that being a substance can be attributed to another meaning, such as being an origin (أصل).
The key phrase: 'لأنه يمكن أن يضاف كونه جوهرا إلى معنى آخر'
3
Contrast with motion example
The author then contrasts this with the case of motion and a moving object. There, the attribute 'moving' can only be caused by motion, so the attachment is causal. For substance, it's not uniquely tied to receiving accidents.
Notice the difference: motion has only one possible attachment, substance does not.
4
Understand the author's own definition
The author hints that the proper definition of substance involves 'being an origin' (أصل) based on linguistic evidence. He is not fully stating the definition here but showing why the rival definition fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما لم يشتمل إضافته إلى معنى آخر فلم يعلم أن تعلق أحدهما بالآخر تعلق العلة بالمعلول، ولهذا لم يجعل قولنا القابل للأعراض حدا للجوهر على ما قررنا، وإن وجد الدوران، لأنه يمكن أن يضاف كونه جوهرا إلى معنى آخر وراء كونه قابلاً للأعراض، ويجعل تعلق قيام الحركة بمحل لاتصاف المحل بكونه متحركا تعلق العلة بالمعلول، لما أن إضافة كونه متحركا يستحيل إلى معنى آخر سوى قيام الحركة به، وفيما نحن فيه لا يستحيل إضافة كونه جوهرا على معنى سوى القيام بالذات وهو كونه أصلا بل وجبت الإضافة إليه لما مر من الدلالة اللغوية على ذلك فلم يجز إضافته إلى كونه قائما بالذات، وهذا الجواب مما لا يمكن التعويل عليه على أصول المعتزلة، لأنهم يجعلون حد العرض مما يستحيل قيامه بنفسه وإن لم يكن في لفظة العرض ما يدل عليه، ولم يجعلوا حده ما يستحيل الدوام عليه وإن كانت لفظة العرض من حيث اللغة منبئا عنه . وكذا النصارى يجعلون هكذا في حد الجوهر، والله الموفق.
As for that which does not include its attribution to another meaning, it is not known that the attachment of one to the other is the attachment of cause to effect. For this reason, we did not make our statement 'the receptive of accidents' a definition of substance, as we have established, even if there is co-existence, because it is possible that its being a substance is attributed to another meaning beyond its being receptive of accidents. And we make the attachment of motion to a place, for the attribute of the place as being moving, the attachment of cause to effect, because the attribution of its being moving is impossible to any other meaning besides the attachment of motion to it. But in our case, the attribution of its being a substance to a meaning other than subsistence by itself is not impossible, and it is being an origin (أصل); rather, the attribution is necessary to it due to the linguistic indication that has passed, so it is not permissible to attribute it to its being self-subsisting. And this answer cannot be relied upon according to the principles of the Mu'tazila, because they define accident as that which cannot subsist by itself, even if the word 'accident' does not indicate that, and they did not define it as that which cannot endure, even though the word 'accident' linguistically indicates that. Likewise, the Christians define substance in this way. And God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage applies the previous principle to the definition of substance (جوهر). The author argues that the definition 'receptive of accidents' (قابل للأعراض) is not a valid definition for substance because it is possible that the attribute of being a substance is due to something else, like being an origin (أصل) that composes a body. He contrasts this with motion and the moving object, where the attribute 'moving' can only be attributed to the meaning of motion, so the attachment is causal. In the case of substance, it is not impossible to attribute being substance to another meaning, so the definition fails. The author then notes that this answer (i.e., the argument that the definition of substance is not simply 'receptive of accidents' but rather based on linguistic indication) is not acceptable to the Mu'tazila because they have their own definitions of accident and substance based on other criteria. Similarly, Christians define substance differently. So the argument works only on the author's own principles.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
قابل للأعراض
أصل
دوران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'receptive of accidents' is a sufficient definition for substance simply because they always coexist.
Correction: Coexistence (دوران) does not guarantee a causal definition; substance may be defined by a deeper attribute like being an origin.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the definition of substance as 'قابل للأعراض' (receptive of accidents)?
A) Because substances never receive accidents.
B) Because the term 'accident' is not defined properly.
C) Because being a substance can be attributed to another meaning, such as being an origin of the body.
D) Because the Mu'tazila accept it.

Key Takeaway
A definition must be based on a unique, non-alternative attribute; otherwise, coexistence alone is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mistaken a pattern in your life (e.g., a bad habit and a negative outcome) for a necessary cause, when the true cause might be something deeper, like your intention or neglect? How can you apply the author's principle of seeking a unique causal link to uncover the real root?
?
The lesson shows that a definition must be based on a unique, non-alternative attribute. In your understanding of Allah's names and attributes, are there attributes you ascribe to Him that are also true of creation (e.g., 'Alim, Qadir)? How does tawheed demand that you purify your concept so that His attributes are uniquely His? What practical step can you take today to deepen your tawheed?

Mu'tazila and Christian Definitions of Substance
Author critiques Mu'tazila and Christians for their alternative definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author's argument against the definition of substance does not apply to the Mu'tazila and how they and Christians define substance differently.

Core Idea
Definitions depend on principles; the Mu'tazila use different criteria for substance and accident.

After presenting his own argument, the author notes that his conclusion cannot be relied upon by the Mu'tazila because they define accident differently. They define accident as 'what cannot subsist by itself' rather than by linguistic origin. Similarly, Christians have their own definition of substance. This lesson examines these sectarian differences.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the passage
Read from 'وهذا الجواب' to the end. Notice the shift from the author's own argument to a commentary on other groups.
The keyword is 'هذا الجواب' (this answer) referring to the previous argument.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili definition of accident
The Mu'tazila define accident as 'that which cannot subsist by itself' (ما لا يقوم بنفسه). They ignore the linguistic meaning of 'عرض' (accident) as something that does not endure.
They do not rely on the linguistic sense of the word.
3
Understand the Christian definition of substance
Christians define substance similarly, but the author does not elaborate. The point is that definitions are based on different principles.
4
Grasp the implication
The author's argument that substance should be defined by being an origin (based on language) only works if one accepts his linguistic premise. Other schools have different foundations.

The Linguistic Dispute over 'Jism'
Nasafi presents the linguistic disagreement over 'jism' and the opponents' definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of 'jism' as composed, and how opponents redefine it to avoid admitting composition, while still relying on analogical arguments.

Core Idea
The word 'jism' is debated: for Maturidis it means composite, for opponents it means existent.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi addresses a key theological dispute: what does the term 'body' (jism) actually mean? While the Maturidis define it as something composed of parts, their opponents—who deny composition in God—give alternative definitions. Yet the author shows that their arguments betray their real belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author frames the dispute as a linguistic one.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the Maturidi definition and the opponents' definitions. Note that the opponents have two variants: existent (mawjud) and self-subsistent (qa'im bi al-dhat).
3
Notice the author's methodology
See how al-Nasafi treats this as a linguistic matter, like other disputed terms. This sets up his later refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام بيننا وبين المخالفين لنا في اسم الجسم مع إنكارهم ما هو حقيقة معنى الجسم من التأليف والتركب ومساعدتهم إيانا على استحالة ذلك على القديم جل ذكره فجار على الاسم أنه في اللغة واقع على ماذا؟ فعندنا: على المتألف المتركب على ما سبق قبل هذا في تحديد الجسم، وعندهم على الموجود، وعند بعضهم على القائم بالذات على ما مر فنتكلم فيه كما نتكلم في سائر الأسامي اللغوية عند اختلاف أرباب اللسان فيها. أن هذا على ماذا يقع؟ وكثير من هؤلاء يتعلقون من حيث المعنى فيقولون: إنه تعالى فاعل ولا فاعل إلا الجسم في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب، وكذا يستدلون بكونه حيا قادرا عالما سميعا بصيرا على حسب ما تعلقت به الطائفة الأولى، وليس لهؤلاء شبهة في المسألة يتعلقون بها، وظني أن مراد هؤلاء من إطلاق هذا الاسم إثبات ما هو معناه في الجثة والتركيب غير أن إبطال خصومهم كلامهم على وجه يمكنهم إنكار ذلك ألجأهم إلى أن ظهروا أنهم غير مريدين بهذا الإطلاق ذلك المعنى، وإنما يريدون به ما يمكنهم بصحيحه من المعنى الثابت كالوجود، والقيام بالذات، وصحة كونه فاعلا، وتأولوا كلام سلفهم بهذا ليستروا بذلك معايب من اتخذوه إماما لهم، أما الدليل على هذا: أن الكرامية زعموا أنه تعالى مماس لجميع الصفحة العليا من العرش مع سعتها وتفضل ذاته عن تلك الصفحة من الجهات الأربع، ولم يتصور ذلك إلا في متركب.
As for the discussion between us and those who disagree with us concerning the name 'body' (jism) while they deny what is the true meaning of body, i.e., composition and structure, yet they agree with us on the impossibility of that for the Ancient (al-Qadim), exalted is His mention, so the discussion revolves around the name: upon what does it apply linguistically? With us: upon the composed and structured, as preceded earlier in the definition of body. With them: upon the existent (al-mawjud), and with some of them upon the self-subsistent (al-qa'im bi al-dhat), as passed earlier. So we discuss it as we discuss other linguistic names when the speakers disagree concerning what it applies to. And many of these people cling to meaning and say: He is a doer (fa'il), and there is no doer except body in the witnessed world, thus similarly in the unseen. Likewise they infer from His being living, powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing, according to what the first group adhered to. And these people have no proof in this issue to cling to. My opinion is that the intent of these people in using this name is to affirm its meaning in terms of body and structure, but the refutation of their opponents of their speech in a way that allows them to deny that compelled them to appear as if they do not intend that meaning by this usage, and rather they intend by it what they can properly affirm of established meaning, such as existence, self-subsistence, and the validity of His being a doer. And they interpreted the speech of their predecessors with this to cover the faults of the one they took as an imam. As for the proof of this: the Karramiyyah claimed that He is in contact (mumass) with the entire upper surface of the Throne despite its vastness, and His essence extends beyond that surface from all four directions, and that cannot be imagined except for something composite (murakkab).
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion on the definition of the term 'body' (jism). The author notes that while the opponents (Mujassima) agree with the Maturidis that composition is impossible for God, they disagree on the linguistic meaning of 'jism'. For the Maturidis, 'jism' means something composed of parts (mu'alif murakkab); for the opponents, it means either 'existent' (mawjud) or 'self-subsistent' (qa'im bi al-dhat). The author then points out that many of these opponents argue from empirical analogy: God is a doer (fa'il) and in the empirical world only bodies are doers, so God must be a body. They also use attributes like living, powerful, etc. The author dismisses their proof as baseless. He then offers his opinion: their real intent in using the name 'body' is to affirm bodily composition and structure, but because their opponents refuted them in a way that allowed them to deny it, they pretended not to mean that, and instead claimed they mean existence, self-subsistence, etc. They reinterpret the words of their predecessors to hide the faults of their imam. The evidence for this is that the Karramiyyah explicitly claimed that God is in contact (mumassa) with the entire upper surface of the Throne and extends beyond it, which is only conceivable for something composite (murakkab). Thus their real belief is that God is a body.

Key Terms
الجسم
التأليف والتركيب
الموجود
القائم بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidis, what does the word 'jism' (body) mean?
A) Existent (al-mawjud)
B) Self-subsistent (al-qa'im bi al-dhat)
C) Composed of parts (al-mu'alaf al-murakkab)
D) A doer (fa'il)

Key Takeaway
The Maturidis define jism as composed, while opponents define it as existent or self-subsistent, but their arguments show they actually mean composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah, do you carefully consider the words you use, or do you assume any familiar term is safe? This lesson shows that even a word like 'body' can be twisted. How will you train yourself to use only the precise language of the Qur'an and Sunnah for Allah's attributes?
?
Have you ever accepted a description of Allah that sounded right but later realized it was imprecise or dangerous? What will you do to ensure you study the correct definitions of key terms before adopting them?
?
The opponents in this passage agreed that composition is impossible for God, yet they still insisted on using a different definition of 'jism'. Are there any beliefs you hold that seem harmless on the surface but could actually undermine Allah's transcendence? Ask yourself honestly: am I willing to abandon any term or concept that does not fully protect Allah's perfection?

Exposing the Real Intent of the Anthropomorphists
Nasafi exposes the opponents' hidden corporealist belief and cites the Karramiyyah's contact claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that the anthropomorphists' true belief is composition, and how the Karramiyyah's claim of God's contact with the Throne proves this.

Core Idea
The opponents' definition of jism is a cover; the Karramiyyah's contact claim reveals their belief in composition.

After discussing the linguistic dispute, Imam al-Nasafi exposes the real agenda of those who call God a body. He argues that their redefinition is just a shield, and provides concrete evidence from the Karramiyyah sect who explicitly claimed that God is in contact with the Throne—something only a composite being can do.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the author's tone and his claim that opponents hide their true intent.
2
Identify the author's claim
Find where al-Nasafi states that the opponents actually want to affirm composition but deny it due to refutation. Underline the key words.
3
Analyze the Karramiyyah evidence
Read how the Karramiyyah described God's relation to the Throne. Why does this prove they believe in composition?
The author says contact (mumassa) can only be imagined for something composite (murakkab).

Correction: The author shows that the opponents' definitions are a strategic cover. The Karramiyyah's explicit claim of God's contact with the Throne proves they believe in composition, which is the real meaning of body for them.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author give to show that the opponents actually intend composition when they say 'body'?
A) They deny that God is a doer.
B) They say 'body' means existent.
C) The Karramiyyah claim that God is in contact with the Throne.
D) They agree with the Maturidis on everything except the name.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyyah's claim that God contacts the Throne proves they believe God is composite, exposing their true understanding of 'jism'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used vague or reinterpreted language to cover a belief that deep down you know conflicts with clear revelation? What does this passage teach you about the danger of such concealment before God?
?
The opponents reinterpreted the words of their predecessors to hide the faults of their imam. Is there a religious figure you admire whose errors you are tempted to explain away? What would true submission to the truth cost you?
?
The Karramiyyah claimed God contacts the Throne—perhaps thinking they were exalting Him. How can you ensure that your desire for closeness to God does not lead you to imagine Him in human terms? Examine your heart: do you ever prefer a 'tame' God who fits your understanding over the transcendent One described in revelation?

Mu'tazila Argument for Similarity
Presents the Mu'tazila definition of similarity and its proof using the most specific attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila definition of similarity (tamathul) and their proof based on the most specific attribute.

Core Idea
Similarity is not about sharing general traits but the most specific attribute.

The Mu'tazila, a prominent theological school, had a particular view on what makes two things similar. In this passage, Abu Hashim and Abu Bakr ibn al-Ikhshid present their definition and a logical argument. Before we can understand later discussions on divine attributes, we need to grasp this foundational concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the gist of the argument. Do not worry about details yet.
2
Identify the definition
Find the sentence where the Mu'tazila define similarity. What is the key term they use for the attribute that must be shared?
The term 'أخص وصف' (most specific attribute) is central.
3
Analyze the first argument
The Mu'tazila give an example of black and white. They argue that sharing general attributes like existence does not make them similar. Why do they reject general attributes?
4
Analyze the second argument
They provide a second reason based on the basis of difference. What is the principle they use? How does it support their definition?
They claim the basis of similarity is the same as the basis of difference.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can apply the concept. Then write the activity to solidify your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو هاشم وأبو بكر بن الأخشيد من جملة المعتزلة : إنهما المشتركان في أخص وصف ، واستدلا بذلك أن لا مماثلة بين السواد والبياض مع اشتراكهما في الوجود وكونهما لونين وعرضين لما أن هذه أوصاف عامة، فلما جاء الاشتراك في كونهما سوادين وهو أخص أوصافهما ثبتت المماثلة، ولأن المماثلة بين المتماثلين تقع بما تقع به المخالفة بين غيرهما، والسواد يخالف البياض بكونه سوادا لا بكونه موجودا أو عرضا أو لونا دل أنه يماثل البياض بكونه بياضا.
Abu Hashim and Abu Bakr ibn al-Ikhshid from the Mu'tazila said: The two are those that share the most specific attribute. They argued by that that there is no similarity between black and white despite their sharing in existence, being colors and accidents, because these are general attributes. When they share in being black, which is their most specific attribute, similarity is established. And because similarity between similar things occurs by that by which dissimilarity occurs between others, and black differs from white by being black, not by being existent, accident, or color, it indicates that it [i.e., black] resembles white by being white.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila definition of similarity (tamathul). Abu Hashim and Abu Bakr ibn al-Ikhshid argue that two things are similar if they share the most specific attribute (akhass wasf). They reason that black and white are not similar based on general attributes like existence, color, or accident, because those are too broad. Only when they both share the attribute of being black (which is specific to black things) does similarity occur. They use the principle that the basis of similarity is the same as the basis of difference: just as black differs from white by being black (its specific attribute), so black resembles another black by that same specific attribute—being black. Conversely, black resembles white by being white? No, actually they say black resembles white by being white? The text says: "يماثل البياض بكونه بياضا" which means black resembles white by being white? That seems odd. Actually careful: They say black differs from white by being black, so it indicates that black resembles white by being white. But presumably the argument is symmetric: black and white separate by their specific attributes, so they would resemble each other only if they shared a specific attribute. The logic is that the attribute causing difference is the same level of specificity that would cause similarity if shared.

Key Terms
تماثل
أخص وصف
سواد
بياض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume similarity requires sharing all attributes or at least many general ones.
Correction: The Mu'tazila only require sharing the most specific attribute; general attributes like existence are irrelevant for similarity.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila view presented, what is the basis of similarity between two things?
A) Sharing in existence
B) Sharing in general attributes like color
C) Sharing in the most specific attribute (akhass wasf)
D) Sharing in all attributes (general and specific)

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila defined similarity (tamathul) as sharing the most specific attribute (akhass wasf), not general ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at another Muslim, do you focus on general attributes like ethnicity, wealth, or nationality, or on the most specific attribute of their faith and devotion? How would your treatment of them change if you saw them primarily as a fellow believer sharing the same iman?
?
If Allah judges you by your most specific attribute—your taqwa and sincere actions—then are you content with merely being a Muslim in a general sense, or do you strive to make your specific attribute of righteousness distinct and pure? What one step can you take today to deepen that specific attribute?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that the same attribute that causes difference can cause similarity. Think of a characteristic that you often use to differentiate yourself from others (e.g., knowledge, wealth). How can you use that same attribute to draw closer to those who share it, rather than to feel superior? What would change in your heart if you saw similarity instead of difference?

4 min
The Criterion of Haqiqa and Majaz
Definition of haqiqa and majaz with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical criterion that distinguishes literal from metaphorical language in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
A term is literal if denying it is false; metaphorical if denying it is true.

Before we can discuss divine names, we need a clear rule for when a word is used literally (haqiqa) or metaphorically (majaz). The author provides a simple but powerful criterion based on truthfulness of denial, illustrated with the example of the word 'lion'.

Guided Path
1
Read the criterion definition
The passage first states the rule: whatever can be truthfully denied is metaphorical; whatever cannot be truthfully denied is literal.
2
Examine the lion example
The author applies the rule to a real lion (literal) and a brave human (metaphorical). Notice that denying 'lion' of the real lion is false, but denying it of the brave human is true.
3
Reflect on the criterion's power
This criterion is objective, based on truth, not subjective opinion. It will be used later to evaluate divine names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : إن العلم الفاصل بين الحقيقة والمجاز أن ما يصح نفيه ولم يكن نافيه في نفيه كاذبا كان مجازا ، وما لا يصح نفيه وكان نافيه في نفيه كاذبا فهو حقيقة، ألا ترى أن من قال الحيوان الذي له زئير إنه ليس بأسد كان كاذبا، ولو قال للآدمي الشجاع: إنه ليس بأسد بل هو آدمي كان صادقا، ثم إذا قلت في أحد الروايتين أن هذه الأسامي الله تعالى مجازا، وللخلق حقيقة كان مقتضي قولك: إن من قال: إن الله تعالى ليس بموجود كان صادقا، وكذا إذا قال : إنه ليس بحي، ولا قادر، ولا عالم، ولا سميع ولا بصير، ولا خالق، وهذا هو التعطيل الصريح، والدهرية المحضة. وإذا قلت على الرواية الأخرى: إن هذه الأسامي الله تعالى حقيقة وللخلق مجاز كان مقتضى قولك : أن من قال الناشى لا يعلم الله تعالى، ولا رسوله، ولا صحة دين الإسلام، ولا بطلان ما وراء الإسلام من الأديان، ولا يعلم نفسه أنه موجود، ولا أنه مؤمن، ولا أنه في الدنيا ولا يعلم السماء ولا الأرض، ولا شيئا من الحقائق كان صادقا في مقالته، وهذا هو السوفسطائية الكبرى. ثم من العجب أن من زعم أن العبد له علم، والله تعالى لا علم له - على زعمه - لنفيهم الصفات ثم يجعل اسم العالم لمن لا علم له حقيقة ولمن له العلم مجازا، وهذه المكابرة ظاهرة، والحمد لله على العصمة والتوفيق.
Then it is said to him: Indeed the criterion that distinguishes between the literal (haqiqa) and the metaphorical (majaz) is that whatever can be denied while the denier is not a liar in his denial is metaphorical, and whatever cannot be denied and the denier would be a liar in his denial is literal. Do you not see that whoever says of the animal that roars (i.e., the lion) 'It is not a lion' is a liar, but if he says of the brave human 'He is not a lion; rather he is a human,' he is truthful? Then if you say, in one of the two reports, that these names are metaphorical for God Most High and literal for creation, the implication of your statement is that whoever says that God Most High is not existent is truthful, and likewise if he says that He is not living, not powerful, not knowing, not hearing, not seeing, not creator — and this is pure ta'til (negation of divine attributes) and pure materialism. And if you say according to the other report that these names are literal for God Most High and metaphorical for creation, the implication of your statement is that whoever says that a human being does not know God Most High, nor His Messenger, nor the validity of the religion of Islam, nor the falsehood of the religions other than Islam, nor does he know himself as existent, nor that he is a believer, nor that he is in the world, nor does he know the heaven nor the earth, nor any reality whatsoever — he would be truthful in his statement, and this is the great sophistry (sūfastiyā). Then it is astonishing that one who claims that the servant has knowledge while God Most High has no knowledge — in his claim due to their denial of attributes — then makes the name 'knower' literal for one who has no knowledge and metaphorical for one who has knowledge. This is manifest obstinacy. Praise be to God for protection and success.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument distinguishing literal (haqiqa) and metaphorical (majaz) usage of names, applied to names used for both God and creation. The author first establishes a criterion: a term is metaphorical if it can be truthfully denied of the subject; a term is literal if its denial results in falsehood. He illustrates with the word 'asd' (lion): applied to a real lion, denial is false; applied to a brave man, denial is true. He then applies this to divine names (like existent, living, etc.) and shows the disastrous consequences of both extreme positions: if the names are metaphorical for God, it implies complete ta'til (negation of attributes); if literal only for God, it leads to universal skepticism (sophistry). Finally, he criticizes those who deny divine attributes yet stubbornly apply the name 'knower' to God metaphorically and to creation literally, which is contradictory.

Key Terms
حقيقة
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the distinction between haqiqa and majaz is subjective or based on common usage.
Correction: The author shows it is based on objective truth: whether the denial of the term is factually true or false.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what distinguishes a metaphorical (majaz) usage from a literal (haqiqa) usage?
A) The term is used in a more common sense.
B) The term can be truthfully denied of its referent.
C) The term is used by theologians.
D) The term applies exclusively to God.

Key Takeaway
The criterion for haqiqa vs majaz is whether denying the term of the subject is false (literal) or true (metaphorical).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe someone you admire as 'a lion' or 'a saint', you are speaking metaphorically. But when you say 'Allah is All-Hearing', how does the criterion from this passage help you know whether that is literal or metaphorical? Are you willing to accept that Allah truly hears in a way beyond your understanding, or do you reduce His attributes to mere metaphors?
?
Think of a time you described a quality in yourself or another person in absolute terms, like 'I am always honest' or 'She is a genius'. According to the passage, would denying that claim be false or true? What does that reveal about your own tendency toward exaggeration? How carefully do you weigh your words when speaking about yourself and about your Creator?

The Danger of Making Divine Names Metaphorical
Consequences of making divine names metaphorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that divine names are metaphorical for God leads to ta'til (negation of attributes) and pure materialism.

Core Idea
If divine names are metaphorical, denying them of God becomes true, leading to atheism.

The author now applies the criterion of haqiqa and majaz to divine names. He considers a view that says these names (like 'existent', 'living') are metaphorical for God but literal for creation. The logical consequence is disastrous: it permits denying all divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic view
The author mentions 'one of the two reports' that claims divine names are metaphorical for God and literal for creation.
2
Trace the logical implication
If 'existent' is metaphorical for God, then denying that God exists would be truthful. Similarly for other attributes like living, knowing, etc.
3
Recognize the outcome: ta'til
The author calls this 'pure ta'til' and 'pure materialism' — negating all divine attributes, which is equivalent to atheism.

Source Text

The Danger of Making Divine Names Literal for Creation Only
Consequences of making divine names literal for God only, and criticism of attribute-deniers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that divine names are literal for God but metaphorical for creation leads to universal skepticism (sophistry) and is self-contradictory.

Core Idea
If divine names are literal only for God, then denying knowledge and existence of everything becomes truthful.

The author examines the opposite extreme: making divine names literal for God but metaphorical for creation. The implication is that any denial of knowledge or existence in creation becomes true, leading to complete skepticism. He then criticizes the inconsistency of attribute-deniers who claim knowledge for humans but not for God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second view
The other report claims divine names are literal for God and metaphorical for creation.
2
Trace its implication to sophistry
If names like 'knowing' are metaphorical for humans, then denying that humans know anything becomes truthful, leading to skepticism about all knowledge.
3
Examine the contradiction of attribute-deniers
The author points out that those who deny divine attributes but affirm human attributes inconsistently make 'knower' literal for those without knowledge (humans) and metaphorical for God who truly knows.

Mā'iyya Defined as Genus and Its Implication of Tashbīh
The author defines mā'iyya linguistically and logically, proving it implies anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of mā'iyya in Islamic theology and why attributing it to God implies tashbīh (anthropomorphism).

Core Idea
Mā'iyya means genus, and claiming God has a genus is anthropomorphism.

This lesson examines a key theological term: mā'iyya. The author argues that mā'iyya is equivalent to genus (jins), and anything with a genus resembles others of its kind. Applying this to God would contradict divine transcendence, so the passage refutes those who attribute mā'iyya to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's argument come through.
2
Understand the claim
The author states that mā'iyya means mujānasa (genus/homogeneity). Identify this claim and note its source citation.
The passage begins by citing a narration from Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Sufyan ibn Sahban.
3
Examine the evidence
The author provides linguistic evidence (question words: hal, ma, kam, etc.) and logical evidence (logicians' definitions of genus and species). Notice how both point to mā'iyya meaning genus.
The key insight: 'ma' asks about genus, not existence.
4
Reflect on the implication
The author concludes that anything with a genus resembles others of its genus, so attributing mā'iyya to God implies tashbīh. Consider why theologians reject this.
This refutes those who claim God has a mā'iyya unknown to us.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علماء أصحاب هشام بن الحكم عن هشام أن الله تعالى مائية لا يعلمها إلا هو، وحكى عن بعض أرباب المقالات هذا القول عن سفيان بن سحبان وهو من متكلمي أصحاب أبي حنيفة والمائية عبارة عن المجانسة، وهي معنى وراء مطلق الوجود، والدليل على أن المائية عبارة عن المجانسة أن الناس يقولون: ما هذا الشيء أي من أي جنس هو وأهل اللغة يقولون : كلمة هل سؤال عن مطلق الوجود، وما سؤال عن جنسه وكم سؤال عن قدره، وكيف سؤال عن صفته، ومتى سؤال عن زمانه وأين سؤال عن مكانه. وأهل المنطق يقولون : في حد اسم الجنس : إنه الاسم الدال على كثيرين مختلفين بالنوع في جواب ما هو، والنوع هو الاسم الدال على كثيرين مختلفين بالشخص في جواب ما هو، فدل أن المائية عبارة عن الجنس، وكل ذي جنس شبيه بذي جنسه من حيث المجانسة، فكان القول بالمائية قول بالتشبيه.
The scholars from the companions of Hisham ibn al-Hakam narrated from Hisham that God Almighty has a mā'iyya (quiddity) that no one knows but Him. And it was narrated from some of the proponents of doctrines this statement from Sufyan ibn Sahban, who is one of the theologians of the companions of Abu Hanifa. And mā'iyya is an expression for al-mujānasa (homogeneity/genus), and it is a meaning beyond absolute existence. The evidence that mā'iyya is an expression for al-mujānasa is that people say: 'What is this thing?' i.e., 'From which genus is it?' And the linguists say: The word 'hal' is a question about absolute existence, 'ma' is a question about its genus, 'kam' is a question about its quantity, 'kayf' is a question about its quality, 'mata' is a question about its time, and 'ayna' is a question about its place. And the logicians say in the definition of the name of the genus: It is the name that indicates many things differing in species in answer to 'what is it?', and the species is the name that indicates many things differing in individual in answer to 'what is it?'. It is thus shown that mā'iyya is an expression for the genus, and everything that has a genus resembles that which has its genus from the perspective of homogeneity. Therefore, the statement of mā'iyya is a statement of tashbīh (anthropomorphism).
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the term 'mā'iyya' and demonstrates that applying it to God implies tashbīh (anthropomorphism). It begins by citing that some scholars narrated from Hisham ibn al-Hakam that God has a mā'iyya unknown except to Him, and that Sufyan ibn Sahban also held this view. Then it states that mā'iyya means al-mujānasa, i.e., homogeneity or genus, which is a meaning beyond mere existence. To prove this, it provides linguistic evidence: the question word 'ma' asks about the genus of a thing, as opposed to 'hal' which asks about existence, and other question words for quantity, quality, time, place. Then it cites the logicians' definition of genus and species in terms of the answer to 'what is it?'. From this, it concludes that mā'iyya is the genus, and anything with a genus is similar to others of its genus, so attributing mā'iyya to God amounts to tashbīh.

مجانسة
تشبيه
الجنس
النوع
ما هو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mā'iyya is a neutral term about essence.
Correction: The author shows mā'iyya means genus, implying homogeneity, which leads to anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the word 'mā'iyya' mean based on linguistic and logical evidence?
A) Absolute existence
B) Genus or homogeneity
C) Divine essence
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Mā'iyya means genus, and applying it to God leads to anthropomorphism, so it must be rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself trying to define 'what' Allah is, what does that reveal about your heart? Are you seeking to comprehend Him within the categories of His creation? This lesson confronts that tendency seriously — what will you do the next time you feel that urge to 'categorize' your Lord?
?
You now know that mā'iyya implies tashbīh. But have you ever described Allah using words that inadvertently assign Him a genus? Examine your own dua and praise — is there a phrase you use that limits Him? Write it down and repent from it today.

The Definition of al-Ghayrayn Refuted
The author refutes the definition of al-ghayrayn as 'two things'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining al-ghayrayn as 'two things' is invalid and the distinction between relational and self-subsistent names.

Core Idea
Al-ghayrayn are relational, not self-subsistent; defining them as 'things' is a category error.

In this passage, the author critiques the Karramiyya and al-Qalanisi's definition of the term 'al-ghayrayn' (the two others). He argues that this term is fundamentally relational, like 'father' or 'son', and cannot be properly defined by the self-subsistent term 'thing'. Understanding this critique sharpens our grasp of how Islamic theology (kalam) handles definitions and relational concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage without analysis
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
2
Identify the definitions presented
Notice the Karramiyya definition (two things) and al-Qalanisi's tweak (a thing and a thing). See how they differ.
Al-Qalanisi avoids saying 'two things' to escape the implication of 'two others'.
3
Understand the refutation
The author argues that al-ghayrayn is a relational name (ism idafi), while 'thing' is self-subsistent (ism dhati). Relational terms cannot be defined by self-subsistent ones.
Also note the condition that a definition should be synonymous with the defined term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما حد الغيرين؟ ليظهر بمعرفته أنا أثبتنا في الأزل ما هو غير الله. فزعمت الكرامية أن حد الغيرين هو الشيئان وربما قالوا الموجودان، ثم لما كان ذات الله تعالى شيئا وصفته شيئا فكانا غيرين ولهذا قالت الكرامية: إن صفة الله تعالى غير الله وساعدهم أبو العباس أحمد بن إبراهيم القلانسي (1) الرازي من متكلمي أهل الحديث على هذا التحديد، فزعم أن الغيرين هما الشيئان أو الموجودان، إلا أنه مع هذا يساعد غيره من أهل السنة أن صفة الله تعالى لا هو ولا غيره، غير أنه يقول: إني لا أقول ذات الله تعالى وصفته شيئان، ولو قلت ذلك للزمني أن أقول هما غيران بل أقول : ذاته شيء وصفته شيء فهما شيء وشيء ولا أقول هما شيئان - وهذا الحد فاسد، فإن الغيرين من الأسماء الإضافية كالأب والابن والعلو والسفل(۲) وأشباه ذلك. ولهذا يقال لا يطلق هذا الاسم على ذات ما إلا باعتبار وجود آخر، والشيء اسم ذاتي يستحقه المسمى به باعتبار ذاته، ولفظة الحد مع لفظة المحدود بمنزلة الاسمين المترادفين لا تفاوت بينهما البتة فمن جعل أحد اللفظين حدا للآخر فهو قليل المعرفة بحقائق الأسامي وأنواعها وشرائط صحة التحديد. والله الموفق.
What is the definition of al-ghayrayn (the two others)? By knowing it, it becomes clear that we affirm in pre-eternity what is other than God. The Karramiyya claimed that the definition of al-ghayrayn is 'two things' and sometimes they said 'two existents'. Then, since the essence of God Almighty is a thing and His attribute is a thing, they are two others. For this reason the Karramiyya said: The attribute of God Almighty is other than God. And Abu al-Abbas Ahmad ibn Ibrahim al-Qalanisi (1) al-Razi, from the mutakallimun of the people of hadith, supported them in this definition and claimed that al-ghayrayn are the two things or the two existents, except that he along with this supports others from the Ahl al-Sunna that the attribute of God Almighty is neither He nor other than He, but he says: I do not say that the essence of God Almighty and His attribute are two things; if I said that, it would necessitate me to say they are two others, rather I say: His essence is a thing and His attribute is a thing, so they are a thing and a thing, and I do not say they are two things. And this definition is corrupt, for al-ghayrayn are from the relational names (al-asma' al-idafiyya), like father and son, high and low, and the like. For this reason it is said that this name is not applied to any essence except with consideration of another existent. And 'thing' is a self-subsistent name that the named thing deserves by virtue of its essence. And the word 'limit' (al-hadd) with the word 'the limited' (al-mahdud) is like two synonymous names, no difference between them at all. So whoever makes one of the two words a limit for the other is one of little knowledge of the realities of names and their types and the conditions for the validity of definition. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. The author first raises the question: 'What is the definition of al-ghayrayn?' to clarify that we affirm something other than God in pre-eternity. He then presents the Karramiyya definition: al-ghayrayn are 'two things' (al-shay'an) or 'two existents'. They apply this to God's essence and attribute, concluding that the attribute is other than God. Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, a mutakallim from the Ahl al-Hadith, agrees but qualifies: he avoids calling them 'two things' to escape the consequence of saying they are 'two others'; instead he says 'a thing and a thing'. The author then refutes this definition. He argues that al-ghayrayn are relational names (asma' idafiyya), like father and son, high and low—they require a relation to another existent. In contrast, 'thing' is a self-subsistent name (ism dhati) that applies to something by virtue of its own essence. Thus defining a relational term with a self-subsistent term is invalid. Furthermore, a definition (al-hadd) should be synonymous with the defined (al-mahdud); here the two words are not equivalent. The author concludes that one who defines al-ghayrayn as shay'an has little understanding of names and the conditions of definition.

Key Terms
الغيرين (al-ghayrayn)
الأسماء الإضافية (al-asma' al-idafiyya)
الشيء (al-shay')
الحد (al-hadd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-ghayrayn can be defined by the word 'thing' because both are nouns.
Correction: Al-ghayrayn is relational, so it requires reference to another entity, whereas 'thing' stands alone.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the Karramiyya definition of al-ghayrayn as 'two things'?
A) Because 'other' is a relational term, not a self-subsistent one.
B) Because God's essence and attribute are not distinct.
C) Because 'two things' is too broad a definition.
D) Because al-Qalanisi's version is better.

Key Takeaway
The definition of al-ghayrayn as 'two things' fails because 'other' is a relational term, not a self-subsistent one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe your relationship with Allah as 'other' than Him, do you realize that this 'otherness' is merely relational—not a separation in essence? How does this affect the way you understand His closeness to you?
?
The Karramiyya's definition led them to affirm that Allah's attributes are 'other' than Him. Are there any concepts in your faith that you have defined carelessly, accepting words without realizing their deeper implications? What might you need to re-examine today?
?
Al-Qalanisi tried to avoid the error by using different wording, but his definition was still flawed. Have you ever settled for a comfortable half-truth—a phrase that sounds orthodox but avoids confronting the full reality of a matter? Will you commit today to seeking the exact truth, even if it costs you intellectual comfort?
