Refutation of Negative Definitions of al-Ghayr
Refutes negative definition of al-ghayr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining 'al-ghayr' (the other) using negation ('not a thing nor a part') is logically invalid according to Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Relational terms require existence; negation cannot define them.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi refutes a common but flawed definition of 'al-ghayr' that relies on negation. He argues that such definitions are self-contradictory because they attempt to define an existential relational term using non-existence. This lesson unpacks his critique of negative definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic carefully, then its literal translation. Let the author's own words set the context.
Notice the use of 'laysa' (negation) and 'ghayr' (other).
2
Identify the definition being criticized
Find the phrase that states the definition: 'what is not a thing nor a part of it is other than it'. Recognize why it uses negation.
3
Understand the argument about relational terms
The author explains that 'al-ghayr' is a relational term (ism iḍāfī) requiring existence of two. Negation implies non-existence. So using negation to define an existential term is contradictory.
The example with Zayd illustrates this confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما داخلا تحت اسم صاحبه فهما متغايران وقد مر إبطال هذا الثاني، على أن الغير من الأسماء تقدير هذا الكلام : أن ما ليس بشيء ولا بعضه فهو غيره، وهذا فاسد لأنه مقسم، ولأن تحديد المتغايرين بأن ليس أحدهما صاحبه باطل، لأن الغير من الإضافية يقتضي وجود اثنين، وكلمة ليس كلمة نفي وهي مقتضية للعدم، ومن فسر ما يقتضي وجودا بما يقتضي عدما كان خارجا عن قضية العقول، فكيف بمن يفسر ما يقتضي وجودين بما يقتضي عدما والدليل عليه أن الحد مع المحدود بمنزلة اسمين مترادفين تتحد الفائدة بهما ولا يتغاير ولا يتفاوت، ومن قال: ليس زيد في الدار ثم قال: غير زيد في الدار لا يفهم من أحدهما ما يفهم من الآخر، فدل أن هذا فاسد وكذا لو قيل ما ليس ببعض الشيء فهو غيره باطل، لأن كل الشيء ليس ببعضه ذلك ليس بغيره، لأن الشيء لا يغاير نفسه فلم يمكن أن يجعل كل لفظ على ومع حياله حدا، ولا يمكن جعل مجموعهما حدا لما مر من إبطال الحدود المقسمة. ويبطل أيضا أن يكون حد الغيرين أنهما اثنان، لأن الغيرين لو كانا اثنين لصار الغير اثنا، والاثن ليس بمستعمل والغير مستعمل، ولأن الاثن لو كان مستعملا لكان عبارة عن الواحد، والواحد لا يصلح أن يكون حداً للغير، لأن الواحد ليس من الأسماء الإضافية وذكره لا يقتضي إلا توحد الذات، والغير من الأسماء الإضافية لا يطلق على ذات ما إلا باعتبار غيره بخلاف اسم الواحد فجعل أحدهما حدا للآخر باطل. والله الموفق.
One of them falling under the name of its companion, so they are distinct, and the invalidation of this second [definition] has already been mentioned. Moreover, [regarding] 'the other' from among the names: the estimation of this statement is that what is not a thing nor a part of it is other than it, and this is invalid because it is a disjunctive [definition], and because the definition of two distinct things as that one is not the companion of the other is false, for 'the other' from relational terms necessitates the existence of two, and the word 'not' is a word of negation and implies non-existence. Whoever interprets what necessitates existence by what necessitates non-existence is outside the judgment of intellects. How much more so is the one who interprets what necessitates two existences by what necessitates non-existence! The proof of this is that the definition and the defined are like two synonymous terms; the utility is the same for both, and they neither differ nor vary. He who says: 'Zayd is not in the house' then says: 'Other than Zayd is in the house'—one does not understand from the one what is understood from the other. This shows that this is invalid. Likewise, if it is said: 'What is not a part of a thing is other than it'—this is false, because the whole of a thing is not a part of it; that is not other than it, because a thing does not differ from itself. So it is not possible to make each word individually a definition, nor to make their combination a definition due to what has been mentioned regarding the invalidation of disjunctive definitions. It is also invalid that the definition of 'the two others' (al-ghayrayn) is that they are two, because if 'the two others' were two, then 'the other' (al-ghayr) would become 'two' (ithnā), and 'two' (al-ithn) is not in use while 'the other' is in use. And if 'two' (al-ithn) were in use, it would be an expression for the one, and the one is not suitable to be a definition for 'the other', because the one is not among the relational names, and its mention only implies the oneness of the essence, while 'the other' is among the relational names and is not applied to any essence except in consideration of something other than it, unlike the name 'the one'. So making one of them a definition for the other is false. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponents' definition of 'al-ghayr' (the other). The author argues against several defective definitions. First, he criticizes the definition that 'what is not a thing nor a part of it is other than it', calling it invalid because it is a disjunctive definition (muqassam) and because defining two distinct things negatively is false. He explains that 'al-ghayr' is a relational term (ism iḍāfī) that implies the existence of two entities, whereas the word 'not' (laysa) is a negation and implies non-existence. Interpreting an existential term by a negative one is irrational. He illustrates with an example: 'Zayd is not in the house' and 'other than Zayd is in the house' do not convey the same meaning, proving the definition is invalid. Then he refutes the definition that 'what is not a part of a thing is other than it', showing that the whole is not a part of itself, but it is not other than itself, so this definition fails. Finally, he refutes the definition that 'al-ghayrayn' (the two others) means 'two' (ithnān), arguing that 'al-ghayr' is not used for the number two, and 'al-ithn' (the two) would imply one if used, making it unsuitable. The passage ends with 'And God is the grantor of success'.

Key Terms
الغير
الإضافية
المقسم
الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can define 'other' purely negatively without invoking existence.
Correction: The author shows that 'other' is a relational term that necessarily implies existence of two distinct entities; negation cannot capture this.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the definition 'what is not a thing nor a part of it is other than it' invalid?
A) Because it is a disjunctive definition
B) Because it uses negation to define an existential term
C) Because it leads to an infinite regress
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
A relational term like 'al-ghayr' cannot be defined by negation, as it inherently requires existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe your faith to others, do you often rely on what Allah is not (e.g., not limited, not a body) rather than positive affirmations of His attributes? How does this lesson expose a weakness in that approach, and what will you do to change it?
?
Think of a time you used a negative definition to simplify a complex concept, only to realize later that it caused confusion. What did this lesson teach you about the necessity of positive, existential definitions, especially in matters of creed?
?
How often do you define your relationship with Allah by what you have not done (sins avoided) rather than by acts of worship and love? Reflect on how a purely negative definition falls short—what one concrete step can you take this week to build a positive definition of your faith?

Refutation of Partitive and Numerical Definitions of al-Ghayr
Refutes partitive and numerical definitions of al-ghayr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Nasafi's refutation of the definitions that rely on 'part' or equate 'al-ghayrayn' with the number two.

Core Idea
Defining 'other' by 'two' or 'part' fails logical and linguistic analysis.

After refuting negative definitions, Imam al-Nasafi turns to two other flawed definitions: one that says 'what is not a part of a thing is other than it' and another that defines 'al-ghayrayn' as 'two'. He shows through linguistic and logical reasoning why these are invalid. This lesson covers both refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic and its literal translation. Notice how the author connects this section to the previous refutation.
The passage begins with 'وكذا' (likewise), indicating a continuation.
2
Examine the 'part' definition and its refutation
The definition says 'what is not a part of a thing is other than it'. The author argues it is false because the whole is not a part of itself, but the whole is not other than itself. So the definition fails for self-identity.
3
Examine the numerical definition
The last part refutes the definition of 'al-ghayrayn' (the two others) as 'two' (ithnān). The author points out that 'al-ghayr' is not used for the number two, and if it were, it would imply one, which is not a relational term.
This shows the importance of linguistic usage in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما داخلا تحت اسم صاحبه فهما متغايران وقد مر إبطال هذا الثاني، على أن الغير من الأسماء تقدير هذا الكلام : أن ما ليس بشيء ولا بعضه فهو غيره، وهذا فاسد لأنه مقسم، ولأن تحديد المتغايرين بأن ليس أحدهما صاحبه باطل، لأن الغير من الإضافية يقتضي وجود اثنين، وكلمة ليس كلمة نفي وهي مقتضية للعدم، ومن فسر ما يقتضي وجودا بما يقتضي عدما كان خارجا عن قضية العقول، فكيف بمن يفسر ما يقتضي وجودين بما يقتضي عدما والدليل عليه أن الحد مع المحدود بمنزلة اسمين مترادفين تتحد الفائدة بهما ولا يتغاير ولا يتفاوت، ومن قال: ليس زيد في الدار ثم قال: غير زيد في الدار لا يفهم من أحدهما ما يفهم من الآخر، فدل أن هذا فاسد وكذا لو قيل ما ليس ببعض الشيء فهو غيره باطل، لأن كل الشيء ليس ببعضه ذلك ليس بغيره، لأن الشيء لا يغاير نفسه فلم يمكن أن يجعل كل لفظ على ومع حياله حدا، ولا يمكن جعل مجموعهما حدا لما مر من إبطال الحدود المقسمة. ويبطل أيضا أن يكون حد الغيرين أنهما اثنان، لأن الغيرين لو كانا اثنين لصار الغير اثنا، والاثن ليس بمستعمل والغير مستعمل، ولأن الاثن لو كان مستعملا لكان عبارة عن الواحد، والواحد لا يصلح أن يكون حداً للغير، لأن الواحد ليس من الأسماء الإضافية وذكره لا يقتضي إلا توحد الذات، والغير من الأسماء الإضافية لا يطلق على ذات ما إلا باعتبار غيره بخلاف اسم الواحد فجعل أحدهما حدا للآخر باطل. والله الموفق.
One of them falling under the name of its companion, so they are distinct, and the invalidation of this second [definition] has already been mentioned. Moreover, [regarding] 'the other' from among the names: the estimation of this statement is that what is not a thing nor a part of it is other than it, and this is invalid because it is a disjunctive [definition], and because the definition of two distinct things as that one is not the companion of the other is false, for 'the other' from relational terms necessitates the existence of two, and the word 'not' is a word of negation and implies non-existence. Whoever interprets what necessitates existence by what necessitates non-existence is outside the judgment of intellects. How much more so is the one who interprets what necessitates two existences by what necessitates non-existence! The proof of this is that the definition and the defined are like two synonymous terms; the utility is the same for both, and they neither differ nor vary. He who says: 'Zayd is not in the house' then says: 'Other than Zayd is in the house'—one does not understand from the one what is understood from the other. This shows that this is invalid. Likewise, if it is said: 'What is not a part of a thing is other than it'—this is false, because the whole of a thing is not a part of it; that is not other than it, because a thing does not differ from itself. So it is not possible to make each word individually a definition, nor to make their combination a definition due to what has been mentioned regarding the invalidation of disjunctive definitions. It is also invalid that the definition of 'the two others' (al-ghayrayn) is that they are two, because if 'the two others' were two, then 'the other' (al-ghayr) would become 'two' (ithnā), and 'two' (al-ithn) is not in use while 'the other' is in use. And if 'two' (al-ithn) were in use, it would be an expression for the one, and the one is not suitable to be a definition for 'the other', because the one is not among the relational names, and its mention only implies the oneness of the essence, while 'the other' is among the relational names and is not applied to any essence except in consideration of something other than it, unlike the name 'the one'. So making one of them a definition for the other is false. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponents' definition of 'al-ghayr' (the other). The author argues against several defective definitions. First, he criticizes the definition that 'what is not a thing nor a part of it is other than it', calling it invalid because it is a disjunctive definition (muqassam) and because defining two distinct things negatively is false. He explains that 'al-ghayr' is a relational term (ism iḍāfī) that implies the existence of two entities, whereas the word 'not' (laysa) is a negation and implies non-existence. Interpreting an existential term by a negative one is irrational. He illustrates with an example: 'Zayd is not in the house' and 'other than Zayd is in the house' do not convey the same meaning, proving the definition is invalid. Then he refutes the definition that 'what is not a part of a thing is other than it', showing that the whole is not a part of itself, but it is not other than itself, so this definition fails. Finally, he refutes the definition that 'al-ghayrayn' (the two others) means 'two' (ithnān), arguing that 'al-ghayr' is not used for the number two, and 'al-ithn' (the two) would imply one if used, making it unsuitable. The passage ends with 'And God is the grantor of success'.

المغايرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can define 'al-ghayrayn' simply as the number two.
Correction: The author shows that 'al-ghayr' is a relational term and not equivalent to 'two', which is a non-relational number; also 'al-ithn' is not used in the same way.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the definition 'what is not a part of a thing is other than it'?
A) Because the whole is not a part of itself yet is not other than itself
B) Because it is a disjunctive definition
C) Because it is circular
D) Because it uses negation

Key Takeaway
Both the partitive and numerical definitions of 'al-ghayr' collapse under logical and linguistic scrutiny, highlighting the need for precise definitions in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do I ever reduce people to 'others' by defining them purely by what they are not (e.g., not part of my group) rather than seeing them as relational beings? How can I practice a more relational understanding of 'otherness' starting today?
?
The author shows that 'other' is inherently relative. When I label something as 'other' in my life—a person, an idea, a challenge—do I forget that my own identity is also defined in relation to it? What would it mean to embrace that interdependence rather than separation?
?
Both false definitions treated 'other' as something absolute (by part or number). Are there areas in my faith where I have reduced complex realities (like God's attributes, or my relationship with Him) to simplistic categories? What would it cost me to abandon those shortcuts and embrace the relational depth the lesson points to?

The Maturidi Definition of al-Ghayrayn and Its Proof
Defines al-ghayrayn and proves it by induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of al-ghayrayn (two distinct entities) and how they prove it through inductive elimination of alternatives.

Core Idea
Two things are distinct if one can exist without the other, proven by eliminating all other possibilities.

This passage presents a foundational Maturidi concept: what does it mean for two things to be distinct? The author defines 'al-ghayrayn' precisely and then provides a rigorous proof by induction, systematically ruling out alternative definitions. By following the reasoning, you will see how Maturidi scholars built their theology on clear logical foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the exact wording of the definition: two existents such that the existence of one is valid with the non-existence of the other.
This definition is minimal and avoids any reference to attributes or accidents.
2
Follow the inductive elimination
The author inductively examines all possible ways to define distinction: through accidents, bodies, or a special attribute (ma'nā). He shows that each alternative fails.
Pay attention to how each alternative is refuted with examples from theology (God and the world) and physics (bodies and accidents).
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
The author argues that if distinction were a separate meaning (attribute), it would itself be distinct from other things, leading to an infinite regress of distinctions.
This is a classic argument in kalam against reifying relations.
4
Reflect on the method
Note that the proof uses induction (istiqrā') - examining all possibilities and eliminating those that lead to contradiction. Only the given definition remains.
The text cuts off before the opponent's objection, but the method is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نشتغل ببيان ذلك على طريق التبرع فنقول – وبالله التوفيق: حد الغيرين عند أصحابنا رحمهم الله أنهما الموجودان اللذان يصح وجود أحدهما مع عدم الآخر. ودليل صحة هذا الحد أنا استقرأنا الأوصاف فعلمنا أن شيئا مما ذكره الخصوم لا يصلح أن يكون حدا لما ذكرنا، وعلمنا أيضا أنهما ما كانا غيرين لأنهما عرضان لثبوت المغايرة بين الباري جل وعلا وبين العالم ولثبوت المغايرة بين الأجسام والأعراض أيضا، ولا لأنهما جسمان لثبوت التغاير بين الأعراض وثبوتها بين الأجسام والأعراض ولثبوت المغايرة بين الأجسام وبين الأجسام والأعراض أيضا، وما كانا غيرين لقيام مغايرة بينهما لجريان التغاير بين الأعراض، وإن كان يستحيل قيام المعنى بها، ولأن المغايرة لو كانت معنى لكانت هي أيضا غير الأجسام وما وراءها من الأعراض فتقوم بها مغايرة أخرى ثم كذلك إلى ما لا نهاية له. وإذا كان كذلك لم يبق له إلا ما ذكرنا، وإذا كان حد المغايرة ينقسم إلى هذه الأقسام وكلها ممتنعة لما بينا من الدلائل ولا امتناع فيما قلنا بعين الصحة على ما هو الأصل في الاستقراء. واعتراض الخصوم على هذا أن التغاير بين الجواهر والأعراض ثابت، ولا يتصور وجود
Then we occupy ourselves with explaining that by way of gratuitousness, and we say—and with God is success: The definition of 'the two others' (al-ghayrayn) according to our associates (aṣḥābinā), may God have mercy on them, is that they are the two existents such that the existence of one of them is valid with the non-existence of the other. The proof of the validity of this definition is that we inductively examined the descriptions (istaqra'nā al-awṣāf) and knew that nothing of what the opponents mentioned is suitable to be a definition for what we have mentioned, and we also knew that they cannot be two others because they are two accidents due to the establishment of distinction (al-mughāyara) between the Creator, exalted and glorious, and between the world, and due to the establishment of distinction between bodies and accidents as well; nor because they are two bodies due to the establishment of distinction between accidents and their establishment between bodies and accidents, and due to the establishment of distinction between bodies and between bodies and accidents as well; and they are not two others due to the establishment of a distinction between them because of the running of distinction among accidents, even if it is impossible for the meaning (al-ma'nā) to subsist in them, and because if distinction were a meaning, then it itself would also be other than bodies and what is beyond them of accidents, so there would subsist in it another distinction, and then so on to infinity. If it is so, nothing remains except what we have mentioned. And if the definition of distinction is divided into these divisions, and all of them are impossible due to the proofs we have shown, and there is no impossibility in what we have said—indeed it is correct—according to what is the principle in induction (al-istiqrā'). And the objection of the opponents to this is that the distinction between substances and accidents is established, and existence is not conceivable...
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi definition of 'al-ghayrayn' (the two distinct entities) and provides a proof via induction (istiqrā'). The author states that the definition according to the Maturidi scholars (aṣḥābinā) is: two existents such that the existence of one is valid with the non-existence of the other. The proof proceeds by inductively examining all possible ways of characterizing distinction. The author claims that they have examined all descriptions and found that none of the opponents' proposed definitions are valid. They then systematically eliminate possibilities: it cannot be that the two are accidents (because distinction holds between the Creator and the world, and between bodies and accidents, so accidents cannot be the sole basis); it cannot be that they are bodies (because distinction holds between accidents themselves and between accidents and bodies); and it cannot be that distinction is a meaning that subsists in them (because that would lead to infinite regress). After eliminating all alternatives, the only remaining possibility is the definition given. The author then notes that the opponents may object that distinction between substances and accidents is established and that existence is not conceivable... but the text cuts off here. The commentary explains the inductive elimination process and the logical reasoning behind it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that distinction requires a separate attribute (ma'nā) that inheres in the two things.
Correction: The passage argues that if distinction were a separate attribute, it would itself be distinct from other things, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, distinction is not a real attribute but simply the fact that one can exist without the other.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi definition of distinction (al-ghayrayn) is minimal: two things are distinct if one can exist without the other, proven by eliminating all alternative definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The definition says two things are distinct if one can exist without the other. Is your faith in Allah distinct from your attachment to worldly comforts in this way? If everything else were taken from you, would your faith still remain? Search your heart: what would actually survive the loss of all your worldly anchors?
?
The author proves by elimination: what cannot be true leaves only what must be true. What false foundations have you been building your life upon—wealth, status, relationships—that you have never honestly eliminated? If you removed all of them, what would be left that you are certain of before Allah?
?
If distinction were a separate attribute, it would lead to infinite regress. In your spiritual life, do you treat your connection to Allah as something 'added on' to your existing identity rather than the very fact that makes you distinct from being lost? What would change if you saw it as foundational rather than additional?

Defining al-Ghayrayn: Two Existents with Existential Independence
The author defines al-ghayrayn and proves the definition by induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of two distinct things (al-ghayrayn) and the inductive proof that establishes this definition as the only valid one.

Core Idea
Two things are distinct if one can exist without the other—existential independence, not mere conceptual difference.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi establishes the technical definition of 'two distinct things' (al-ghayrayn) as used in Maturidi theology. He does not simply state the definition; he proves it by systematically eliminating every alternative proposal through logical induction. This method demonstrates the rigorous foundation of kalam terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
The passage uses technical terms like al-ghayrayn, al-mawjudan, al-istiqra'.
2
Identify the definition of al-ghayrayn
Locate the exact definition given by the author. Notice it involves two existents and the condition of one existing without the other.
This is the positive definition he advocates.
3
Understand the inductive method
The author examines alternative definitions (being accidents, bodies, etc.) and shows each fails. Follow his reasoning for each rejection.
Induction (istiqra') here means surveying all possibilities.
4
Spot the infinite regress argument
One key argument is that if otherness were a real attribute, it would require another otherness ad infinitum. See how this refutes the attribute view.
This is a classic kalam argument against eternal regress.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that only his definition remains. Ask yourself why this definition is foundational for discussing distinctions in theology.
This definition is used in proofs for the existence of God and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد الغيرين عند أصحابنا أنهما الموجودان اللذان يصح [؟رحمهم؟] أحدهما مع عدم الآخر . ودليل صحة هذا الحد أنا استقرأنا الأوصاف فعلمنا أن شيئا مما ذكره الخصوم لا يصلح أن يكون حدا لما ذكرنا، وعلمنا أيضا أنهما ما كانا غيرين لأنهما عرضان لثبوت المغايرة بين الباري جل وعلا وبين العالم (ولثبوت المغايرة بين الأجسام والأعراض أيضا) (۲) ولا لأنهما جسمان لثبوت التغاير بين الأعراض وثبوتها بين الأجسام والأعراض ولثبوت المغايرة بين الأجسام وبين الأجسام والأعراض أيضا (۳) وما كانا غيرين لقيام مغايرة بينهما لجريان التغاير بين الأعراض، وإن كان يستحيل قيام المعنى بها، ولأن المغايرة لو كانت معنى لكانت هي أيضا (٤) غير الأجسام وما وراءها من الأعراض فتقوم بها مغايرة أخرى ثم كذلك إلى ما لا نهاية له. وإذا كان كذلك لم يبق له إلا ما ذكرنا. وإذا كان حد المغايرة ينقسم إلى هذه الأقسام وكلها ممتنعة لما بينا من الدلائل ولا امتناع فيما قلنا بعين الصحة على ما هو الأصل في الاستقراء.
The definition of the two distinct things (al-ghayrayn) according to our companions is that they are the two existents of which it is valid [؟] that one of them exists without the other. And the proof of the validity of this definition is that we examined the properties by induction (istiqra') and we knew that nothing of what the opponents mentioned is suitable to be a definition of what we mentioned. And we also knew that they are not two distinct things because they are two accidents due to the establishment of otherness between the Exalted Creator and the world, and due to the establishment of otherness between bodies and accidents also; nor because they are two bodies due to the establishment of otherness between accidents and its establishment between bodies and accidents, and due to the establishment of otherness between bodies and between bodies and accidents also; and they are not two distinct things because of the subsistence of otherness between them due to the occurrence of otherness between accidents, even if the subsistence of the attribute in them is impossible; and because if otherness were an attribute, it would itself also be distinct from bodies and what is beyond them of accidents, so another otherness would subsist in it, and then likewise ad infinitum. And if it is so, nothing remains for us except what we mentioned. And if the definition of otherness is divided into these divisions, and all of them are impossible because of the proofs we have shown, and there is no impossibility in what we said, but rather it is true based on what is the principle in induction.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-ghayrayn' (two distinct things) according to the Maturidi school and provides a proof by induction. The author first states the definition: two distinct things are two existents such that it is possible for one to exist without the other. He then proves this definition by a process of inductive examination of all possible alternative definitions. He lists several possibilities that opponents might suggest: that they are distinct because they are accidents, or because they are bodies, or because they subsist in something, etc. He refutes each by showing that it either leads to an infinite regress or fails to cover all cases. For example, if otherness were a real attribute, it would itself require another otherness, leading to an infinite chain. Since all alternative definitions are impossible, the only valid definition is the one he gave. The induction establishes that this definition is correct.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that two things are distinct merely because they have different attributes or concepts.
Correction: The passage defines distinctness as existential independence: one can exist without the other. Mere conceptual difference is insufficient.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi definition of two distinct things is based on the possibility of one existing without the other, proven by eliminating all other logical possibilities through induction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that otherness cannot be a real attribute because it would lead to infinite regress. In your own life, are there attributes you claim for yourself (like intelligence or goodness) that you think are inherent, but actually they come from Allah? How can you realign your self-perception with the truth that nothing exists independently of Him?
?
The inductive proof eliminates all false definitions of otherness. Think of a belief you hold about your own identity or relationship with others that you have not critically examined. Is it based on your existential dependence on Allah or on a false sense of autonomy? How will you test that belief against the definition given in this lesson?

Speech as Meaning, Not Instrument
Nasafi defines kalam as a meaning and refutes instrument-dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Nasafi defines speech (kalam) as a meaning opposed to silence, not as an action dependent on an instrument, and why this distinction is crucial for understanding divine attributes.

Core Idea
Speech is a meaning, not a sound produced by an instrument.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabirat al-Adilla tackles a fundamental question: is divine speech like human speech, dependent on instruments? Nasafi argues that speech, silence, and muteness are meanings (ma'ani) rather than physical actions. This has profound implications for how we understand God's attributes like hearing and seeing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation without overanalysis. Get a feel for Nasafi's argumentative style.
Notice the repetition of 'بل لأنه معنى' (but because it is a meaning).
2
Identify the definitions
Find how Nasafi defines 'kalam' (speech), 'khars' (muteness), and 'sukut' (silence). Each is a meaning, not an instrumental action.
Compare his positive definitions with the negative ones he rejects.
3
Trace the analogy for hearing and seeing
Nasafi extends the argument to the attributes of seeing (basar) and hearing (sam'). Understand how the same logic applies: if they required instruments, God would not have them.
Ask yourself: what is the 'instrument' for hearing and seeing in humans?
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that God, being free of instruments, possesses attributes as meanings. Consider how this refutes anthropomorphism.
This is a key point in Maturidi kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة الآلة دل أن الكلام ما كان كلاما لأنه استعمال الآلة بالنطق بل لأنه معنى ينافي السكوت والآفة، فكذا الخرس ما كان خرسا لأنه فساد في آلة النطق، بل لأنه آفة منافية للكلام، والسكوت ما كان سكوتا لأنه ترك استعمال آلة الكلام، بل لأنه معنى ينافي للكلام ممن يصح أن يوصف بالكلام، فإذا كان الكلام بالآلة كان السكوت بالآلة والآلة من القرائن اللازمة وإن كان الكلام لا بالآلة فكذا كان السكوت لا بالآلة وكذا بم ينفصل ممن يقول البصر صحة الآلة وكذا السمع فمن الآلة كان سميعا بصيرا، ومن لا فلا والله تعالى يستحيل عليه الآلة صحيح فيستحيل أن يكون سميعا بصيرا، ويعتمد في ذلك مطلق الوجود كما فعلت أنت؟ فبأي جواب أجبت فهو جواي لك.
The impossibility of the instrument indicates that speech is not speech because it is use of the instrument by utterance, but because it is a meaning that is opposed to silence and deficiency. Likewise, muteness is not muteness because it is a defect in the instrument of utterance, but because it is a deficiency opposite to speech. And silence is not silence because it is abandonment of the use of the instrument of speech, but because it is a meaning opposite to speech from one who can be described as speaking. So if speech is by the instrument, then silence is by the instrument, and the instrument is among the necessary concomitants. And if speech is not by the instrument, then likewise silence is not by the instrument. And similarly, how can one differentiate from one who says that seeing is soundness of the instrument, and likewise hearing? So whoever has the instrument is seeing and hearing, and whoever does not, then not. And God Almighty, it is impossible for Him to have an instrument, correct? Thus it is impossible for Him to be seeing and hearing. And you rely on absolute existence as you did? So whatever answer you give, it is my answer to you.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage refutes the notion that divine speech requires an instrument. It defines speech, muteness, and silence as meanings (ma'ani) rather than physical actions. The argument uses analogy to hearing and seeing to show that if attributes were instrument-dependent, God would lack them. But God is free of instruments.

Key Terms

آلة
سكوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speech must be a sound produced by an instrument (vocal cords, tongue).
Correction: Nasafi argues that speech is a meaning (ma'na) opposed to silence, not dependent on any instrument. This allows God to be described as speaking without physical limitation.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the nature of speech (kalam) that distinguishes it from being dependent on an instrument?
A) Speech is a sound produced by the tongue.
B) Speech is a meaning opposed to silence.
C) Speech is an action of the speaker.
D) Speech is the use of the vocal cords.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defines speech as a meaning opposed to silence, not dependent on an instrument, and argues that God's attributes are similarly free from instruments.

?

?
If you have ever doubted that God hears you because you think of hearing needing ears, how does Nasafi's argument reshape your confidence in God's hearing? Are you willing to abandon all anthropomorphic notions in your worship?
?
This lesson defines speech as a meaning opposed to silence. In your personal relationship with God, do you treat the Qur'an as a mere sound or as a meaning that demands a response? What needs to change today?

Proof That Speech Is Inner Meaning, Not Letters
The author cites poetry and rational argument to prove that speech is inner meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and textual evidence that speech is an inner meaning distinct from the letters and sounds that manifest it.

Core Idea
Speech is the inner meaning, not the outer letters.

This lesson delves into a key argument from Imam al-Nasafi, drawing on both poetic evidence and rational reasoning. The author shows that Arab usage and logical analysis both point to speech as an attribute of the heart, not of the tongue. You'll see how the mind distinguishes meaningful utterance from mere noise.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from poetic quote to rational argument to refutation.
Pay attention to the key term 'دليل' (guide).
2
Examine the poetic evidence from al-Akhtal
The author cites a line of poetry that explicitly states speech is from the heart. Identify how this supports the claim.
The poet is a Christian Arab, showing this understanding was common.
3
Understand the rational argument
The author argues that sounds and letters only become speech when they guide to meanings in the soul. If they are not guides, they are nonsense. This proves letters are not speech in themselves.
Focus on the phrase 'ما جعل منها دليلا'.
4
Consider the objection and its refutation
The author anticipates an objection that the argument might be about knowledge, not speech. He refutes by noting that will is also in the heart but is not knowledge.
This shows the heart can have multiple attributes.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the opponents' premise is false, so their conclusion is false. Reflect on why this matters for the definition of kalam.
This is a foundational argument in Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الأخطل : إن الكلام من الفؤاد وإنما جعل اللسان على الفؤاد دليلا وفي رواية: إن الكلام لفي الفؤاد. ونظائر هذا في كلام العرب كثير. وفي القدر الذي أوردنا كفاية لمن ينصف ولا يكابر، والمعقول يدل على ذلك وهو أن الأصوات والحروف الجارية على اللسان ما جعل منها دليلا على المعاني الثابتة في النفوس يعد كلاما، وما لم يجعل منها دليلا عليها لم يعد كلاما، وكل عبارة أو لفظ حصل في محل لم يكن دليلا على ما في الضمير لم يتعلق به حكم وعد هذيانا في نفسه، فدل أنها وضعت لإظهار الكلام لا أن تكون هي معتبرة لأنفسها. ولا يقال إنما يذكرونه صفة العلم لا صفة الكلام لما مر بالدلائل أنه كلام، وليس من ضرورة كونه حاصلا في محل العلم وهو القلب في الشاهد أن يكون علما، ألا ترى أن الإرادة في الشاهد صفة القلب وليس بعلم. وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة يعرف أنما ذكرنا من ادعائهم كون الكلام في الشاهد من جنس الحروف مقدمة كاذبة مجحودة، فكانت النتيجة الخارجة عنها كاذبة. والله الموفق.
And the saying of al-Akhtal: 'Indeed speech is from the heart, and the tongue was only made a guide over the heart.' And in a narration: 'Indeed speech is in the heart.' And parallels of this in Arab speech are many. And in what we have mentioned there is sufficiency for one who is fair and does not dispute stubbornly. And the intellect indicates that: that the sounds and letters that flow upon the tongue, that which is made a guide to the meanings established in the souls is counted as speech, and that which is not made a guide to them is not counted as speech. And every expression or utterance that occurs in a place and is not a guide to what is in the conscience, no ruling attaches to it and it is counted as nonsense in itself. So this indicates that they (letters) are placed for manifesting speech, not that they are considered in themselves. And it cannot be said that what they mention is the attribute of knowledge, not the attribute of speech, because it has passed with proofs that it is speech, and it is not necessary that because it occurs in the locus of knowledge, which is the heart in the witnessed world, that it should be knowledge. Do you not see that will in the witnessed world is an attribute of the heart and is not knowledge? And by examining this summary, it is known that what we mentioned of their claim that speech in the witnessed world is of the genus of letters is a false and denied premise, so the conclusion resulting from it is false. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof combined with a refutation of the opponents' claim that speech consists of letters and sounds. The author begins by citing a line of poetry from al-Akhtal, in which the poet explicitly states that speech comes from the heart and the tongue is merely a guide to it. This shows that the Arabs themselves understood speech as inner meaning. He then presents an intellectual argument: sounds and letters become speech only when they serve as guides to meanings in the soul; otherwise they are meaningless. Therefore, letters are not speech in themselves but are created to manifest it. The author then anticipates an objection—that the argument might be about knowledge, not speech—by noting that the heart is the locus of both speech and will, but will is not knowledge, so speech is also distinct. He concludes that the opponents' premise that speech in the witnessed world is of the genus of letters is false, making their conclusion false as well.

Key Terms
كلام
فؤاد
دليل
حروف
هذيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poet's quote is merely poetic license and not evidence for theology.
Correction: The author uses it as evidence of common Arabic usage, showing that Arabs understood speech as inner meaning, not just letters.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what makes sounds and letters count as speech?
A) Being articulated clearly by the tongue
B) Being a guide to meanings in the soul
C) Being composed of letters with parts
D) Being written in a book

Key Takeaway
Speech is an inner meaning; letters and sounds are merely its external guides.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Speech Requires Inner Meaning
Author defines speech as requiring inner meaning and refutes Mu'tazilites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why valid speech requires an inner meaning subsisting in the soul, and how the Mu'tazilite view undermines divine speech and revelation.

Core Idea
Speech is not mere words; it requires inner meaning.

The author establishes a fundamental criterion for speech: it must convey an inner meaning. He argues that without this, utterances are not true speech. He then applies this to the divine attribute of speech, refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that God's speech consists only of created letters and sounds, showing that such a view destroys the basis of all revealed law.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow before you interpret.
Notice the dense style and the logical progression.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the opening statement: mere expression is not valid unless it indicates a meaning in the soul. This is the criterion for valid speech.
3
Understand the analogy
The author gives an analogy from the witnessed world: if someone utters commands, prohibitions, or information without any inner meaning, those utterances are not considered actual speech. Grasp how this analogy supports the criterion.
This shows that meaning is essential for speech to carry obligations.
4
See the refutation's consequence
The author applies the criterion to the Mu'tazilite view. If their view were correct, divine speech would not be speech at all, nullifying all revealed laws. Note the strong conclusion.
The author warns that holding this view removes one from religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العبارة فقط لا تكون معتبرة إذا لم تكن دالة على معنى قائم بالنفس، وهم جعلوها معتبرة وهو خلاف المعقول . ألا ترى أن في الشاهد لو أجرى ذات صفة الأمر والنهي والخبر على اللسان وثبت لنا بالدليل الضروري أن ليس في نفس المجري معنى الأمر والنهي والخبر، وليست هذه الألفاظ بأداء لتلك المعاني ولا بعبارة عنها. أن ذلك لم يعد كلاما ولا أمرا ولا نهيا ولا خبرا، ولا يعتقد وجوب ما يتناوله لفظة الأمر ولا حرمة ما تتناوله لفظة النهي ولا ثبوت ما تتناوله لفظة الخبر، فلو كان الأمر على ما زعمت المعتزلة، في استحالة قيام الكلام بالذات، ووجوب الحروف والأصوات في محل من غير أن يكون شيء منها دليلا على صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى وإيجاب حظرهما صفتيه، لم تكن هذه الحروف البتة كلاما، ولا وجب بها شيء ولا حظر بها فعل وتعطلت الشرائع بأسرها فالقائل به منسلخ عن الدين، بل عن الأديان السماوية ويلتحق بالدهرية والمعطلة والحمد لله الذي عصمنا عن الوقوع في مثله بمنه وفضله.
Mere expression is not considered valid if it is not indicative of a meaning subsisting in the soul, and they considered it valid, which is contrary to reason. Do you not see that in the witnessed world, if one were to utter expressions of command, prohibition, and information on the tongue, and it is established for us by necessary proof that there is no meaning of command, prohibition, and information in the soul of the speaker, and these words are not conveyors of those meanings nor expressions of them, then that is not counted as speech, nor command, nor prohibition, nor information, nor is the obligation of what the expression of command entails believed, nor the prohibition of what the expression of prohibition entails, nor the affirmation of what the expression of information entails. If the matter were as the Mu'tazila claim, regarding the impossibility of speech subsisting in the essence, and the necessity of letters and sounds in a locus, without any of them being evidence for an attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah, and imposing [the obligation of] their prohibition [being] His two attributes, then these letters would never be speech, nor would any obligation be established by them, nor any prohibition of an act, and all revealed laws would be nullified. Thus the one who says this is stripped of religion, rather of the heavenly religions, and joins the materialists and negators. Praise be to Allah who protected us from falling into the like of this, by His grace and favor.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. The author begins by stating the criterion for valid speech: a mere expression is not valid if it does not indicate a meaning subsisting in the soul. The Mu'tazila are criticized for deeming expressions alone valid, which contradicts reason. An analogy from the witnessed world follows: if someone utters words of command, prohibition, or information without any corresponding inner meaning, those utterances are not considered actual command, prohibition, or information, and entail no obligations. The author then applies this to the divine context: if the Mu'tazilite view were true, that speech consists solely of letters and sounds created in a locus with no attribute of speech in God's essence, then those letters would not be speech at all. Consequently, all revealed laws would be invalidated, and the proponent of this view leaves the fold of religion altogether.

Key Terms
العبارة (expression)
المعنى القائم بالنفس (meaning subsisting in the soul)
الكلام (speech)
الحروف والأصوات (letters and sounds)
الصفة (attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speech consists only of letters and sounds created in a locus, with no inner meaning in God's essence.
Correction: Speech requires an inner meaning subsisting in the soul; without it, utterances are not valid speech.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if the Mu'tazilite view of speech were correct?
A) The Quran would be uncreated.
B) All revealed laws would be nullified.
C) Humans would have free will.
D) God would have no attributes.

Key Takeaway
Valid speech requires an inner meaning; without it, expressions are not true speech, and the Mu'tazilite view undermines divine revelation.

Recitation is Like, Not Identical to Divine Speech
The author distinguishes recitation from divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the reader's recitation and the speech of Allah, and why the recitation is considered like it but not identical.

Core Idea
The reader's recitation resembles Allah's speech but is not the same eternal speech.

This passage tackles a key theological nuance: how does our recitation of the Quran relate to the eternal speech of Allah? The author, citing rational consensus, argues that the recitation is created and multiple, while divine speech is uncreated and one. He concludes that the recitation is 'like' the speech but not 'identical' to it. This distinction guards against confusing the created act of recitation with the uncreated attribute of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its rhythm. Then read the English translation. Notice the key contrast at the end: 'like' versus 'very speech'.
Pay attention to the phrase 'باستحالة بقاء كلام الله' as the basis of the argument.
2
Identify the premise
The author begins with a consensus that the speech of Allah cannot remain (as an accident). Understand why that premise matters: if Allah's speech were an accident, it would not endure. This leads to the conclusion about the recitation.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the reader's recitation is like (mithl) the speech of Allah, not identical ('ayn). This is the key doctrinal point: the recitation is a created act that conveys the uncreated speech.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some mistakenly think the recited words are themselves the eternal speech. This passage corrects that: the recitation is similar but not identical. Consider why this distinction matters for theology.
This distinction protects the uncreatedness of Allah's attribute of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون ذلك حقيقة كلام الناظم الأول بإجماع العقلاء باستحالة بقاء كلام الله تعالى، وكل كلام، وبما قام من دليل استحالة القول ببقاء الأعراض. وجاء من هذا أن قراءة القارئ ينبغي أن تكون عندهم مثل كلام الله تعالى لا عين كلامه.
That is the reality of the speech of the first author by consensus of the rational people, due to the impossibility of the remaining of the speech of God (Allah ta'ala), and every speech, and based on the proof that has been established of the impossibility of the statement about the remaining of accidents. And from this it follows that the reader's recitation should, in their view, be like the speech of God (Allah ta'ala), not the very speech of Him.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is a definitional statement establishing the relationship between the reader's recitation and the divine speech. The author begins by referring to a consensus of rational people about the impossibility of the speech of God (kalam Allah) remaining (baqa') as an accident. He then draws the conclusion that the reader's recitation (qira'at al-qari') is like (mithl) the speech of God, not identical to it ('ayn kalamihi). This distinction is crucial: the recitation is created and multiple, whereas the divine speech is uncreated and one. The author does not elaborate further here but sets the stage for a polemic against those who conflate the two.

Key Terms

كلام الله
عين كلامه
بقاء الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that when a person recites the Quran, the sounds and letters they produce are literally the eternal speech of Allah.
Correction: This passage clarifies that the recitation is only like (mithl) the speech of Allah, not identical ('ayn). The recitation is a created accident (qira'ah) that conveys the uncreated speech but is not itself the uncreated speech.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The reader's recitation of the Quran is a created act that resembles the uncreated speech of Allah; it is similar but not identical.

Takwin: The Eternal Attribute of Bringing into Existence
Al-Nasafi defines takwin and lists its synonyms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that takwin and its synonyms refer to a single eternal meaning: bringing the non-existent into existence, and that this attribute is distinct from the created things.

Core Idea
Takwin is one eternal act, not a series of temporal events.

Al-Nasafi begins an important discussion by clarifying that the terms used for divine creation—takwin, takhliq, khalq, and others—are not different acts but synonyms for one eternal attribute. This definition sets the stage for arguing that God's act of bringing things into existence is uncreated and eternal. Let's read his words carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the list of terms and the definition.
Pay attention to the structure: chapter heading, then definition.
2
Identify the synonyms
Find all six terms al-Nasafi lists as synonyms. Note that he says they all intend one meaning.
The terms are: takwin, takhliq, khalq, ijad, ihdath, ikhtira'.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why al-Nasafi specifies 'takwin' and why he emphasizes that this meaning is one and eternal. This connects to the larger argument that God's attribute of creation is uncreated.
The commentary will help clarify why this distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث: الكلام في أن التكوين غير المكون وأنه أزلي غير محدث ولا حادث.

اعلم أن التكوين والتخليق والخلق والإيجاد والإحداث والاختراع، أسماء مترادفة يراد بها كلها معنى واحد وهو إخراج المعدوم من العدم إلى الوجود. فنخص استعمال لفظة التكوين اقتفاء لآثار أسلافنا - رحمهم الله في ذلك.
The third chapter: The discourse on that takwin is other than the thing brought into existence (al-mukawwan) and that it is eternal, not originated nor contingent.

Know that takwin, takhliq, khalq, ijad, ihdath, and ikhtira' are synonymous names, all of which intend one meaning: the bringing out of the non-existent from non-existence into existence. So we specify the use of the term takwin, following the traces of our predecessors—may God have mercy on them—in that.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage opens the third chapter of the section, which argues that takwin (bringing into existence) is distinct from the created thing itself and is eternal. Al-Nasafi begins by defining takwin and listing its synonyms: takhliq, khalq, ijad, ihdath, and ikhtira'. He states all these terms are synonymous and refer to a single meaning: extracting the non-existent from non-existence into existence. He then notes that he will specifically use the term 'takwin' in this discussion, following the practice of his predecessors. This definition is foundational for the subsequent argument about takwin being an eternal attribute of God, distinct from the temporal entities that are brought into existence.

Correction: Al-Nasafi defines takwin as an eternal attribute; it is not a temporal event but the eternal act of bringing things into existence.

C) The act of shaping and forming.
D) The act of commanding and decreeing.

Key Takeaway

The Maturidi View of Takwin as an Eternal Attribute
Nasafi states the Maturidi position on takwin as an eternal attribute of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi affirmation of takwin as an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence and its distinction from the temporal things brought into existence.

Core Idea
Takwin is eternal like power; the created thing is temporal.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla presents the core Maturidi position on the divine attribute of takwin (bringing into existence). It clarifies that takwin is not a temporal act but an eternal attribute of Allah, analogous to other eternal attributes such as power and will. This distinction is crucial for understanding the Maturidi rejection of views that conflate the attribute with its effect.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that Nasafi begins by stating the position of 'the people of truth' (Ahl al-Haqq) that takwin is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, similar to knowledge, power, hearing, and sight.
The phrase 'qāla ahl al-ḥaqq' signals the Maturidi position.
2
Examine the analogy with power and will
Nasafi draws a direct analogy: just as power (qudra) is eternal while the object of power (maqdur) is originated, takwin is eternal while the thing brought into existence (mukawwan) is originated. Similarly for will (irada) and the thing willed (murad).
The analogy reinforces the principle that an eternal attribute relates to temporal effects.
3
Consider the common misunderstanding
Reflect on the common mistake of thinking takwin is an act that occurs in time. The passage insists takwin is a single eternal attribute that relates to each created thing at its moment of existence, just as will relates to each thing at its time.
This helps preserve divine immutability and transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أهل الحق : إن التكوين صفة أزلية قائمة بذات الله تعالى كصفة العلم والقدرة والسمع والبصر ، فكان التكوين أزليا والمكون حادثا، كالقدرة كانت أزلية والمقدور حادثا ، وكذا الإرادة والمراد فيكون التكوين لكل مكون تكوينا له لوقت وجوده كإرادة وجود كل موجود يكون إرادة لوجوده لوقت وجوده.
And the people of truth said: Indeed takwin is an eternal attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah Most High like the attribute of knowledge, power, hearing, and sight. Thus takwin is eternal and the thing brought into existence (mukawwan) is originated, just as power is eternal and the thing empowered (maqdur) is originated, and likewise will and the thing willed. So takwin is for every brought-into-existence a bringing-into-existence for the time of its existence, like the will for the existence of every existing thing is a will for its existence for the time of its existence.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage defines the Maturidi position on the attribute of takwin. It begins by stating that takwin is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, similar to other eternal attributes like knowledge, power, hearing, and sight. The author then draws an analogy: just as power (qudra) is eternal and the object of power (maqdur) is temporal, takwin is eternal and the thing brought into existence (mukawwan) is temporal. Similarly, will (irada) and the thing willed (murad) follow the same pattern. The point is that the attribute itself is eternal, but its effect occurs in time. The Maturidis argue that takwin is a single eternal attribute that relates to each created thing at the moment of its creation, just as will relates to each thing at its time.

Key Terms
التكوين
المكون
القدرة
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think takwin is an act-attribute that occurs in time, but the Maturidis affirm it is an eternal attribute subsisting in the essence.
Correction: The passage explicitly states takwin is eternal and the thing brought into existence is originated, analogous to power and its object.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidis, is the attribute of takwin eternal or originated?
A) Eternal
B) Originated
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The Maturidis affirm takwin as an eternal attribute of Allah, distinct from its temporal effects, preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If takwin is an eternal attribute of Allah, meaning that He is constantly 'bringing into existence' without ever changing, how does that challenge your assumption that creation is a one-time past event? How should this shape your daily awareness of being continuously dependent on Him?
?
The passage compares takwin to power and will: all are eternal, but their effects appear in time. If takwin were merely a temporal act, what would that imply about Allah's relationship with your very existence at this moment? Can you truly live as if every breath is an immediate act of takwin?
?
You likely affirm that Allah is 'Al-Khaliq' (The Creator). But do you really believe that the attribute of takwin is eternally active, meaning that at every instant you are being created anew? Take a moment: what thought, word, or action today can you trace directly to the reality that takwin is happening right now?

Disagreement on Ism and Musamma Among Grammarians
Nasafi reports the dispute between Sibawayh and Mubarrad on ism and musamma, and Ash'ari's threefold division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different theological and grammatical positions on the relationship between the name (ism) and the named (musamma), including the Ash'ari threefold division.

Core Idea
The name and the named are not always identical; Ash'ari divided them into three categories.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi reports a key dispute among grammarians and theologians regarding the relationship between 'ism' (noun/name) and 'musamma' (the named thing). This debate is crucial for understanding how different schools view divine names and attributes. We will examine the views of Sibawayh, al-Mubarrad, and the unique threefold division introduced by Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the content and flow.
Pay attention to the technical terms: اسم, مسمى, تسمية.
2
Identify the three positions
Look for the views of Sibawayh, al-Mubarrad, and al-Ash'ari. Note how each defines the relationship between ism and musamma.
Sibawayh equates them; Mubarrad separates them; Ash'ari divides them into three.
3
Focus on Ash'ari's threefold division
Al-Ash'ari presents three categories: ism identical to musamma, ism other than musamma, and ism neither identical nor other. Understand how this mirrors the division of attributes.
4
Understand the theological implication
Nasafi uses this dispute to challenge the opponent's reliance on Sibawayh's authority. Reflect on why this matters for discussions of divine names.
This shows that even within the Ash'ari school, earlier authorities were not followed blindly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصناعة قالوا: الصحيح ما ذهب إليه سيبويه لأنه عنى بالأسماء المسميات دون التسميات كما هو مذهب أهل السنة أن الاسم والمسمى واحد، وذهب المبرد إلى أن الاسم غير المسمى وهو عبارة عن التسمية. ثم إن صاحبكم الأشعري لم يقبل من سيبويه أن الاسم هو المسمى ولا من أبي عبيدة، إذ روي عنه هذا بل جميع من تقدمه وقال : إن الاسم هو الصفة، وينقسم الاسم انقسام الصفات فيكون اسم هو عين المسمى واسم هو غير المسمى، واسم لا يقال هو المسمى ولا يقال هو غيره. وكذا التسمية مع الاسم، وطول هو وأصحابه في تقاسيم ذلك.
The craft said: the correct [view] is what Sibawayh held, because he meant by nouns the named things, not the naming, as is the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna that the noun and the named are one. And al-Mubarrad held that the noun is other than the named, and it is the expression for the naming. Then your companion al-Ash'ari did not accept from Sibawayh that the noun is the named, nor from Abu Ubayda, since this is narrated from him, rather from all who preceded him, and said: the noun is the attribute, and the noun divides like the division of attributes: there is a noun that is identical to the named, a noun that is other than the named, and a noun of which it is not said that it is the named nor that it is other than it. And likewise the naming in relation to the noun, and he and his companions went on at length about its divisions.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage discusses the grammatical and theological dispute about the relationship between 'ism' (noun/name) and 'musamma' (named thing). Nasafi reports that the grammarians (ahl al-sina'a) consider the correct view to be that of Sibawayh: that 'ism' refers to the named entity itself, not the act of naming. This aligns with the Ahl al-Sunna position that the name and the named are identical. Al-Mubarrad held the opposite: the name is the naming act, distinct from the named. Then Nasafi points out that al-Ash'ari rejected both Sibawayh and Abu Ubayda, and all previous grammarians, asserting that 'ism' is an attribute. He divided names into three categories: (1) name that is identical to the named, (2) name that is other than the named, and (3) name that is neither said to be identical nor other. This threefold division mirrors the division of attributes. Ash'ari and his followers elaborated extensively on these distinctions. Nasafi uses this to undermine the opponent's reliance on Sibawayh's authority: since Ash'ari himself rejected Sibawayh, how can the opponent force others to accept Sibawayh's view?

Key Terms
اسم
مسمى
تسمية
صفة
أشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Ash'aris accept Sibawayh's definition of ism as identical to musamma.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Ash'ari himself rejected both Sibawayh and Mubarrad, proposing a threefold division instead.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of al-Mubarrad on the relationship between ism and musamma?
A) They are identical.
B) They are different; ism is the naming act.
C) They are both attributes.
D) Some are identical, some are different, and some are neither.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari introduced a threefold division of ism that goes beyond the simple identity view of Sibawayh and the distinction view of al-Mubarrad, reflecting a nuanced understanding of divine names and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah' or recite His names, do you consider the name to be identical to His essence or merely a label? How does your answer affect the reverence and weight you give to His names in your daily life?
?
Al-Ash'ari, the founder of your school (if you follow it), rejected the view of Sibawayh that the name is identical to the named. Have you ever questioned an established authority in your own understanding of faith? What would it take for you to critically examine a view you've always accepted?
?
The precise classification of ism into three categories shows that language matters deeply in theology. Are you careless with words when speaking about Allah? What is one phrase you use about God that you should reconsider, and how will you change it starting today?

Fi'l in Kalam is Masdar, Not the Linguistic Verb
Nasafi distinguishes theological fi'l from linguistic fi'l.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why theologians treat 'fi'l' as a masdar (infinitive) rather than a verb, and how this differs from the linguists' definition, highlighting a key terminological dispute.

Core Idea
Theologians and linguists speak different languages about 'fi'l' — knowing which is crucial.

In this passage, Nasafi continues his refutation by exposing a fundamental terminological confusion. When theologians speak of 'acts' (af'al), they mean the infinitive forms like 'hitting' or 'eating', which linguists classify as nouns, not verbs. Nasafi explains the linguistic markers that distinguish nouns from verbs, showing that the theologians' 'fi'l' actually falls under the linguists' category of nouns. This sets the stage for a logical trap for his opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and its translation to grasp the flow of Nasafi's argument.
2
Identify the linguistic markers
Notice how Nasafi lists the markers of nouns (al-, tanwin, idafa) and verbs (qad, sawfa). Understand why the masdar takes noun markers.
3
See the conclusion
Nasafi ends this part by asking: how can you force your opponents to accept the linguists' definition when what you call 'fi'l' is not a verb according to them? This is the key point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الفعل عندك وعند أهل الكلام أجمع ما هو المصدر عند أهل اللغة، وهم لا يعدون ذلك فعلا بل يعدونه اسما، ولهذا اختص بعلامات الأسماء وهي دخول لام التعريف أو التنوين أو الإضافة دون علامات الأفعال وهي قد وسوف، ودخل فيما هو من حد الاسم عندهم، وهو ما دل على معنى غير مقترن بزمان محصل أو كلمة تدل على المعنى دلالة الإشارة دون الإفادة، وخرج من حد الأفعال عندهم وهو ما دل على خلاف، وما هو الفعل عند أهل الكلام ليس بفعل عند أهل اللغة. فكيف تلزم خصومك الأخذ بقولهم. ثم إنه سمى المصادر أحداث الأسماء، فكان ضرب زيد وأكل خالد وشرب محمد أحداثا لهؤلاء، أتقول أنت: إن هذه المعاني حدثت عنهم؟ فإن قلت: نعم، فقد جعلت للعباد أحداثا وإن قلت: لا، فقد خالفت أهل اللغة .
Then the act/verb (fi'l) according to you and according to the theologians (ahl al-kalam) altogether is what is the infinitive (masdar) according to the linguists, but they [the linguists] do not count that as a verb, rather they count it as a noun. And for this reason it is distinguished by the markers of nouns, which are the entry of the definite article (al-), or nunation (tanwin), or annexation (idafa), without the markers of verbs, which are 'qad' and 'sawfa'. And it enters into what is within the definition of a noun according to them, which is that which indicates a meaning not connected to a determinate time, or a word that indicates the meaning by way of reference (ishara) without predication (ifada). And it exits from the definition of verbs according to them, which is that which indicates the opposite. And what is a verb (fi'l) according to the theologians is not a verb according to the linguists. So how can you compel your opponents to accept their statement? Further, he called the infinitives 'events of the nouns' (ahdath al-asma), so 'Zaid's hitting', 'Khalid's eating', and 'Muhammad's drinking' are events of these [persons]. Do you say that these meanings occurred from them? If you say 'yes', then you have attributed events to the servants; if you say 'no', then you have contradicted the linguists.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation by clarifying the terminological divergence between theologians (ahl al-kalam) and linguists (ahl al-lugha). Nasafi states that when theologians speak of 'fi'l' (act), they mean the masdar (infinitive), such as 'hitting' or 'eating'. But linguists do not consider the masdar a verb; they classify it as a noun (ism). Linguists identify nouns by markers like the definite article (al-), nunation (tanwin), and annexation (idafa), while verbs are marked by 'qad' and 'sawfa'. Nouns indicate a meaning not tied to a specific time, whereas verbs indicate time. Thus, the theological term 'fi'l' falls under the linguistic category of nouns, not verbs. Therefore, the opponent cannot force Nasafi to accept the linguists' definition of fi'l without acknowledging that the thing they call 'fi'l' is not what linguists call 'fi'l'. Next, Nasafi highlights another inconsistency: the opponent calls masdars 'events (ahdath) of the nouns'. For example, 'Zaid's hitting' is considered an event of Zaid. Nasafi asks: does the opponent hold that these meanings (hitting, eating) actually occur from the persons (i.e., are created by them)? If yes, then humans become creators of events—contradicting mainstream Sunni theology which restricts creation to God. If no, then the opponent contradicts the linguists who treat these as events of the persons. Either way, the opponent is trapped.

Key Terms
فعل
مصدر
اسم
علامة
أهل الكلام
أهل اللغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That theologians and linguists mean the same thing by 'fi'l'.
Correction: Nasafi shows they mean different things: theologians mean the masdar, linguists mean the verb form with time reference.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what markers does the masdar take?
A) Qad and sawfa.
B) Al-, tanwin, and idafa.
C) The feminine marker.
D) The dual marker.

Key Takeaway
Theologians and linguists use 'fi'l' differently; the theologians' fi'l is actually a noun according to linguists, undermining the opponent's reliance on linguistic definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran or hadith, do you ever force your own linguistic understanding onto terms that scholars have defined technically? Where in your life has this caused you to misunderstand the intended meaning? What will you do to seek the intended meaning first?
?
The opponent was wrong because they assumed their definition of 'fi'l' was universal. In your interactions with others, how often do you assume the other person means what you mean by a word? Can you recall a conflict that arose from such an assumption? How can you humbly clarify terms before arguing?
?
Nasafi's argument exposes the danger of holding others to definitions they never claimed. Are you guilty of holding Allah or His Messenger to your own definitions, rather than accepting the meanings He has established? Repent of this and commit to learning the intended meanings before judging.

The Dilemma of Calling Masdars 'Events'
Nasafi presents a dilemma about calling masdars 'events'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that calling masdars 'events' traps the opponent into either attributing creation to humans or contradicting linguistic usage.

Core Idea
Calling masdars 'events' forces a dilemma: either humans create events or you contradict language.

After clarifying the terminological confusion, Nasafi presents a second argument. His opponents have referred to masdars as 'events of the nouns' (ahdath al-asma). For example, 'Zaid's hitting' is called an event of Zaid. Nasafi then poses a decisive question: do you affirm that these meanings (hitting, eating) actually occur from the persons? If you say yes, you attribute creation to humans, which conflicts with Sunni theology. If you say no, you contradict the linguists who consider them events of those persons. Either way, the opponent is trapped.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second part of Nasafi's argument.
2
Identify the opponent's term
Note that they call masdars 'events of the nouns'. Example: 'Zaid's hitting'.
3
Understand the dilemma
Nasafi asks: do these meanings occur from the persons? Yes leads to attributing creation to humans; No contradicts linguistic usage.
4
See the trap
Either answer undermines the opponent's position. This is a classic logical dilemma.

Source Text
