The Principle of Derived Names
Al-Nasafi defines derived names and states the agreed rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidis define derived names and why 'Khaliq' describes the essence in which creation subsists.

Core Idea
Derived names describe the essence, not the act.

Al-Nasafi establishes a foundational principle for understanding divine names like 'Creator'. He explains that a derived name such as 'Khaliq' does not refer to the act of creation externally, but to the essence in which the meaning of creation subsists. This principle is agreed upon by Maturidis and Ash'aris alike.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the definition and examples.
2
Identify the rule
Find the sentence that states the rule for derived names: 'the derived name from a meaning is a description of one in whom the meaning subsists'.
3
Consider the agreement
Note that Al-Nasafi says Maturidis and Ash'aris agree on this principle.
This agreement will be challenged later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكان حادثا لا بغيره، فلم يدل على غير أوجده، فكان القول به قولا بنفي الصانع. يحققه : أن الخالق اسم مشتق من الخلق، كالأكل اسم مشتق من الأكل، والضارب من الضرب، وكذا الموجود من الإيجاد ثم عندنا الاسم المشتق من معنى يكون وصفا لمن قام به المعنى أو لمن له المعنى بأن كان المعنى راجعا إلى الذات، كالساكن اسم لما قام به السكون، وكذا الحركة والسواد والموجود اسم لمن كان وجوده راجعا إلى ذاته. وانعقد الاتفاق على هذه الجملة بيننا وبين الأشعرية. ونسبنا جميعا المعتزلة إلى الجهل حيث زعموا أن المتكلم اسم لموجد الكلام لا للمحل الذي قام به الكلام، والكافر اسم لفاعل الكفر لا لمن قام به الكفر. وادعينا التناقض والخروج عن المعقول حيث ساعدونا على أن المتحرك من قامت به الحركة لا من أوجدها، وكذا الساكن والأسود والأبيض والحي والميت والصحيح والفاسد والمريض . ثم إن هؤلاء الأشعرية تركت هذا الأصل وزعمت أن الخالق غير من قام به الخلق، والموجد غير من قام به الإيجاد وهذا تناقض ظاهر وترك لأصول أهل الحق بل خروج عن إجماع العقلاء والمعتزلة في هذه المسألة أسعد حالا منهم، فإنهم وقعوا فيما وقعوا لإلجاء أصلهم الفاسد في استحالة قيام معنى بذات الله تعالى إلى هذا، وهذا الإلجاء في حق الأشعرية منعدم فإنهم ساعدونا على وجود القول بقيام الصفات بذات الباري جل وعلا.
It would be an originated thing not by another, so it does not indicate a creator other than the one who brought it into existence, so holding that view is a statement denying the Maker. He verifies this: that 'Creator' (al-Khaliq) is a name derived from 'creation' (al-khalq), just as 'eater' is a name derived from 'eating', and 'striker' from 'striking', and likewise 'the existing' (al-mawjud) from 'bringing into existence' (al-ijad). Then with us, the derived name from a meaning is a description of one in whom the meaning subsists or of one to whom the meaning belongs, such that the meaning is referred back to the essence, like 'the moving' is a name for that in which motion subsists, and likewise motion and blackness, and 'the existing' is a name for one whose existence is referred back to his essence. And agreement is concluded on this principle between us and the Ash'ariyya. And we all attributed ignorance to the Mu'tazila where they claimed that 'the speaker' (al-mutakallim) is a name for the one who brings about speech, not for the locus in which speech subsists, and 'the disbeliever' (al-kafir) is a name for the doer of disbelief, not for the one in whom disbelief subsists. And we alleged contradiction and departure from reason where they agreed with us that 'the moving' is the one in whom motion subsists, not the one who brings it about, and likewise 'the stationary', 'the black', 'the white', 'the living', 'the dead', 'the healthy', 'the corrupt', and 'the sick'. Then these Ash'ariyya abandoned this principle and claimed that 'the Creator' is other than the one in whom creation subsists, and 'the bringer into existence' is other than the one in whom bringing into existence subsists—and this is an apparent contradiction and abandonment of the principles of the people of truth, indeed a departure from the consensus of the rational people. And the Mu'tazila in this matter are more fortunate than them, for they fell into what they fell into due to the compulsion of their corrupt principle of the impossibility of a meaning subsisting in the essence of Allah, the Most High, to this. And this compulsion is absent in the case of the Ash'ariyya, for they agreed with us on the existence of the statement of the subsistence of attributes in the essence of the Creator, the Mighty and Majestic.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument about the meaning of derived names, specifically 'Khaliq' (Creator). Al-Nasafi begins by continuing a previous point: if something were originated without a cause, it would deny the existence of a Creator. He then verifies this by defining how derived names work: the name 'Khaliq' comes from 'khalq' (creation), just as 'ākil' (eater) comes from 'akl' (eating). In the Maturidi view, a derived name describes the entity in which the meaning (ma'nā) subsists or to which it is attributed, with the meaning referring back to the essence (dhāt). Examples: 'sākin' (stationary) names that in which stillness subsists. 'Mawjūd' (existing) names one whose existence is self-referential. Al-Nasafi states that Maturidis and Ash'aris agree on this principle. Both groups criticize the Mu'tazila for inconsistency: Mu'tazila say 'mutakallim' (speaker) is the one who creates speech, not the one in whom speech subsists, and similarly for 'kāfir' (disbeliever). Yet they agree with the principle in other cases like 'mutaḥarrik' (moving). Then Al-Nasafi points out a contradiction in the Ash'aris: they abandon the same principle when it comes to 'Khāliq', claiming that God is Khāliq even though creation does not subsist in Him. This, he says, is a clear contradiction and departure from the consensus of rational people. The Mu'tazila are somewhat excused because their error follows from their false premise that a meaning cannot subsist in God's essence. But the Ash'aris have no such excuse, since they agree that attributes subsist in God's essence.

Key Terms
اسم مشتق
الخالق
الخلق
معنى
قيام المعنى بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Khaliq' means the one who brings about creation, not the one in whom creation subsists.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explains that derived names refer to the essence in which the meaning subsists, not the external act.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Nasafi, what is the rule for a derived name like 'Khaliq'?
A) It describes the one who creates the action.
B) It describes the one in whom the meaning (creation) subsists.
C) It is a name for the action itself.
D) It is purely metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Derived names like 'Khaliq' describe the essence in which the meaning subsists, not the external act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If 'Khaliq' describes Allah's essence, not merely an act, then at every moment His attribute of creation is present in Him. Do you live with the awareness that your very existence is being constantly brought into being? How would your actions today reflect that reality?
?
The passage teaches that derived names like 'al-Mawjud' refer back to the essence. When you say 'Allah exists,' do you truly link that existence to His essential nature, or do you think of it as a distant concept? What would it mean for you to know that your own existence is derived from His?
?
Al-Nasafi emphasizes that this principle is agreed upon by Maturidis and Ash'aris. If scholars have united on this foundational insight, what is stopping you from internalizing it? Commit to one specific way you will remind yourself of Allah's essential attributes today.

The Inconsistency of the Ash'aris regarding 'Khaliq'
Al-Nasafi critiques Mu'tazila and Ash'aris on the rule of derived names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi critique of the Ash'ari position on the name 'Khaliq' and why it is considered a contradiction.

Core Idea
Ash'aris agree on the rule but abandon it for 'Creator'.

Al-Nasafi now turns to criticize both the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ariyya. He points out that the Mu'tazila violate the principle of derived names by denying that attributes subsist in God's essence. However, the Ash'aris are in a worse position because they accept the principle for all other derived names but reject it for 'Khaliq'. This, Al-Nasafi argues, is a clear contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique of Mu'tazila
Note that Mu'tazila say 'mutakallim' is the one who creates speech, not the locus.
Al-Nasafi considers this ignorant.
2
Identify the Ash'ari inconsistency
Al-Nasafi says Ash'aris abandoned the principle for 'Khaliq' despite agreeing on it.
3
Understand the reasoning
Al-Nasafi explains that Mu'tazila were forced due to their false principle, but Ash'aris have no excuse.
This highlights the Maturidi view on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكان حادثا لا بغيره، فلم يدل على غير أوجده، فكان القول به قولا بنفي الصانع. يحققه : أن الخالق اسم مشتق من الخلق، كالأكل اسم مشتق من الأكل، والضارب من الضرب، وكذا الموجود من الإيجاد ثم عندنا الاسم المشتق من معنى يكون وصفا لمن قام به المعنى أو لمن له المعنى بأن كان المعنى راجعا إلى الذات، كالساكن اسم لما قام به السكون، وكذا الحركة والسواد والموجود اسم لمن كان وجوده راجعا إلى ذاته. وانعقد الاتفاق على هذه الجملة بيننا وبين الأشعرية. ونسبنا جميعا المعتزلة إلى الجهل حيث زعموا أن المتكلم اسم لموجد الكلام لا للمحل الذي قام به الكلام، والكافر اسم لفاعل الكفر لا لمن قام به الكفر. وادعينا التناقض والخروج عن المعقول حيث ساعدونا على أن المتحرك من قامت به الحركة لا من أوجدها، وكذا الساكن والأسود والأبيض والحي والميت والصحيح والفاسد والمريض . ثم إن هؤلاء الأشعرية تركت هذا الأصل وزعمت أن الخالق غير من قام به الخلق، والموجد غير من قام به الإيجاد وهذا تناقض ظاهر وترك لأصول أهل الحق بل خروج عن إجماع العقلاء والمعتزلة في هذه المسألة أسعد حالا منهم، فإنهم وقعوا فيما وقعوا لإلجاء أصلهم الفاسد في استحالة قيام معنى بذات الله تعالى إلى هذا، وهذا الإلجاء في حق الأشعرية منعدم فإنهم ساعدونا على وجود القول بقيام الصفات بذات الباري جل وعلا.
It would be an originated thing not by another, so it does not indicate a creator other than the one who brought it into existence, so holding that view is a statement denying the Maker. And we alleged contradiction and departure from reason where they agreed with us that 'the moving' is the one in whom motion subsists, not the one who brings it about, and likewise 'the stationary', 'the black', 'the white', 'the living', 'the dead', 'the healthy', 'the corrupt', and 'the sick'. Then these Ash'ariyya abandoned this principle and claimed that 'the Creator' is other than the one in whom creation subsists, and 'the bringer into existence' is other than the one in whom bringing into existence subsists—and this is an apparent contradiction and abandonment of the principles of the people of truth, indeed a departure from the consensus of the rational people. And this compulsion is absent in the case of the Ash'ariyya, for they agreed with us on the existence of the statement of the subsistence of attributes in the essence of the Creator, the Mighty and Majestic.

What Is Kalam?

Why Speech Cannot Be Shared: Act vs. Speech in Dual Attribution
Al-Qattan and al-Qalanisi define fi'l and kalam and explain their dual attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between act (fi'l) and speech (kalam) and why speech cannot be attributed to two speakers while acts can.

Core Idea
Speech is a unique act that cannot belong to two speakers.

In this passage, the early theologians al-Qattan and al-Qalanisi grapple with a key question: if both God and the servant have power over actions, why can't they both be speakers of the same speech? Their answer hinges on a careful definition of 'act' and 'speech'. Let's read how they distinguish these two categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the flow and key terms.
2
Identify the definitions of fi'l and kalam
The passage defines 'act' broadly as anything within power and acted-upon, and 'speech' as a specific act contrary to silence. Notice the difference in scope.
Fi'l is general; kalam is a specific kind of fi'l.
3
Understand the argument: why kalam can't be shared
Consider the reasoning: because kalam negates silence in its locus, and it's impossible for one instance of kalam to negate silence in two loci simultaneously. This is why speech cannot be attributed to two speakers.
This distinction resolves the apparent conflict of dual attribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجابهم من سلم منكم أن للعبد فعلا وهو عبد الله بن سعيد القطان وأبو العباس القلانسي فقال : بأن الفعل اسم عام لكل ما هو مقدور مفعول، وقد ثبت أن فعلنا مقدور الله تعالى ومخلوقه فكان خلقه وفعله، والكلام اسم لفعل خاص وهو المعنى المنافي للسكوت والخرس والآفة، وهو ينافي سكوت المحل الذي قام به آفته ويستحيل قيامه بمحلين لنفي السكوت والآفة عن محلين فلم يكن كلاما لمحلين. فأما الفعل فهو اسم لما يقع بالقدرة وهو مقدور الله تعالى ومقدور العبد، فكان فعلا لهما، حتى إن الكلام لما كان يقع بقدرة الله خلقا وقدرة العبد كسبا كان أيضا فعلا لفاعلين أما لم يكن كلاهما لمتكلمين لما مر.
They answered them, from those among you who accepted that the servant has an act, and they are Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, saying: that 'act' (al-fi'l) is a general name for everything that is empowered (maqdur) and acted-upon (maf'ul), and it has been established that our act is empowered by God Most High and His creation, so it is His creation and His act. And 'speech' (al-kalam) is a name for a specific act, which is the meaning contrary to silence, dumbness, and defect, and it is contrary to the silence of the locus in which its defect resides, and it is impossible for it to exist in two loci because of the negation of silence and defect from two loci, so it was not speech for two loci. As for the act, it is a name for what occurs by power (qudra), and it is empowered by God Most High and empowered by the servant, so it is an act for both of them. So much so that speech, since it occurs by God's power as creation (khalq) and by the servant's power as acquisition (kasb), was also an act for two agents, but it was not such that both were speakers, for the reason that passed.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage is a definition and distinction between two key terms: act (fi'l) and speech (kalam). The author presents the view of two scholars who accepted that the servant has an act. They define fi'l as a general term encompassing everything that is within power and is an object of action. They then establish that human acts are both empowered by God and created by Him, thus being His creation and act. Then they define kalam as a specific act that is the opposite of silence, dumbness, and defect. Kalam cannot exist in two loci simultaneously because it negates silence and defect from only one locus. Therefore, kalam cannot be attributed to two speakers. In contrast, fi'l is a name for what occurs by power, and it is empowered by both God and the servant, so it can be an act for both. Even kalam, when considered as created by God and acquired by the servant, is an act for both agents, but not speech for both speakers. The argument follows the order: definition of fi'l, its relation to God and servant, definition of kalam, its impossibility of dual attribution, and then the conclusion that fi'l can be attributed to two agents while kalam cannot.

Key Terms
فعل (fi'l)
كلام (kalam)
قدرة (qudra)
كسب (kasb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can speech (kalam) not be attributed to two speakers according to this passage?
A) Because it is a general act.
B) Because it negates silence and defect from only one locus.
C) Because it is created by God alone.
D) Because it is not an act.

Key Takeaway
Speech (kalam) is a special type of act that, by its definition, can only be attributed to one speaker, whereas acts in general can be attributed to multiple agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you truly know that every word is both created by God and acquired by you? How does that awareness challenge your pride in your own eloquence or your excuses for harmful speech? This lesson forces you to ask: If your speech is not solely your own, what right do you have to use it carelessly? Will you not account for every utterance before the One who created it?
?
If God's speech is exclusive to Him and cannot be shared, how do you approach the Qur'an when you recite it? Do you recognize that your recitation is not God speaking, but you acquiring His speech? How does that change the reverence and humility you bring to your tilawah? Are you truly listening to the Owner of that speech, or are you merely performing?
?
The argument in this lesson shows that speech, by its nature, cannot be attributed to two speakers. Consider the conversations where you have claimed or echoed words that were not originally yours—whether in debate, gossip, or teaching. Are you honest about the source of your words? Do you take credit for ideas or statements that are not your own, or do you attribute them to their rightful speaker? This lesson calls you to be truthful about who speaks what.

Distinguishing Deniers of Prophethood
Nasafi categorizes deniers of prophethood and states the proper starting points for debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi categorizes deniers of prophethood and why he prioritizes proving the Creator and the means of knowledge before discussing prophethood itself.

Core Idea
Before proving prophethood, we must first establish the Creator and the validity of transmitted knowledge.

This lesson introduces two categories of people who deny prophethood: those who deny the Creator and those who affirm Him but deny that messengers can bring knowledge. Nasafi explains that with the first group, the discussion must focus on proving the Creator; with the second, the focus is on proving that reports can be a source of knowledge. This sets the stage for the later discussion on prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups of deniers
Read the passage and note the two groups: those who deny the Creator (مَنْ أَنْكَرَ الصَّانِعَ) and those who deny prophethood while affirming a Creator (like السمنية and البراهمة).
2
Understand the starting point for each group
Notice that Nasafi says we do not begin discussing prophethood with these groups; with the first, we discuss proving the Creator; with the second, we discuss proving that خبر is a means of knowledge.
3
Connect to the broader methodology
Reflect on why this prioritization is important. Nasafi is showing that theological discussions must follow a logical order: foundations first.
This lesson shows the depth of kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما من أنكر الصانع فلا شك أنه منكر للرسالة إذ القول بالرسول ولا مرسل محالاً. إلا أن الكلام معهم يقع في إثبات الصانع. وكذا من أنكر الرسالة من السمنية (١) والبراهمة (٢) لما أن الخبر ليس من أسباب المصارف فلا يفيد (٣) خبر الرسول معنى فلا فائدة في إرساله. لا نتكلم معه في هذه المسألة ابتداء بل نتكلم فى إثبات كون (4) الخبر سببًا من أسباب المعارف، وقد مر في أول الكتاب ما يقع به الفنية (٥) بحمد الله تعالى. وقبل أن نجيء إلى إقامة الدلالة على صحة الرسالة، لا بد أن نتكلم في تحديد الرسالة لتعلم في نفسها ثم نقيم الدلالة على ثبوتها فنقول: معنى الرسالة والرسول: أما الرسالة في اللغة فهي تحميل جملة من الكلام إلى المقصود بالدلالة، ولا شك في صحة هذا الحد إذ كل رسالة فيما بين الخلق فهي داخلة في جملة هذا الحد وأما الرسالة التي اختلفنا في ثبوتها، فقد قيل في حدها أنها هي سفارة العبد بين الله تعالى وبين ذوي الألباب من خليقته ليزيح بها عللهم فيما (٦) قصرت عنه عقولهم من مصالح داريهم. ولا خفاء على من كان عارفًا بشرائط صحة الحد لصحته عنده، وبعد حصول المعرفة بحدها يجب صرف العناية إلى إثبات إمكانها. فنتكلم في إثبات إمكانها أولاً ثم نتكلم في الاختلاف في وجوبها بين المتكلمين. ثم نتكلم في إقامة الدلالة على ثبوتها، ونأتي في كل فصل بما يوجب صحته من الدلائل - إن شاء الله تعالى.
As for the one who denies the Creator, there is no doubt that he denies the message, since affirming a messenger without a sender is impossible. However, discussion with them occurs in proving the Creator. Likewise, the one who denies the message from among the Samaniyya (1) and the Brahmins (2), because information (al-khabar) is not among the means of knowledge, so the report of the messenger conveys no meaning, so there is no benefit in sending him. We do not speak with him about this issue initially, but we speak about proving that (4) information is one of the means of knowledge, and what suffices has already passed at the beginning of the book (5), by the praise of God Almighty. And before we come to establishing the proof of the validity of the message, we must speak about defining the message to know it in itself, then we establish proof of its affirmation. So we say: The meaning of the message (al-risāla) and the messenger (al-rasūl): As for the message in language, it is the entrusting of a body of speech to the intended person by indication, and there is no doubt about the validity of this definition, for every message among people falls under this definition. As for the message whose affirmation we differ about, it has been said in its definition that it is the embassy of the servant between God Almighty and those of intellect among His creation, to remove their needs in what (6) their intellects fall short of regarding the interests of their two abodes. And it is not hidden from one who knows the conditions of the validity of a definition that it is sound to him. And after obtaining knowledge of its definition, attention must be turned to proving its possibility. So we will speak first about proving its possibility, then we will speak about the disagreement among theologians regarding its obligation. Then we will speak about establishing the proof of its affirmation, and we will bring in each chapter what necessitates its validity of proofs, God willing.
Source pages p. 670

Commentary
This passage begins by distinguishing between two types of deniers of prophethood: those who deny the Creator entirely (atheists) and those who affirm the Creator but deny prophethood, like the Samaniyya and Brahmins. Nasafi explains that with atheists, the discussion must first focus on proving the Creator. With those who affirm the Creator but deny prophethood, the initial discussion is not about prophethood itself but about proving that transmitted reports can be a source of knowledge, since they deny that. He references earlier content on the means of knowledge. Next, Nasafi states the need to define prophethood before proving it. He gives two definitions: the linguistic definition (transmitting a message) and the technical definition (an embassy from God to rational beings to supply what their intellects cannot reach regarding worldly and otherworldly interests). He affirms that this definition is valid for those familiar with the conditions of definitions. After defining it, he outlines the three-step plan: first prove possibility (imkān), then discuss obligation (wujūb), then establish actual occurrence (thubūt). This sets the structure for the coming chapters.

Key Terms
الرسالة
الرسول
السمنية
البراهمة
أسباب المعارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can immediately argue for prophethood without first establishing the existence of God and the validity of transmitted knowledge.
Correction: Nasafi shows that with different opponents, we must start from more foundational premises: either proving the Creator or proving that reports can be knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what should be the initial topic of discussion with someone who denies the Creator?
A) Proving prophethood
B) Proving the existence of God
C) Proving the possibility of revelation
D) Refuting the Samaniyya

Key Takeaway
Nasafi teaches us to address foundational issues before discussing prophethood, tailoring the debate to the opponent's starting point.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone who denies prophethood, do you first ensure they affirm the Creator, or do you jump straight to proving prophethood? What does that reveal about your own understanding of the foundations of faith?
?
Have you ever assumed that all opponents of Islam share the same doubts, and used a one-size-fits-all response? How might that have harmed your da‘wah, and what will you change after learning Nasafi’s approach?
?
If you were to debate a person who accepts God but rejects the reliability of transmitted knowledge, could you defend the validity of reports as a source of knowledge? What would you say, and why does it matter for your own faith?

intermediate

Defining Prophethood and Outlining the Proof Plan
Nasafi defines prophethood and states the plan to prove its possibility, obligation, and actuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definition of الرسالة and the three-step logical plan for proving prophethood.

Core Idea
Prophethood is an embassy from God to supply what intellect alone cannot reach.

After addressing the deniers, Nasafi turns to defining prophethood itself. He gives both a linguistic definition (transmitting a message) and a technical definition (an embassy between God and rational beings). He then outlines a three-phase plan: prove possibility, obligation, and then actual occurrence. This lesson establishes the conceptual framework for the following chapters.

Guided Path
1
Learn the two definitions of الرسالة
Read the linguistic definition (تحميل جملة من الكلام) and the technical definition (سفارة العبد بين الله وبين ذوي الألباب). Note the purpose: to remove needs that intellects cannot fulfill.
2
Understand the conditions for a valid definition
Nasafi notes that this definition is valid for those who know the conditions of definition. Reflect on why defining terms is important in theological debate.
3
Outline the three-phase proof plan
After definition, Nasafi says we first prove possibility (إمكان), then discuss obligation (وجوب), then prove actuality (ثبوت). This is the logical sequence for demonstrating that prophethood occurred.
This plan structures the entire discussion on prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما من أنكر الصانع فلا شك أنه منكر للرسالة إذ القول بالرسول ولا مرسل محالاً. إلا أن الكلام معهم يقع في إثبات الصانع. وكذا من أنكر الرسالة من السمنية (١) والبراهمة (٢) لما أن الخبر ليس من أسباب المصارف فلا يفيد (٣) خبر الرسول معنى فلا فائدة في إرساله. لا نتكلم معه في هذه المسألة ابتداء بل نتكلم فى إثبات كون (4) الخبر سببًا من أسباب المعارف، وقد مر في أول الكتاب ما يقع به الفنية (٥) بحمد الله تعالى. وقبل أن نجيء إلى إقامة الدلالة على صحة الرسالة، لا بد أن نتكلم في تحديد الرسالة لتعلم في نفسها ثم نقيم الدلالة على ثبوتها فنقول: معنى الرسالة والرسول: أما الرسالة في اللغة فهي تحميل جملة من الكلام إلى المقصود بالدلالة، ولا شك في صحة هذا الحد إذ كل رسالة فيما بين الخلق فهي داخلة في جملة هذا الحد وأما الرسالة التي اختلفنا في ثبوتها، فقد قيل في حدها أنها هي سفارة العبد بين الله تعالى وبين ذوي الألباب من خليقته ليزيح بها عللهم فيما (٦) قصرت عنه عقولهم من مصالح داريهم. ولا خفاء على من كان عارفًا بشرائط صحة الحد لصحته عنده، وبعد حصول المعرفة بحدها يجب صرف العناية إلى إثبات إمكانها. فنتكلم في إثبات إمكانها أولاً ثم نتكلم في الاختلاف في وجوبها بين المتكلمين. ثم نتكلم في إقامة الدلالة على ثبوتها، ونأتي في كل فصل بما يوجب صحته من الدلائل - إن شاء الله تعالى.
As for the one who denies the Creator, there is no doubt that he denies the message, since affirming a messenger without a sender is impossible. However, discussion with them occurs in proving the Creator. Likewise, the one who denies the message from among the Samaniyya (1) and the Brahmins (2), because information (al-khabar) is not among the means of knowledge, so the report of the messenger conveys no meaning, so there is no benefit in sending him. We do not speak with him about this issue initially, but we speak about proving that (4) information is one of the means of knowledge, and what suffices has already passed at the beginning of the book (5), by the praise of God Almighty. And before we come to establishing the proof of the validity of the message, we must speak about defining the message to know it in itself, then we establish proof of its affirmation. So we say: The meaning of the message (al-risāla) and the messenger (al-rasūl): As for the message in language, it is the entrusting of a body of speech to the intended person by indication, and there is no doubt about the validity of this definition, for every message among people falls under this definition. As for the message whose affirmation we differ about, it has been said in its definition that it is the embassy of the servant between God Almighty and those of intellect among His creation, to remove their needs in what (6) their intellects fall short of regarding the interests of their two abodes. And it is not hidden from one who knows the conditions of the validity of a definition that it is sound to him. And after obtaining knowledge of its definition, attention must be turned to proving its possibility. So we will speak first about proving its possibility, then we will speak about the disagreement among theologians regarding its obligation. Then we will speak about establishing the proof of its affirmation, and we will bring in each chapter what necessitates its validity of proofs, God willing.
Source pages p. 670

Commentary
This passage begins by distinguishing between two types of deniers of prophethood: those who deny the Creator entirely (atheists) and those who affirm the Creator but deny prophethood, like the Samaniyya and Brahmins. Nasafi explains that with atheists, the discussion must first focus on proving the Creator. With those who affirm the Creator but deny prophethood, the initial discussion is not about prophethood itself but about proving that transmitted reports can be a source of knowledge, since they deny that. He references earlier content on the means of knowledge. Next, Nasafi states the need to define prophethood before proving it. He gives two definitions: the linguistic definition (transmitting a message) and the technical definition (an embassy from God to rational beings to supply what their intellects cannot reach regarding worldly and otherworldly interests). He affirms that this definition is valid for those familiar with the conditions of definitions. After defining it, he outlines the three-step plan: first prove possibility (imkān), then discuss obligation (wujūb), then establish actual occurrence (thubūt). This sets the structure for the coming chapters.

Misconception: Defining prophethood is sufficient to prove it; no further argument needed.
Correction: Nasafi clearly states that after definition, we must proceed to prove possibility, obligation, and actuality through evidence.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is defined as an embassy from God to supply what reason alone cannot provide, and its proof follows a logical sequence from possibility to obligation to actuality.

?

?
Nasafi outlines a progression from possibility to actuality. In your own belief, do you merely acknowledge that prophethood is possible, or have you fully submitted to its actuality in your daily choices? What step are you missing?

The Rational Status of Prophetic Mission
Presents the dispute on the rational status of prophetic mission and defines 'wajib' as necessarily existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute over whether prophetic mission is rationally possible or necessary, and the precise meaning of 'wajib' in this context.

Core Idea
Prophetic mission is deemed necessary because it follows from divine wisdom, not because God is obligated.

This passage from Tabirat al-Adilla presents a key theological dispute: Is sending prophets rationally possible or necessary? The author clarifies that those who say 'necessary' mean it is inevitably realized due to God's wisdom, not that God is under an obligation. Let's explore this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage and note the two views on prophetic mission: possible (mumkin) or necessary (wajib). Who holds each view?
The majority of mutakallimun of ahl al-hadith hold it is possible, except al-Qalanisi.
2
Understand 'wajib' in context
Those who affirm rational ethics say prophetic mission is wajib. But they do not mean God is obligated. Instead, they mean it is strongly confirmed in existence (mutahaqqiq al-wujud).
The term 'wajib' here indicates inevitable existence, not moral obligation.
3
Trace the reasoning from divine wisdom
The passage argues that prophetic mission is required by divine wisdom (hikma). Since non-existence from wisdom would be foolishness (safah), and foolishness is impossible for God, prophetic mission must exist necessarily.
This is similar to how what God knows will exist becomes necessarily existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالسمع وتخرج العقول من أن تكون آله للاطلاع على ما ذهب إليه جميع متكلمي أهل الحديث سوى أبي العباس القلانسي. هل الرسالة من قبل الممكنات في العقل أم من جملة الواجبات؟ فذهب جميع متكلمي أهل الحديث سوى أبي العباس القلانسي إلى أنها من جملة الممكنات، وذهب القائلون بأن العقل آلة معرفة الحسن والقبيح ووجوب شكر المنعم إلى القول بوجوبها، ولا يعنون بكونها واجبة أنها وجبت على الله بإيجاب أحد أو بإيجابه على نفسه بل يريدون أنها متحققة الوجود، ويقولون: إن الوجوب الحقيقة لفظة بها عن فضل تأكد لوجود المذكور، كما أن الامتناع لفظة يعبر بها عن تأكد لا وجوده، وهما في الحقيقة متقابلان تقابل الأضداد، وإنما يقولون: إنها متأكدة الوجود لأنها من مقتضيات حكمة القديم جل وعلا، ويستحيل أن لا يوجد ما كان وجوده من مقتضيات حكمته لما أن انعدامه يكون من باب السفه وهو يستحيل على القديم عز وجل، هذا كما أن ما علم الله وجوده يتحقق وجوده لا محالة ويكون واجب الوجود أي متأكد الوجود، لا أن وجوبه بإيجاب أحد على الله تعالى بل لأن وجوده يتحقق لا محالة لما أن انعدامه يوجب الجهل به، وهو مما يستحيل على القديم عز وجل وإلى هذا القول ذهب جميع من يقول بوجوب شكر المنعم.
By audition (al-sam') and the intellects come out from being instruments for discerning what all the mutakallimun of the people of hadith except Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi have held. Is the prophetic mission (al-risala) among the possibles (al-mumkinat) in the intellect, or is it among the necessary (al-wajibat)? So all the mutakallimun of the people of hadith except Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi held that it is among the possibles. And those who held that the intellect is an instrument for knowing the good and the bad (al-husn wa al-qubh) and the obligation to thank the benefactor (wujub shukr al-mun'im) went to the saying of its being obligatory (wujub). They do not mean by its being obligatory that it is incumbent upon God due to someone's imposition or His imposing upon Himself, but rather they intend that it is realized in existence (mutahaqqiq al-wujud). They say: 'Obligatory (al-wujub) in reality is an expression for the strong confirmation of existence of the mentioned thing, just as impossibility (al-imtina') is an expression for the strong confirmation of its non-existence, and they are in reality opposites, opposed as contraries.' They only say that it is strongly confirmed in existence because it is from the requirements of the wisdom (hikma) of the Pre-Eternal, Exalted and Majestic. It is impossible that what whose existence is from the requirements of His wisdom should not exist, because its non-existence would be from the category of foolishness (safah), which is impossible for the Pre-Eternal, Mighty and Majestic. This is similar to what God knows to exist: it is realized inevitably and is necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud), i.e., strongly confirmed in existence. Not that its necessity is due to someone imposing on God, but because its existence is realized inevitably since its non-existence would necessitate ignorance of it, which is impossible for the Pre-Eternal. And this view is held by all who affirm the obligation to thank the benefactor.
Source pages p. 676

Commentary
This passage presents and clarifies a fundamental dispute among Islamic theologians regarding the rational status of prophetic mission (al-risala). The author begins by stating that the intellects, when guided by revelation, emerge as instruments for understanding the positions of scholars, specifically all mutakallimun of ahl al-hadith except Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi. He then frames the question: Is prophetic mission rationally possible (mumkin) or necessary (wajib)? The majority, including most ahl al-hadith mutakallimun, hold it is possible. Those who affirm rational ethics (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih) and the obligation to thank the benefactor (wujub shukr al-mun'im) hold it is wajib. Importantly, the author clarifies that these latter scholars do not mean wajib in the sense of an obligation imposed on God, but rather that prophetic mission is necessarily existent (mutahaqqiq al-wujud) due to its being a requirement of God’s wisdom. They explain that 'wajib' here is a term indicating the strong confirmation of existence, just as 'impossible' (mumtani') indicates strong confirmation of non-existence; these two are opposites like contraries. The strong confirmation arises because prophetic mission is a requirement of divine wisdom, and it is impossible for what is required by wisdom not to exist, as that would entail foolishness (safah), which is incompatible with God. This reasoning parallels the case of what God knows will exist: it becomes necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud) in the sense of being inevitably realized, not because anyone imposes it on God, but because its non-existence would imply ignorance in God, which is impossible. Thus, all who uphold the obligation to thank the benefactor hold this view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'wajib' applied to prophetic mission means that God is obligated to send messengers, as if someone imposes a duty on Him.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that 'wajib' here means strongly confirmed in existence (mutahaqqiq al-wujud), not an obligation imposed on God. It is a requirement of divine wisdom, not a duty.

Checkpoint

A) It is an obligation that God must send messengers.
B) It is rationally possible, not necessary.
C) It is strongly confirmed in existence because it follows from divine wisdom.
D) It is impossible for God to not send messengers.

Key Takeaway
The key point is that 'wajib' in this context means necessarily existent due to divine wisdom, not a duty imposed on God, and this understanding resolves the apparent conflict between divine freedom and the necessity of prophetic mission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face situations where you feel God 'should' do something for you, are you imposing your own definition of 'obligation' onto Him? How does this passage's clarification of 'wajib' challenge that assumption?
?
If you truly believed that God's wisdom automatically brings about what is required, how would it change the way you respond to unanswered prayers or delayed relief?
?
The passage equates non-existence of what wisdom requires with foolishness. Where in your life have you failed to act on what wisdom clearly demands, thereby implicitly attributing foolishness to God's design? What will you do about it today?

Attributing Taklif to Other Than God is Invalid
Al-Nasafi argues that taklif necessarily refers to God alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why any acknowledgment of obligation or prohibition logically requires a divine source, and why attributing taklif to other than God is incoherent.

Core Idea
Obligation and prohibition inherently point to God as their sole source.

Al-Nasafi presents a concise logical argument that demonstrates the necessary connection between the concept of obligation (taklif) and God. He shows that anyone who admits something is obligatory, permissible, or prohibited has already implicitly acknowledged a legislator. This passage shows that attributing such authority to anyone other than God is logically self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the two hypothetical positions Al-Nasafi examines.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) someone attributes obligation or permission to other than God, and (2) someone claims an act is prohibited but not obligatory or permissible.
The second scenario is meant to avoid acknowledging obligation, but Al-Nasafi shows it still does.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Al-Nasafi demonstrates that both positions ultimately acknowledge God as the source of taklif. The first is obviously foolish; the second implicitly admits obligation because prohibition implies a command (nahy), which is a form of obligation.
The phrase 'la quwwata illa billah' emphasizes reliance on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرع وتكليف، وإن قال : أوجب ذلك عليه أو أباحه له غير الله تعالى فقد هذى، حيث جعل لغير الله تعالى ولاية التكليف ولم يجعل ذلك الله تعالى فإن قال: هو محظور وليس بواجب ولا مباح فقد أقر أيضا بالتكليف إذ الحظر لن يثبت إلا بالنهي، وهذا يوجب إبطال كلامه، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Legislation and obligation: if he says that someone other than God made it obligatory upon him or permitted it for him, then he has spoken foolishly, since he assigned authority of obligation to other than God, and God did not assign that. If he says: it is prohibited, not obligatory nor permissible, then he has also acknowledged obligation, because prohibition is only established by prohibition (command). This necessitates the invalidation of his statement, and there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage presents a brief logical argument about the concept of taklif (obligation). The author, al-Nasafi, first states two key terms: 'shar' (legislation) and 'taklif' (obligation). He then examines a hypothetical case: if someone claims that an obligation or permission comes from other than God, he has erred because he grants the authority of taklif to a being other than God, and God did not grant that. Alternatively, if the person says the action is prohibited (mahzur) but not obligatory nor permissible, he still implicitly acknowledges taklif because a prohibition only exists through a divine command (nahy). Thus, the person's own admission demonstrates the self-contradiction of his position. The passage concludes with the phrase 'la quwwata illa billah' (there is no power except through God), emphasizing reliance on God's authority. The core idea is that any recognition of obligation or prohibition necessarily points back to God as the sole source of such rulings.

أوجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can logically conceive of obligation or prohibition without reference to a legislator.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that both obligation and prohibition intrinsically imply a legislative authority. To claim something is prohibited without acknowledging a commander is incoherent, because a prohibition only makes sense as a command from a superior.

Checkpoint

A) He avoids any acknowledgment of obligation.
B) He still acknowledges obligation because prohibition requires a command.
C) He has successfully avoided both obligation and prohibition.
D) He must be a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
Any talk of obligation or prohibition unavoidably points to God as the sole legislator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you discover it is not from God, will you abandon it? If it is from God, will you commit to following it solely for His sake, even if others reject it?
?
When you feel obligated to do something, do you instinctively ask, 'Has God commanded this?' or do you accept obligations from human authorities without hesitation? What does this passage expose about the origin of all genuine obligation? Today, consciously choose one action you will perform only because God commanded it—not because of habit or human approval.

How does this passage challenge you to align your understanding of right and wrong with divine revelation? What is one specific prohibition from God that you have been avoiding? Will you now embrace it as coming from the only true Legislator?

The Linguistic Derivation of Mu'jiza
Linguistic derivation of mu'jiza from 'ajz and hyperbolic form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic roots of the term mu'jiza and why the ta' marbuta indicates exaggeration.

Core Idea
The miracle is named for the inability it creates in the opponent.

The author begins by explaining the basis of the term miracle. He traces it to the word 'ajz (inability) and shows how the morphology of the word emphasizes the inability of those challenged. This linguistic analysis sets the stage for the technical definitions that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on linguistic derivation
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Notice how the author explains the origin of the word 'mu'jiza' from 'ajz (inability).
The term is built on the pattern of exaggeration.
2
Identify the relationship between mu'jiza and 'ajz
Understand that the miracle is called mu'jiza because it manifests the inability of the opponent to oppose it. The author emphasizes this causal link.
3
Understand the function of the ta' marbuta
The author explains that the 'ha' (ta' marbuta) is for exaggeration, like in 'allama (great scholar). This intensifies the meaning of inability.
This morphological nuance is important for precise understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل يسمى معجزة وهو في نفسه ينقسم إلى قسمين فيحتاج إلى القول في مأخذها وحدها وبيان قسميها ووجه كيفية دلالتها على صدق الآتي بها. فأما مأخذها فهو العجز الذي هو نقيض القدرة سميت معجزة لأنها تظهر عجز من يتحدى به عن معارضتها ثم المعجز في الحقيقة وإن كان اسماً لمثبت العجز كالمقدر اسم لمثبت القدرة إلا أن المظهر للعجز يسمى به مجازا، ثم الهاء الداخلة في لفظها للمبالغة، كما في العلامة والنسابة والراوية، فكانت داخلة فيها للمبالغة في الخبر عن عجز المرسل إليهم. وحدها على رأي الفلاسفة: أنها هي الفعل الجزئي المحكم الإلهي الذي يفوت منتهى القوة الطبيعية والقوى النفسانية. وحدها على طريقة المتكلمين أنها ظهور أمر خلاف العادة في دار التكليف لإظهار صدق مدعي النبوة مع نكول من يتحدى به عن معارضته بمثله. وإنما قيد بدار التكليف؛ لأن ما يظهره الله تعالى في الآخرة من الأمور الخارجة عن العادة، ليست بمعجزة، وإنما قلنا: لإظهار صدق مدعي النبوة ليقع الاحتراز به عما يظهر على يد مدعي الألوهية، إذ ظهور نقض المادة على يده جائز على ما نبين في خلال كلامنا - إن شاء الله تعالى - وعما يظهر على يدي الولي، لأن ظهور الناقض للعادة على يد الولي كرامة له جائز على ما نبين، إلا أنه لا يدعي النبوة، ولو ادعاها لكفر من ساعته وبطلت ولايته وصار عدوا الله، فلا تظهر على يده الكرامة بعد ذلك.
Then the proof is called a miracle (mu'jiza), and it is in itself divided into two parts, so one needs to discuss its basis and its definition, and the explanation of its two types, and the manner of its indication of the truthfulness of the one who brings it. As for its basis, it is inability ('ajz), which is the opposite of ability (qudra). It is called a miracle because it manifests the inability of the one challenged to oppose it with the like of it. Then the miraculous thing (al-mu'jiz) in reality, although it is a name for one who establishes inability, just as al-muqaddir is a name for one who establishes ability, except that the one who manifests inability is called by it metaphorically. Then the 'ha' (ta' marbuta) entering into its word is for exaggeration (mubalagha), as in al-'allama, al-nassaba, al-rawiya. So it enters into it for exaggeration in reporting about the inability of those to whom he is sent. Its definition according to the philosophers (falasifa) is that it is the particular, perfectly ordered divine act that surpasses the utmost of natural power and psychic powers. Its definition according to the method of the theologians (mutakallimun) is that it is the appearance of something contrary to custom (khariq li-l-'ada) in the abode of obligation (dar al-taklif) to demonstrate the truthfulness of the claimant of prophethood, with the withdrawal of the one challenged from opposing it with its like. It is restricted to the abode of obligation because what Allah the Exalted manifests in the Hereafter of things contrary to custom are not miracles. And we said 'to demonstrate the truthfulness of the claimant of prophethood' so that by it there is avoidance of what appears at the hand of a claimant of divinity, for the appearance of a breach of custom at his hand is possible as we will explain in the course of our speech – if Allah wills – and of what appears at the hands of a saint (wali), because the appearance of a breach of custom at the hand of a saint is a miracle of friendship (karama) for him, permissible as we will explain, except that he does not claim prophethood. If he claimed it, he would become an unbeliever instantly, his sainthood would be invalidated, and he would become an enemy of Allah, so the karama would not appear at his hand after that.
Source pages p. 694

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. The author begins by stating that the proof of prophethood is called a miracle, and it is divided into two types (this division is not elaborated here). He then explains the linguistic basis: the word mu'jiza comes from 'ajz (inability), the opposite of qudra (ability). The term is hyperbolic (like 'allama) to emphasize the inability of the opponents. Next, the author gives two definitions: First, the philosophers' definition: a divine act that surpasses natural and psychic powers. Second, the theologians' definition: a breach of custom (khariq li-l-'ada) in dar al-taklif (the abode of obligation, i.e., this world) to prove the truthfulness of a claimant of prophethood, along with the challenger's inability to produce its like. The author then explains why it is restricted to dar al-taklif: miraculous events in the afterlife are not miracles. He also distinguishes miracles from the acts of a claimant of divinity (which are possible but not for proving prophethood) and from the karama of a saint, which is a breach of custom but not a miracle because the saint does not claim prophethood. If a saint claimed prophethood, his karama would cease and he would become an unbeliever.

The term mu'jiza derives from 'ajz (inability) and its hyperbolic form emphasizes the opponent's inability.

The Two Definitions of Miracle: Philosophers and Theologians
Presents two definitions of miracle and clarifies conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main definitions of miracle and the conditions that distinguish it from other supernatural events.

Core Idea
A miracle is a divine act that breaks custom to prove prophethood in this world.

After explaining the linguistic basis, the author presents two technical definitions: one from the philosophers and one from the theologians. The theologians' definition is more detailed and includes restrictions that avoid confusion with other extraordinary events. This lesson examines both definitions and the purpose of the conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' definition
The philosophers define miracle as a 'particular, perfectly ordered divine act that surpasses the utmost of natural power and psychic powers.' Notice the emphasis on divine origin and surpassing natural abilities.
2
Read the theologians' definition
The theologians define it as 'the appearance of something contrary to custom in the abode of obligation to demonstrate the truthfulness of the claimant of prophethood, with the withdrawal of the one challenged.' Pay attention to each component.
3
Understand the condition of dar al-taklif
The author restricts miracles to the abode of obligation (this world). Events in the afterlife, though extraordinary, are not miracles because they serve a different purpose.
4
Understand the distinction from other claims
The definition excludes events done by a claimant of divinity and by a saint (wali). A saint's karama is a breach of custom but not a miracle because he does not claim prophethood.
If a saint claims prophethood, his karama ceases and he becomes an unbeliever.
5
Reflect on the significance of these conditions
Consider why the theologians included these conditions: to avoid confusion and to maintain that miracles are specific proofs for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل يسمى معجزة وهو في نفسه ينقسم إلى قسمين فيحتاج إلى القول في مأخذها وحدها وبيان قسميها ووجه كيفية دلالتها على صدق الآتي بها. فأما مأخذها فهو العجز الذي هو نقيض القدرة سميت معجزة لأنها تظهر عجز من يتحدى به عن معارضتها ثم المعجز في الحقيقة وإن كان اسماً لمثبت العجز كالمقدر اسم لمثبت القدرة إلا أن المظهر للعجز يسمى به مجازا، ثم الهاء الداخلة في لفظها للمبالغة، كما في العلامة والنسابة والراوية، فكانت داخلة فيها للمبالغة في الخبر عن عجز المرسل إليهم. وحدها على رأي الفلاسفة: أنها هي الفعل الجزئي المحكم الإلهي الذي يفوت منتهى القوة الطبيعية والقوى النفسانية. وحدها على طريقة المتكلمين أنها ظهور أمر خلاف العادة في دار التكليف لإظهار صدق مدعي النبوة مع نكول من يتحدى به عن معارضته بمثله. وإنما قيد بدار التكليف؛ لأن ما يظهره الله تعالى في الآخرة من الأمور الخارجة عن العادة، ليست بمعجزة، وإنما قلنا: لإظهار صدق مدعي النبوة ليقع الاحتراز به عما يظهر على يد مدعي الألوهية، إذ ظهور نقض المادة على يده جائز على ما نبين في خلال كلامنا - إن شاء الله تعالى - وعما يظهر على يدي الولي، لأن ظهور الناقض للعادة على يد الولي كرامة له جائز على ما نبين، إلا أنه لا يدعي النبوة، ولو ادعاها لكفر من ساعته وبطلت ولايته وصار عدوا الله، فلا تظهر على يده الكرامة بعد ذلك.
Then the proof is called a miracle (mu'jiza), and it is in itself divided into two parts, so one needs to discuss its basis and its definition, and the explanation of its two types, and the manner of its indication of the truthfulness of the one who brings it. As for its basis, it is inability ('ajz), which is the opposite of ability (qudra). It is called a miracle because it manifests the inability of the one challenged to oppose it with the like of it. Then the miraculous thing (al-mu'jiz) in reality, although it is a name for one who establishes inability, just as al-muqaddir is a name for one who establishes ability, except that the one who manifests inability is called by it metaphorically. Then the 'ha' (ta' marbuta) entering into its word is for exaggeration (mubalagha), as in al-'allama, al-nassaba, al-rawiya. So it enters into it for exaggeration in reporting about the inability of those to whom he is sent. Its definition according to the philosophers (falasifa) is that it is the particular, perfectly ordered divine act that surpasses the utmost of natural power and psychic powers. Its definition according to the method of the theologians (mutakallimun) is that it is the appearance of something contrary to custom (khariq li-l-'ada) in the abode of obligation (dar al-taklif) to demonstrate the truthfulness of the claimant of prophethood, with the withdrawal of the one challenged from opposing it with its like. It is restricted to the abode of obligation because what Allah the Exalted manifests in the Hereafter of things contrary to custom are not miracles. And we said 'to demonstrate the truthfulness of the claimant of prophethood' so that by it there is avoidance of what appears at the hand of a claimant of divinity, for the appearance of a breach of custom at his hand is possible as we will explain in the course of our speech – if Allah wills – and of what appears at the hands of a saint (wali), because the appearance of a breach of custom at the hand of a saint is a miracle of friendship (karama) for him, permissible as we will explain, except that he does not claim prophethood. If he claimed it, he would become an unbeliever instantly, his sainthood would be invalidated, and he would become an enemy of Allah, so the karama would not appear at his hand after that.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. The author begins by stating that the proof of prophethood is called a miracle, and it is divided into two types (this division is not elaborated here). He then explains the linguistic basis: the word mu'jiza comes from 'ajz (inability), the opposite of qudra (ability). The author then explains why it is restricted to dar al-taklif: miraculous events in the afterlife are not miracles. He also distinguishes miracles from the acts of a claimant of divinity (which are possible but not for proving prophethood) and from the karama of a saint, which is a breach of custom but not a miracle because the saint does not claim prophethood. If a saint claimed prophethood, his karama would cease and he would become an unbeliever.

The Linguistic Derivation and Intensive Form of Mu'jiz
Al-Nasafi clarifies the linguistic meaning of mu'jiz and its intensive form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the term mu'jiz and the role of the intensive form in emphasizing the incapability of the recipients of a miracle.

Core Idea
The 'ha' in mu'jiza is not feminine but an intensive form emphasizing the miracle's power to show incapability.

Before we discuss the theological definition of a miracle, Al-Nasafi first clarifies the linguistic meaning of the term mu'jiz. He explains its derivation and the significance of the feminine ta' that is actually an intensive. This linguistic grounding is essential for understanding the precise theological usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the linguistic analysis. Notice the comparison with muqaddir and the examples.
2
Identify the two linguistic points
First, the literal vs. metaphorical use of mu'jiz. Second, the function of the ha' as intensification.
The author gives clear analogies.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Why does Al-Nasafi begin with this linguistic analysis? Consider how precise language matters in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المعجز في الحقيقة وإن كان اسماً لمثبت العجز كالمقدر اسم لمثبت القدرة إلا أن المظهر للعجز يسمى به مجازا، ثم الهاء الداخلة في لفظها للمبالغة، كما في العلامة والنسابة والراوية، فكانت داخلة فيها للمبالغة في الخبر عن عجز المرسل إليهم.
Then the miraculous (al-muʿjiz) in reality, even though it is a name for that which establishes incapability (al-ʿajz) just as al-muqaddir is a name for that which establishes power (al-qudra), except that the one who manifests incapability is called by it metaphorically. Then the letter hāʾ that enters its form is for intensification (al-mubālagha), as in al-ʿallāma, al-nassāba, and al-rāwiya. Thus it enters it for intensification in the report concerning the incapability of those to whom he is sent.
Source pages p. 695

Commentary
This passage examines the linguistic derivation of the term mu'jiz (miraculous). The author explains that mu'jiz is literally a name for that which establishes another's incapability (ʿajz), analogous to muqaddir which establishes power. However, the word is used metaphorically for the thing that manifests incapability. He then notes that the feminine tā' (hā') at the end of the word (muʿjiza) is for intensification (mubālagha), similar to words like ʿallāma (great scholar), nassāba (expert genealogist), and rāwiya (great narrator). The purpose of this intensive form is to emphasize the report of the incapability of those who are challenged by the miracle.

العجز
المبالغة
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the 'ta' in mu'jiza indicates femininity.
Correction: The 'ha' is actually for intensification, as the author shows with examples like 'allama and nassaba.

C) To indicate a single instance

Key Takeaway
The linguistic form of mu'jiz emphasizes the intense incapability of those faced with a miracle, which is central to its miraculous nature.

Two Definitions of Miracle: Philosophers and Theologians
Al-Nasafi presents two definitions of miracle and explains each qualification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of miracle (muʿjiza) according to philosophers and theologians, and the key conditions that distinguish a true miracle from other extraordinary events.

Core Idea
A miracle is not just any wonder; it must meet specific conditions to prove prophethood.

In this passage, al-Nasafi provides two competing definitions of miracle. The philosophers focus on the act's divine origin and surpassing natural powers, while the theologians emphasize its role in confirming a prophet's truthfulness under specific conditions. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping the Islamic theology of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the two definitions
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the concise philosophical definition and the more detailed theological definition.
The philosophical definition is shorter; the theological definition includes conditions.
2
Analyze the theological conditions
Identify the four qualifications in the theological definition: (1) 'contrary to custom', (2) 'in the abode of obligation', (3) 'to manifest truthfulness', (4) 'with the non-response of the challenged'. Understand why each condition is necessary.
Each condition excludes a potential counterexample.
3
Examine the example of the false prophet
Read the example of the false prophet whose claimed miracle is used to expose his lie. Notice how this illustrates the condition 'to manifest truthfulness'.
The same event can be a miracle for the true prophet but not for the false claimant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحدها على رأي الفلاسفة: أنها هي الفعل الجزئي المحكم الإلهي الذي يفوت منتهى القوة الطبيعية والقوى النفسانية. وحدها على طريقة المتكلمين أنها ظهور أمر خلاف العادة في دار التكليف لإظهار صدق مدعي النبوة مع نكول من يتحدى به عن معارضته بمثله. وإنما قيد بدار التكليف؛ لأن ما يظهره الله تعالى في الآخرة من الأمور الخارجة عن العادة، ليست بمعجزة، وإنما قلنا لإظهار صدق مدعي النبوة ليقع الاحتراز به عما يظهر على يد مدعي الألوهية، إذ ظهور نقض العادة على يده جائز على ما نبين في خلال كلامنا إن شاء الله تعالى وعما يظهر على يدي الولي، لأن ظهور الناقض للعادة على يد الولي كرامة له جائز على ما نبين، إلا أنه لا يدعي النبوة، ولو ادعاها لكفر من ساعته وبطلت ولايته وصار عدوا الله، فلا تظهر على يده الكرامة بعد ذلك. وإنما قدمنا بقولنا لإظهار الصدق، فإنه لو ظهر لإظهار كذبه لا يكون معجزة. كما لو ادعى المتنبئ أن معجزته نطق هذه الشجرة فأنطق الله تعالى تلك بتكذيبه، لا يكون ذلك معجزة له ودليلاً لصدقه، بل يكون دليلاً على كذبه. وإنما قلنا مع نكول من يتحدى به عن معارضته بمثله، لأنه لو ظهر الناقض للعادة على يدي مدعي النبوة ثم ظهر مثله على يدي المتحدى به، لخرج عند المعارضة عن الدلالة إذ ذاك كذبه في دعواه وظهر له مثل دليله فصار دليلاً يدعي على الشيء وضده، وما هذا سبيله لا يكون دليلاً.
And its definition according to the philosophers: it is the particular divine well-ordered act that surpasses the utmost limit of natural power and psychical powers. And its definition according to the method of the theologians (al-mutakallimūn): it is the emergence of something contrary to custom (khilāf al-ʿāda) in the abode of obligation (dār al-taklīf) to manifest the truthfulness of one who claims prophethood, with the non-response (nukūl) of the one challenged to oppose it with its like. And it was restricted by 'abode of obligation' because what Allah Almighty manifests in the Hereafter of matters that are beyond custom are not miracles. And we said 'to manifest the truthfulness of one who claims prophethood' in order to avoid including what appears at the hands of one who claims divinity, since the emergence of breaking custom at his hand is possible as we will explain in the course of our speech, Allah willing, and to avoid what appears at the hands of the saint (walī), because the emergence of what breaks custom at the hand of the saint is a miracle of grace (karāma) for him, possible as we will explain, except that he does not claim prophethood, and if he claimed it, he would become an unbeliever instantly and his sainthood would be nullified and he would become an enemy of Allah, so no miracle of grace would appear at his hand after that. And we prefaced our statement 'to manifest truthfulness' because if it were to manifest his falsehood, it would not be a miracle. Just as if a false prophet (mutanabbī) claimed that his miracle is the speech of this tree, and Allah made it speak to deny him, that would not be a miracle for him nor a proof of his truthfulness, but rather a proof of his falsehood. And we said 'with the non-response of the one challenged to oppose it with its like' because if what breaks custom appears at the hands of the claimant of prophethood, then its like appears at the hands of the one challenged, it would exit from being an indication at the time of opposition, because then his claim would be false and its like would appear as his proof, so it would become a proof that claims both the thing and its opposite, and whatever has this property cannot be a proof.
Source pages p. 695

Commentary
This passage provides two definitions of a miracle (muʿjiza). First, the philosophers define it as a divine act that surpasses natural and psychical powers. Second, the theologians define it as an extraordinary event occurring in the abode of obligation to confirm the truth of a prophet, accompanied by the inability of the challenged to produce its like. The author then explains each qualification. 'Abode of obligation' excludes events in the afterlife. 'To manifest truthfulness' excludes events at the hands of false gods or saints (since saints do not claim prophethood). 'Non-response of the challenged' ensures that if someone could reproduce it, the miracle would lose its indicative power. An example illustrates that if a false prophet's claimed miracle is used to expose his falsehood, it is not a miracle for him. The final condition prevents the possibility of counter-challenge invalidating the proof.

Key Terms
المعجزة
الفلاسفة
المتكلمين
خلاف العادة
دار التكليف
النكول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think any extraordinary event, including magic or karamat of saints, qualifies as a miracle.
Correction: The theological definition explicitly excludes events from saints (karamat) and false gods by requiring the event to confirm the truthfulness of a prophet and to occur in the abode of obligation with a challenge unmet.

Checkpoint
Why is the condition 'with the non-response of the challenged' necessary?
A) To ensure the miracle is beyond human power
B) To prevent someone from reproducing it and thus nullifying its evidence
C) To show that the miracle is performed by God
D) To indicate that the miracle must be witnessed by many

Key Takeaway
A true miracle (muʿjiza) must be extraordinary, occur in this world, confirm a prophet's truth, and be unchallenged by anyone able to replicate it.

?

?
The condition of 'non-response of the challenged' means no one can replicate the miracle. Think of a time you doubted a truth of Islam because you thought of a possible alternate explanation. Are you willing to humbly admit that you may be the 'challenger' who cannot produce a similar proof? What would it take for you to stop rationalizing and submit to the evidence?
?

Two Types of Miracles
Al-Nasafi divides miracles into two types and explains their common nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's division of miracles into two categories and why both are considered breaches of custom.

Core Idea
A miracle can be doing the unusual or preventing the usual; both break custom.

Al-Nasafi now explains that miracles are not monolithic; they come in two distinct forms. The first is performing an act outside normal experience, and the second is preventing a normal act from occurring. Both are ways in which the natural order is suspended, confirming the extraordinary nature of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the author's exact wording before analysis.
Notice how al-Nasafi uses 'two types' (قسمين) to structure his discussion.
2
Identify the first type
The first type is 'an act not customary' – something unprecedented. Think of examples like the staff turning into a snake.
3
Identify the second type
The second type is 'incapacitation from a customary act' – preventing a normal event from happening. Example: fire not burning Ibrahim (AS).
4
Reflect on the key point
Both types are breaches of custom. Understand why prevention is also a breach: it goes against what normally occurs.
This connects the two types under the single category of khāriq li-l-ʿāda.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما انقسامه فإلى قسمين: أحدهما: فعل غير معتاد. والثاني: تعجيز عن الفعل المعتاد، وهذا أيضا في الحقيقة نقض العادة إذ المنع عن المعتاد نقض للعادة وخروج عنها.
As for its division, it is into two types: one of them: an act not customary. And the second: the incapacitation from the customary act. And this also is in reality a breach of custom, because prevention from the customary is a breach of custom and an exit from it.
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage classifies miracles into two kinds. The first is an act that is not customary (ghayr mu'tād), such as a sudden new event. The second is the prevention (taʿjīz) from a customary act, meaning that something normally possible becomes impossible as a miracle. The author points out that even this second type is also a breach of custom (naqḍ al-ʿāda) because preventing something that usually occurs goes against the normal course of things. Thus both types are considered khāriq li-l-ʿāda (extraordinary).

Key Terms
انقسام
فعل غير معتاد
تعجيز
نقض العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think miracles only involve doing something unprecedented, not preventing normal acts.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explicitly includes prevention as a second type, and explains that it too is a breach of custom.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, how many types of miracles are there?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Miracles are of two types: doing the unprecedented or preventing the usual; both are breaches of custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there customary acts in your life—like waking up, walking, or eating—that you take for granted as guaranteed? What would it mean for your faith if Allah prevented them from happening, and how would you respond?
?
If miracles are breaches of custom, do you live as though Allah can interrupt your normal life at any moment? What would change in your daily routine if you truly believed that?
?
The second type of miracle shows that even the usual is not independent of Allah. Do you act as if your daily routines are automatic, or do you consciously acknowledge Allah's constant maintenance of them? What can you do today to recognize His power in the ordinary?

Two Kinds of Prophetic Signs
Classification of prophetic signs into sensory and rational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main categories of prophetic signs—sensory and rational—and how sensory signs are defined as extraordinary phenomena.

Core Idea
Prophetic signs are either seen or reasoned.

Before delving into the miracles of the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ, the author establishes a foundational classification. He divides all signs proving prophethood into two types: those perceived by the senses and those grasped by reason. This lesson examines the first category and its definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the clear, categorical language the author uses to introduce the two divisions.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two divisions
The author states that the signs are divided 'first into two divisions'. Find the names of these two categories in the text.
The terms are 'sensory' (ḥissiyya) and 'rational' (ʿaqliyya).
3
Understand the definition of sensory signs
The author defines sensory signs as wonders Allah causes to appear to the eyes that diverge from the normal course of nature and habit. Ask yourself: what makes a sign 'sensory'?
This definition sets the stage for later discussion of miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آيات رسالته لا تنقسم أولا إلى قسمين حسية وعقلية. أما الحسية منها: وهي ما يظهره الله تعالى للأعين من العجائب المباينة لمجرى الطبائع والبدائع المفارقة للمعهود من العادة وكان له من ذلك ما لا يحصى كثرة.
The signs of his message are divided first into two divisions: sensory and rational. As for the sensory ones: they are what Allah the Exalted causes to appear to the eyes of wonders that diverge from the course of natures and marvels that depart from what is customary in habit, and he had an incalculable abundance of that.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage provides the initial classification of the signs (āyāt) that prove the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him). The author states that these signs are first divided into two categories: sensory (ḥissiyya) and rational (ʿaqliyya). He then defines the sensory signs: they are extraordinary events that Allah causes to be seen with the eyes, which are contrary to the normal course of nature and habitual custom. He emphasizes that the Prophet was given an incalculable number of such signs.

Repent and ask Allah to open your eyes to His signs.
